Rabbinic Authority V

Preface

The present volume is the fifth in a series of volumes intended as an introduction to a subject perhaps unfamiliar to many – the dynamics of rabbinic authority in our <i>halakhic</i> tradition. The subtitle, “The Vision and the Reality,” points to the themes being addressed in this work.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As we know, <i>Halakhah</i> distinguishes between the theoretical law, which emerges from an abstract study of the sources of <i>Halakhah</i>, and the rule that is applied in a particular factual context, i.e. <i>Halakhah lema’aseh</i>. See <i>Bava Batra</i> 130b and <i>Talmud Yerushalmi Beitzah</i> 2:1 (R. Yohanan’s statements). However, whereas “the vision” portion of the presentation deals with the decisions of <i>Halakhic</i> authorities as memorialized in <i>sifrei pesak</i> (restatements of <i>Halakhah</i>) and <i>sifrei teshuvot</i> (responsa), “the reality” portion of our presentation focuses upon the <i>halakhic</i>-judicial rulings of a <i>beit din</i>.</i>
This volume, following in the footsteps of volumes three and four of our series entitled Rabbinic Authority, deals with <i>halakhic</i> divorce in general and with the varying solutions offered throughout the centuries by rabbinic authorities dealing with the plight of the modern-day <i>agunah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Whether a woman is to be labeled an <i>agunah</i> is a consequence of a rabbi or a <i>beit din</i> determining that there is a ground (<i>ilat gerushin</i>) to coerce or obligate a <i>get</i>. Upon a wife claiming and proving that the physical defect of the husband, such as impotence or having a contagious disease such as AIDS, the husband’s conduct such as his failure to perform conjugal relations or spousal abuse, his violation of a basic precept of <i>Halakhah</i> such as engaging in spousal and/or child abuse, or causing his wife to transgress a provision of <i>Halakhah,</i> is the ground(s) for her request for a <i>get</i>, a <i>beit din</i> will issue a divorce judgment.<br>Alternatively, in the absence of a ground for divorce, should a couple be separated from each other for one year, and according to other authorities for eighteen months, with no prospects for marital reconciliation (<i>shalom bayit</i>), a <i>beit din</i> may decide that the giving of a <i>get</i> is <i>halakhically</i> proper. In such a situation, the identification of the woman as an <i>agunah</i> may serve as grounds under certain conditions to compel or obligate a <i>get</i>. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>: <i>The Vision and the Reality</i> (hereinafter: <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>) vol. 3, 328–33, vol. 4, 143–161.<br>Once a divorce ruling is handed down, the husband is obligated to adhere immediately to the judgment and give a <i>get</i>. In the event that the <i>get</i> is not forthcoming, the woman is deemed an <i>agunah</i>.<br>See also <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 66, 103; <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, Bayit 3; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 8; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua EH</i> 80; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:151 (1).</i> the married woman who is unable to receive a <i>get</i>, a Jewish writ of divorce, from her husband, in particular.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Dating back to the Middle Ages, decisors were addressing solutions to void a marriage of an <i>agunah</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Rashi</i>, Elfenbein ed., 198; <i>Teshuvot Ba’alei ha-Tosafot</i> 133; <i>Ohr Zarua</i> 1:761 in the name of Rabbi Simha of Speyers; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:130.</i> In our earlier presentations we have not only examined techniques which have been advanced for centuries for voiding a marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 134–176, 212–345, vol. 4, 143–296. As we have noted elsewhere, we are examining the notion of voiding (<i>bittul kiddushin</i>) rather than annulling (<i>hafka’at kiddushin</i>) a Jewish betrothal (loosely translated throughout the monograph: a Jewish marriage). See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 135, n. 1. See also, File no. 905457/10, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, September 11, 2017 who astutely distinguishes between the two concepts. See the addendum. Cf. Rabbi Berlin who utilizes both concepts interchangeably in the same responsum. See <i>Teshuvot Nishmat Hayyim</i> 126.<br>For some of the rabbinical court rulings given under Israel’s Chief Rabbinate which address the merits of voiding a marriage due to a major defect in the husband, see <i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR)</i> 3:225, 10:241, 15:1, 20:239, File no. 1-14-1393, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, March 5, 2003; File no. 1-22-1510, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, September 7, 2004; File no. 306044470-21-4, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, June 10, 2008; File no. 2433-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, February 3, 2010; File no. 589138/2, Haifa Regional Beit Din, June 7, 2011; File no. 861252/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, January 23, 2012; File no. 373701/10, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, March 12, 2012; File no. 271091/10, Netanya Regional Beit Din, July 2, 2012; File no. 861974/1, Tzfat Regional Beit Din, January 21, 2013; File no. 917387/1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, November 17, 2013; File no. 914652/2, Petah Tikvah Regional Beit Din, March 24, 2014; File no. 861974/2, Tzfat Regional Beit Din, May 20, 2014; File no. 996047/2, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, December 25, 2014; File no. 870175/4, Haifa Regional Beit Din, December 29, 2014; File no. 1011498, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, May 10, 2015; File no. 818315/7, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, September 21, 2015; File no. 1061015/2, Haifa Regional Beit Din, February 16, 2016; File no. 1068330/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, January 12, 2017; File no. 932006/1, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, February 1, 2017; File no. 1097040/10, Haifa Regional Beit Din, June 6, 2017; File no. 905457/10, Tel Aviv-Yaffo, September 11, 2017; File no. 1064682/4, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, September 12, 2017; File no. 1097040/10, Haifa Regional Beit Din, November 6, 2017; File no. 989812/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, February 2, 2018 (<i>mamzerut</i>); Haifa Regional Beit Din, 5 Tammuz 5778 (Rabbis Shloush, Edrei and Hazan- unpublished opinion); File 1227676/1, Ashdod Regional Beit Din, August 1, 2019. For drawing <i>halakhic</i> analogies from a case of a wife’s defects to a situation of a husband’s defects, see File no. 1129170/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 18, 2018.<br>For additional Israeli <i>beit din</i> decisions relating to voiding a marriage, see <i>Shurat ha-Din</i>, volumes 1–17 and <i>ha-Din veha-Dayan</i>, volumes 1–50.<br>Some contend that seeking solutions to matters of <i>get</i> recalcitrance is to be relegated only to Torah luminaries who are “giants of their generation” (<i>gedolei ha-dor</i>) such as Rabbi Yitzhak E. Spektor and Rabbi Yosef Baer Soloveitchik. See Rabbi A. Sherman, <i>Al She’veir bat Ami</i>, 5779, 45–51. Clearly a review of the above Israeli rabbinical court decisions will demonstrate that the majority of rabbis who served as arbiters (<i>dayanim</i>) for these cases were <i>halakhically</i> credentialed to address these issues without being viewed as “Torah giants” by our community in general and by other rabbis in particular. See <i>Teshuvot Divrei Emet</i> 9, <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>EH</i> 2, Teshuvot Nosafot 9 (Machon Yerushalayim ed.) and see further this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 21, n.28.<br>For the qualifications for being a credentialed <i>halakhic</i> arbiter, see <i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Sanhedrin</i> 4:6; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 25:2; Maharshal, <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i>, Introduction, <i>Hullin</i>, First Introduction, Second Introduction; <i>Maharal</i>, <i>Netivot Olam,</i> Netiv ha-Torah, Perek 15; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, Introduction, <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe</i>, Introduction to <i>OH</i>, <i>YD</i> 1:101. See the addendum.<br>Furthermore, the majority of authorities contend that it is a practice (a <i>nohag</i>) rather than a <i>halakhic</i> duty to enlist the support of an outside rabbinic decisor(s) prior to a <i>beit din</i> or three rabbis rendering a decision regarding marriage and divorce in general and voiding a marriage in particular. See Rabbi Refael Shlomo Daichovsky, “May a <i>dayan</i> retract his decision?” (Hebrew) 37 <i>Tehumin</i> 343, 345 (5777) and this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 256–262.</i> we equally exposed the reader to the <i>halakhic</i> authorities who opposed these solutions for varying reasons.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 14–16, 139–140, vol. 4, 159, 185, 191, 200, 213, 246, 265, 283.</i> Whereas in the previous two volumes we have advanced argumentation to validate these solutions, this volume is devoted primarily to demonstrate how a <i>beit din or a</i> rabbinical <i>authority (ies</i>) <i>may void a marriage based upon those decisors who reject the varying options to void the marriage in principle.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It is a matter of dispute whether one requires a <i>beit din</i> of three, a single rabbi or an individual Jew to address matters of divorce such as <i>get</i> coercion, obligating a <i>get</i> and voiding a marriage. See <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i>, Pesakim u-Ketavim 139; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 17:39; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, ad. locum 78; <i>Beit Shmuel,</i> ad. locum 124; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA EH</i> 17:131; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 3:1–2; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 3:1; <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 168; <i>Teshuvot Yehuda</i> (Gordin), <i>EH</i> 51:2; <i>Teshuvot Ma’aseh Hiyah</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:54, 64–65<i>, HM</i> 177; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 167:1, 178:2; <i>PDR</i> 6:265, 269; File 957–61, Beit Din Yerushalayim for Monetary Matters and Yuhasin, vol. 7, 515; File no. 448866/3, Tel Aviv- Yaffo Regional Beit Din, July 11, 2013; File no. 1086123/1, Be’er Sheva Regional Beit Din, December 20, 2018; S. Tzvi Gartner, <i>Kefiyah be-Get</i>, 25–44; Z.N. Goldberg, <i>Lev Mishpat</i> 1, 149–150.<br>Yet we should note that there is at least one decisor who mandates that the individual Jew must be “important and extraordinary” (<i>hashuv u-muflug</i>). See <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i> 3:9; <i>Ma’aseh Hiyah</i>, op. cit.; File no. 448866/3, op.cit.<br>In the absence of a party attending a divorce proceeding regarding the matter of a <i>get</i> rather than a divorce-related matter, the <i>beit din</i> or rabbinic authority(ies) may render a decision provided that the participating party is known to possess integrity and would not lie. See <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah</i>, <i>Tur HM</i> 17:19. See infra 4H and 4J. See further, this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 216, n. 2.</i> By factoring into the consideration those who oppose voiding a marriage, we may invoke, under certain conditions, a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (a <i>sefeik sefeika de’dina</i>) which may serve as the vehicle to free a wife to remarry without the execution of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbis Yosef Ibn Lev and Ovadiah Yosef argue that a sequence of <i>halakhic</i> uncertainties due to the existence at least two <i>halakhic</i> controversies should be construed as a single doubt, namely one uncertainty regarding <i>Halakhah</i> and therefore they rule stringently. See <i>Teshuvot Mahari Ibn Lev</i> 2:9, 3:41, 51; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6(5), 4, <i>EH</i> 5(11). For a listing of numerous arbiters who reject the above posture, see Rabbi Avraham Abukara, <i>Ben Avraham</i>, Beit ha-Safek, 32; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, op. cit.<br>In other words, the implicit premise of our study is that we may view two <i>halakhic</i> controversies as a <i>double halakhic</i> doubt and <i>may</i> rule leniently and it is not to be viewed as one name (<i>shem a’had</i>). See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4<i>, EH</i> 5(11), 6, <i>EH</i> 6(5). For additional authorities who recognize a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, see <i>infra</i> chapter 2, n. 23.<br>Furthermore, a second implicit premise of our study is that rabbis or a <i>beit din</i> may under certain conditions choose a path of leniency by <i>ab initio</i> (<i>le-hathilah</i>) voiding a marriage by invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt. See R. S. Emarliv, <i>Teshuvot Kerem Shlomo EH</i> 25(77c); R. M. Emarliv, <i>Teshuvot Devar Moshe</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 31(59c); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>OH</i> 42(2); R. Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi</i>, Yerushalayim, 5773, 50–51, 131, n. 18.<br>Though there are authorities who only subscribe to employing a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage in an <i>ex post facto</i> situation (<i>be’di’avad</i>), clearly in an <i>agunah</i> case which has been characterized in numerous circumstances as “an hour of emergency” and therefore is <i>halakhically</i> construed as an <i>ex post facto</i> situation (see <i>Teshuvot ha-Reim</i> 1:36; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22(25); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov EH</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Agudat Ezov Midbari EH</i> 9) they would concur and sanction under certain conditions the utilization of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage. Though the cited responsa address the case of the classic <i>agunah</i> when the whereabouts of the husband are unknown, nonetheless, arbiters employ identical terms such as “an hour of emergency” regarding the modern day <i>agunah</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6(end).</i>
For example, there is a <i>halakhic</i> debate whether, if a <i>get</i>-recalcitrant husband fails to disclose prior to marriage that he has a major defect such as being impotent, gay or mentally dysfunctional, under certain conditions one may void the marriage. Consequently, we have a doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be. Furthermore, the same husband may have acted inappropriately during the marriage, such as being physically and emotionally abusive towards his children. And there is a <i>halakhic</i> dispute as to whether one can void the marriage based upon the clear expectation that had his wife known that that such behavior would transpire, she never would have married him. As such, we have a second doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be. In short, we are dealing with a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, known in rabbinic parlance as <i>sefek sefeika de’dina</i>. As such, the question is whether one can void the marriage by invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.
Prior to focusing upon this concept of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated: marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For our presentation, we knowingly refer to mistaken betrothal (<i>kiddushin</i>) and mistaken marriage (<i>nissuin</i>) as synonymous, even though the concepts are <i>halakhically</i> distinguishable.</i>), we first need to understand the emergence and ramifications of the notion of a doubtful marriage (<i>a safeik kiddushin</i>). To state it differently, prior to examining the notion of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, one needs to briefly introduce the notion of a <i>single halakhic</i> doubt as it relates to the act of <i>kiddushin.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For a detailed analysis of the concept of a doubtful marriage in <i>Halakhah</i>, see P. Shifman, <i>Doubtful Marriage in Israeli Law</i> (Hebrew), Yerushalayim, 1975, 11–99. For contemporary discussions of the principles of the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, see <i>Noam Siah</i>, vol. 1, chapters 5–10; <i>Sas Anokhi</i>, chapters 1–9, 14; R. O. Yosef, <i>Yehaveh Da’at</i>, vol. 6, 25–29; <i>The Principles of Double Doubt in Yabia Omer</i>, (Hebrew), 1–56; R. Y. Yosef, <i>Ein Yitzhak</i>, vol. 2, 281–354; File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, October 1, 2017.</i>
Both the acts of <i>kiddushin</i> (betrothal) and <i>nissuin</i> (marriage) which transpire under the wedding canopy (the <i>hupah</i>) entail a consensual agreement between a Jewish man and a Jewish woman. It is a very special agreement that establishes a personal status between the parties, namely that the betrothed woman (the <i>mekudeshet</i>) is designated for a particular man (the <i>mekadesh</i>) and prohibited to all others.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Kiddushin</i> 2b notes: “The woman becomes prohibited to all others in the same way as consecrated object” (<i>hekdesh</i> – AYW).</i> The presumptive status of a married woman (<i>hezkat eishet ish</i>) renders both the man and woman subject to various prohibitions - e.g. sexual relations with various relatives become prohibited.
What happens if a <i>halakhic</i> doubt emerges regarding the <i>kiddushin</i>? For example, if the man betroths the woman with a stone-embellished ring<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 31:2.</i> or is coerced to betroth her,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 42:1 (one opinion).</i> such conduct is deemed a doubtful <i>kiddushin</i>. One might have thought that the presumption of personal status (<i>hazakah</i>) of her being a single woman is determinative since in a case of a doubt on a <i>biblical</i> level we do not deviate from her status of being a single woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ran on Rif Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 18; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh</i>, <i>MT Edut</i> 6:7; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1<i>, YD</i> 111; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:1128 in the name of Rambam and Mahari ibn Lev; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed of Lublin OH</i> 15:4; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1<i>, EH</i> 59. Cf. others who claim that the presumption of being a single woman is applicable if there is a factual doubt. See <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, MT Tumat Tzara’at</i> 2:1; <i>Teshuvot Masat Binyamin</i> 50; <i>Pri Megadim YD</i> 384:18; <i>Teshuvot Brit Avraham EH</i> 71:2; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 1:37.</i>
However, in the wake of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding the <i>kiddushin,</i> when the wife wants to be divorced and her husband refuses to give a <i>get,</i> we coerce him to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Zarua</i> 126 in the name of Rama; <i>Beit ha-Behirah Kiddushin</i> 65a; <i>MT Ishut</i> 4:5, <i>Kesef Mishneh,</i> ad locum; <i>SA EH</i> 42:5; <i>Teshuvot Maharil Diskin,</i> Pesakim 48; <i>Teshuvot Ohr Gadol</i> 5 (11). Cf. <i>Teshuvot Ridvaz</i> 115 (41); <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 6 (possibly); <i>Teshuvot Divrei Rivot</i> 378; <i>Hazon Ish, Ketuvot</i> 69:23.<br>On the other hand, if he is prepared to betroth her a second time, we do not coerce him to give a <i>get</i>. See <i>SA</i>, op. cit.; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 42:18; <i>Tiv Kiddushin</i> 48:2.</i> In other words, in the absence of receiving a <i>get,</i> she is prohibited to others on rabbinic grounds.
In other words, should the husband be intransigent regarding the <i>get</i>, a <i>beit din</i> may coerce him to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The assumption is that civil law such as Israeli law recognizes that the giving of a <i>get</i> may be coerced by a rabbinical court.</i> In the event that the <i>beit din</i> isn’t legally empowered to issue a <i>get</i> compulsion order or the husband is recalcitrant regarding the <i>get</i> and who intentionally or unintentionally fails to disclose to his wife a major defect prior to their marriage a <i>get</i> under certain conditions should not be required,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Zarua</i>, supra n. 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Cremona ed. 77 in the name of Rav’yah; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 221 (in theory rather than in practice); <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:16; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:79, 80. For additional examples of a marriage which can be characterized as “a marriage in error”, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 141, n.10 and <i>infra</i> chapter 4g, h, and j.<br>There is a Talmudic presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) about women that “it is better to live as two than to remain in widowhood” (<i>tav le-meitav tan do mi-le-meitav armalu</i>). See <i>Yevamot</i> 118b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 75a; <i>Kiddushin</i> 7a, 41a; <i>Bava Kama</i> 110b-111a. There is a debate whether this presumption is based upon the expectations of the majority of women or whether it may be invoked in light of the expectation of a minority of women. Even according to Rabbi Yosef Baer Soloveitchik, who endorses the latter approach (see <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:3), even a minority opinion would concur that in our case, where there is not even a minority (“a <i>meut matzui</i>”) of women who would be satisfied with marrying an impotent husband, consequently the giving of a <i>get</i> would not be required. See <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Ketuvot</i> 7:10 (deals with a barren woman - <i>ailonit</i>); <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 221; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, op. cit., <i>EH</i> 3:48.</i> nonetheless some will argue that on rabbinic grounds we coerce a <i>get</i> for a matter of stringency (<i>get le’humra</i>) due to the fact that we are dealing with a doubtful marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Borenstein’s understanding of <i>Shitah Mekubetzet Ketuvot</i> 72b. See <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 176:7. See also Dayan Yosef Goldberg, who contends that some other early authorities (<i>Rishonim</i>) concur with this conclusion. See <i>Elu she-Kofin le-Hotzi</i>. Yerushalayim: 5773, 114, 200–201.<br>Cf. others who claim that the requirement of a <i>get</i> for matters of stringency is rabbinically mandated in order to avoid the public perception that a wife may divorce her husband without receiving a <i>get</i>. See <i>Yevamot</i> 88b-89a; <i>Rashi</i>, ad. locum., s.v. <i>gezerah</i>; <i>Shitah Mekubetzet Ketuvot</i> 73b in the name of Rashi, Mahadura Kama; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Yevamot</i> 89a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Yevamot</i> 88b; <i>Lehem Mishneh, MT, Ishut</i> 4:10; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Dvar Eliyahu</i> 48.<br>In other words, we are dealing with <i>halakhic</i> logic (<i>sevara</i>), namely to avoid a public perception that a married woman may be free without the execution of a <i>get</i>. Therefore we mandate the giving of a <i>get</i>. Seemingly, invoking <i>halakhic</i> logic entails a biblical mandate. As the Talmud exhorts us in a few places, “What need is there for a biblical verse? It is a <i>sevara</i>-the view may be based upon reasoning”. See <i>Berakhot</i> 4b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 22a; <i>Bava Kama</i> 46b. Upon further reflection, since the <i>get</i> is being given on rabbinic grounds we are dealing with imposing a stringency in order to protect the undermining of the status of a married woman lest a husband leave his spouse without giving a <i>get</i>. As such we are dealing here with invoking <i>halakhic</i> reasoning which is rabbinically mandated. See <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 3:135; <i>Tzlah, Berakhot</i> 35a. To state it differently, some <i>halakhic</i> logic is biblically grounded and others are rabbinically mandated. In other words, as Rabbi Ya’akov Reicher contends some <i>halakhic</i> logic is strong and others are weak. See <i>Shevut Ya’akov</i>, op. cit. Whether post-Talmudic <i>halakhic</i> logic is construed as biblically or rabbinically grounded is subject to controversy. See <i>Tzlah</i>, op.cit.; <i>Kol Kitvei ha-Maharatz Hayoth</i>, 1; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Berakhot</i> 35a; <i>Teshuvot Orah Mishpat</i> 112.<br>Nevertheless, as we demonstrated in this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 134–176, 231–333; vol. 4, 143–298 and in this monograph, chapters 2–4, there is adequate proof that one may under certain conditions void a particular marriage. Consequently, in accordance with arbiters such as Rashba and Rabbi O. Yosef who contend that with the submission of proof we may set aside the <i>halakhic</i> reasoning of earlier authorities, the above <i>halakhic</i> logic may be rejected. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322 and <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, 7, <i>OH</i> 33 (2), vol. 9, Introduction, 10, <i>OH</i> 55(13). Nevertheless, there are arbiters such as Rabbi Yitzhak Elhanan Spektor who argue that even if the marriage is voided based upon a marriage in error (<i>kiddushei ta’ut)</i> regarding a husband’s defects, one requires the execution of a <i>get</i> on a rabbinic level. See <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24(43); Cf. <i>Teshuvot Ohr Zarua</i> 1:761; <i>Teshuvot Dvar Eliyahu</i> 48, <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer EH</i> 27 in the name of Noda be-Yehudah and <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:79 who contend that under such circumstances, the wife may be freed without a <i>get</i>. For additional authorities who espouse this view, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 141, n.10. See the addendum.</i> In other words, in the wake of a doubtful marriage (<i>safek kiddushin</i>), under certain conditions such as a husband’s failure to disclose a major defect prior to the marriage such as impotency, we can coerce a <i>get</i> but we cannot void the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Zarua</i>, supra n. 3; <i>MT Ishut</i> 4:5, <i>Kesef Mishneh</i>, ad locum; <i>Beit ha-Behirah Kiddushin</i> 65a; <i>SA EH</i> 42:5; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 154:2; <i>Teshuvot Maharil Diskin</i>, <i>Pesakim</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 13.</i> The rationale for permitting <i>get</i> coercion is that we do not mandate that the wife to remain married under such circumstances, and should she want to be divorced we empower her by compelling her spouse to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Dayan S. Tenna, <i>Teshuvot Birkat Shlomo</i> 12:30–32. Under such circumstances, given that the <i>get</i> is being given on rabbinic grounds, one may argue that we do not run afoul of the strictures of a coerced <i>get</i> (“a <i>get me’useh</i>”). See <i>Teshuvot Even Yekarah Tlitah</i> 53.</i>
In short, a doubtful <i>halakhic</i> marriage may serve under certain conditions as a basis to issue a g<i>et</i> compulsion order.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Some will contend that <i>get</i> coercion concerning a doubtful <i>kiddushin</i> can be advanced only as a supporting argument (<i>senif</i>) accompanied by other reasons for mandating <i>get</i> compulsion. See <i>PDR</i> 1:33, 37–38, 4:33, 59; A. Sheinfeld,” Obligating a <i>get</i> in a doubtful <i>kiddushin</i> (Hebrew),” 9 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 158, 163 (5765).</i> However, in the wake of a doubtful <i>halakhic</i> marriage, some say one cannot void a marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">On the other hand, in the event that there are at least two <i>factual</i> doubts regarding the <i>kiddushin,</i> there may be grounds to void the marriage and reinstate her status as a single woman. See <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2:135; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 10 (9).</i>
However, others argue once we move out of the world of a doubtful marriage and traverse into the universe of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, under certain conditions a marriage may be voided. In addressing the “<i>halakhic</i> vision” of rabbinic authority, we examine various issues which emerge from the concept of the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt as it relates to marriage and divorce matters. Firstly, we will explore the two opposing conceptions of <i>halakhic</i> doubt crystallized in the Mishnah, Talmud and post-Talmudic authorities and thinkers in order to understand conceptually what constitutes a <i>halakhic</i> doubt, and then briefly explore the implications of dealing with <i>halakhic</i> doubt as it relates to the prohibitions (<i>issurim</i>) involving marriage and divorce. In chapter two, we offer a detailed analysis of the foundations which establish the basis for a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to serve as the avenue to free a wife to remarry without receiving a <i>get</i> from her spouse, i.e. voiding a marriage (<i>bittul kiddushin</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The implicit premise of invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in marriage as a means to leniency assumes that a double doubt will be be effective with two bodies, namely a man and a woman. See <i>Rashi Ketuvot</i> 14a in the name of Rabbi Y.T. Elem; <i>ha-Kereti u-Peleti</i>, 110, Kuntres Beit ha-Safek, <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:60. Cf. <i>Tosafot Yevamot</i> 2b; <i>Sha’ar ha-Melekh, Mikva’ot</i>, Kelal 3.</i> We then deal with how a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt may serve as a vehicle to void a marriage in the context of <i>get</i> coercion.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For the employment of a wife’s plea of repulsion (<i>ma’is ali</i>) concerning <i>get</i> compulsion as an avenue to invoke a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt resulting in the voiding of a marriage, see infra chapter 3 and in particular n. 45(end) and chapters 4E, n. 82 and 4F, text accompanying nn. 40–41. Alternatively, we may advance an additional double <i>halakhic</i> doubt which would result in voiding the marriage. The first doubt is that there is a debate whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> based upon a plea of repulsion. Moreover, there is a second doubt whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> concerning a particular ground for divorce such <i>as a</i> husband who is physically abusive to his spouse. Based upon this double <i>halakhic</i> doubt one may coerce a <i>get</i> (see <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:3 (15); <i>PDR</i> 15:145, 153) and therefore we may void the marriage. See R. Y.Goldberg, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi</i>, Yerushalayim, 5773, 50–51, 131, n. 8.</i>
In addressing “the reality” of rabbinic authority, we have included ten presentations inspired by the reasoned opinions handed down as a <i>dayan</i> serving on a <i>beit din</i> or as serving as a rabbi on a panel of three rabbis engaging in resolving ritual <i>Halakhah</i> (<i>mo’reih horo’ah</i>) such as a matter of divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In other words, this is a question of the <i>halakhic</i> laws of prohibitions and permissibility (<i>dinei issur ve-heter</i>) whether a husband is obligated to give a <i>get</i> to his wife and one may resolve this question in front of three rabbis in the absence of the husband, a husband who was not summoned to the hearing. See supra n. 6.</i> Many of these decisions were handed down while serving at the International Beit Din located in New York City.
Emerging from these presentations, the reader will encounter four techniques which may be implemented under certain conditions which may serve to void a marriage (<i>mevateil kiddushin</i>): the discovery of a <i>halakhic</i> impropriety(ies) in the marriage ceremony, a husband who intentionally or unintentionally engages in misrepresentation and/or fails to disclose prior to the marriage a major defect (“<i>kiddushei ta’ut”</i>), a major expectation that the wife desired at the time of the marriage (“<i>umdana de’mukha”</i>) which failed to materialize during the course of the marriage and the application of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (“<i>sefek sefeika de’dina</i>”).
Implicitly in adopting one of these techniques is that we ought to refrain from being stringent regarding <i>halakhic</i> engagement (<i>kiddushin</i>) lest “the stringency would lead to a leniency” resulting in the promiscuity of the wife due to her being a victim of her husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 42; <i>Hagahot Maimoniyot, Ishut</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 3:130.</i>
In each presentation, we offer a rendition of the facts followed by a discussion of the <i>halakhic</i> issues emerging from the claims,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the majority of these cases, we summoned the husband to attend the <i>beit din</i> hearing in order to address the matter of the <i>get</i> and he refused to appear. In his absence, we conducted a hearing with his wife. Indeed, some other rabbinical courts in the United States have also adopted this posture. For the <i>halakhic</i> grounds for conducting a hearing in his absence and ensuring that certain procedural safeguards were in place in order to arrive at a <i>halakhically</i> informed ruling, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 216, n. 2.<br>In a few situations, the husband was not summoned to attend the hearing and the case was resolved by a panel of three rabbis serving as “<i>mor’eh hora’ah</i>” (credentialed arbiters of <i>Halakhah</i>) rather than rabbis serving as <i>dayanim</i> (rabbinic arbitrators). See infra Chapter 4H and 4J.<br>In the absence of the husband, a <i>beit din</i> or a rabbi(s) may hear the submission of evidence by witnesses in so far as it relates to matters of personal status (<i>ishut</i>). See <i>Teshuvot Oholei Ya’akov</i> 27 in the name of Meiri and Ridvaz; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash ha-Hadashot</i> 14 in the name of Ramah; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:200; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:19; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 46, 287; <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 70; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 30, 123, 124; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov EH</i> 1:4; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:84; <i>Teshuvot ha-Maharnah</i> 1:68. In the event that one deals with an <i>agunah</i>, the situation characterized as “an hour of emergency” and as such is <i>halakhically</i> viewed <i>ex post facto</i> (see supra 14, n. 7) and therefore, evidence in matters related to personal status may be submitted in the absence of the husband. See <i>Maharnah</i>, op. cit.; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 12. Cf. others who mandate that both parties must be present when dealing with a matter of personal status. See <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 5:168; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 4:53; <i>SA EH</i> 11:4. Others argue that the requirement of having both parties present at the <i>beit din</i> proceeding depends upon the circumstances of the case. See <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 11:16; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 11:4.<br>Cf. <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 11:4, <i>HM</i> 28:15; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 106; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 11:16; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 11:11; <i>Teshuvot Panim Meirot</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 104; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 21, 27.<br>For further discussion, see S. Shilo, “Testimony in the Absence of a Party in Matrimonial Matters,” (Hebrew) 5 <i>Shenaton Ha-Mishpat Ha-Ivri</i> 321 (1978).</i> followed by a decision rendered by the <i>beit din</i> panel. To preserve the confidentiality of the parties involved in these cases, all names have been changed, and some facts have been changed and/or deleted. Any changes or deletions of the facts do not impact upon the integrity of the <i>beit din’s</i> analysis and the actual decision. Appended to some of the decisions is a section entitled “final afterthoughts” in which we offer an alternative approach to voiding the marriage and/or present additional information regarding the decision.
Hopefully our discussion will educate our community on the parameters and scope of rabbinic authority in general and “shatter the silence” surrounding the solutions which may be implemented under certain conditions in order to deal with the plight of the modern-day <i>agunah</i> in particular. For those who avail themselves of the services of a <i>beit din</i>, it may be a life-defining moment. Consequently, it behooves our community to understand the institution, to become attuned to the dynamics of its decision-making process, and to perform due diligence in deciding in which <i>beit din</i> to pursue one’s matters.

Part 1; Rabbinic Authority; The Vision

Chapter 1; The varying definitions of a halakhic doubt

<b>The varying definitions of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt</b>
The Hebrew word <i>safek</i> means doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 37b.</i> <i>Halakhah</i> distinguishes between an uncertainty as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (<i>safek de’dina</i>) and a doubt concerning the <i>metzi’ut</i>, a factual uncertainty. In addressing issues relating to marriage, in the <i>Mishnah</i> we already encounter the need to focus upon factual uncertainties. For example:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Mishnah Yevamot</i> 3:8.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">How is it that <i>kiddushin</i> (lit.: betrothal – loosely translated: marriage) is called into question? (If) he threw a token of betrothal to her (his prospective spouse) (in a public place), and there is a doubt whether it was closer to him or closer to her – this is a doubtful betrothal.</span>
If one is standing in a public domain, the <i>halakhah</i> is that one can acquire ownership of an object which falls within four cubits of where one is standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 10a.</i> Our <i>Mishnah</i> refers to a case where the man and woman were standing exactly eight cubits away from each other. Clarifying the precise location of the object determines who is deemed the owner of the object. If the object is within four cubits of the man, he remains the owner and the betrothal is invalid. If the object is within four cubits of the woman then the betrothal is valid.
On the other hand, the same <i>Mishnah</i> addresses a parallel issue that of divorce, regarding a second type of uncertainty or <i>halakhic</i> doubt. For example, if a husband writes a writ of divorce in his own handwriting and it bore no signatures or witnesses, or if it bore signatures but no date, or if it bore the signature of only one witness, this is a case of doubtful divorce.
The implicit premise is that a Jew who possesses no rabbinic education, or whose learning skills in studying and determining <i>Halakhah</i> is limited, can never apply these principles out of ignorance. Thus, for example, one who is ignorant of the <i>halakhot</i> of blessings and who therefore has a doubt what blessing ought to be recited, cannot rely upon the rule that one acts leniently in a case of doubt regarding blessing recitation. Rather, he must seek rabbinic help to educate him in the <i>halakhot</i> of blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Berurah SA OH</i> 210:4.</i> In other words, doubt which emerges due to the absence of knowledge cannot be construed as a <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taz SA YD</i> 98:6; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 98:9, 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 34. Cf. <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 154:34.</i> Doubt stems from a comprehensive understanding of <i>Halakhah</i> by an individual who is proficient in <i>Halakhah</i> and yet concludes that there is a doubt as to how the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be resolved concerning a particular issue.
The threshold question which must be asked is: why did <i>halakhic</i> controversy arise? After all, given that we are dealing with a religious legal system, are we not engaging in the deliberation of divine truth? As such, ought such matters evoke unanimity rather than debate?
Explicitly or implicitly relying upon various exegetical interpretations of the Torah (<i>Midrashim</i>) and Talmudic passages,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Shemot Rabba</i> 41:6; <i>Berakhot</i> 33a; <i>Nedarim</i> 33a; <i>Temurah</i> 15b, 16a.</i> we may conclude that the controversies emerged due to the fact that many <i>halakhot</i> were lost or forgotten. Consequently, doubts as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be, arose due to controversies between arbiters. As Talmud Temurah 16a states:
<span class="poetry indentAll">R. Yehudah said in the name of Rav: When Moshe was about to depart this world . . . he told Yehoshua: Ask me about any uncertainties you have. Yehoshua responded: Have I ever left your presence for a moment? . . . Immediately, Yehoshua grew weak; he forgot 300 <i>halakhot</i> and 700 doubts arose in his mind . . . Hashem informed him: For Me to communicate the <i>halakhot</i> is impossible. . . .</span>
To state it differently, doubt arose in the universe of the <i>halakhic</i>-legal system due to forgetfutlness, and it was a Jew’s rather than Hashem’s responsibility to reconstruct the traditions transmitted from the time of Moshe and divine revelation at Sinai.
Rambam vehemently attacks this notion that attributes <i>halakhic</i> dispute to forgetfulness, categorically stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Introduction to Commentary to the Mishnah</i>, Y. Kapach, trans., Yerushalayim: Mossad Harav Kook, 1963, 20.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">But as for their saying that when the students (of Hillel and Shammai) who had insufficiently studied increased, dispute increased, this matter is crystal clear, for when two individuals are similar in comprehension and in learning and knowledge of the principles based upon their study, there will not be any disagreement between them in what they study in one of the hermeneutic principles (13 <i>middot</i> that one expounds the Torah) . . . just as we have encountered controversies between Hillel and Shammai other than a few <i>halakhot</i>, for their methodologies of study in all that they would learn by one of the principles were similar to one another, and also the correct principles which were espoused by one were endorsed by the other.</span>
In contrast to the previous approach, for Rambam, accompanying the tradition which was received from Moshe and transmitted to future generations, arbiters were <i>halakhically</i> authorized to introduce new interpretations of the Torah. Consequently, due to the emergence of new interpretations, doubt arose which led to <i>halakhic</i> debate. As Rambam observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 35.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And when the study of their students (Hillel and Shammai) diminished and the methods of (<i>halakhic</i>) reasoning became weakened for them in comparison to Hillel and Shammai their teachers, controversies transpired during the exchange on numerous matters, due to the fact that each reasoned according to the level of his intellect and in pursuance to the principles known to him. . . . And in this fashion disagreement emerged, rather than that they erred in their receiving of tradition and one’s tradition is true and the other’s false. . . .</span>
Contrary to both the above views, Rabbi Avraham Ibn Daud, in his introduction to the classic medieval work, Sefer ha-Kabbalah, writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Kabbalah, The Book of Tradition</i> ed. G. Cohen, Philadelphia, JPS, 1967, 3–4.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Now should anyone infected with heresy attempt to mislead you, saying: “It is because the rabbis differed on a number of issues that I doubt their words,” you should retort bluntly and inform him that he is “a rebel against the decision of the court;” and that our rabbis of blessed memory never differed with respect to a commandment in principle, but only with respect to its details; for they heard the principle from their teachers, but had not inquired as to its details, since they had not waited upon their masters sufficiently. As a case in point, they did not differ as to whether or not it is obligatory to light the Sabbath lamp; what they did dispute was “with what it may be lighted and with what it may not be lighted”. . . . This holds true for all their discussions.</span>
Implicitly rejecting Midrash Shemot Rabba 41:6, Midrash va-Yikra Rabba, be-Hukkotai 19:6 and Talmud Yerushalmi <i>Pe’ah</i> 1:1 which inform us that the details of <i>Halakhah</i> were not revealed to Moshe,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Mekhilta</i>, <i>Shemot</i> 21:1 which contends that even the details were known by Moshe and transmitted to the Jewish people. On the other hand, Tosefot Yom Tov in his <i>Introduction to the Mishnah</i> claims that Moshe received everything but withheld certain matters.</i> Rabbi Avraham Ibn Daud argues that both the details as well as the principles were transmitted by Moshe. However, controversy emerged due to the fact that, in the words of seventeen century author Rabbi David Nieto:<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Matteh Dan</i>, 63.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">They studied insufficiently. They didn’t remain with teachers who had ample time to receive the interpretation of the principles and therefore disagreement emerged.</span>
Whereas for Rabbis Nieto and Ibn Daud, the arguments in the Mishnah and Talmud could have been avoided had the students been attentive to their masters, for Rambam the debates stemmed from their differing mental capacities and methods of interpretation.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Introduction to Commentary to the Mishnah</i>, supra n. 7, 20–21, 35.</i>
On the other hand, Rashi contends:<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 57a</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is plausible to say, “these and those are the words of the Living Hashem” – sometimes this consideration is applicable and sometimes another consideration is applicable. For the relevant consideration reverses itself according to a change of things and situations . . .</span>
For Rashi, controversy is determined by the times.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For another understanding offered by Rashi, see <i>Rashi</i>, supra n. 13.</i>
In short, forgetfulness and loss of <i>halakhic</i> tradition (<i>mesorah</i>), details of <i>halakhot</i> were not revealed to Moshe, differing times and differing mental capacities and methods of interpretation have all been advanced as reasons for the emergence of controversy.
The underlying premise of the varying positions is that there is only one answer to a <i>halakhic</i> issue. The fact that there is debate is due to extraneous reasons, e.g. forgetfulness and loss of <i>mesorah</i>, differing mental capacities and methods of interpretation.
Construing the <i>halakhic</i> system as monistic in character already was alluded to in medieval times by the author of the Sefer ha-Hinnukh, who explains the rationale for preferring the adoption of the majority view rather than the absolute truth. As he observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Hinnukh</i>, Mitzvah 408</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is better to suffer one error and all to be subservient to the understanding of the rabbis rather than for each of us to act as he thinks, for in that direction lays the destruction of religion . . . and the anarchy of the nation.</span>
However, it is Rabbeinu Nissim (known by the acronym: Ran) who drives the point home, positing that one position in a particular debate reflects divine truth. As Ran teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Derashot ha-Ran</i>, nos. 3 and 5. For other perspectives of endorsing a <i>halakhic</i> view arising from a disagreement as reflective of one absolute truth, see Meiri, <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Avot</i> 5:19; Ramban, <i>Devarim</i> 17:11; Ramban, <i>Milhamot ha-Shem</i>, Introduction; Rabbi Yosef Karo, <i>Kelalei Gemara</i> in <i>Halikhot Olam</i>, Portnoy ed., Machon ha-Mishpat, 61; Maharal of Prague, <i>Be’air ha-Golah</i>, Be’air Rishon; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 192; <i>Teshuvot Zivhei Tzedek</i>, <i>YD</i> 66; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer HM</i> 92; Rabbi Elhanan Wasserman, <i>Kovetz Shiurim</i>, vol. 2, 109 (5), 112 (16); <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6 (14) in the name of Maharashdam.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Once Hashem transferred the power of decision-making to the Sages, whatever they would agree upon, that is what Hashem commanded regarding that issue . . .<br>There is no other alternative fashion to understand this matter. For since the words of those who opine something ritually impure and those who rule it ritually kosher are contradictory, it is impossible for both parties to the debate to be conforming to the truth . . .</span>
Though there is no explicit mention that a <i>halakhic</i> resolution reflects truth, Rabbi Yisrael Salanter claims that <i>halakhic</i> disagreement entails arriving at the truth. Focusing upon the disputes between Beit Hillel and Beit Shammai, Rabbi Salanter states:<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Yisrael</i>, 44–46.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The Heavenly Voice told the people that each side genuinely felt objectively correct, and therefore even the rejected words of Beit Shammai are the words of the living Hashem, and one who studies them is studying the Torah of Hashem.</span>
However, Rabbi Salanter observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Man does not have the power to arrive at the true intellect, totally dismembered and separated from the subliminal forces of the psyche.</span>
Finally, given that in the context of a <i>beit din</i> proceeding we resolve matters in accordance with the majority, the underlying assumption for certain authorities (<i>Poskim</i> ) is that their <i>psak din</i>, decision, reflects the truth and the minority view embodies falsehood.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Bava Kama</i> 27b, <i>s.v. ka mashma lan</i>; <i>Tumim</i> 25, <i>Kitzur Tokfo Kohen</i> 123–124; <i>Hiddushei Rabbi Hayyim ha-Levi on Shas, Bava Kama</i> 27b, 129. See also, <i>Teshuvot Hut ha-Meshullash</i> 8.</i>
In contradistinction to this monistic perspective of <i>Halakhah</i>, there are authorities who advocate for the notion that the system is pluralistic in character, and therefore the presence of controversy entails a debate of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths. Rashi expounds:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, supra n. 13.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">When two Talmudic scholars are arguing whether a matter is permitted or prohibited . . . then there is no falsehood. . . . Each one is arriving at his own conclusion. This individual submits the reason that it should be permitted while the other one gives the rationale that it ought to be prohibited. This person analogizes (<i>hekesh</i> – AYW) to a particular subject and this individual analogizes to another matter. Here we may state, “These and those are the words of the living Hashem.”</span>
Subsequently, the Rabbis of medieval France query: How is it possible for contradictory views to both represent the truth? Relying explicitly or implicitly upon midrashim and the Tosefta and the Talmud,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesikta Rabbati, Pesika</i> 21; <i>Tosefta Sotah</i> 7:11–12; <i>Talmud Yerushalmi, Sanhedrin</i> 4:2; <i>Eruvin</i> 13b; <i>Hagigah</i> 3b; <i>Massekhet Soferim</i> 15:6–7.</i> their reply was the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva Eruvin</i> 13a. See also, <i>Tosafot Shantz, Eduyot</i> 1:5; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 144; <i>Teshuvot Yosef Omeitz</i> 51; <i>Yavin Shemu’ah</i>, 81b.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">When Moshe ascended to receive the Torah, it was proven to him that every issue was subject to forty-nine leniencies and forty-nine stringent approaches. When he questioned the matter, Hashem responded that the arbiters of every generation were empowered to decide among these views in order to establish the normative <i>Halakhah</i>.</span>
A few centuries later, adopting the model of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths, Maharshal argues:<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Introduction of Yam shel Shlomo, Tractate Bava Kama</i>. See also, Introduction of <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Tractate Hullin</i>.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">When a debate centers upon the attribution of a position to a particular person, there is only room for one truth. However, when two <i>Talmudic</i> sages participate in a <i>halakhic</i> controversy, each one claiming the merits of his view. . . . there exists no absolute truth and falsehood. Regarding such matters, one can state that both reflect the view of the living Hashem. Sometimes one approach will prove to be more authentic while under other conditions the other view will seem to be more persuasive.</span>
Others have endorsed this model of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>va-Ya’as Avraham, Kuntres Pri ha-Aretz</i>, 506–510.</i>
Whereas Rashi, the French rabbis and Maharshal contend that the truths are equal, Maharal of Prague claims that they are unequal:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharal</i>, supra n. 16.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Each understood the Torah from his own vantage point according to his intellectual capacity as well as the . . . character of his individual soul. This accounts for the difference in comprehension, as one concluded that a thing was <i>tame</i> (ritually impure - AYW) in the extreme, another viewed it as clearly <i>tahor</i> (ritually pure – AYW), while a third person contends the ambivalent state of the object is in question. . . . Consequently, the learned men stated that in a controversy amongst true scholars, all views represent a form of truth . . .</span>
In his classic essay regarding this issue, Rabbi Michael Rosensweig eloquently formulates his analytical understanding of Maharal’s position, elucidating the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">“<i>Elu va-Elu Divrei Elokim Hayyim</i>: <i>Halakhic</i> pluralism and theories of controversy,” <i>Tradition</i> 26:3 (1992), 13–14.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">There is often no one decisive response to the issue of <i>tahara</i> or <i>tuma</i>, for example, since overall proximity to the ideal form represented by the classic case rather than a specific combination of components determines this status. Thus, one may speak of approximately the ideal sufficiently but not fully, and by the same token substantially but not sufficiently, and consequently, a whole hierarchy of truths would emerge. Dilution of some components and combination with competing and undermining characteristics may also contribute to the creation of a quasi-status, whole ultimate fate in the realm of practical <i>halakhah</i> is likely to be debated.</span>
Utilizing an analogy, Maharal notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharal</i>, supra n. 16.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The tree, for example, is composed of four elements (water, earth, fire and air) but the principal component is air. . . . Likewise, though any single matter has various <i>halakhic</i> aspects associated with it – all endowed by Hashem – one of them is the most paramount, and that is the determining component, and that is the <i>Halakhah</i>.</span>
In short, <i>halakhic</i> controversy is either a representation of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths, or exists due to extraneous reasons such as forgetfulness and loss of <i>halakhic</i> tradition.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A third approach espoused by <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, infra n. 35 and Rabbi Moshe Feinstein, infra n. 35 is that from the perspective of Hashem there exists one truth while from the vantage point of the <i>halakhic</i> arbiter there is one truth which he is dutibound to fulfill, namely “the truth in rendering a ruling” (<i>emet le’hora’ah</i>). Consequently, if two decisors disagree regarding an issue both are “the words of the living God”. However, only one position reflects the truth vis-à-vis heaven. See Shimshon Ettinger, “Controversy and truth-on truth in the halakhic context,” (Hebrew), 21 <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> 37 (2000).</i>
The emerging question is: how do these two diametrically opposing perspectives regarding the nature of <i>halakhic</i> controversy impact on how one perceives <i>halakhic</i> doubt (<i>safek de’dina</i>)? If we adopt the notion that there is only one position in a <i>halakhic</i> debate which represents truth, then the opposing view is false, as it emerged by dint of extraneous factors such as forgetfulness or a loss in transmission of the <i>mesorah</i>. On the other hand, espousing the doctrine of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths inexorably leads one to the conclusion that all the positions in a particular <i>halakhic</i> disagreement represent <i>halakhic</i> truth.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, if the arbiter has erred in his position, depending on the type of error, this will impact on whether the <i>psak din</i>, civil ruling or ritual ruling, remains valid, or if it becomes null and void as a result. See <i>Sanhedrin</i> 33a.</i> Consequently, it is unsurprising to encounter Rabbi Yitzhak Yosef, a contemporary authority, citing the following in the name of Rabbi Refael Hazan of eighteenth-century Izmir:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ein Yitzhak</i>, 2:304.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Hashem gave the determination of <i>Halakhah</i> to the scholars of Yisrael which Hashem showed to Moshe forty-nine leniencies and forty-nine prohibitions. . . . Therefore, if the decisors are in debate and the <i>Halakhah</i> was not decided, whoever practices in accordance with one of these opinions has whom to rely upon. . . .</span>
As such, even if one subscribes to the notion of the existence of multiple <i>halakhic</i> truths concerning a particular issue, lest one assume that a decisor (a <i>posek</i>), may refrain from performing due diligence in deciding which view to accept and choose the stringent view, Talmud Yerushalmi exhorts us:<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Talmud Yerushalmi, Terumot</i> 5:3; <i>Pnei Moshe</i>, ad locum; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 242, <i>Kitzur be-Hanhagat Hora’ot Issur ve-Heter</i>, 9. See also <i>Berakhot</i> 28b. This approach emerges from <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:6.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Just as one is prohibited to purify the impure, similarly, one cannot declare that the pure is impure.</span>
For example, one of the techniques to void a marriage is labeled “<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>” (loosely translated: a marriage in error). Prior to invoking the tool of <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> and claiming there was an error in the creation of the marriage, one of the preconditions that must be obtained is that the husband’s defect must be a major defect (a <i>mum gadol</i>) such as sexual impotency, refusing to have children, insanity or homosexuality which preexisted the marriage, and that the husband failed to disclose this defect to his prospective wife prior to their marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 134–176, 294–327; vol. 4, 184–201, 284–297.</i> A review of our <i>halakhic</i> tradition will indicate that the classical restatements of <i>Halakhah</i> such as Alfasi, Rambam, Rosh, Tur, Shulhan Arukh and Rema do not address the propriety of utilizing this technique as a vehicle for voiding a marriage concerning a husband’s defects. However, there are over 90 responsa (<i>teshuvot</i>) which address the propriety of employing this tool. A review of these responsa will demonstrate that over 50 authorities endorse this approach and more than 25 decisors prohibit it.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 138, n. 4, 140, n. 8.</i> In light of the fact that the classical legists did not address this matter and there does not exist normative <i>Halakhah</i> addressing the legitimacy of utilizing this technique,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Though the majority of legists support utilizing this technique, nevertheless, applying the rule of following the majority (“<i>aharei rabbim le-hatot</i>”) is limited to disputes transpiring in a <i>beit din</i> setting rather than intergenerational controversies. See <i>Beit Yosef HM</i> 13 (end) in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:104; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 3:304, 5:126; <i>Get Pashut, Kelalim, kelal</i> 1:5; <i>Teshuvot Maharlbah</i> 147. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:116.<br>Even in accordance with the view that majority rule is applicable where there are authorities engaging in intergenerational disputes, <i>Sefer ha-Hinnukh</i>, mitzvah 78 contends that the authorities have to possess the same level of <i>halakhic</i> proficiency. See also <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev, OH</i> 496:96; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 2:24, 3:29. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 50–52. Secondly, whether one follows the majority or a minority who are greater in wisdom is subject to debate. See <i>Ran on Rif, Yoma</i> 4b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban Sanhedrin</i> 32a; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 242, supra n. 31, at 2. Finally, for the complexity in ascertaining a particular arbiter’s proficiency, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rid</i> 62. Consequently, the propriety of employing <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> as a technique to void a marriage has and continues to this very day to be addressed and debated by arbiters throughout the centuries.</i> decisors teach us that the arbiter is empowered to employ his knowledge and skills to address, deliberate upon this matter and arrive at a decision.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322; <i>Sheilat David, Kuntres ha-Hiddushin</i>, 73–74; <i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 31, at 4; <i>Taz’s Introduction to Shulhan Arukh, YD; Ketzot ha-Hoshen, Introduction</i>; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe, Introduction to Orah Hayyim</i>, <i>YD</i> 1:101, <i>YD</i> 3:88, <i>OH</i> 4:11, 39; <i>Dibrot Moshe Shabbat</i> 11.</i> Clearly, arbitrarily choosing to align with one position is in contravention to the aforesaid Talmud Yerushalmi’s dictum.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Upon analyzing an issue, an arbiter must distinguish between staking out a position based upon the understanding of the <i>Mishnaic</i> and <i>Talmudic</i> sources as interpreted by post-<i>Talmudic</i> authorities as well as the invoking of public policy considerations of <i>Halakhah</i>. In other words, it may be that an arbiter sincerely accepts that a particular practice is permitted by the authorities but nevertheless chooses to prohibit it due the fact that it may create a desecration of God’s name (a <i>hillul ha-Shem</i>) it may lead to people to engage in other prohibitions, it may undermine a certain revered <i>halakhic</i> institution or it may undermine another important <i>halakhic</i> value. See A. Frimer and D. Frimer, “Women’s Prayer Services – Theory &amp; Practice, Part 1,” <i>Tradition</i> 32:2, 5, 39, 60–69 (1998) and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 21–48. As such, an arbiter’s decision grounded upon public policy considerations would not run afoul of the Talmud Yerushalmi’s teaching.</i>
An authority<i>’s</i> failure to adhere to this teaching entails a commission of malfeasance regarding the rendering of a ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Devar Yehoshua</i> 3:2.</i> If he was remunerated for his ruling, he has transgressed the sin of theft<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i>, 1 <i>YD</i> 2.</i> and may be obligated to compensate the party for any ensuing damages incurred due to his judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ma’aseh Avraham HM</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Havazelet ha-Sharon</i> 1 <i>YD</i> 49; <i>Be’air Hetev, SA YD</i> 1:12.</i>
Regardless of whether we endorse the view that there are multiple truths concerning an issue or only one truth, the question is: how does an authority resolve a matter which is<i>,</i> an uncertainty as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (a <i>safek de’dina</i>)? As we know, <i>Halakhah</i> consists of both financial matters (<i>dinei mamonot</i>) and ritualistic issues (<i>issur ve-heter</i>). <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">There is a third realm of <i>Halakhah</i> such as the formulation of, a blessing (<i>berakha</i>), reading of the Torah and prayer <i>(tefillah</i>), which neither is to be identified with financial mattrers nor ritual issues. See <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:49, 153; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 55, 351. This realm is beyond the scope of our presentation.</i> At first glance, giving that monetary matters such as theft or injuring a fellow man entail the violation of prohibitions (<i>issurim</i>), the matter of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt ought to be dealt with in the same way in both realms. Namely, such issues ought to be resolved like a matter of dietary laws (<i>kashruth</i>), marriage and divorce and other ritual questions where we act <i>le’humra</i>, stringently. Thus, can we derive the <i>halakhot</i> of monetary matter from a ritual issue?
Implicitly relying upon the position of Rabbi Shimon ben Tzemah Duran (known by the acronym: Tashbetz),<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:158.</i> Rabbi Shimon Shkop, the dean of the early twentieth century Yeshiva in Grodno, Lithuania replies:<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sha’arei Yosher</i> 5:1. Translation is culled from Menahem Elon, <i>Jewish Law</i>, JPS, 1994, 136. See also Moshe Amiel, <i>Middot le-Heker ha-Halakhah</i>, Middah 11, Metzi’ut ve-Din.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Civil laws (loosely translated: <i>mamonot</i> – AYW) which govern the relations among people, operate differently from the other commandments of the Torah. In respect of all other commandments. . . . our obligation to fulfill them rests on the duty to obey God’s command. Matters of <i>mamon</i> . . . however, are different; there must be a legal duty before a religious obligation arises to pay damages or make restitution . . . When we deal with a legal right in a chattel or with a lien, the focus of our concern is not the observance of a religious commandment but rather the objective circumstances, who has the legal ownership of the object or is legally entitled to its possession.</span>
In other words, the initial step is the need to determine who the owner of the object is or who has to pay damages, i.e. the legal right. Once that fact has been established, and then we can proceed to identify the party who violates the prohibition associated with this legal right, either by stealing from the owner or causing injury to the owner. Consequently, it is unsurprising that in a case of <i>a halakhic</i> doubt regarding a monetary matter, we do not remove the object from possessor (the <i>muhzak</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 32b; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, supra n. 31; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 1:8; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 25:2.</i> In fact implicitly relying upon two Talmudic precedents emerging from Rabba’s ruling,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 32a-b.</i> citing the responsum of Maharam of Rothenberg,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot of Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Cremona edition, 159.</i> Rabbi Asher ben Yehiel (known by the acronym: Rosh) states the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 85:11. See also, <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, supra n. 43; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, supra n. 31; <i>Mordekhai, Ketuvot</i> 4:154.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">This is what I received from our Rabbi Meir z”l, in a case where there is a controversy what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be, we do not extract a monetary asset out of doubt and it remains with the possessor.</span>
To state it differently, there is a a presumption of the rightful possession by the defendant (<i>hazakah</i>) the possessor of the object.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Should a Jew swear that he will not sell one of his assets and he sells it, the sale is void due to the fact that he transgressed his oath. See Rabbi Yitzhak ben Rabbeinu Peretz, <i>Hagahot Mordekhai Shevuot</i>, 3:784; <i>Rema SA YD</i> 230; <i>Sma, SA HM</i> 208:3. In other words, in pursuance to this position, we extract the asset from the <i>muhzak</i>, the buyer. In other words, the violation of an <i>issur</i>, namely noncompliance with an oath, impacts upon a monetary matter resulting in the <i>muhzak</i> losing his asset! However, others reject this view. See <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 208:2; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer YD</i> 2:308. Accordingly, the asset remains with the <i>muhzak</i>.</i> Pursuant to <i>beit din</i> procedure, a party in a dispute regarding a monetary matter may argue as follows: “I want the court to rule in my favor, which is based on the position of Rabbi X who affirms my claim.”
Under certain prescribed conditions, we will accept his position even if Rabbi X’s view represents the minority, and the majority rule differently. Such an argument, “he sides with me” (known as “<i>kim li</i>”) can be invoked either by the <i>muhzak</i>, who is in possession of the disputed item or by a <i>beit din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding the authorization of a <i>beit din</i> to advance a plea for “<i>he sides with me</i>,” see <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>HM</i> 38; <i>Teshuvot Beit David HM</i> 5. For additional authorities who either endorse or reject this position, see E. Batzri, <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, vol. 4, 143, n. 3.</i> In short, in monetary matters, we do not follow the majority, and the governing rule is that the plaintiff has the burden of proving his claim is (<i>ha-motzi mi-havero alav ha-re’ayah</i>) . The claim of “<i>he sides with me</i>” reinforces the defendant’s presumption and is memorialized by Rema with the caveat that a <i>dayan</i> who has expertise in dealing with <i>halakhic</i> doubts ought to render his independent decision.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rema, HM</i> 25:2. For the limitations upon advancing such a plea, see <i>Sma, SA HM</i> 25:16; <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 25:17; <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, <i>Kitzur Tokfo Kohen</i>, (<i>HM</i>, following section 25), 124; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, 7, <i>HM</i> 2:1; <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, supra n. 48, 141–158.</i> In sum, the recognition of the plea “<i>he sides with me</i>” reinforces that in a case of <i>a</i> double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding a monetary matter, we do not remove the object from the possessor. In fact, “even 1000 doubts (<i>halakhic</i>) will not extract money from the possessor.”<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kitzur Tokfo Kohen</i> 120; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat, Kelalei ha-Tefisah</i> 24; <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, supra n. 48, 151, n. 12. On the one hand, <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, supra n. 49, 123–124, argues that one cannot advance a plea of “he sides with me” regarding an opinion which is not cited by SA and Rema. However, some decisors claim alternatively that in a situation of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt one can submit such a plea. See File no. 1097040/11, Haifa Regional Beit Din, 9 Sivan 5778. Secondly, once parties agree to authorize a <i>beit din</i> to address their contentious matter, neither litigant may submit a plea of “he sides with me”. See <i>Teshuvot Darkhei Noam HM</i> 15; R. Z.N. Goldberg, “In Praise of Compromise,” (Hebrew) 1 <i>Mishpetei Eretz</i> 78, 79 (2002). See further, supra n. 48.<br>Moreover, one is preempted from advancing such a plea against the accepted rabbinical decisor (<i>mara d’atra</i>). See <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 2:825; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 165; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>HM</i> 2.</i>
On the other hand, when dealing with prohibitions (<i>issurim</i>), such as whether a <i>beit din</i> may obligate a husband to give a <i>get</i>, a party to the divorce or the <i>beit din</i> must refrain from advancing an argument of “<i>he sides with me.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Darkhei Noam EH</i> 40; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah EH</i> 82, <i>ha-Gahot ha-Tur</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam YD</i> 62; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 6.</i>
Moreover, it should be no surprise to find that the Talmud exhorts us to refrain from drawing an analogy (a <i>hekesh</i>) between the two realms of monetary matters (<i>mamon</i>) and ritual issues (<i>issur ve-heter)</i>: “no analogy in a matter involving ritual law may be established from a monetary matter,”<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berakhot</i> 19b. For exceptions to this rule, see E. Shochetman, “On analogy in decision-making in Jewish law and the Foundations of Law Act,” (Hebrew), 13 <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> 307, 324–344 (1988).</i> and conversely, “no analogy in a monetary matter may be established from a matter concerning ritual law.”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 46b. For exceptions to this rule, see E. Shochetman, supra n. 52.</i> For example, we cannot invoke a <i>plea “he sides with me”</i> (<i>kim li</i>) regarding child placement because we are dealing with <i>issurim</i>, namely obligations between man and God as well as potential child endangerment.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Noam</i>, supra n. 51; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah,</i> supra n. 51. See also, <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 144; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 148.</i>
Comparably, in a monetary matter (<i>mamon),</i> the contentious parties may choose to engage in compromise (<i>pesharah</i>), such as apportioning an asset between the two parties, while concerning a <i>safek</i> in an issue of <i>issura</i>, ritual law, one cannot raise a compromise between two parties. Moreover, whereas in matters of ritual law, an hour of emergency (a <i>she’at ha-dehak</i>), we may rely upon a minority opinion,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 44, 105; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 36; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 17:47; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 188; <i>Sedei Hemed ha-Shalem, Kelalim, Ma’arkehet Kaf</i>, 109–110. Cf. <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 17:31.<br>See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 247–250, 252–256.</i> in issues of monetary matters, we may invoke the rule that the plaintiff has the burden of proving his claim rather than relying upon a minority view.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:33.</i> Finally, the systemic rule is that in a case of <i>doubt</i> regarding a monetary manner we act leniently, while in a situation of prohibitions we act stringently.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 73b.</i> The corollary to this rule regarding matters of prohibitions is that if the doubt emerges from an issue that is of a biblical nature (is <i>de-oraita</i>) we resolve it stringently, whereas in a matter of doubt that arises from a matter of a rabbinic nature (a <i>de-rabbanan</i>) we act leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berakhot</i> 19b; <i>Shabbat</i> 34a; <i>Beitzah</i> 3b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 60a.</i> As such, given the distinction between monetary issues and ritual matters, we cannot derive the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual issues from the <i>halakhot</i> of financial matters<i>,</i> and conversely we cannot derive the <i>halakhot</i> of monetary issues from matters of ritual law.
In sum, <i>halakhic</i> doubt may reflect the notion that both opinions are true or that only one view is true. In cases of <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding prohibitions which is the subject of this study, there exist specific <i>halakhot</i> which teach us that this realm of <i>Halakhah</i> is readily distinguishable from <i>halakhic</i> doubt concerning monetary matters.
The implication of our presentation is that the emergence of a doubt is interrelated with the issue being examined. In other words, should an individual be bereft of knowledge of <i>Halakhah,</i> a personal doubt arises as to how he should conduct himself. His ignorance of <i>Halakhah</i> does not constitute a <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taz SA YD</i> 98:6; <i>Shakh</i>, ad locum 9; <i>Shakh SA YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 34. Even if the entire world is ignorant on a given matter, it is not deemed a <i>halakhic</i> doubt. See <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 154:34. Cf. <i>Taz</i>, op. cit.; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 12.</i> The <i>halakhic</i> doubt emerges from the differing understandings of credentialed and educated authorities who attempt to resolve what ought to be the <i>Halakhah</i> regarding a particular question.

Chapter 2; The foundations for invoking a double halakhic doubt to void a marriage

<b>The foundations for invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage</b>
On July 29, 1999, the Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, the highest rabbinical court under Israel’s Chief Rabbinate, addressed the case of a person who was married Jewishly in the Soviet Union, where the rabbis who officiated at marriages and divorces were unfamiliar with the relevant <i>halakhot</i>. Subsequent to getting divorced, she remarried, sired a daughter and immigrated to Israel. Upon reaching maturity and desiring to marry, the child submitted her parents’ divorce papers to the Israeli rabbinate in order to be recognized as a member of the Jewish community and have her marriage recognized by the Rabbinate. As we know, in accordance with Israeli law, the Chief Rabbinate, accompanied by its institutions such as its network of <i>battei din</i> (rabbinical courts), has exclusive jurisdiction regarding matters of marriage and divorce. Consequently, we understand her request to receive recognition from the Chief Rabbinate.
Varying reasons were given by the <i>beit din</i> which allowed her to receive recognition.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR</i>) 21:10</i> One of the arguments on behalf of recognition offered by Rabbi Shlomo Amar, a member of the panel, is the articulation of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (hereinafter: a double doubt/uncertainty or a <i>sefek sefeika</i>). The implementation of this technique allowed Rabbi Amar to engage in voiding the marriage (<i>bittul kiddushin</i>) as well as the divorce. In order to properly understand the <i>halakhic</i> background of his line of reasoning for voiding the marriage, we shall introduce the reader to a few concepts.
