The establishment of a marital relationship between a Jew and a Jewess may be loosely said to be an agreement, albeit a very special one, which establishes “a personal status,” known in <i>halakhic</i> parlance as a presumption of being a married woman (“a <i>hezkat eishet ish</i>”), based upon the willing consent of the parties. One of the numerous consequences of being accorded this status is that a wife cannot engage in an extramarital affair with another man, and a husband cannot take part in an illicit affair with another woman. Such behavior is deemed an act of adultery (“<i>ne’uf </i>”) and is a violation of Biblical law, i.e. an <i>issur de’oraita</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra</i> 18:20, 20:10; <i>Sefer ha-Hinnukh</i>, Mitzvat lo-Ta’aseh 34.</i>
However, if the execution of the marriage ceremony is not conducted in a proper <i>halakhic</i> fashion, under certain conditions there emerges “a <i>safek kiddushin,</i>” loosely translated as a doubtful marriage. In other words, her presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) as a married woman is placed into doubt, or, to state it differently, there is a doubt on a biblical level (a <i>safek de’oraita</i>) if she actually is married. In cases of doubt, under certain conditions, we apply the systemic rule: any doubt which emerges from a biblical matter is ruled stringently; a doubt which arises from a rabbinic matter we rule leniently (<i>kol sefeika de’oraita le’humra, kol sefeika de’rabbanan le’kula</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beitzah</i> 3b.</i>
As such, the question arises as to whether the application of the rule: any doubt which emerges from a biblical matter is ruled stringently (<i>kol sefeika de’oraita le’humra</i>) means that we rule stringently on a biblical level or on a rabbinic level? Is the presence of a doubt regarding the <i>Halakhah</i> (<i>sefeika de’dina</i>) to be distinguished from a doubt concerning factual reality such as a doubtful marriage (a <i>safek kiddushin</i>), such that it will be resolved stringently on a biblical level? Or is it to be resolved stringently on a rabbinic level?
A review of the responsa (<i>teshuvot</i>) of many later authorities (<i>Aharonim</i>) will show that it is their understanding that “the opinion of a few” (a <i>da’at me’ut</i>) is that under certain conditions a biblical doubt (a <i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be is to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yeshuot Ya’akov OH</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 41; <i>Teshuvot Be’er Yitzhak YD</i> 1, <i>anaf</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev YD</i> 1, 118, 260; <i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer OH</i> 30; <i>Torat ha-Shelamim</i>, <i>Kuntres ha-Sefeikot</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 2:2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Saba Kadisha</i> 3:23; <i>Teshuvot Shivat Tzion</i> 49; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, Gerushin</i> 8:11; <i>Teshuvot Mishkenot Ya’akov OH</i> 260; <i>Kehunat Olam</i> 78; <i>Petah ha-Devir</i> 1:190; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Hullin</i> 10b; <i>Erekh Shulhan YD</i> 62; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Olam YD</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 1, <i>OH</i> 8. For additional <i>Aharonim</i> who identify this position as the majority view, see <i>Birkat Ya’akov</i> 10. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:93; <i>ha-Saba Kadisha</i>, op. cit.; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 12 (19).</i> Well-known and well-respected early authorities (<i>Rishonim</i>) and later authorities (<i>Aharonim</i>) alike such as Rashi, Tosafot, Ramban, Rashba, Ran, Tur, Rabbi Y.E. Spektor, Rabbi Ya’akov Reicher, Rabbi A. Eiger, Rabbi Ya’akov of Karlin, Rabbi Y. Orenstein, Rabbi Shalom Schwadron and Rabbi Shalom Messas align themselves with this position.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Kiddushin</i> 73a in the name of Rashi; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110 in the name of Rashi; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 1 in the name of Tosafot, 25; <i>Birkei Yosef, OH</i> 588 (20) in the name of Tosafot; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Hullin</i> 22a; <i>Teshuvot R. Yosef me-Slutsk</i> 37 in the name of Ramban; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:188 in the name of the Ramban; <i>Teshuvot Maharit YD</i> 1 in the name of <i>Ramban; Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 73a, <i>Hullin</i> 53b; <i>Torat ha-Bayit, Bayit Revi’i</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:401; <i>Ran on Rif, Kiddushin</i> 16b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Tur YD</i> 110; <i>Be’er Yitzhak</i>, supra n. 4; <i>Torat ha-Shelamim</i>, supra n. 4; <i>Sefer Yam ha-Talmud le-Ba’alei Mefrashei Hayam</i>, 64a; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 1; <i>Darkhei Teshuvah YD</i> 110:105; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi A. Eiger</i>, <i>Mahadura Tinyana</i> 68; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov OH</i> 260, <i>EH</i> 43; <i>Yeshuot Ya’akov</i>, supra n. 4; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:156, 3:95; <i>Da’at Torah YD</i> 57; <i>Mizrah Shemesh</i>, <i>Beit ha-Safek</i> (end).</i>
On the other hand, there is the majority opinion, including: Bahag, Rif, Rambam, Rosh (possibly), Ra’vaad, Semag, Ramah, Meiri, Ritva, Rabbi Yosef Karo (possibly), Rabbi David ibn Zimra, Rabbi Mordekhai ha-Levi, Hida, Rabbi Ya’akov Emden and Rabbi Ovadiah Yosef, who rule that under certain conditions (e.g. a doubt what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be), a biblical doubt (a <i>sefeika d’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha’amek Sheilah</i> 68 <i>in the name of Bahag; MT, Tumat ha-Met</i> 9:12<i>, Avot ha-Tumah</i> 16:1<i>, Issurei Biah</i> 18:17<i>, Kilayim</i> 10:27<i>; Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> 310; <i>Alfasi, Pesahim</i> 40b, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 42 in the name of Rosh; <i>Teshuvot Mutzal me-Aish</i> 12 in the name of Rosh; <i>Sedei Hemed ha-Shalem</i>, 5, <i>Ma’arekhet Samakh</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 10 (Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Yoma</i> 8:7, <i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer OH</i> 30 and <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 2 in the name of Rosh); <i>Ra’vaad, Tumat ha-Met</i>, op. cit., <i>Kilayim</i>, op. cit.; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur</i> YD 228 in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Teshuvot Yad Eliyahu of Lublin</i> 49; <i>Semag</i>, Negative Commandments 121; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramah, Kiddushin</i> 39a; <i>Sefer Beit ha’Behirah, Shabbat</i> 23a, <i>Hagigah</i> 4a, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 39b; <i>SA OH</i> 616:2, <i>YD</i>, 110:9, 302:1, <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>OH</i> 3(1) in the name of Shulhan Arukh; (Cf. <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 13 in the name of Shulhan Arukh) (Cf. <i>Noam Siah,</i> vol. 1, 252–275 who argues that it is unclear what Rabbi Karo’s stance is concerning this issue)<i>; Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:93; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 8; <i>Birkei Yosef</i> 184:1; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 1, <i>OH</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Mutzal me-Aish</i> 1:12; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev YD</i> 1:87 (Cf. <i>YD</i> 2:4, 118); <i>Teshuvot Ta’alumot Lev</i> 2:15; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 3, 6, <i>EH</i> 3, 6 <i>YD</i> 24. See also, <i>Pri Megadim Petihah le-Birkot ha-Shahar</i>; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yosef YD</i> 19; <i>Mizgeret Shulhan</i> 110; <i>Mahazik Berakha</i> 589:6; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, 10, <i>YD</i> 6:9–10. Cf. <i>Pri Hadash</i> who claims that there is a consensus regarding <i>halakhic</i> doubts, even in pursuance to Rambam and Ra’avad that a doubt on the biblical level is to be treated stringently on a biblical level. See <i>Pri Hadash,</i> supra n. 5 in the name of the famous view of Ran on Rif, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b. Subsequently, <i>Pri Hadash</i> changes his mind.<br>There are over 25 additional “later authorities” (<i>Aharonim</i>) who either subscribe to this view and/or who state that this view is reflective of the majority of decisors. See <i>Noam Siah</i> 12 (2). In fact, some authorities note that the majority of decisors subscribe to this view. See <i>Ridvaz</i>, op. cit.; <i>Pnei Yehoshua Pesahim</i> 9b, s.v. ha’nu; <i>Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> op. cit.; <i>Zikhron Yosef,</i> op. cit. Cf. other decisors who argue that the opposing approach is the dominant view. See infra n. 13.</i>
Assuming that we are permitted to invoke a double doubt beyond those that are explicitly mentioned in the Talmud and among early authorities and it can be employed <i>a priori</i> (<i>le-khatehillah</i>), in pursuance to Rabbi Yosef Karo’s ruling, a double uncertainty (a <i>sefek sefeika</i>) can nullify a biblical prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In contradistinction to <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110, <i>Sefeik Sefeika</i> 36, we ascribe to the position that one can introduce a double doubt which is not explicated or mentioned in the Talmud and early authorities. <i>See Kereti u-Peleti</i>, <i>Beit ha-Safek</i>; <i>Beit Yitzhak</i>, <i>Sha’ar ha-Sefeikot</i>, <i>Sefek Sefeika</i> 21; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 18 in the name of Maharit; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>YD</i> 24.<br>Furthermore, we subscribe to the posture that one can invoke a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt <i>a priori</i>. See infra n. 9; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:117; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 4; Rabbi Y. Abulafia, <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak OH</i> 3; <i>Shulhan Gavoah</i>, <i>Kelalei Safeik Derabbanan</i> 93; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Shlomo</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Lev Hayyim</i> 121; <i>Teshuvot Perah Shushan EH Kelal</i> 3, <i>siman</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 1 <i>YD</i> 21; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 131. <i>SA YD</i> 110:4.</i> Assuming that “a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level” applies to a doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (<i>sefeika de’dina</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Gittin</i> 46a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur YD</i> 228 in the name of Ran; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 22:18; <i>Sheilat Ya’avetz</i>, supra n. 6; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed (</i>Lublin) <i>EH</i> 37 (end); <i>Pri Hadash,</i> supra n. 5, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Nahalat Yehoshua</i> 6; <i>Yabia Omer</i>, supra n. 6. Cf. those who contend that in <i>sefeika de’dina</i> Rambam concurs that a <i>safek de’oraita</i> is resolved stringently on a biblical level. See <i>Kapot Temarim</i>, <i>Sefeika De’oraita</i>; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev, YD</i> 1:260; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim YD</i> 75, <i>EH</i> 1; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov EH</i> 43; <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama 1:256; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Tinyana 68; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 2:1; <i>Darkhei Teshuvah, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i>, 205; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, op. cit. In other words, according to these authorities, Rambam’s view, supra n. 6, is only applicable to cases of factual doubt.</i> “If there exists a double doubt one must rule leniently” is to be understood as meaning that the first doubt is converted from a biblical prohibition to a rabbinic one and results in a stringent position (namely that the matter is prohibited rabbinically) which is compounded by the second doubt which creates a rabbinic doubt, and therefore, by dint of rabbinic <i>Halakhah</i> (“<i>din de’rabbanan</i>”) one may rule leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 34a; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Pesahim</i> 9b-10a, <i>Ketuvot</i> 9a; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh YD</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo EH</i> 39 (30); <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 20:3; <i>Hazon Ovadiah</i> 1:13; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Yavetz</i>, supra n. 6; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed of Lublin OH</i> 7:4; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Birkat Yosef Landa YD</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i> 8, s.v. <i>ha’ta’am ha’sheni</i>.<br>Whether one can rule leniently regarding a rabbinic doubt <i>a priori</i> (<i>le-khatehillah</i>) or <i>ex post facto</i> (<i>be’di’avad</i>) regarding a double <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty (<i>sefeik sefeika</i>) and/or a rabbinic doubt is subject to controversy. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>OH</i> 42 (2).</i> In other words, the ramifications of invoking a double doubt in a particular case is that the matter is permitted (“<i>heter gamur</i>”) without any taint of a prohibition (“<i>issur</i>”).<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Hayyim ben Attar, <i>Rishon le-Tzion</i>, <i>Sefek Sefeika</i>; <i>Torat Hesed</i>, ­supra n. 8.</i> As we have explained, this conclusion is based upon the fact that we adopted the position of Rambam and others that a biblical uncertainty (a <i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pnei Yehoshua</i>, supra n. 6; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3:1, 7:4; <i>Sheilat Ya’avetz</i>, supra n. 6; <i>Shem Aryeh</i>, supra n. 9; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yosef Landa YD</i> 49; <i>Beit Ephraim</i>, supra n. 8; <i>Teshuvot Amudei Eish</i>, <i>Kuntres ha-Sefeikot</i> 7.</i> In other words, there is a correlation between recognizing the effectiveness of a double uncertainty and one’s posture regarding whether a biblical doubt is to be treated stringently on a biblical level or if it is to be treated as a rabbinic innovation. Until now, we have been arguing that in order for a “double doubt” to be effective resulting in a leniency, one must adopt the view that a biblical doubt is to be treated stringently as a rabbinic decree.
On the other hand, espousing the view that a biblical uncertainty ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level does not necessarily mean that the application of a double doubt will be ineffective. The consequent leniency associated with the implementation of a “double uncertainty” is due the rule of “following the majority” (“<i>aharei rabbim le’hattot</i>”). The existence of one doubt creates a situation of a 50/50 uncertainty (“<i>ke-mehtza al mehtza dami</i>”), while the second uncertainty then creates a majority which results in treating the matter leniently on a biblical level.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashba</i>, supra n. 5; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Eruvin</i> 5b, s.v. ve-safek; <i>Shakh, SA YD 110</i>, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 27 in the name of Rashba; <i>Pri Hadash YD</i> 110:49; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3:4; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 2, <i>YD</i> 3. For alternative explanations regarding the effectiveness of the double uncertainty even according to those authorities who contend that a biblical doubt is resolved stringently on a biblical level, see <i>Tal Hayyim</i>, 372, 379–383.</i> In accordance with many decisors (<i>Poskim)</i>, this approach is the dominant understanding as to why the employment of a double uncertainty will be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>YD</i> 14, 7 <i>EH</i> 6:5; <i>Taharat ha-Bayit</i> 1, 135–136.</i> That being said, it is unsurprising that some authorities who subscribe to the posture that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level will understand the dynamics of a double doubt based upon the rule of the majority principle (“<i>rov</i>”).<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen, Pesakim u-Ketavim</i> 129 (end) in the name of Semag; <i>Maharit</i>, supra n. 5; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, supra n. 5, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i>, 1.</i> In other words, the effectiveness of a double <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty (a <i>sefek sefeika</i>) may be based upon the majority and is unrelated to one’s position regarding the debate as to whether a biblical uncertainty (a <i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level or by virtue of being a rabbinic innovation.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Yosef mi-Slutsk</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Shemen Rokeah, Tlitai YD</i> 3.</i>
Assuming that one endorses the view that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently by dint of being a rabbinic decree, and therefore the deployment of a double doubt is operative, or that a double uncertainty is effective by virtue of its own inner <i>halakhic</i> logic unrelated to the ramifications of the existence a doubt regarding a biblical prohibition, are there grounds to void a marriage utilizing the mechanics of a double doubt in cases of a <i>halakhic</i> controversy?
Some contend that when there is a presumption of a prohibition (<i>it’hazek issura</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosefta and Talmud Yerushalmi the term used is “<i>hu’khah</i>” or a variant term such as “<i>mo’khiah</i>”. See <i>Tosefta Mikvaot</i> 2:6–7, Zuckermandel ed.; <i>Talmud Yerushalmi Eruvin</i> 3:4.</i> such as when a piece of non-kosher meat mixes with two pieces of kosher meat, “even a thousand doubts” will be ineffective in providing a leniency, allowing the non-kosher meat to be consumed.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Issur ve-Heter</i> 26:2; <i>Darkhei Moshe Tur YD</i> 57 (end); <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, Tumat Tzara’at</i> 2:1; <i>Pnei Yehoshua</i>, <i>Kuntres Aharon, Ketuvot</i> 3b; <i>Pri Megadim YD</i> 110, <i>Sefek Sefeika</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i>, <i>Likutei Teshuvot</i> 113; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 49:6, 188:6; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19; Rabbi S. Shkop, <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i>, <i>Sha’ar</i> 1, 18–19; <i>Teshuvot Zivhei Tzedek YD</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:2(9).<br>However, if there are three doubts, Shakh and others will render a lenient ruling. See <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i>, 29–30; <i>Teshuvot Kol ben Levi</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon EH</i> 5.</i> On the other hand, numerous decisors claim that the application of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt will be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Moshe</i>, supra n. 17; <i>Torat ha’Hatat</i> 43:7; <i>Teshuvot R. Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Kama 37; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, supra n. 5, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 16; <i>Kereti u-Peleti YD</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Noda bi-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama <i>EH</i> 57; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22 (18); <i>Get Pashut</i> 129:13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, 6, <i>YD</i> 23 (5) in the name of Pri Hadash, Maharit and Maharam ibn Haviv, <i>EH</i> 3: 12, 15.</i>
Clearly, there are decisors who will employ a double uncertainty in the case of a biblical prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pri Hadash</i>, supra n. 5, 49; <i>Teshuvot Maharit YD</i> 2. See infra n. 23.</i> Moreover, as we mentioned earlier, the creation of a <i>halakhic</i> marriage establishes “a personal status,” known in <i>halakhic</i> parlance as a presumption of being a married woman (a <i>hezkat eishet ish</i>). As such, will the construction by an arbiter of a double uncertainty to permit an <i>agunah</i> to remarry be effective in the face of a presumption of a biblical prohibition (<i>it’hazek issura</i>), namely the prohibition of being a married woman? Implicitly or explicitly relying upon the view of Rabbi Mordekhai ben Hillel,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 21. Hence, it is of no surprise that Rabbi Mordekhai ben Hillel subscribes to the posture that <i>sefeika de’oraita</i> is to be resolved stringently on a biblical level. See <i>Mordekhai, Yevamot</i>, 88a.</i> some decisors contend that the construction of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (a <i>sefek sefeika</i>) under such circumstances will be ineffective.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 38; <i>Taz SA YD</i> 110:15; <i>Shakh</i>, ad locum 62; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Marhash</i> 30; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 68, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 149 in the name of Tumat Yesharim; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot, Kuntres Aharon</i> 46; <i>Avnei Miluim</i> 27:18; <i>Teshuvot Havot Da’at YD</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher EH</i> 1:29 in the names of Rabbis Alfandri, Ramaz Mahari Kubo and Simhat Yom Tov; <i>Teshuvot Be’rakh Moshe</i> 34. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv</i>, 4, Helek 3:118 who argues that this posture requires further deliberation.</i>
On the other hand, assuming that we are dealing with a <i>halakhic</i> doubt concerning a biblical matter, one may act leniently on a biblical level while one must act stringently regarding rabbinic law.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Beit Yosef Tur YD</i> 228 in the name of Ran; <i>Pri Hadash YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Nahalat Yehoshua</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev YD</i> 1:87; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 1 <i>OH</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Mikhtam le-David Paredo OH</i> 17. In the wake of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding a biblical matter, others contend that we act stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level. See <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 1; <i>Pri Megadim, YD</i> 384; 110, <i>Sefeik Sefeika</i> 25; <i>Teshuvot R. Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 68.<br>In other words, if one subscribes to the view that a biblical doubt is to resolved stringently on rabbinic grounds, we will apply the same approach to a <i>halakhic</i> doubt. Similarly, if one endorses the contrasting posture that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved rabbinically in a strict fashion, we will apply the same view to a <i>halakhic</i> doubt. See <i>Noam Siah</i> 5 (10).<br>Alternatively, as we explained earlier, in the text accompanying notes 14–15, the effectiveness of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt may be based upon the majority rule and is unrelated to the controversy as to whether a biblical uncertainty ought to be resolved stringently on a biblical level or by virtue of being a rabbinic innovation. In other words, even the decisors who endorse the position that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved rabbinically in a strict fashion may concur that the effectiveness of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt is based upon the principle of majority.</i> As such, numerous decisors, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike, argue that in the wake of a “double doubt” or a series of doubts as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (known in <i>halakhic</i> terminology as “<i>sefeika de’dina</i>”) regarding the <i>halakhic</i> impropriety of a ceremony of betrothal and marriage (<i>seder kiddushin ve’nissuin</i>), may, under certain conditions, nullify the presumption of a married woman and thus, in effect, will allow the woman to remarry without receiving a <i>get</i> from her <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak,</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 24 (48) (release from a levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22 (18), 62; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 1:68; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 2:51; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 130, <i>EH</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 21, 33, 48, 62, 64; <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 5, 8, 35, 48; <i>Teshuvot Ba’ei Hayyai, YD</i> 216; <i>Teshuvot Perah Shushan EH</i> 3:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:29, 31; <i>Get Pashut Gittin</i> 129:13; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Pesakim 37, Mahadura Tinyana 45; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo EH</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 111; <i>Kereti u-Peleti</i>, <i>YD</i> 110; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD</i> 110:5, 16; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 1:55 (7); <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev</i> 12; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Minhat Moshe EH</i> 11; <i>Get Pashut</i> 129:13; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 65, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 22–23 in the name of a dozen decisors; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Be’er Moshe</i>, Kuntres Binyan Yerushalyim 18; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev EH</i> 8; A. Makovsky, <i>Teshuvot Ohalei Aharon</i> 1:19; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19 (release from levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim EH</i> 3:11 (end); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 18, 6 <i>EH</i> 3 (8), 6, 8; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6; S. Messas, “ A doubt in coercion of a <i>Get</i>,” (Hebrew) 23 <i>Tehumin</i> 120, 123–124 (5763); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:73; <i>PDR</i> 21:10; File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, 11 Tishrei 5778; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rishon le-Tzion</i> 2:8 For additional authorities who endorse this posture, see numerous responsa, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike cited in <i>Sefer Rav Berakhot</i>, 126; <i>Mishpat ha-Get</i>, vol. 3, 495–499; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 3 (14), 6, <i>EH</i> 3 (9, 14–15), 6 (5) and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 159, n. 47.<br>In short, in the wake of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, we deem the matter as “an hour of emergency” (<i>sh’at ha-dehak</i>) and <i>ex post facto</i> (<i>be-di-avad</i>) we can void the marriage based upon a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt. See <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Ma’arekhet Get 30(6) and ha-Samekh 30(3) in the name of Rashba and Ridvaz. For the merging of the two <i>halakhic</i> concepts of an hour of emergency and <i>ex post facto</i> in the case of an <i>agunah</i>, see <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov EH</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24 (30).<br>For decisors who contend that a double doubt dealing with what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be can trump a presumption of the existence of a prohibition (a <i>hezkat issur</i>), see <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Kama 47; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD Shehitah</i> 18. Cf. <i>Pri Hadash</i>, supra n. 5, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i>, 16; <i>Eshel Avraham OH</i> 161:7 in the name of Pri Hadash.<br>For the debate as to whether one may employ a double doubt regarding the classical <i>agunah</i> where the whereabouts of her husband are unknown, see <i>Otzar ha-Poskim, EH</i> 17:15, subsection 123 (9–30).</i> Examples of this type of scenario may include under certain conditions the absence or the ineligibility of witnesses attesting to the establishment of betrothal (<i>eidei kiddushin</i>), witnesses failing to observe the prospective husband giving his prospective wife a ring, the propriety of utilizing a borrowed ring for the act of <i>kiddushin</i> or major flaws, either physiological or psychological, in the husband’s persona unknown to the wife prior to the wedding.
To state the above differently, as we mentioned, in the case of a double uncertainty, the first doubt converts the prohibition from a biblical prohibition to a rabbinic one, resulting in a stringent position by dint of rabbinic <i>Halakhah</i>. For example, if a doubt emerges regarding the validity of a <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated as marriage), one might have thought that the woman’s presumption of personal status as a single woman is determinative, since in a case of a doubt we do not deviate from her status as a single woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pri Hadash,</i> supra n. 5<i>, Kelalei Sefek Sefeika.</i></i> However, though she may remarry on a biblical level, nevertheless rabbinically she is required to receive a <i>get</i> before marriage due to a concern for the prohibition of being a married woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ran on Rif, Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:138.</i> While a <i>get</i> is required by rabbinic law in this case, and therefore one cannot void a marriage based upon a doubt regarding the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i>, nevertheless, since on a biblical level the woman is permitted to remarry, we may utilize this ruling as a supporting argument (a <i>senif</i>) to act leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 1:56; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 11, 13.</i> With the emergence of a <i>second doubt</i> based upon the dynamics of invoking “a double doubt” in accordance with certain preconditions, there may be a basis to void the marriage under certain conditions. In other words, the presumption of being a single woman allows for the employment of the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to be effective and void the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Get Pashut</i>, supra n. 23; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah</i>, supra n. 23; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6 (6).</i>
On the one hand, according to the dominant approach of Rambam and others, the voiding of the marriage would be based upon the rule that we act leniently regarding a rabbinic doubt. On the other hand, pursuant to Rashba and others, even though we are dealing with a case of a prohibition (“<i>it’hazek issura</i>”), a double uncertainty will be effective due to the application of the majority principle.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying n. 18.</i>
Now that we conveyed the <i>halakhic</i> underpinnings of the well-trodden tradition to void a marriage based upon the employment of a double doubt in cases of a <i>halakhic</i> controversy regarding <i>halakhically</i> questionable aspects of a marriage ceremony or a husband’s flaws (<i>mumim</i>), we may better understand Rabbi Amar’s ruling in the case submitted to the beit din panel regarding his application of a double uncertainty. As we presented, the <i>beit din</i> addressed the case of a person who was married Jewishly in the Soviet Union, where the rabbis who officiated at marriages and divorces were unfamiliar with the <i>halakhot</i> regarding these matters. Subsequent to the person’s divorce, she remarried, sired a daughter and immigrated to Israel. Upon reaching maturity and desiring to marry, the child submitted her parents’ divorce papers to the Israeli rabbinate in order to be recognized as a member of the Jewish community and have her marriage recognized by the Rabbinate.
Rabbi Amar’s recognition of the child as a member of the Jewish community is presumably based upon the argument that she was not deemed to be a <i>halakhic</i> bastard (a <i>mamzer</i>, a child sired from an incestuous relationship).<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Kiddushin</i> 3:12; <i>SA EH</i> 4:13.</i> In a case of a woman who was betrothed in a ceremony which is characterized as a doubtful marriage (“<i>kiddushei safek</i>”) and subsequently sired a child from a third party, the child is “a doubtful bastard” (a <i>safek mamzer</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 4; <i>SA EH</i> 4:24.</i> Though biblically a doubtful bastard can be a member of the Jewish community,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 73a; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:69.</i> however, rabbinically, the child cannot enter the community due to a doubt(s) concerning the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 74a; <i>MT, Issurei Bi’ah</i> 16:21.</i> Nevertheless, there emerges a debate regarding the legitimacy (the <i>kashrut</i>) of a doubtful bastard whether we rule stringently or leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Poskim, EH</i> 4:24; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 116.</i> Implicitly aware of this controversy and concerned for the stringent opinion, in a pithy three lines Rabbi Amar concludes that given that in our case we have a double uncertainty regarding the <i>kiddushin,</i> we invoke the ruling of Rabbi Akiva Eiger that there is no concern for <i>halakhic</i> bastardy.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 4 in the name of <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger,</i> Pesakim 128; <i>PDR</i>, supra n. 1, 22.</i> As such, she would be accepted into the Jewish community.
However, the primary thrust for her recognition is argued in seven pages by delineating a series of <i>halakhic</i> doubts in the <i>kiddushin</i> that transpired in her parents’ marital ceremony in Russia. His posture is predicated upon two foundations. Firstly, well aware that the establishment of a marital relationship between a Jew and a Jewess may be loosely said to be a contract, albeit a very special contract, which establishes “a personal status,” known in <i>halakhic</i> parlance as the presumption of a married woman (“a <i>hezkat eishet ish</i>”), Rabbi Amar aligns himself with the view that the employment of a “double doubt” will be effective to void the marriage in the wake of the existence of a presumption of being a married woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See text supra accompanying n 18 and n. 23.</i> As such, given that the marriage was <i>halakhically</i> invalid, the child that was sired by the couple was born out of wedlock. In other words, whereas in secular law she would be labeled as a bastard, in <i>Halakhah</i>, the child would be legitimate and bereft of any taint of <i>halakhic</i> bastardy (<i>mamzerut</i>) due to the absence of matrimonial ties of her parents.
Secondly, though absent from his presentation as well as from a responsum dealing with voiding a marriage as to whether a biblical uncertainty ought to be resolved stringently by dint of being of biblical origin or rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6:10</i> Rabbi Amar argues that we employ a double doubt by virtue of its own inner <i>halakhic</i> logic.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See supra nn. 14–15.</i>
In light of the aforementioned foundations, Rabbi Amar meticulously reviews the doubts emerging from her parents’ wedding ceremony. Given that the marriage ceremony was performed by a rabbi who was ignorant of the <i>halakhot</i> of performing a <i>kiddushin</i>, there were various doubts concerning the propriety of the <i>kiddushin</i>. Firstly, the learned men in this community testified that the witnesses required for validating the marriage (“<i>eidei kiddushin</i>”) were either relatives who are invalid as witnesses or nonrelatives who were equally ineligible to serve as witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i>, supra n. 1, 23.</i>
Furthermore, among the questions asked was whether the prospective husband gave his prospective wife a ring that he had purchased, was the recitation of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula, which may be loosely translated as the man’s designation of the woman as his wife (“<i>harei at mekudeshet li</i> . . .”) recited by the man <i>before</i> the giving of the ring, and did the witnesses observe the giving of the ring and hear the words of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula? Lest one challenge these issues by arguing that there is an assessed expectation (an “<i>umdana</i>”) that the ceremony was performed properly, invoking Rema,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 42:1</i> Rabbi Amar contends that with regard to matters of <i>kiddushin</i> we cannot rely upon an assessed expectation.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i>, supra n. 1, 22.</i> Following in the footsteps of Maharam of Rothenberg,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i> 4:993.</i> we may understand Rema’s posture due to his concern for the wife having the status of being a married woman as well as her desire to marry any man. Based upon the above, there was no intent to be married <i>halakhically</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, if the facts of the case indicate that there is a major assessment of expectation (an “<i>umdana de’mukha</i>”) that the parties intended that act of <i>kiddushin</i> ought to transpire, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be valid. See <i>Hazon Ish EH</i> 52:3; <i>Teshuvot Ezrat Kohen</i> 41; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 2:81–82.</i>
Lest one challenge his conclusion that there are ample doubts regarding the <i>kiddushin</i> by invoking the opinion that a double doubt regarding <b><i>halakhic uncertainty</i></b> will be ineffective when the double doubt as well as all the other doubts lead to the same result (“<i>shem ehad</i>”), namely the <i>halakhic</i> impropriety of the <i>kiddushin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The reasoning is that construction of “a double doubt” regarding at least two debates of what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be is to be construed as a single doubt concerning what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be. Hence, it is not deemed a double uncertainty. See <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 9a, s.v. ve’ei; Y. ibn Lev, <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 3:41, 51. Whether in fact Rabbi Yosef ibn Lev rejects this approach in his other rulings, see <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam YD</i> 91, <i>EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alshakar</i> 26; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 1:109. For additional responsa (<i>teshuvot</i>), see <i>Yosef Da’at</i> 10.</i> numerous legists support Rabbi Amar’s conclusion that it is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Magid Mishneh, MT, Issurei Biah</i> 3:2 in the name of Rambam; <i>Teshuvot Be’er Yitzhak EH</i> 6 (9); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 7 (2); <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 11 (7), 13 in the name of Rambam; <i>Teshuvot Matteh Aharon</i> 2:16; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 37.</i>
Finally, Rabbi Amar addresses the contention that they lived together for many years as a husband and wife and consequently, their engagement in intimate relations should be viewed as marriage via intercourse (“<i>kiddushei bi’ah</i>”). While it is true that the Mishnah Kiddushin 1:1 teaches us that one may consummate a marriage via having conjugal relations, this ruling is inapplicable. Firstly, today we do not establish a <i>halakhic</i> marriage via this method.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Teshu<i>vot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 42 in the name of Sha’agat Aryeh. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 237.</i> Secondly, the parties’ intent was to establish the marriage based upon the ceremony of <i>kiddushin</i> occurring under the wedding canopy (under the <i>hupah</i>). Since that ceremony was not performed in a proper fashion, consequently the marriage never materialized.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See infra chapter 4C, text accompanying notes 48–63.</i>
In conclusion, explicitly following the <i>halakhic</i> tradition (a <i>mesorah</i>) outlined above,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See text supra accompanying n.23. Finally, unmentioned in Rabbi Amar’s ruling, there exists a clear tradition amongst the authorities that one may implement a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in situations of <i>halakhic</i> bastardy. See File no. 102-59, Piskei Din-Yerushalayim, Dinei Mamonot and Be’rurei Yahadut, 217, 226 (Rabbi O. Yosef’s opinion); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:4(4).<br>May one advance the argument of a double doubt when it contradicts the ruling of Shulhan Arukh? If we accept the rulings of Shulhan Arukh as final and thus dismissive of contrary opinions (see <i>Teshuvot Maharif</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat u-Tzedekah be-Ya’akov</i> 2:5; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Yosef YD</i> 30; Rabbi Krochmal, <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 165), then one cannot utilize his position to establish a <i>halakhic</i> doubt, much less a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt. <br>On the other hand, if one accepts a Shulhan Arukh’s judgment as a <i>halakhic</i>-judicial determination between differing opinions (see Rabbi H. Sofer, <i>Zekhor le-Avraham</i>, 65–111, 5760–5761) implicitly we may be dealing with the resolution of a biblical <i>halakhic</i> doubt and therefore, one may join this doubt with another doubt and establish a double doubt. In other words, Rabbi Yosef Karo, author of the Shulhan Arukh may act under certain conditions leniently if he knew that there was a second <i>halakhic</i> doubt. See <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev</i> 2, <i>HM</i> 63; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>YD</i> 6:5. Based upon the latter position, Rabbi O. Yosef invokes on numerous occasions a double doubt to establish a leniency even contrary to the expressed ruling(s) of Shulhan Arukh. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 2, <i>OH</i> 8(18), 3, <i>OH</i> 28(17), 5 <i>YD</i> 15(1), 8, <i>OH</i> 8(2). For others who employ a double doubt even contrary to the view(s) found in Shulhan Arukh, see Rabbi Yitzhak Yosef, <i>Ein Yitzhak</i>, 3:115–116 in the name of numerous authorities.<br>Alternatively, one may contend that upon rendering a decision, Rabbi Karo himself was aware of combining the <i>halakhic</i> doubt emerging from one controversy with the <i>halakhic</i> doubt produced by a second dispute. Since the matter was a case of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt, Rabbi Karo would have resolved the matter leniently. Said conclusion is based upon the grounds that a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt even on a biblical level mandates leniency. See <i>Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>OH</i> 108(5). <br>Moreover for example, a review of Rabbi Yosef Karo’s rulings memorialized in <i>SA EH</i> 39:1–6 will demonstrate that he does not address explicitly whether there is a basis for voiding a marriage under certain conditions if a prospective husband fails to disclose to his prospective wife before the marriage that he possessed certain major flaws (a case of <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>). Similarly, a review of his rulings in Even ha-Ezer will demonstrate that Rabbi Karo does not stake out a position whether the Talmudic notion of a clear expectation (<i>umdana demukhah</i>) that “upon this assumption she did not betroth herself” (<i>ada’ata dekhahki lo kidshah nafshah</i>) may serve under certain conditions a vehicle to void a marriage. The emerging question is whether one can invoke a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt when both the above techniques to void a marriage have not been dealt with explicitly in Shulhan Arukh’s judgments? Given that Rabbi Karo neither articulated his position regarding this case of “a marriage in error” nor his view regarding a wife’s clear expectation, there exists no logical reason to refrain from deploying a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage, assuming other conditions are applicable in the particular case to allow for voiding the marriage. To state it differently, regardless of whether the Shulhan Arukh’s decisions are construed as final or a resolution of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt, in the event that Shulhan Arukh does not address a particular issue, the rabbinic consensus is that there emerges a <i>halakhic</i> doubt which can be joined with a second <i>halakhic</i> doubt to create a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.(This conclusion is premised upon the fact that the particular arbiter endorses utilizing a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage under certain conditions).</i> Rabbi Amar utilizes the technique of discovering a series of doubts as a vehicle to transform the child’s mother from a married woman into a single woman, thereby eliminating the concern of <i>halakhic mamzerut</i> (<i>halakhic</i> bastardy) from the child’s status.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Based upon the foregoing presentation, we have outlined the foundations for invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt as a vehicle to void a Jewish marriage. Moreover, the adoption of the position that a <i>halakhic</i> doubt emerging from a biblical matter may be resolved strictly on a rabbinic level equally extends to reliance upon a single view even if the matter is one of a biblical nature. Said conclusion is based upon a conflation of two postures. Firstly, as we know, according to numerous decisors, the application of the procedural rule “we follow the majority” is limited o resolving issues within the confines of a rabbinical court proceeding. See <i>Hullin</i> 11a; <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i> of <i>Rambam</i>, mitzvah 175; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:104; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur HM</i> 13 (end) in the name of Rashba; <i>Teshuvot Maharalbah</i> 147; <i>Teshuvot Torat Emet</i> 207; <i>Get Pashut</i>, Kelalim, kelal 1:5. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:116. Therefore, in the absence of a give and take among disputants and/or if the controversy is intergenerational, the majority rule is inapplicable according to Torah law. Subscribing to this opinion and endorsing the above position that a doubt which arises from a biblical matter we may rule leniently on a rabbinic level, argues Rabbi O. Yosef, we may void a marriage based upon a reliance upon a minority view concerning a biblical matter. See <i>Teshuvot Yehaveh Da’at</i>, at 1 Kelalei Hora’ah, nos. 16–17. For further discussion regarding the import of a single opinion in a biblical matter, see this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 239–262.<br>The second implicit premise of invoking a double doubt in order to void a marriage is that the <i>beit din</i> must initially identify at least two grounds for divorce which in effect give credence to a wife declaring “a marriage in error” or “on this understanding she did not give herself in marriage” and therefore, the execution of a <i>get</i> is only required on rabbinic grounds lest the public infer that a married woman is exiting her marriage without a <i>get.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Devar Eliyahu</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27. Given that the giving of the <i>get</i> is only mandated rabbinically, consequently, we may deploy a double doubt and void the marriage based upon the foundations that we have outlined in our presentation. See supra pp. 38–51.<br>For an alternative rationale for mandating the execution of a <i>get</i> on rabbinic grounds, see <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24(43); <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:48.</i>

Chapter 3; The employment of a double halakhic doubt regarding get coercion in order to void a marriage

<b>The employment of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion in order to void a marriage</b>
Addressing the situation of a husband who failed to disclose to his wife prior to their marriage that he was convicted for child abuse, the Yerushalayim Regional <i>Beit Din</i>, a rabbinical court under Israel’s Chief Rabbinate explores the possibility of whether such a flaw, marked by deception, would be grounds to coerce a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 917387/1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, November 17, 2013. In this ruling authored by Rabbi Y. Goldberg, an internationally respected authority regarding <i>get</i> coercion, briefly outlined the dynamics of utilizing a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion in voiding a marriage. Subsequently, Rabbi Goldberg reaffirmed the employment of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt concerning <i>get</i> coercion as a means to void a marriage in his work, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi</i>, Yerushalayim, 5773, 50–51, 131, n. 18.</i> To understand the background of this decision and its implications for employing a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt concerning <i>get</i> coercion as a vehicle to void a marriage, let us present a few <i>halakhot</i>.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>1. The nature of a <i>get</i> compulsion order</b></span>
Unlike American law, where a civil divorce is a decree of a civil court, <i>Halakhah</i> requires the volition of the husband and wife to dissolve a marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rubin v. Rubin</i>, 75 Misc. 2d 776, 348 N.Y.S. 2d 61, 64 (Fam. Ct. 1973). For the requirement of the husband’s volition, see <i>SA EH</i> 119:3, 6. Regarding the wife’s volition, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1; <i>Rema</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 119:6.<br>See further, J. David Bleich, “Jewish Divorce: Judicial Misconceptions and Possible Means of Enforcement,” 16 <i>Conn. Law Rev</i>. 201 (1984), who demonstrates how various American courts misconstrue the nature of a Jewish bill of divorce.</i> That being said, one of the ramifications of marriage is the creation of the status of a married woman (an <i>eishet ish</i>) which means, amongst other matters, that the decisor (the <i>Posek)</i> must tread cautiously in releasing a married woman from the bonds of matrimony prior to ascertaining whether there are grounds for executing a <i>get</i>. Absent any grounds for permitting the divorce, should a <i>get</i> be executed it may be viewed as a coerced <i>get</i> (a “<i>get me’useh</i>”), and according to the majority of authorities that <i>get</i> would be null and void on <i>biblical</i> grounds.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For a list of these authorities, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality</i>, vol. 3, 30, n.11.</i> Therefore, should the wife rely upon this <i>get</i> and subsequently remarry and have children, her offspring would be <i>halakhically</i> stigmatized as <i>halakhic</i> bastards (<i>mamzerim</i>), children born from incestuous relationships and would be living in sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Gerushin</i> 10:4, <i>Issurei Bi’ah</i> 15:7, 21.</i> In other words, the biblical prohibition associated with the presumption of being a married woman remains intact.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 134:1, 5, 7; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum, 10.</i> As such, we can understand why the <i>beit din</i> panel understood that in the event that there is a <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding whether one is permitted to coerce a <i>get</i> or not entails a biblical doubt. Should an arbiter err in mandating <i>get</i> coercion in a particular case, it results in the commission of a biblical infraction which produces a coerced bill of divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">However, regarding prohibited marriages such as a <i>kohen</i> who marries a divorcee, or in situations where <i>Halakhah</i> mandates that there are grounds to coerce a <i>get</i>, then there will be a <i>beit din</i> ruling of <i>get</i> coercion and the coerced <i>get</i> will be valid. See <i>SA EH</i> 154:21, <i>Rema</i> ad locum; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum 34.<br>As we know, in the face of a dispute whether we coerce a <i>get</i> in a particular case, we are dealing with a biblical doubt. See <i>Teshuvot Avodat Gershuni</i> 39; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 154:4. Given the existence of said doubt, the concern to avoid producing a coerced <i>get</i> emerges. See further infra n. 45. Consequently, it is unsurprising to encounter that if at all possible a <i>beit din</i> or rabbinic authorities would join various grounds for divorce together (<i>tziruf ilot gerushin</i>) in order to mandate <i>get</i> coercion. Each ground for divorce is self-sufficient to serve as a basis for <i>get</i> coercion. Yet, due to the specter of a coerced bill of divorce in order to strengthen the ground (and in effect, the ruling) we join it with a second ground or additional grounds for divorce. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:1, 73:6; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yosef, Yibbum and Halitzah</i> 2; Rabbi E. Ashkenazi, <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 96; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 20; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 262; <i>Teshuvot Ne’eman Shmuel</i> 66; <i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR</i>) 1:33, 38, 4:244, 251, 9:171, 184. For a lively exchange regarding the parameters of joining grounds for divorce in order to coerce a <i>get</i>, see File no. 467862/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, January 16, 2011.<br>In effect, upon invoking more than one ground for divorce, we may coerce a <i>get</i> based upon the existence of a double biblical doubt. If we are dealing with two doubts, each ground is subject to controversy whether <i>get</i> coercion is appropriate. For example if we are addressing whether we can coerce a <i>get</i> from a husband who engages in philandering and spousal battery, there are <i>two halakhic</i> doubts. Firstly, it is an uncertainty whether the <i>Halakhah</i> follows those decisors who claim that one can coerce a batterer to give a <i>get</i>. And should one argue that you cannot coerce a <i>get</i> under such circumstances, maybe the <i>Halakhah</i> is in accordance to those legists who contend that one can coerce a philanderer to give a <i>get</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Noseh ha-Ephod</i> 32.<br>Whether one can void a marriage in a situation of a double biblical doubt is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 167; <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Get 30 (6). See further infra text accompanying notes 45–55.</i>
The outstanding question which remains is if a <i>beit din</i> renders a compulsion order which is <i>halakhically</i> proper, what is the consequence of this ruling? Does a husband’s failure to comply with the compulsion order render the marriage annulled (<i>hafka’at kiddushin</i>) or not?
To reply to these questions, we need to address the basis for justifying a <i>get</i> compulsion order. Seemingly, the ongoing discussions and debates in <i>Halakhah</i> relating to the grounds to compel a divorce fly in the face of our conclusion that the execution of a Jewish divorce is a consensual process. How does a <i>beit din</i> compel a spouse to give or accept a <i>get</i> if the voluntary agreement of the parties is required?
Relying upon the Talmud’s validation of coercion regarding a sales transaction (“it is reasonable to assume that under the pressure he really resolved in his mind to sell”),<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 48b.</i> numerous authorities, (<i>Poskim</i>) conclude that this reasoning also applies to matters of divorce. In other words, consent stemming from coercive measures is to be construed as acting voluntarily in giving a <i>get</i> and is in consonance with what constitutes a willingness to accept <i>Halakhah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Yevamot</i> 53a in the name of <i>yesh omrim</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Yevamot</i> 53a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Kiddushin</i> 50a, <i>Yevamot</i> 53a; <i>Tosafot, Bava Batra</i> 48a, s.v. <i>ileima</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Bava Batra</i> 3:51; <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 53a; <i>Magid Mishneh, MT, Issurei Bi’ah</i> 1:9.<br>For a similar view in other realms of <i>Halakhah</i>, see <i>Tosafot, Avodah Zarah</i> 54a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 387; <i>Teshuvot Maharik ha-Hadashot</i> 29.</i>
One avenue to validate coercion is for a wife to pay for her <i>get</i>. As we know, concerning a sales transaction in a situation of duress, he resolved to execute the sale because he was paid for the item (“<i>agav oneseh ve-zuzei gamar u-makneh</i>”).<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Bava Batra</i> 47b.</i> Whether the coercing party must simply obligate himself to pay or must actually remit payment is subject to debate.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA HM</i> 205:1; <i>Sma SA HM</i> 205:5; <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 205:2.</i> However, the consensus is that the duty to pay or the actual repayment will bring finality to a sales agreement executed under duress. The same conclusion ought to apply to a divorce agreement executed under duress in order for it not to run afoul of the strictures of a coerced <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Bava Batra</i> 48a, s.v. ileima; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 35; <i>Ohr Zarua</i> 1:754; <i>Teshuvot Maharah Ohr Zarua</i> 126; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rid</i> 22; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 134 in the name of Rashba; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:76; <i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i> 35; <i>Beit Meir EH</i> 134 (end); <i>Teshuvot Mikhtav me-Eliyahu</i>, Sha’ar 7, siman 15; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 167:6; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 125; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:37, 3:44, 4:106; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 10:11. Cf. <i>Beit ha-Behirah Bava Batra</i> 48a.</i> To date, once a <i>beit din</i> under Israel’s Chief Rabbi’s network of rabbinical courts obligates a Jewish divorce, in the light of earlier authorities, the panel will attempt to persuade the wife to remit money or other benefits to the husband in order that he would be willing to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo EH</i> 80 (2); <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 262, 263; <i>PDR</i> 3:13, 5:71, 7:111, 8:36, 9:65, 4:106; File no. 467862/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, 11 Shvat 5771. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 151–152. See the addendum.<br>For a divorcee’s monetary relief from the consequences of an exploitative divorce agreement, see this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 82–96.</i>
One of the classic rationales given for this anomaly of being able to coerce a <i>get</i>, despite the fact that divorce must be based upon the parties’ consent, is briefly alluded to in the Talmud<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra,</i> supra n. 7.</i> and is expanded upon by Rambam who writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 2:20. (Cf. <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, Positive Commandment 222; <i>MT, Gerushin</i>, introduction) Precedential opinion may be found in <i>Rashi, Kiddushin</i> 50a. Various arbiters agreed with Rambam’s understanding. See <i>Tosafot Rid, Gittin</i> 88b; <i>Yad Ramah, Bava Batra</i> 48a; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:68; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat, SA, HM</i> 208, <i>Biurim</i> 9.<br>Explicit in this ruling is that the imposition of coercion of a <i>get</i> serves as an illustration of a means to enforce compliance with a religious commandment. See also, <i>Tosafot Bava Batra</i> 48a, s.v. elemah; <i>Teshuvot Maharit Zahalon</i> 83. In other words, there is a commandment to become divorced under certain conditions. Cf. others who emphasize that the commandment is to be divorced in a procedurally proper fashion. See <i>Sefer ha-Hinnukh</i> 455.<br>Alternatively, given that as a member of the Jewish convenant faith community, we are responsible for our fellow Jew which encompasses his performance of commandments (<i>arevut</i>), we are mandated to coerce the noncompliant to fulfill his divine obligations such as divorce if so required. See <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev OH</i> 1:48.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A person regarding whom <i>Halakhah</i> indicates that we should force him to divorce his wife and who refuses to divorce, a beit din in every place and at every time beats him until he says “I am willing” and he writes a <i>get</i> and this <i>get</i> is valid . . . Why is this <i>get</i> not void, as it was the product of compulsion . . . ? Since we do not talk of compulsion apart from one who was pressured and coerced to do a thing that he is not commanded by the Torah to do—for instance someone who was beaten until he executed a sale or donated a gift. However, in the case of one whose evil inclination drives him to avoid doing a religious commandment or to commit a sin, and was beaten until he complied with his duty . . . this (subsequent conduct) is not compelled from him rather he compelled himself due to bad judgment. Therefore, someone who does not desire to divorce (when <i>Halakhah</i> states that he ought to divorce – AYW), it follows from the fact that he wants to be part of the community of Israel that he wants to perform the religious commandments . . . and it is his (evil – AYW) inclination that is driving him and because they beat him until his inclination was subdued and he stated “I am willing” he has divorced willingly. . . .</span>
Aware of the doctrine of the consensual nature of <i>halakhic</i> divorce as well as the legitimacy of a <i>beit din</i> compelling divorce under certain prescribed conditions, Rambam introduces the notion that one must distinguish between “the real will” and “the actual will” of a Jew who is committed to <i>Halakhah</i>. The real will of this Jew is to comply with the dictates of our <i>halakhic</i> tradition (<i>mesorah</i>), including—but not limited to—delivering (or receiving) a <i>get</i> if <i>Halakhah</i> mandates such an action. However, his actual will, which may be driven by desires that are beyond his control, direct him (or her) to disobey the <i>Halakhah</i>. In such circumstances, by coercing the miscreant to obey the <i>beit din</i> by acquiescing to the divorce, we have not forced him (or her) to do what he does not want; on the contrary, we have enabled him (or her) to do what he (or she) really wants! As Rambam explains, the logic behind the legitimacy of a coerced <i>get</i> is that the husband does indeed want in his heart of hearts to obey the teachings of Torah scholars, <i>mitzvah le’kayeim divrei hakhamim</i>. And others have proffered the same reason for validating a coerced <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot ha-Rid, Gittin</i> 88b; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:68; <i>Teshuvot Ralbah</i> 36.</i> Clearly, the implication of staking out this position precludes the possibility of freeing a woman to remarry without the giving of a <i>get</i>. The raising of a doubt(s) regarding the validity of the marriage will not lead to the dissolution of the marriage without a <i>get</i>. In fact, whether our Torah scholars will mandate <i>get</i> coercion in a situation of what may be loosely translated as a doubtful marriage (<i>safek kiddushin</i>), such as a husband who fails to disclose to his wife prior to marriage that he has epilepsy or is impotent, is a matter of debate.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Cremona ed., 77 in the name of Rav’yah; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:101; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 176:6; A. Sheinfeld, “Obligating a <i>Get</i> in a doubtful marriage,” (Hebrew) 9 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 158 (5765). Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu she-Kofin Le-hotzi</i>, (Hebrew) Yerushalayim: 5773, 277–287.</i> According to certain decisors, the possibility of voiding a marriage under such circumstances is found explicitly or implicitly to be unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Elu she-Kofin Le-hotzi,</i> supra n. 16, 113–114, n.19.</i>
One of the implications of adopting Rambam’s view is that the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion is predicated upon the fact that a husband must be a committed member of our covenant faith community. Should he be an apostate, one cannot posit that his real will is to comply with <i>Halakhah</i> and consequently validate the coercion of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharitz</i> 1<i>:</i>83; <i>Mikhtav Eliyahu, Gittin</i>, Sha’ar 7:15; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:60; <i>Teshuvot ha-Gershuni</i> 39. Cf. <i>Ohr Sameah</i>, <i>Gerushin</i> 2:20.</i> As such, in dealing with a nonobservant Jew, some decisors have concluded that one cannot coerce a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahaneh Hayyim</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 1; S. Yisraeli, “<i>Get</i> coercion in <i>Halakhah</i>,” (Hebrew), 2 <i>be</i>-<i>Tzomet ha-Torah ve-ha-Medinah</i>, 108 (5751).</i> Hence, it is of no surprise that Rabbi Yosef Colon seeks another reason to validate <i>get</i> coercion, concluding that the imposition of coercion will annul the marriage (<i>hafka’at kiddushin</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 63.</i> Other authorities, such as Rabbi David ibn Zimra, argue that in a situation of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband who has a polypus (whose nose or mouth has a repulsive smell) and the like which engenders repulsiveness, it is incumbent to coerce a <i>get</i> which means for these decisors that the marriage is annulled.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:1228 (=1:157); <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 226; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban Yevamot</i> 46b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Yevamot</i> 46b, s.v. <i>dilma</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva Yevamot</i> 46b; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 124; <i>Teshuvot Bah ha-Hadashot</i> 92; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat HM</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 226. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Be’air Yitzhak EH</i> 10 (3); <i>PDR</i> 15:145, 157 (Rabbi Sha’anan’s opinion).<br>Rabbi Meir Urbach argues that <i>get</i> coercion is based upon marital annulment as well as the <i>mitzvah le’kayeim divrei hahamim</i>. See <i>Imrei Binah, HM, Dayanim</i> 1.</i> To state it differently, the solution of marital annulment is not only raised concerning a Torah nonobservant Jew but equally with regard to a Torah observant husband who possesses a major flaw(s) (<i>mum gadol</i>) physiologically, or for that matter psychologically, in his personality.
Adopting the notion that <i>get</i> coercion entails marital annulment, the emerging question is whether a <i>get</i> has to be executed, or if marital annulment suffices and she is free to remarry without receiving a <i>get</i>. Under what conditions can this annulment transpire?
A cursory review of its application in Talmudic times will provide an answer to these questions. Should a husband appoint an agent to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife, as long as the <i>get</i> did not reach the wife, according to Biblical <i>halakhah</i>, he is authorized to cancel the <i>get</i> in the absence of the agent and without the wife’s knowledge. Given that she may marry someone else in the interim and her children from the second marriage would be <i>halakhic</i> bastards, Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel introduces as a rabbinic legislation (<i>takanah</i>) that the voiding of the <i>get</i> may only be done in the wife’s presence. What is the <i>halakhic</i> consequence of the cancellation of the <i>get</i> in violation of the legislation? According to Rabbi Yehuda ha-Nasi, the <i>get</i> is null and void even though the legislation was voided and consequently the wife could not remarry. However, in pursuance to Rabban Gamliel, the <i>get</i> is valid, although biblically the <i>get</i> is null.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 33a; <i>Yevamot</i> 90b.</i>
A second Talmudic case where <i>kiddushin</i> was annulled at the time of divorce deals with a husband who gives a <i>get</i> to his wife on his deathbed but then recovered from his illness. Rabbi Huna claims that the <i>get</i> ought to be like a deathbed gift, which if he recovers ought to be invalidated. On the other hand, Rabba and Rava disagree, arguing that a deathbed <i>get</i> is to be distinguished from a deathbed gift which remains valid even after the husband recovers. However, they agree that on biblical grounds the <i>get</i> is invalid. Nonetheless, our Sages are empowered to nullify the <i>kiddushin</i>, permitting the wife to remarry another man.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 73a.</i>
The common denominator of these two cases recorded in the Talmud is that we are dealing with the authority of the rabbis to annul a marriage (<i>hafka’at kiddushin</i>) and that the annulment is accompanied by the husband giving a <i>get</i> to his wife against his will.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Ketuvot</i> 3a, s.v. <i>kol hamekadesh</i>; <i>Rashi, Gittin</i> 33a, s.v. <i>adatei de’rabbanan mekadesh</i>; <i>Tosafot Ri ha-Lavan</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 3a, s.v. <i>kol ha-mekadesh</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1162; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Shitah Mekubetzet, Ketuvot</i> 3a in the name of Ritva, Ra’ah, and Ramban; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ra’ah, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Yam shel Shlomo Ketuvot</i> 3:5.<br>Invoking the term annulment (<i>hafka’at kiddushin</i>) should not be confused with the term voiding a marriage (<i>bittul kiddushin</i>) in which a decisor is authorized to void a marriage by deploying the tool of “a mistaken marriage” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>), a wife’s clear expectation (<i>umdana</i>) regarding an emerging physiological/psychological condition or inappropriate behavior after the onset of the marriage, by invalidating the act of <i>kiddushin</i> (e.g. the absence of eligible witnesses standing under the wedding canopy) or the employment of a double doubt. Marital annulment, on the other hand in contemporary parlance entails the propriety of retroactively nullifying the marriage of a recalcitrant husband who refuses to give a <i>get</i>. In Talmudic and post-talmudic the technique was employed in other contexts. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 135, n. 1. See also, File no. 905457/10, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, September 11, 2017 who astutely distinguishes between the two concepts.<br>Upon addressing whether there are grounds for voiding a marriage in a particular situation, from time to time some authorities utilize the term of marital annulment. The employment of this technique within the context of voiding a marriage is beyond the scope of our presentation.</i> Unlike the incident of the <i>kiddushin</i> executed under duress, where the annulment transpired at the time of the questionable <i>kiddushin</i> and without a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas we mentioned in our presentation two cases of marital annulment which mandate the giving of a <i>get</i>, albeit a flawed <i>get</i>, nonetheless the two other Talmudic cases recorded in <i>Yevamot</i> 110a and <i>Bava Batra</i>, supra n. 7 dealing with annulling a <i>kiddushin</i> due to a prospective husband acting inappropriately during the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> does not require the giving of a <i>get</i>. Lest one argue that we are empowered to annul a marriage without a <i>get</i> in other situations such as an <i>agunah</i> where a husband refuses to give a <i>get</i>, our authorities rule that annulment. There are instances where our authorities utilize language of annulment when engaging in argumentation to void a marriage. See <i>Teshuvot Nishmat Hayyim</i> 126; <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i>, Mahadura Tinyana <i>EH</i> 104; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 6:159. This matter requires further deliberation. <br>In short, whereas under certain conditions a <i>beit din</i> is empowered to void a marriage, namely free a wife to remarry without a <i>get</i>, a <i>beit din</i> is proscribed from annulling such a marriage since a <i>get</i> is mandated and the husband is refusing to give one to his wife.</i> the annulment in the other case happened after the <i>kiddushin</i> and a <i>get</i> was required.
Given that our case of the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din deals with marital issues emerging a long time after the <i>kiddushin</i>, and as such is similar to the case of the cancellation of the <i>get</i> in the absence of its deliverer and a deathbed <i>get</i>, is annulment permissible? In the wake of the view that <i>get</i> coercion is based upon marital annulment in the <i>beit din</i> situation, are the grounds for authorizing marital annulment accompanied by the giving of a <i>get</i> today rooted in <i>get</i> compulsion on biblical or on rabbinic grounds? Prior to the fourth or fifth century C.E., rabbis received their authority to resolve matters from their immediate predecessors who “laid their hands” upon them, a process dating back to the time of Moshe who ordained Yehoshua. Given that there is the presumption of a married woman, according to Rabbi Yitzhak Lampronti, arbiters weren’t authorized to annul a marriage in the light of a double uncertainty or even a series of doubts.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pahad Yitzhak,</i> vol. 5, <i>Safek</i>, 107b. Though Pahad Yitzhak’s posture addresses a case which there are <i>factual</i> uncertainties, <i>a fortiori</i>, his view ought to equally apply to a situation entailing a series of <i>halakhic</i> doubts.</i> With the lapse of classical rabbinic ordination (<i>semikhah</i>), the Talmud teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 88b.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Abaye once found Rabbi Yosef sitting in a <i>beit din</i> and compelling certain men to give a bill of divorce. He said to him: Surely we are only laymen (without the classic ordination where arbiters {<i>dayanim</i>} received their authority from their immediate predecessors who “laid their hands” upon them dating back to the time of Moshe - AYW). . . . He replied: We are carrying out their mandate.</span>
Explicitly or implicitly relying upon this Talmudic passage, some authorities conclude that matters of divorce in general can be executed in contemporary times on rabbinic grounds.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Gittin</i> 88b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ran Gittin</i> 88b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Yevamot</i> 46b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, ad locum; <i>Teshuvot Be’er Yitzhak, EH</i> 10:3; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 1; <i>Beit ha-Levi</i> 1.<br>Some argue that annulment is limited to Talmudic times. See <i>Sefer ha-Yashar</i>, <i>Teshuvot</i> 24; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i> 64a; <i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur EH</i> 7:17 in the name of Terumat ha-Deshen.</i> Whether one adopts the rationale of the reinstatement of the husband’s real will, compliance with rabbinic authority or marital annulment as the grounds for <i>get</i> coercion, clearly legally as well as <i>halakhically</i> today, the Israeli rabbinical courts under the Chief Rabbinate are empowered to coerce a <i>get</i>, and should the husband be recalcitrant he may be imprisoned. In other words, even if a <i>beit din</i> subscribes to Ridvaz’s view that <i>get</i> coercion entails annulment, the implicit assumption is that the annulment is accompanied by some kind of <i>get</i> (<i>get kol de-hu</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Text accompanying supra n. 24.</i> Which of the above rationales (Rambam vs. Ridvaz) serves as the grounds for <i>get</i> coercion is unclear from reading the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din’s ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if the <i>beit din</i> adopts the Rambam’s rationale, then invoking the double doubt, the marriage ought to be voided. However, if the <i>beit din</i> is endorsing Ridvaz’s line of reasoning then the employment of the double uncertainty will result in the marriage being annulled and a <i>get</i> would be required. Given the problematic implications of such a position (see E. Shochetman, “Annulment of marriage – a possible way of solving the problem of refusal to provide a Get?” (Hebrew), 20 <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> 349, 5755–5757), we assume that the <i>beit din</i> is subscribing to Rambam’s rationale or some other line of reasoning. See also, <i>PDR</i> 15:145, 147.</i> Given that even if the panel would have adopted Ridvaz’s understanding of <i>get</i> coercion that it entails annulment, a <i>get</i> would still be required to be executed. As such would there be any grounds to argue that given that the Beit Din advanced two bases for <i>get</i> coercion, one may void the marriage?
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. The parameters of the repulsion plea (<i>mais ali</i>)</b></span>
Let us now understand the basis for the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din’s ruling.
Two types of argument concerning rebellion are found in the Talmud (Ketuvot 63b) on the part of the wife:
(1) <b>The rebellious wife by virtue of an argument</b> (“she torments him and grieves him”) – a wife who does not wish to divorce but refuses to engage in marital relations in order to cause her husband grief due to an argument that she had with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT</i>, <i>Ishut</i> 14:9; <i>SA, EH</i> 77:2. See the addendum.</i> However, in accordance with Rabbeinu Tam,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 63b, s.v. <i>aval</i>.</i> should the rebellious wife wish to be divorced without providing reasons, causing her husband grief in order that he divorce her and give her the value of her marital agreement (the <i>ketubah</i>), she is equally deemed a rebellious wife (a <i>moredet</i>) by virtue of an argument.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Perishah</i>, <i>Tur EH</i> 77:18.</i>
(2) <b>The rebellious wife due to revulsion</b> (“he is repulsive to me”– <i>mais ali</i>) – a wife who can no longer bear engaging in intimate relations with her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 63b, s.v. <i>aval amra</i>.</i>
In the present case, the wife’s reason for rebelling cannot be classified as the first type, for she has no interest in causing her husband grief, neither because of an argument nor so that he should pay the <i>ketubah</i>. On the other hand, the wife is not claiming that the husband is repulsive to her in conjugal relations, but rather she is repulsed by their life together, and does not wish to be married to him any longer. The question is, therefore, if the second type of argument regarding rebellion due to repulsion is limited only to cases in which the wife can no longer bear having intimate relations with her husband, or is it possible to argue that the same applies in circumstances in which the wife finds married life with her husband intolerable due to his general conduct towards her?
Rabbi A. Sasson struggles with this question, particularly in understanding Rambam’s view:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Torat Emet</i> 186. Cf. Rabbi Aharon Halevy, <i>Ketuvot</i> 63b, s.v. <i>heikhi dami</i>; <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 63b, s.v. <i>ugedolei hamehabrim; Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i> 1:2.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">That which is written in the Talmud “he is repulsive to me,” i.e., that she alleges that she cannot have relations with him because he is repulsive, like the meaning of “repulsive” in relation to pork, etc. But if her allegation does not relate to intimate relations, then it will not be an allegation of “he is repulsive to me,” and even if she says, “I do not want him because I hate him,” or that “he will no longer be called my husband,” etc., as is written in this context, this language does not give rise to a claim of “he is repulsive to me,” because it is possible that the hatred is not due to repulsion, but only because of an argument and a fight or something else, and then her argument is not to be reflective of the plea “he is repulsive to me.” . . .<br>And one can also say the opposite, that the meaning of “he is repulsive to me” is that “I hate him” and “I do not want him,” etc., i.e., he is repulsive to me, which means I hate him, and as we found in Rambam himself, who said at the end that “she is not like a captive that she should be forced to engage in sexual relations with one she hates, etc. .” From the fact that he said “hates” and not “repulsive,” we may derive that hatred and repulsion have the same legal status.
