And the reason that the Talmud did not use the expression “hatred” is not to exclude hatred, but in fact “repulsion” does include the pretext of hatred.</span>
After discussing the various proofs for each side of the argument, he concludes:
<span class="poetry indentAll">All this causes me doubts on this subject, and I have not found anywhere in the writings of the Sages a firm determination, or even some kind of determination on either of these sides, and it is possible that they did not mention it because it is so straightforward, but in any case the thrust of my argument, any expression of “I do not want him, I hate him, he is no longer called my husband, etc.” will fall into the category of “he is repulsive to me.”</span>
In any case, according to many other decisors, the claim of repulsion includes circumstances in which the woman is not interested in continuing to live with her husband, and a clear statement of repulsion is not required.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharik, shoresh</i> 102; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam, EH</i> 41; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 77:11; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek, EH</i> 262:11; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 1:55.</i> In short, the <i>halakhot</i> that regulate the situation of a rebellious woman due to repulsion also apply when the woman claims that she no longer wants to live with her husband.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>The evidence required to prove revulsion</b></span>
Even though several authorities are of the opinion that acceptance of the claim “he is repulsive to me” is contingent upon production of proof of the circumstance which generates the repulsion,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah, Ketuvot</i> 63b, in the name of Rambam; <i>Teshuvot Maharit, EH</i> 40; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:145; <i>Hazon Ish, EH</i> 79:16; File no. 992236/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, May 17, 2015.</i> many decisors contend that there is no need for admissible proof for this purpose, and it is enough if it emerges either from the wife’s words, from the circumstances,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 63b, s.v. <i>aval</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i>, cited in <i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 77; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 40. See also, <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18:2.</i> or if the wife provides an explanation, a “pretext” (an <i>amatla</i>), for her claim of repulsion.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Rid, Ketuvot</i> 64a; <i>Teshuvot Rosh</i> 43:8, in the name of Maharam of Rothenberg; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 4 (<i>Hut ha-Meshullash</i>) 3:35; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 93. <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 77:3 in the name of Tur and in the name of Maharam of Rothenberg.</i>
As for the “pretext,” most legists contend that it is not sufficient to provide just any explanation for the repulsion in order for the <i>beit din</i> to accept the claim. The explanation must be clear and convincing, and in their words, a “clear pretext” is required.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tashbetz,</i> supra n. 39<i>; Rema,</i> SA <i>EH</i> 77:3 and the commentaries, ad locum; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer 3, EH</i> 18:3–4; <i>PDR</i> 15:145.</i> The assumption is that despite the emotional and psychological aspects of “he is repulsive to me,” the wife who is suffering is capable of expressing her feelings to the court rationally, by clarifying the source or the reason for these feelings with a “clear pretext,” and it is incumbent upon the rabbinical court to determine whether the grounds are true and justified.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>3. The application of a double doubt (a <i>sefek sefeika</i>) regarding <i>get</i> coercion as a vehicle to void a marriage</b></span>
The aforementioned decision of the Yerushalayim Regional <i>Beit Din</i> focuses upon a suit for divorce filed by a woman on grounds of repulsion of her husband following a verdict in the civil court that convicted the husband of obscene acts carried out on minors. In its argumentation, the panel addresses whether there is a basis for coercing a <i>get</i> when a wife advances a plea of “he is repulsive to me” accompanied by a clear pretext. Though there were a few decisors who subscribe to <i>get</i> coercion under these circumstances,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashbash</i>, supra n. 39; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin v’Boaz</i>, 2:21 in the name of Maharam; <i>Teshuvot Hut ha-Meshullash</i>, <i>ha-Tur ha-Shlishi</i>, 35 in the name of the Maharam; <i>Tosafot Rid, Ketuvot</i> 63b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i>, 63a; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i>, 17:53.<br>Lest one argue that one cannot rely upon Rashbash’s ruling since it was offered in theory (<i>le-halakhah</i>) and not in practice (<i>le’ma’aseh</i>), nonetheless numerous decisors understand that one can coerce a <i>get</i> even in accordance to Rashbash. See <i>Teshuvot Seder Eliyahu Rabbah</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Maharmit EH</i> 40; <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv,</i> Mahadura Tlita’ah 1:350.</i> the majority reject this view.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Meisharim</i>, <i>Netiv</i> 23, <i>Helek</i> 8; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i> 126; <i>Teshuvot Mekor Barukh</i> 17; <i>Netivot Mishpat</i> (Algazi), 214a; <i>Ra’ah ha-Levi</i>, <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 149.</i> Moreover, some authorities would not even obligate a <i>get</i> in these circumstances.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed., 946; <i>PDR</i> 12:339 in the name of Rosh, Tur and Maharshal; <i>Teshuvot Ateret Devorah</i> 1:33.</i> Secondly, the <i>beit din</i> explores whether there would be grounds to coerce a <i>get</i> due to the fact that the husband acted deceitfully when he refrained from disclosing to his wife prior to their marriage that he was a convicted pedophile. Here again, upon <i>beit din</i> review; it was found that the propriety of a <i>get</i> compulsion order was equally a matter of <i>halakhic</i> debate.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 77:3; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 117:24; <i>Teshuvot Ohr Gadol</i> 5; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 35 (34). Cf. <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 77:8 in the name of <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:116; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:110; <i>PDR</i> 1:5, 10–11, 5:193, 241, 247.</i>
In the wake of this understanding of these two issues, what emerges from the decision of the <i>beit din</i> panel is that there are two <i>halakhic</i> doubts (hereinafter: double doubts): namely, whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> in light of a husband’s abusive behavior and whether one ought to coerce a <i>get</i> due to his deception. Without elucidation, the panel posits that since a doubt regarding the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion entails a biblical doubt, therefore we are dealing with a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt on a biblical level, where a <i>get</i> may be coerced.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A doubt involving the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion under particular circumstances is to be construed as a biblical doubt. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban Yevamot</i> 46a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, ad locum; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 70:3 in the name of Hatam Sofer and Beit Meir. In other words, if there is a <i>halakhic</i> controversy whether to coerce a <i>get</i>, due to the existence of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt we refrain from coercing a <i>get</i>. See <i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 70a; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 154:21. Should a <i>get</i> be coerced under such conditions, the <i>get</i> is null and void.<br>The assumption is that if <i>Halakhah</i> mandates the <i>get</i> ought to be coerced we are dealing with a situation that the wife can no longer live with her spouse and consequently, she is not bound (<i>shi’bud</i>) to him. As Dayan Izirer notes,<br>“The nullification of the servitude empowers her with the right to be liberated from all of the husband’s servitudes, including the primary servitude that she is prohibited due to him to others and she is preempted to receive all her marital rights because she was married to him.” See H. Izirer, “The Duty of the <i>Get</i> and Maintenance to a Rebellious Wife who is Repulsed by Him,” (Hebrew) 2 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 64, 99 (5754).<br>In other words, where there is an obligation to give a <i>get</i>, the woman may still be capable to live with her husband and therefore she is still bound to engage in conjugal relations with her spouse and this duty has not been annulled biblically. On the other hand, where <i>Halakhah</i> mandates <i>get</i> coercion it is the authority(ies)’s  determination that the woman is under duress by still remaining with him and consequently, she is no longer bound to have conjugal relations with her husband. See Izirer, op. cit., p. 102.<br>In short, the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion entails a biblical doubt regarding the presumption that she is a married woman. See further supra n. 6. Whether there are grounds to coerce a <i>get</i> is a biblical issue, but the actual implementation of the mechanism of <i>get</i> coercion is a rabbinic enactment, see Izirer, op. cit., 102–103; File no. 846913/2. Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, May 9, 2011. Therefore, a doubt regarding whether to apply coercion entails a biblical doubt whether there is a basis to coerce a <i>get</i>.<br>Whether one can void the status of “a married woman” in case of a compounded biblical doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>), see the debate in <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 167; Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi</i>, 51, n. 46, 131, n. 18 and infra n. 55.<br>Based upon the arguments submitted to the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, one can argue that we are dealing with three <i>halakhic</i> doubts. Firstly, given that the wife is advancing a plea of repulsion ( <i>ma’is ali</i>) we have a doubt whether we adopt Rambam’s approach to coerce a <i>get</i>. And if you should conclude that we do not coerce a <i>get</i> due to the suspicion that the wife is interested in another man, possibly where there is a “clear pretext”, we coerce a <i>get</i>. And if you should conclude that the existence of a clear pretext one does not mandate the compulsion of a <i>get</i>, possibly we should rule in accordance with Rif in the name of a Gaonic enactment that we coerce a <i>get</i>. Based upon the foregoing, Rabbi O. Yosef mandated <i>get</i> coercion due to the invoking of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (<i>sefek sefeika</i>) comprised of three <i>halakhic</i> doubts. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18(4), 20(35). Utilizing this double <i>halakhic</i> doubt concerning <i>get</i> compulsion in dealing with the plea of repulsion and in pursuance to the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din’s judgment as well as <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi,</i> supra n. 1 that a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in biblical matters permits us to void a marriage. The different <i>halakhic</i> planes for a plea of repulsion as outlined above are applicable to numerous marital situations which lead to divorce and as such may be invoked as a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in order to void a marriage in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance. See infra chapters 4E, n. 82 and 4F, text accompanying nn. 40–41.<br>Alternatively, we may advance an additional double <i>halakhic</i> doubt which would result in voiding the marriage. The first doubt is that there is a debate whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> based upon a plea of repulsion. Moreover, there is a second doubt whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> concerning a particular ground for divorce such as a husband who is physically abusive to his spouse. Based upon this double <i>halakhic</i> doubt one may coerce a <i>get</i> (see <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:3 (15); <i>PDR</i> 15:145, 153) and therefore we may void the marriage. See R. Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-Hotzi</i>, Yerushalayim, 5773, 50–51, nn. 46–47, 131, n. 8. <br>See Teshuvot Osher Hanan who rejects the invoking of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in regard to <i>get</i> coercion involving a wife’s plea of repulsion due to the fact that both Rabbis Moshe Sofer and Eliyahu Abergil would argue that in the wake of a <i>halakhic</i> dispute concerning whether there are grounds for <i>get</i> coercion one cannot coerce a <i>get</i> in order to avoid the strictures of a coerced <i>get</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i> 1:116; <i>Teshuvot Dibrot Eliyahu</i> 4:27; <i>Teshuvot Osher Hanan</i> 4, <i>EH-HM</i> 77(10).<br>(As Rabbi Ariel Holland of Tekoa, Israel notes, we know that from time to time there may be a <i>halakhic</i> controversy which is even (<i>shakul)</i> which means that there is an equal number of decisors who endorse one view and an equal number of arbiters who adopt the opposing opinion and <i>Halakhah</i> has not determined which position will be adopted as normative. In pursuance to Hatam Sofer’s grandson, his grandfather’s definition of a coerced <i>get</i> is limited to a dispute which is even. Consequently, in cases where the debate is uneven, there is no possibility of a coerced <i>get.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer EH</i> 59. As such, in accordance with this interpretation of Hatam Sofer’s position, there <i>may</i> be grounds to entertain the possibility of invoking a double doubt in relation to <i>get</i> coercion. However, most authorities apply a coerced <i>get</i> in situations of uneven controversies and as such would be unable to entertain the possibility of a double doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion.)<br>Obviously, Rabbis Yosef and Goldberg reject this posture and will invoke a double doubt under these circumstances. The implicit premise of their position is predicated upon the notion of a husband’s willingness to accept the <i>bet din’s</i> authority to render a judgment, a ruling which may not reflect <i>halakhic</i> consensus rather than constructive consent based upon <i>halakhic</i> unanimity serving as grounds for <i>get</i> coercion. See <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak, EH</i> 2:5, 35; <i>Hazon Ish, EH</i> 69:23; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:2.<br>For these two diametrically opposing perspectives in dealing with <i>get</i> coercion, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 35–43. <br>For a differing rationale for avoiding the specter of a coerced <i>get</i> due to a <i>beit din’s</i> reluctance to subscribe to one opinion emerging from a <i>halakhic</i> controversy that would permit <i>get</i> coercion, see <i>Tosafot Yevamot</i> 64a, s.v. yotzi; <i>Tur, EH</i> 154 in the name of Rosh; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam YD</i> 146; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 1:14.<br>Finally, may one advance the argument of a double doubt dealing with the repulsion plea when it contradicts the ruling of Shulhan Arukh? For example, given that Shulhan Arukh opposes <i>get</i> coercion in a situation where a wife advances a plea of repulsion (<i>mais ali</i>) and a plea of repulsion with a clear pretext (see <i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 77; <i>SA EH</i> 77:2, <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA EH</i> 77:6; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 77:5) as we presented earlier in this note, may one contend that there exists an argument for a double doubt regarding get coercion? If we accept the ruling(s) of Rabbi Yosef Karo, author of the Shulhan Arukh as final and thus dismissive of contrary opinions (see <i>Teshuvot Maharif</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat u-Tzedekah be-Ya’akov</i> 2:5; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Yosef YD</i> 30; Rabbi Krochmal, <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 165), then one cannot utilize his position to establish a <i>halakhic</i> doubt, much less a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<br>On the other hand, if one accepts Shulhan Arukh’s judgments as a <i>halakhic</i>-judicial determination between differing opinions (see Rabbi H. Sofer, <i>Zekhor le-Avraham</i>, 65–111, 5760–5761) implicitly we may be dealing with the resolution of a biblical <i>halakhic</i> doubt and therefore, one may join this doubt with another doubt and establish a double doubt. In other words, Shulhan Arukh may act under certain conditions leniently if he knew that there was a second doubt. See <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev</i> 2, <i>HM</i> 63; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>YD</i> 6:5. Based upon the latter position, Rabbi O. Yosef invokes on numerous occasions a double doubt to establish a leniency even contrary to the expressed ruling(s) of Shulhan Arukh. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 2, <i>OH</i> 8:18, 5, 8, <i>OH</i> 8, <i>YD</i> 15:2, 6, <i>YD</i> 24, 9, <i>YD</i> 6:4, 10, <i>YD</i> 43(2). For others who employ a double doubt even contrary to the view(s) found in Shulhan Arukh, see Rabbi Yitzhak Yosef, <i>Ein Yitzhak</i>, 3:115–116 in the name of many authorities. Consequently, it is unsurprising to find that Rabbi O. Yosef employs a double doubt regarding the plea of repulsion as it relates to <i>get</i> coercion. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18(4), 20(35).</i> In light of a controversy whether we can permit a wife to remarry when there is a biblical double uncertainty, the panel aligns itself with the posture which would allow for this remarriage. However, since it presumes that each doubt has to be even (<i>shakul</i>), which means that there must be equal number of decisors who will coerce a <i>get</i> versus an equal number of decisors who reject this possibility prior to invoking the technique of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt; this method could not be implemented. As we mentioned, the majority of authorities oppose <i>get</i> coercion vis-à-vis a husband who engages in pedophilia. And regarding the matter of misrepresentation, it is unclear whether the number of decisors who endorse <i>get</i> coercion that corresponds to the number of those who reject the implementation of <i>get</i> coercion.
In other words, in this particular case there were no grounds for <i>get</i> coercion due to the invoking of a double <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty.
As we mentioned, it is clear from reading the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din’s decision that the fact that most authorities rule against the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion prevents the invoking of a double doubt, and said conclusion is also supported by other decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 9a, s.v ve’i; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 33; 129:28; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 126, <i>Erekh Shulhan HM</i> 3.</i> However, it is our understanding that Bahag, Rif, Rambam, Rosh (possibly), Ra’avad, Semag, Ramah, Meiri, Ritva, Rabbi Yosef Karo (possibly), Rabbi David ibn Zimra, Rabbi Mordekhai ha-Levi, Hida, Rabbi Ya’akov Emden and Rabbi Ovadiah Yosef all rule that under certain conditions (e.g. a doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be), a biblical doubt (a <i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha’amek Sheilah</i> 68 in the name of Bahag; <i>MT, Tumat ha-Met</i> 9:12, <i>Avot ha-Tumah</i> 16:1, <i>Issurei Biah</i> 18:17, <i>Kilayim</i> 10:27; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> 310; <i>Alfasi, Pesahim</i> 40b, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Mordekhai, Yevamot</i> 21; <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 42 in the name of Rosh; <i>Teshuvot Mutzal me-Aish</i> 12 in the name of Rosh; <i>Sedei Hemed ha-Shalem</i>, 5, <i>Ma’arkehet Samakh, Kelal</i> 10 (Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Yoma</i> 8:7, <i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer OH</i> 30 and <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 2 in the name of Rosh); <i>Ra’avad, Tumat ha-Met</i>, op. cit., <i>Kilayim</i>, op. cit.; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur YD</i> 228 in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Teshuvot Yad Eliyahu of Lublin</i> 49; <i>Semag, Negative Commandments</i> 121; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramah, Kiddushin</i> 39a; <i>Sefer Beit ha’Behirah, Shabbat</i> 23a, <i>Hagigah</i> 4a, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 39b; <i>SA OH</i> 616:2, <i>YD</i>, 110:9, 302:1, <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>OH</i> 3(1) in the name of Shulhan Arukh; (Cf. <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3, EH 13 in the name of Shulhan Arukh) (Cf. <i>Noam Siah</i>, vol. 1, 252–275 who argues that Rabbi Karo’s view is unclear); <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:93; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim, Kelal</i> 8; <i>Birkei Yosef</i> 184:1; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 3, 6, <i>EH</i> 3, 6 <i>YD</i> 24. See also, <i>Pri Megadim Petihah le-Birkot ha-Shahar</i>; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yosef YD</i> 19; <i>Mizgeret Shulhan</i> 110; <i>Mahazik Berakha</i> 589:6; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 10, <i>YD</i> 6:9–10.</i> In pursuance to this tradition that we are dealing with a rabbinic matter, there is no requirement that the double doubt represent that the legists are equally divided (i.e. <i>shakul</i>) to determine whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> in order to free the wife without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed</i> (Lublin), <i>EH</i> 9 (5); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4, <i>YD</i> 12 (14), 6, <i>YD</i> 24:13, 10, <i>OH</i> 38 (4). Cf. <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 7:93.<br>Moreover, <i>Get Pashut</i> 120:26 and File no. 917387/1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, November 17, 2013 disagree with our conclusion. Clearly, many of the authorities cited infra n. 55 do not require that the arbiters be equally divided regarding the propriety of the issuance of a g<i>et</i> compulsion order or voiding a marriage prior to invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.</i> Even if one side of the doubt reflects a minority opinion, the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt will be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For the definition of “even” as referring to the arbiters equally divided regarding a particular issue, see <i>Teshuvot Zivhei Tzedek</i> 2, 110:158; <i>Teshuvot Beit David YD</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Yosef</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot Dvar Moshe</i> 3, <i>YD</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Hesed le-Avraham OH</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>OH</i> 16 (7); <i>Get Pashut,</i> ibid<i>.</i>; File no. 917387/1, ibid.</i> Finally, given that the rule of “following the majority” is applicable only to resolving issues within the confines of a <i>beit din</i> proceeding, one cannot speak of a majority opinion and minority opinion concerning intergenerational disputes.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:104, 3:304, 5:126; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ritva</i> 85; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur HM</i> 13 (7) in the name of Rashba; <i>Teshuvot Maharlbah</i> 147.</i> As such, the requirement that the decisors be equally divided regarding a pending issue is not mandated regarding a double doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tal Hayyim</i>, 523.</i>
Moreover, even according to Rashba and others that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level, does not necessarily mean that the application of a double uncertainty will be ineffective. The consequent leniency associated with the implementation of a double uncertainty is due to the rule of following the majority (“<i>aharei rabbim le’hattot</i>”). The existence of one doubt creates a situation of 50/50 uncertainty (“<i>ke-mehtza al mehtza dami</i>”), and then the second doubt creates a majority which results in treating the matter leniently on a biblical level.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 9a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:401; <i>Pri Hadash</i> 110:49; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3:4; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 2, <i>YD</i> 3. For alternative explanations for the effectiveness of the double uncertainty even according to those authorities who contend that a biblical doubt is resolved stringently on a biblical level, see <i>Tal Hayyim</i>, 372, 379–383.</i> In accordance with many authorities this approach is the dominant understanding as to why the employment of a double doubt will be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>YD</i> 14, 7 <i>EH</i> 6:5; <i>Taharat ha-Bayit</i> 1, 135–136.</i> Given the above lines of reasoning,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying n. 48.<br>Though in pursuance to <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 7:2, the requirement of an even number of decisors on both sides of the doubt applies to Rambam’s approach regarding doubt on a biblical level one must act stringently, nevertheless we have implicitly subscribed to the view that this posture would not necessarily mandate such a requirement. See <i>Teshuvot Yehaveh Da’at</i> 2:74 (271–272). A discussion of this matter is beyond the scope of our presentation.</i> there is no prerequisite that the doubt must be even, namely that the arbiters would be equally divided concerning the propriety of the issuance of a <i>get</i> compulsion judgment prior to employing the rule of the double doubt. As such, relying upon a well-trodden tradition (<i>mesorah)</i> of Ashkenazic as well as Sephardic decisors that sanctions the employment of a double uncertainty concerning biblical prohibitions including prohibitions of nakedness (<i>issurei ervah</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak,</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 24 (48) (release from a levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22 (18), 62; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 1:68; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 2:51; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 130, <i>EH</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 21, 33, 48, 62, 64; <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 5, 8, 35, 48; <i>Teshuvot Ba’ei Hayyai, YD</i> 216; <i>Teshuvot Perah Shushan EH</i> 3:2; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 1:55(7); <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:29, 31; <i>Get Pashut Gittin</i> 129:13; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Pesakim 37, Mahadura Tinyana 45; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo EH</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 111; <i>Kereti u-Peleti</i>, <i>YD</i> 110; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD</i> 110:5, 16; <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev</i> 12; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Minhat Moshe EH</i> 11; <i>Get Pashut</i> 129:13; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 65, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 22–23 in the name of a dozen decisors; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Be’er Moshe</i>, Kuntres Binyan Yerushalyim 18; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev EH</i> 8; A. Makovsky, <i>Teshuvot Ohalei Aharon</i> 1:19; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19 (release from levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim EH</i> 3:11 (end); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 18, 6 <i>EH</i> 3 (8), 6, 8; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6; S. Messas, “ A doubt in coercion of a <i>Get</i>,” (Hebrew) 23 <i>Tehumin</i> 120, 123–124 (5763); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:73; <i>PDR</i> 21:10; File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, 11 Tishrei 5778; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rishon le-Tzion</i> 2:8; Y. Goldberg, supra n. 16, 51, n. 47, 131, n. 18. For additional authorities who endorse this posture, see numerous responsa, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike cited in <i>Sefer Rav Berakhot</i>, 126; <i>Mishpat ha-Get</i>, vol. 3, 495–499; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 3 (14), 6, <i>EH</i> 3 (9, 14–15), 6 (5) and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 159, n. 47. Cf. those decisors who reject the application of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in various realms of <i>Halakhah</i>, see this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 159, n. 46.</i> the invoking of the double doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion under certain conditions will trump the presumption that she is a married woman and will reinstate the original presumption of being a single woman (a <i>penuyah</i>).
As such, freeing a wife without a <i>get</i> may be based upon the employment of a double doubt relating to two different debates as to whether one can <i>coerce</i> a <i>get</i> concerning two different grounds for divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, given that numerous authorities who opine that in the wake of a <i>halakhic</i> controversy regarding whether there are grounds for <i>get</i> coercion, one cannot coerce a <i>get</i>. See supra n. 45(end). Consequently, logically one may never employ a double doubt in so far as <i>get</i> coercion in order to void a marriage. As such, these decisors would not endorse voiding the marriage in our case. In pursuance to this approach, whether there may be conditions which sanction the implementation of a double doubt as it relates to <i>get</i> coercion we leave as an open question.</i> In short, in the wake of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, we deem the matter as “an hour of emergency” (<i>sh’at ha-dehak</i>) and a <i>priori</i> (<i>le-hatehillah</i>) we can void the marriage based upon a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Ma’arekhet Get 30(6) and ha-Samekh 30(3) in the name of Rashba and Ridvaz.<br>The implicit premise of invoking a double doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion in order to void a marriage is that the <i>beit din</i> must initially identify at least two grounds for divorce which in effect give credence to a wife declaring “a marriage in error” or “on this understanding she did not give herself in marriage” and therefore, the execution of a <i>get</i> is only required on rabbinic grounds lest the public infer that a married woman is exiting her marriage without a <i>get.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Devar Eliyahu</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27. Given that the giving of the <i>get</i> is only mandated rabbinically, consequently, we may deploy a double doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion and void the marriage based upon the foundations that we have outlined in our presentation. See supra pp. 64–70.</i>

Part 2; Rabbinic Authority; The Reality

Chapter 4; Case studies of a wife's claim for voiding a marriage and levirate marriage ("yibum")

a) The halakhic impropriety of a marriage ceremony

<b>A. The <i>halakhic</i> impropriety of a <i>kiddushin</i> ceremony</b>
In the summer of 2000, Avraham (hereinafter: the defendant) married Sarah (hereinafter: the plaintiff). Subsequently, the couple separated in September 2010 and thereafter the plaintiff requested that the defendant give her a <i>get</i>. Years later, we summoned the defendant to resolve the matter of the <i>get</i> in our <i>beit din</i>, but he refused to comply with the summons. We set a date for the hearing and heard the arguments of the plaintiff, and in June 2017 we obligated the defendant to give the plaintiff a <i>get</i>. To date, he persists in his refusal to give a <i>get</i>.
Under the wedding canopy (<i>hupah</i>), standing near the officiating rabbi, groom and bride, were relatives from both families of the couple who are ineligible to give testimony concerning the <i>kiddushin</i>. Standing in the back of the canopy were three adult Jewish males who were nonrelatives. One was a rabbi who is known to be Torah observant, who “came and went” during the ceremony. Additionally, there was an adult Jewish male, and we were unable to ascertain whether he was Torah observant. There was a third adult male who was a nonrelative, whom, we are told, was secular. In fact, we spoke to the man and he informed us that he was non-observant. The three men were not designated by the officiating rabbi to serve as witnesses for the <i>kiddushin</i>. The ring set in diamonds given by the defendant to the plaintiff during the <i>kiddushin</i> was bought by the plaintiff and the defendant did not reimburse her for the purchase. No marriage agreement (<i>ketubah</i>) was executed at the wedding. Though the plaintiff brought a <i>ketubah</i> to the hall and offered it to the officiating rabbi, he declined to use it or any other <i>ketubah</i>.
As Rabbi Avraham Shapiro, former Chief Rabbi of the State of Israel observes in a case which did not entail a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>):<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Minhat Avraham</i> 4:6.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">We do not work excessively hard to consider carefully all potential leniencies but in the case of an <i>agunah</i>, the <i>beit din</i> must work hard to identify a leniency in order that she not remain an <i>agunah.</i></span>
Accordingly, we have canvassed the <i>halakhot</i> to void the <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated: the marriage) of the above couple as it relates to the propriety of the marriage ceremony.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Discussion</b></span>
<b>A. <i>Kiddushin</i> conducted by a person without the requisite credentials</b>
The Talmud states:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 6a. See also <i>SA EH</i> 49:1 and the glossators ad loc.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Rabbi Yehudah said in the name of Shmuel: Whoever is not well-versed in the <i>halakhot</i> of divorce and marriage has no business rendering decisions in such cases.</span>
Moreover, the one who officiates at the wedding ceremony (the <i>mesadeir kiddushin</i>) must be ordained as a rabbi.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 3:121; <i>Teshuvot Knesset Yehezkel</i> 72; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 4:119, 5:223.</i>
That being established, we will delineate some of the functions of the officiating rabbi, in general, and his <i>halakhic</i> responsibility in overseeing the <i>kiddushin</i> process, in particular. We will examine whether in fact in the case before us, the officiating rabbi performed his duties properly.
1. The groom must give the bride a <i>ketubah,</i> which is an agreement signed by two Torah observant Jewish males which outlines the obligations of the husband vis-à-vis his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>SA EH</i> 66.</i> The drawing up of a <i>ketubah ­</i>exists even in countries where Rabbeinu Gershom’s ban against divorcing a wife against her will was accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>SA EH</i> 66:18; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 66:11.Whether the monetary obligations memoralized in the <i>ketubah</i> emerge at the inception of the marriage or at the time of divorce or the husband’s demise is subject to debate. See File no. 1199921, Netanya Regional Beit Din, June 3, 2019.</i>
In the present case, no <i>ketubah</i> was executed. Moreover, in our case the plaintiff purchased a <i>ketubah</i> and brought it to the wedding hall and handed it to the officiating rabbi<i>,</i> but he refrained from using it. He wrote something down on paper but did not give it to the couple.
Had a <i>ketubah</i> been executed, it would stand to reason that the fact that the bona fide <i>halakhic</i> document (the <i>shtar</i>) deals with a husband’s marital duties upon the inception of marriage, and it was signed by two witnesses, attests to the fact that there was <i>kiddushin</i>. However, given that usually the <i>ketubah</i> is executed prior to the <i>kiddushin</i>, one cannot submit it as proof that in fact the act of <i>kiddushin</i> transpired.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 45:3. However, if the wife has the <i>ketubah</i> in her possession, we assume that the <i>kiddushin</i> occurred. See <i>Taz, SA EH</i> 45:9.</i> Even if this panel would endorse the view that a properly signed <i>ketubah</i> can be construed as a document serving as proof that <i>kiddushin</i> occurred (a <i>shtar ra’yah</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 42:4. Cf. <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 42:10.</i> or that the opinion which rejects the <i>ketubah</i> as a confirmation that the <i>kiddushin</i> was executed agrees that the wife retaining possession of the <i>ketubah</i> attests that the <i>kiddushin</i> took place,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah, Kiddushin</i> 50b; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, supra n. 7.</i> everyone agrees that if it is demonstrated that there were improprieties in the <i>kiddushin</i>, the marriage may yet be invalid.
Finally, even though some authorities argue that the witnesses are to sign the <i>ketubah</i> during the bridal ceremony<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 66:1.</i> after observing the act of the <i>kiddushin</i>, nonetheless, in our case, following the more prevalent opinion the witnesses signed the <i>ketubah</i> prior to the act of <i>kiddushin</i>, prior to the wedding ceremony.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, ibid in the name of Mordekhai; <i>Teshuvot Ramah me-Fano</i> 65; <i>Mishpat ha-Ketubah,</i> vol. 2, 548–554.</i> As such, how could the witnesses of the <i>ketubah</i> attest to the propriety of the <i>kiddushin</i> since the act did not transpire till later!
2. The officiating rabbi must establish that the groom purchased the <i>kiddushin</i> ring (i.e. was the owner of the ring), the wedding ring, in accordance with <i>Halakhah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 28; <i>SA and Rema EH</i> 28:1.</i> In our case, the plaintiff purchased the ring.
3. The groom must pay for the ring prior to the wedding in such a fashion that he owns it. According to Avnei Millium, if the wedding transpired prior the purchase of the ring by the groom, the marriage is valid under rabbinic law, though some disagree with his posture.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avnei Milluim EH</i> 28:33; <i>Otzar ha-Poskim EH</i> 28:1 (13).</i>
In our case, the defendant did not pay for the ring and the officiating rabbi did not inquire of the defendant if the ring belonged to him.
4. <i>Kiddushin</i> may not be established with something whose value is not commonly known, for it is possible to be mistaken as to its value, which would result in the absence of mental acquiescence to the act of <i>kiddushin</i> on the part of the bride (<i>gemirat da’at</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 31:2.</i> Consequently, the actual validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> will be in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 31:2.</i>
Even if there is no issue with mental acquiescence to the <i>kiddushin</i> such as in our situation, where the plaintiff bought the ring and therefore she is aware of its value,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei Talmid ha-Rashba, Kiddushin</i> 7b; <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev</i> 14.</i> nevertheless, the officiating rabbi must ask the witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i> prior to the act of <i>kiddushin</i> if the ring is worth one <i>perutah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 31:12. The value of a <i>perutah</i> which is a coin is 1/1244 of a troy ounce (1/40 gram) of pure silver. See <i>Hazon Ish HM</i> 16:30.</i> Indeed, even if the ring is set with a gem, she is considered married <i>ex post facto</i>, but <i>ab initio</i>, <i>kiddushin</i> may not be executed with a gem,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Teshuvah, SA EH</i> 31:4 in the name of Kehillat Ya’akov. Marrying with such a ring creates a <i>safek kiddushin</i>, a doubtfully effective marriage. See <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 31:4; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 31:3.</i> even if its appraised value is known.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nahalat Shivah</i> 12:12; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 31:8.</i> In the present case, the officiating rabbi did not ask anyone who was standing under the wedding canopy (the <i>hupah</i>) who was eligible to be a witness to the <i>kiddushin</i> if the ring was worth one <i>perutah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, supra n. 14; <i>Be’air Hetev, EH</i> 27:1 in the name of Maharshal.</i> In the final analysis, a ring which was set with diamonds was used for the <i>kiddushin</i>.
5. The officiating rabbi must explain to the bride and groom the nature of <i>kiddushin</i> in general and the importance of giving a ring which belongs to him and the formulation of the <i>kiddushin</i> in particular.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 42:4; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 28:49; Rabbi David Feder, <i>Imrei David</i> 29; <i>Shulhan Ezer</i> 2:128:2.</i>
In the present case, the officiating rabbi did not explain these matters to the bride and groom and failed to ask him if he owned the ring.
Implicitly following the understanding of marriage as establishing an exclusive conjugal relation between a Jewish man and a Jewish woman rather than an act of gifting, Rabbi Osher Weiss notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, October 1, 2017. The transfer of money or its equivalent (such as a ring) for the purposes of <i>kiddushin</i> by the groom to the bride is known as “money by undertaking an obligation” (<i>kesef kiddushin</i>) and is readily distinguishable from “acquisition by money” (<i>kinyan kesef</i>) which entails acquisition of an object in exchange for money. See <i>Sma, SA HM</i> 190:1; <i>Imrei Binah, Kuntres ha-Kinyanim</i> 1 in the name of Tosafot Rid; <i>Even ha-Azail, Mekhirah</i> 1:4 in the name of Tosafot.<br>Rabbi Weiss’s utilization of the word of “acquisition” and understanding of the act of <i>kiddushin</i> denotes a Jew’s decision to undertake an obligation(s) upon marriage rather than viewing his prospective spouse as an acquisition of chattel. See further, <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Kiddushin</i> 3a, 6b; <i>Avnei Milluim</i> 42:1, 44:4; <i>Minhat Asher, Kiddushin</i> 1, 2, 5; <i>Minhat Osher</i>, <i>Bereshit</i>, Hayei Sarah, 26; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:117. To state it differently, generally speaking, in matters of commercial matters the execution of a <i>kinyan</i> may occur in two different contexts. On one hand, the execution of a <i>kinyan</i> may be required in order to acquire an item. For example, one party may purchase an item from a seller of the item via the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 345; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur HM</i> 195:20 in the name of Rashba; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 197:6. On the other hand, the execution of the <i>kinyan</i> may be mandated in order to establish a <i>hithayivut</i> (undertaking an obligation). For example, a buyer may <i>obligate himself</i> to buy an item from a seller via the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>. See <i>Beit Yosef, Tur HM</i> 195:20; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 212:1. For the distinction between a <i>kinyan</i> of acquisition and a <i>kinyan</i> of <i>hithayivut (</i>duty), see B. Lifshitz, <i>Employee and Independent Contractor –Acquisition and Obligation in Contract</i>, (Hebrew), Jerusalem: 1993, 12–23; I. Warhaftig, <i>Undertaking in Jewish Law</i>, (Hebrew), Jerusalem: 2001, 7–8.<br>In our case in dealing with a matter of marriage, the establishment of the <i>kiddushin</i> is being obtained via an act of <i>kinyan</i>, namely the giving of a ring to his prospective spouse and with the prospective husband’s recitation of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula (“you are designated to me…according to the law of Moshe and Yisrael.”) in the presence of two eligible witnesses. See <i>Mishnah Kiddushin</i> 1:1; <i>Tosefta Kiddushin</i> 1:1; <i>SA EH</i> 42:2. In other words, whereas according to some arbiters (e.g. <i>Rashi Kiddushin</i> 9b, s.v. af khan and <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 244:2) the requirement of mandating this recitation is grounded in the need that the prospective wife be aware of what is transpiring (‘<i>da’at ha-isha</i>”), according to others, the recitation is an integral ingredient in establishing the status of the <i>kiddushin</i>, namely the prohibition of becoming a married woman. See <i>Birkat Shmuel</i> 1 in the name of Rabbi Hayyim Soloveitchik; Rabbi Z. N. Goldberg, “Regarding recitation in <i>kiddushin</i> and <i>kinyanim</i>,” (Hebrew) 5 <i>Da’at Moshe</i> 45 (Iyar 5742) in the name of Netivot ha-Mishpat and ha-Mikneh; Rabbi D. Wolf, <i>Minhah le-Aharon</i>, 32–37. As such, the act of <i>kiddushin</i> binds the woman (<i>shi’bud</i>) to the man in order to engage in conjugal relations which subsequently will be transformed into one of the husband’s duties upon the completion of the act of marriage (<i>ni’ssuin</i>) rather than the <i>kinyan</i> serving as a means for acquiring ownership of one’s spouse. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Nedarim</i> 15b; <i>Perishah, Tur HM</i> 97:41; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Gittin</i> 9a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Kiddushin</i> 2a; <i>SA EH</i> 69:1–3; <i>Bnei Ahuvah, Ishut</i> 10:2; <i>Minhat Osher</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 1(11); this writer’s, “Contractual Consequences of Cohabitation in American Law and Jewish Law,” 20 <i>The Jewish Law Annual</i> 279, 305, n. 88 (2013). Clearly, <i>Halakhah</i> does not allow a person to do what he pleases with his possessions. Analogously, <i>Halakhah</i> proscribes a husband from assaulting his spouse and engaging in spousal rape. Utilizing the words “<i>kinyan</i>” and/or “<i>hefetz</i>” evoke the language of ownership but does not necessarily <i>halakhically</i> mean that a wife is owned by her husband.<br>The woman is exclusively designated to live intimately with her prospective spouse and prohibited to all others via the husband’s act of <i>kiddushin</i> which is consummated with the execution of an undertaking which establishes a prohibition, a <i>kinyan issur.</i> See <i>Kiddushin</i> 2b; <i>Tosafot Kiddushin</i> 2b, s.v. de’asar; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Kiddushin</i> 16a, s.v. <i>zot omeret; Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Kiddushin</i> 15a, s.v. <i>mokheir atzmo; Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Gittin</i> 75a, <i>Nedarim</i> 15a; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 4:35; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 16; <i>Teshuvot Mahaneh Hayyim</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 44; <i>Avnei Milluim</i> 44:4; <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i>, Sha’ar 5, Perek 22; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:25(32). Consequently, the execution of this <i>kinyan issur</i> and the resulting status of the woman becoming a married woman (<i>eishet ish</i>) preempts another man from executing the act of <i>kiddushin</i> with this woman. See <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Gittin</i> 43a, s.v. amar Rabbi Hisda; Rabbi D. Wolf, <i>Minha le-Aharon</i>, 60. After the execution of the act of <i>kiddushin</i> should either spouse decide to opt out of becoming married a <i>get</i> would be mandated. See <i>SA EH</i> 26:3.To state it differently, the status of becoming a married woman, albeit without being a beneficiary of her husband’s marital duties which transpires upon the consummation of the act of marriage mandates that a <i>get</i> be forthcoming should the parties resolve to dissolve the <i>kiddushin</i>.<br>Additionally, the act of <i>kiddushin</i> establishes that the husband possesses certain rights vis-à-vis his prospective spouse’s property. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Gittin</i> 77a, s.v. amrei; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:960, 2:132; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 404; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 92:1; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 92:4; <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 66:134; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 209:12; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, <i>Biurim</i> 209:6; <i>Avnei Millium</i> 92:5; <i>Hazon Ish, EH</i> 77:9. Finally, should the husband divorce her prior to the marriage, she is entitled to receive the value of her <i>ketubah</i>. See <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Ketuvot</i> 56(5); <i>Tur EH</i> 55; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113.<br>Cf. <i>SA EH</i> 55:6; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 55:19; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 52:13; <i>Hiddushei Hatam Sofer, Gittin</i> 71a; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:166 who contend that a prenuptial agreement may be executed even prior to the act of <i>kiddushin</i>. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 49–61.<br>The <i>kinyan</i> establishes the act of <i>kiddushin</i> which creates the status of a married woman (<i>eishet ish</i>) accompanied by certain rights of the husband regarding his prospective spouse’s property and a wife’s right to the value of her <i>ketubah</i> and allows <i>Halakhah</i> to set up a system of a husband’s marital duties (as well as rights) which subsequently will become operative upon the consummation of the act of marriage. In the words of Rabbi Shimon bar Tzemah Duran, the <i>kinyan</i> and prohibition occur simultaneously (“<i>kinyano ve’issura ba’im ke’ahad</i>”). See <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:67; <i>Kehillot Ya’akov</i> 13. Whether the prohibition is derivative from the execution of the <i>kinyan</i> or the <i>kinyan</i> is derivative of the prohibition is beyond the scope of our presentation. Cf. Rabbi Gustman’s approach that the giving of the ring entails the undertaking of a duty <i>(kinyan</i>) to become betrothed (<i>mekudeshet</i>) and with the consummation of the act of betrothal, the prohibition of becoming a married woman emerges. See <i>Kuntresei Shiurim, Kiddushin</i>- Shiur 1(2).<br>With the execution of the <i>kinyan</i> of <i>kiddushin</i>, the couple is bound to each other (a reciprocal <i>shi’bud</i>) to engage in conjugal relations to the exclusion of all others. See <i>Nedarim</i> 15b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, ad. locum. With the inception of the act of marriage, the couple is then permitted to engage in intimacy. See <i>Tosafot Yoma</i> 13b, s.v. le’hada; <i>MT Ishut</i> 10:1.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A husband’s act of <i>kiddushin</i> is different according to our Torah . . . from what is customary in the modern world. According to the Torah, the husband acquires his wife by giving her a ring as <i>kiddushin</i> of money . . . The <i>kiddushin</i> is effectuated by him but one requires her consent. . . . However, the modern understanding in the world is that we have a mutual obligation each for the other without any symbolic act of undertaking an obligation (<i>kinyan</i> – AYW) . . . it is a sign of endearment and love. Therefore, the practice is to exchange rings, and the primary validity of the betrothal according to their outlook is in their declaration . . . rather than money.</span>
In the present case, the officiating rabbi did not talk to the couple about the act of <i>kiddushin,</i> and it is clear based upon the background of the couple that we are dealing with individuals who are infused with the modern outlook of matrimony rather than with the notion of a <i>halakhic</i> marriage where the parties intend that the act of matrimony is for the sake of <i>kiddushin.</i>
As Rabbi Moshe Feinstein aptly notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 3:25.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even if he gives her a ring, the fact that she also gives him a ring proves that the ring that he gave was also a mere gift on account of them becoming man and wife, but it does not relate in any fashion to executing <i>kiddushin</i>.</span>
6. The practice is that the officiating rabbi designates two witnesses to attest and establish that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> occurred in accordance with <i>Halakhah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 198; <i>Avnei Milluim</i> 42:6; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 42:31. For the basis of invalidating a witness to the act of <i>kiddushin</i> in the absence of the husband from the <i>beit din</i> proceeding, see supra p. 19, n. 26.</i> In this case, there was no designation of witnesses.
Under the canopy, standing near the officiating rabbi, groom and bride, were relatives from both sides of the couple who are ineligible to give testimony concerning the <i>kiddushin</i>. On one hand, there is a consensus that relatives of the father are ineligible based upon biblical law. However, on the other hand, some decisors claim that relatives of the mother are ineligible based upon rabbinic law.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maggid Mishneh, MT, Edut</i> 13:1 in the name of Geonim; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:151 in the name of Rambam; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 75.</i> As such, if they are rabbinically invalidated, then we must be concerned that <i>kiddushin</i> was consummated and a <i>get</i> is required on rabbinic grounds. Nonetheless, most authorities concur that their testimony is invalidated on biblical grounds.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 10, <i>EH</i> 25. See the addendum.<br>For the validity of invalidating witnesses based upon inquiring of the bride and her family rather than mandating testimony to clarify their eligibility, see <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 5:12.</i>
7. The witnesses must observe the ring being given by the groom to the bride in order to validate the <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:780, 1193; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 42:4; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 30; <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Kiddushin</i> 3:20; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 2:448; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 43; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 66.</i> They must see the bride holding out her index finger (the finger next to her thumb) of the right hand to receive the ring,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishberei Yam</i> 22.</i> and that the ring remains on her finger for a certain length of time.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:50.</i> The three adult Jewish males who were nonrelatives were standing in the back at the time of the <i>hupah,</i> and therefore could not attest to the giving of the ring by the groom to the bride and to the placement of the ring on the bride’s finger.
8. Even assuming that that were two eligible witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i> who observed the ring being given and who heard the words of the <i>kiddushin</i>, in the absence of the designation of the witnesses, does the act have any validity? Clearly, there is no obligation to designate as witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 42:4.</i>
Nevertheless, the practice in many communities is to designate them.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagahot ha-Semag</i>, mitzvah 183 (7); <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 36; <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 36:8.</i> Though some argue that the presence of an ineligible witness does not invalidate an eligible witness as it relates to the establishment of <i>kiddushin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Makot</i> 6a, s.v. <i>Shmuel</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Makot</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Besamim Rosh</i> 37 in the name of Rid; <i>Pithei Teshuvah, EH</i> 42:9 in the name of Noda be-Yehudah; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 36:6; <i>Avnei Milluim</i> 42:6. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 172; <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 221; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar EH</i> 31; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:50.</i> others argue that either the intent of the ineligible witness to testify<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:57; <i>SA HM</i> 36:1 (first opinion); <i>Rema SA EH</i> 42:2; <i>Shakh</i>, ad locum, 17.</i> or the observance of eligible witness along with the invalid witness invalidates the testimony for <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Edut</i> 5:5; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i> (in the name of his teacher, Ra’ah), <i>Kiddushin</i> 43, <i>Makot</i> 6b. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 245–250, 252–255.<br>Whether a woman standing under the wedding canopy is to be deemed an ineligible witness and therefore may invalidate an eligible witness standing under the canopy is subject to debate. See <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat HM</i> 36:10 in the name of Tumim; <i>Turei Even, Rosh Hashanah</i> 22a in the name of Rabbi Y. Elhanan Spektor.</i> In the wake of these latter authorities who argue that an ineligible witness may invalidate an eligible one, the custom emerged to designate the two witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i>. Notably, some decisors rule in accordance with Ritva who endorses this latter view.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ritva</i>, supra n. 33; <i>Rabbeinu Yeruham, Sefer Meisharim</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37 (9) in the name of Ritva “and many ruled like him”. For a detailed examination of Ritva’s posture, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority,</i> vol. 3, 245–250.</i> However, other arbiters regard his opinion only as a supporting argument for leniency (a <i>senif</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 28; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i> 2:101; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 11.</i> In our case, we take this approach as a supporting argument for invalidating the <i>kiddushin</i>.
Let us reinforce this point with the incisive ruling of Rabbi Yehoshua Ehrenberg who elucidates:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Dvar Yehoshua EH</i> 3:20.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Since the witness was a transgressor of the Shabbat, there is a suspicion that the rabbi who performed the <i>kiddushin</i> for them also did not perform the <i>kiddushin</i> in a way that caused them to have effect . . . for the witnesses may not have seen the ring being given, or if the word “<i>li</i>” (to me – AYW) might have been omitted, which according to section 27:4 means that she is not married. . . . The officiating rabbi must make sure of this, and there is not a problem if the rabbi who is conducting the ceremony is a religious person and expert in the nature of <i>kiddushin</i>, we may rely upon his presumption of competence and we assume that the <i>kiddushin</i> were performed in a valid manner. . . . But so long as we lack witnesses regarding this particular matter, then particularly if the officiating rabbi is not established as an expert or as having performed the ceremony properly, the entire marriage is in doubt, and the woman reverts to her status as a single woman, even if we know that there were [other] eligible witnesses at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> for the purpose of its validation.</span>
In other words, even if the witnesses were eligible, according to Rabbi Ehrenberg, the woman is presumed to be unmarried because the officiating rabbi was not competent in the <i>halakhot</i> of the process of marriage and divorce!
In the issue at bar, the possibility of validation of the two witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i> is absent. According to the plaintiff, we know that there were three adult Jewish males who were present under the bridal canopy who were not related to the groom and the bride. One was a rabbi who is known to be Torah observant, who “came and went” during the ceremony. We do not know whether he observed the giving of the ring and was attentive to the words of the <i>kiddushin</i> (“<i>harei at mekudeshet</i> . . .”). In addition to this rabbi, there was a Jewish man of Yemenite descent. We do not know whether he was Torah observant. There was a third man whom we were told was secular. In fact, we spoke to the man and he informed us that he was non-observant.
Even if all the witnesses had been eligible, as we noted, in pursuance to Rabbi Ehrenberg, the woman is deemed to be unmarried because the officiating rabbi was not versed in the <i>halakhot</i> dealing with <i>kiddushin</i>. Moreover, following in the footsteps of Ritva and others, the two eligible witnesses were invalidated due to the ineligible witnesses (relatives and a Jewish secular man who was a non-relative). The woman is deemed to be single due to the fact that the officiating rabbi was not versed in the <i>halakhot</i> of marriage.
Seemingly, even in the absence of two qualified witnesses in our case, we must adopt the rulings of Havot Yair, Hatam Sofer, Iggerot Moshe and others, whereby observant Jews who were amongst the audience and observed the wedding ceremony, without hearing the words “you are hereby betrothed to me . . .” and without seeing the ring being given by the groom to his bride, may serve as witnesses ( “we are witnesses”- <i>anan sahadi</i> ) to the <i>kiddushin</i>. Had we been dealing with invalid <i>kiddushin</i> due to an ineligible witness, and our intention was to validate the marriage by means of the observers from the audience, we would consider invoking the approach of Havot Yair et al.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:100 cited in <i>Pithei Teshuvah, SA EH</i> 42:11; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:76–77; 3:32; 4:13; <i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 5:193, 4:160; <i>Teshuvot Ma’amar Mordekhai</i> 1, <i>HM</i> 3.Even if the witnesses were designated by the groom, one can invoke the concept of “we are witnesses”. See <i>Havot Yair</i>, op. cit.; <i>Hatam Sofer</i>, op.cit. However, others disagree and contend that that the mechanism of “we are witnesses” cannot be employed where witnesses have been designated by the groom. See <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37 in the name of Mahari Weill, Maharshach, Maharashdam and Ein Yitzhak. See the addendum.</i> However, in the case before us, we are looking for a way to invalidate the <i>kiddushin</i> due to a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance! Even though many authorities claim that traditionally, all strict views must be considered in matters concerning forbidden sexual relations,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Yevamot</i> 36b; <i>Tosafot Kiddushin</i> 50b; <i>SA EH</i> 17:31; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 17:61; <i>Mahatzit ha-Shekel, SA EH</i> 17:56; <i>Teshuvot Kedushat Yom Tov</i> 9 in the name of Rabbi Yom Tov Algazi, 11; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan, EH</i> 42:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Rahamim Franco, EH</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 1:22; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1:10, 13; <i>Erekh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 42:2; Cf. <i>Taz SA EH</i> 17:15.</i> nevertheless, in a matter of a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance, many hold that the lenient view ought to be adopted.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 51:2, <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 92; <i>Teshuvot Maharik,</i> shoresh 121; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:135; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 103; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah EH</i> Mahadura Kama 29, 57; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:110; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 3, 8 (19).</i> As Rabbi Sinai emphasizes:<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Ani</i> 51.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In dealing with such matters when the fate of <i>agunot</i> is involved, it is not proper to be stringent on the basis of critical and forced distinctions. It is preferable to apply the principle of leniency, since people’s lives are hanging in the balance.</span>
In light of our lenient ruling, we should subscribe to the position of these authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi O. Yosef, <i>Teshuvot Yehaveh Da’at, Kelalei ha-Hora’ah</i>, 32, no. 9; <i>PDR</i> 4:166 (Rabbis Hadaya, Elyashiv and Zolty).</i> Therefore, in view of the fact that most of the authorities reject the position of Havot Yair, Hatam Sofer, and Rabbi Feinstein, we have refrained from adopting it.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 1:28; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> 20; <i>Teshuvot Kerem Shlomo OH</i> 25; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh</i> 1:31; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 64; <i>Teshuvot Or Li</i> 73b.</i> Decisors in contemporary times who endorse a posture contrary to these decisors are Rabbis O. Yosef, Osher Weiss and Tzion Boaron.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 3:8 (5), 11:15; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 2:83; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 2:11, 3:22.</i>
Finally, resorting to implementing the concept of “we are witnesses” is contingent upon the fact that the officiating rabbi is a <i>halakhically</i> observant Jew and proficient in the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>kiddushin,</i> including executing a <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See supra notes 2–3. According to Taz, too, the officiating rabbi must possess basic knowledge of the <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to the conduct of the wedding ceremony from the time of preparation and signing the <i>ketubah</i> until the consummation of the act of couple’s seclusion in a room, <i>yi’hud</i>.</i> The fact that the officiating rabbi did not designate witnesses for the <i>kiddushin,</i> was ignorant regarding various <i>halakhot</i> dealing with the wedding ceremony and was unaware of the problem that is liable to emerge as a result of Ritva’s view (and others), attests to the lack of <i>halakhic</i> proficiency on the part of the officiating rabbi. In short, even Rabbi Feinstein argues we cannot invoke “we are all witnesses,” which presumes implicitly that the expertise of the supervising rabbi can be relied upon.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:76–77.</i> Consequently, the onlookers observing the ceremony cannot serve as witnesses to establish the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>B. Owner of the <i>Kiddushin</i> Ring – the Defendant or the Plaintiff?</b></span>
As we mentioned earlier, prior to the wedding, the defendant did not have a ring, i.e. he did not buy the ring. It belonged to the bride. At the wedding ceremony, the daughter of the plaintiff (sired from her first marriage), who was then six years old, brought the ring down the aisle to the wedding canopy and handed it over. According to the plaintiff, there is uncertainty as to who was given the ring by her daughter– the plaintiff or the defendant.
As such, two possibilities must be addressed: that the ring was given to the defendant and alternatively that it was given to the plaintiff.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>1. The ring was given by the plaintiff to the defendant</b></span>
A gift is the bestowing of an item without receiving benefit such as monetary payment (i.e. consideration). In our case, the plaintiff purchased the ring and the defendant did not reimburse her for the ring. However, the plaintiff claims that she did not state explicitly to the defendant that the ring is a gift (nor did she lend the ring to the defendant for use during the <i>kiddushin</i>). A gift is valid even when the owner of the item (i.e. the plaintiff) does not say specifically that she is giving the item to the recipient of the gift, provided that it can be inferred from the circumstances, via a presumption, that the donor wishes to give a gift to the recipient.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 358:1; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 195:1, 244:1.</i> In the present case, the plaintiff purchased the ring because she knew that a ring was needed for the wedding ceremony.
Secondly, the fact that she did not ask the defendant for money for buying the ring shows clearly that this was indeed a gift.
A prerequisite for transfer of ownership is that an act of acquisition (<i>kinyan</i>) of the ring was executed.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 84; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 281:10.</i> In effect, the transfer of the ring from the plaintiff to the defendant’s hand entailed the execution of a physical act of acquisition by the defendant (a <i>kinyan yad</i>).
As we verified, the defendant was a convert to Judaism and was unfamiliar with the <i>halakhot</i> of acquisitions in general and the <i>halakhot</i> dealing with <i>kiddushin</i> in particular. As such, we are dealing with a situation of acquiring a gift by a physical act of acquisition without the knowledge of the recipient, the defendant. Accordingly, according to Rashi one cannot execute such an acquisition without intent to acquire the item.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi Ketuvot</i> 31a; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 268:2.</i> However, in pursuance to other authorities, this type of execution will be accomplished even without the intent to acquire by the recipient, i.e. the defendant.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, supra n. 48 in the name of Tosafot; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:150; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 200:15.</i>
On the other hand, though there are many decisors who argue that a woman does know how to <i>halakhically</i> facilitate the transfer of ownership to another individual,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi Gittin</i> 20b, s.v. <i>de’i</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Gittin</i> 2:21; <i>Ran on Rif, Gittin</i> 10a; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Gittin</i> 20b; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 124:19; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2:49; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:86.</i> Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol notes that the consensus among later authorities (<i>Aharonim</i>), is to follow the view of Rashba and Makneh that a woman does not know how to facilitate such a transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Gittin</i> 20b; File no. 993982/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, July 21, 2015.</i> As such, this acquisition never materialized in our case, and therefore, the woman is not deemed married.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Amirah Ne’imah Tinyana</i>, Letter <i>Kuf, Ma’amar</i> 84; <i>Teshuvot Pri ha-Aretz</i> 1:4.</i> Furthermore, expanding the scope of this <i>halakhah</i>, Rabbi Alfandri rules:<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 2433-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 2, 2010, in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Sabba Kadisha</i>, 1 <i>EH</i> 13.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">One cannot rely and say that a woman knows how to facilitate the execution of a symbolic act of undertaking an obligation (<i>kinyan</i>), and also regarding men there are some ignorant ones who know nothing. . . .</span>
In other words, even if one would endorse the opposing view that a woman had intent to gift the ring, her husband in our case was ignorant of the <i>halakhah</i> that the ring had to belong to him.
Moreover, even those authorities who contend that a woman knows how to transfer ownership mandate that there must be a verbal communication that a transfer is going to transpire.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel</i>, supra n. 50; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>EH</i> 124:7.</i> In the present case, the plaintiff did not say, “I am giving the ring to the defendant,” and consequently the acquisition is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA HM</i> 241; <i>Pithei Teshuvah, Kinyanim</i> 1:7.</i> He also did not say, “Give it to me,” and therefore it is construed as an “implied giving” (an <i>umdana</i> – an assessment of his expectations) which has no validity unless the donor explicitly states that the item is being given as a gift.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim, HM</i> 200:12; <i>Erekh ha-Shulchan, HM</i> 88:12.</i> As such, from the perspectives of the plaintiff (the donor) and the defendant (the recipient of the gift), the transfer of ownership never happened.
In short, the ring must belong to the person executing the marriage, namely the defendant, and must belong to him prior to the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 28.</i> And therefore one cannot use a ring that does not belong to the groom, the defendant, such as one that is stolen.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i> and <i>Rema EH</i> 28:1.</i> And even if she already accepted a ring that was stolen from her as <i>kiddushin</i>, in accordance with the view of Rema in the name of Ran, if the groom did not pay for the ring, she is not deemed married.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 28:2.</i>
In short, the plaintiff never transferred the ownership of the ring to the defendant, and therefore the <i>kiddushin</i> was consummated with a stolen ring!
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. The six-year old daughter gave the ring directly to the defendant.</b></span>
For two reasons, one cannot draw the conclusion that the defendant indeed acquired ownership of the ring due to the item being handed to him by his step daughter.
Firstly, a minor child, such as a girl below the age of twelve, cannot give a gift.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Talmud Yerushalmi Gittin</i> 5:9; <i>Hiddushei Maharam Schick, Gittin</i> 64a; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan HM</i> 243:17.</i> Even though a minor cannot give a gift under <i>Halakhah</i> on a biblical level, our Sages enacted a rule (a <i>takanah</i>) whereby a child who is a mature minor – one who understands the nature of transactions (attained <i>onat ha’pe’utot</i>) may transfer chattels via the medium of a gift,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA HM</i> 235:1.</i> even a substantial one such as a diamond ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA HM</i> ibid.</i> The rabbinic enactment applies to a male child of six years and over on condition that he understands the nature of the transactions at the time of the transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 60.</i> This enactment equally applies to a minor female.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Imrei Binah, Kuntres on Property Halakhah</i>, 24.</i> In the present case, the plaintiff’s daughter was six years old but she did not understand the nature of transactions and therefore there is no basis for maintaing that she can give a gift to the defendant.
Secondly, even if we were to assume that the child possesses ­<i>halakhic</i>-legal capacity (i.e. knowledgeable in transactions), in our case the ring was owned by the plaintiff and therefore the only way the defendant could acquire the ring via the plaintiff’s daughter would be if the plaintiff appointed her an agent (a <i>shaliah</i>). However, a minor, regardless of gender, is not qualified to serve as an agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA HM</i> 188:2.</i> Consequently, based upon the foregoing, in our case the wedding ring remained the property of the plaintiff.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>C. Marital status as a benefit of intrinsic value (“a woman prefers to be together with her spouse”) serves as a medium for consummating the <i>kiddushin</i></b></span>
In relation to a woman who was married with a ring that belonged to her prior to the <i>kiddushin</i>, and therefore prima facie did not properly express her wish to be married, Rabbi Ya’akov Breisch states:<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov EH</i> 61.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A woman’s desire to be divorced is not similar to her desire to be married, for “a woman prefers to be together . . .” (“<i>tav le-meitav tan du</i>”).</span>
To state it differently, Rabbi Breisch’s implicit assumption that “a woman prefers to be together” expresses an abstract outcome of the desire of a woman for marriage, and one can therefore conclude that, from a <i>halakhic</i> perspective, this constitutes an example of a benefit, namely <i>tovat hana’ah,</i> which is a thing that possesses no real substance, a <i>davar she’ein bo mamash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagahot Maimoniyot, Mekhirah</i> 5:10, <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2, <i>YD</i> 5.</i>
Consequently, we must determine whether a benefit of intrinsic value (a <i>tova’at hana’ah</i>) constitutes something with a monetary value which can be considered significant for undertaking an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>) for the purposes of creating <i>kiddushin</i>, since [the <i>tova’at hana’ah</i> of] the marital status is of intrinsic value to serve as a <i>kinyan</i>.
We read in the Talmud that there is a dispute between Ulla and Rabba as to whether a betrothal performed with a priestly tithe on produce (<i>terumot</i>) and other tithes (and gifts) which are a benefit is valid. Rabba is of the opinion that a benefit of intrinsic value is not considered to have monetary value and thus it is invalid, whereas Ulla is of the opinion that a benefit of intrinsic value is considered to have a monetary value and thus it is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 58a.</i>
Post-talmudic decisors were also in disagreement about this matter. Some authorities agreed with Ulla that an intangible benefit <i>is</i> considered to have monetary value<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahaneh Ephraim, Tovat Hana’ah</i>, s.v. <i>venimtza</i>; <i>Teshuvot Na’ot Ya’akov</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2, <i>HM</i> 93.</i> and the woman is considered married. On the other hand, there were numerous authorities who decided in accordance with Rabba that a benefit of no real substance has no monetary value and is not a means of acquisition and therefore she is not married.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Ishut</i> 5:6 and <i>Magid Mishneh</i>, ad. loc.; <i>Hiddushei Rabbi Yehonatan ha-Kohen of Lunel, Kiddushin</i>, supra n. 68; <i>Mordekhai, Shevuot</i> 766 in the name of Maharam; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 203:1; <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:46; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 276:4.</i>
In our case, therefore, in pursuance with the view that contends that such benefit does not have monetary value, we have determined that the Talmudic statement “for a woman prefers to be together . . .” (“<i>tav le-meitav tan du</i>”) does not constitute a means of acquisition for executing an act of <i>kiddushin</i> where the plaintiff gave her ring to the defendant in order that he marry her with it.
Based upon the foregoing, various doubts have emerged regarding the <i>kiddushin</i>. The first doubt is if the execution of <i>kiddushin</i> was in accordance with <i>Halakhah</i>. There remains a doubt as to whether in fact there were qualified witnesses for the <i>kiddushin</i>. Additionally, there exists a doubt if the couple understood that the transfer of money (e.g. the giving of a ring) is a symbolic act of undertaking an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>) and was it understood by the prospective couple as integral part of the ceremony which established the act of <i>kiddushin</i>. An additional uncertainty is if the couple understood if the recitation of the formula of <i>kiddushin</i> (“<i>harei at</i> . . .”) is an introduction to the transfer of money which reflects that the couple’s intent is to marry for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i> and that the ring transfer is not an act of gifting.
