When dealing with a doubtful marriage (<i>kiddushei safek</i>), there are numerous early authorities as well as other decisors who argue that we reinstate the original presumption of the wife, namely she has the presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) of being a single woman and she is <i>biblically</i> permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 23a, s.v. <i>ma’i</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 23a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 23a; <i>Helkat Mehokeik SA EH</i> 47:1; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum 2–4.<br>Implicit in this approach is that the presence of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt reinstates the presumption of being a single woman. See <i>Ran on Rif Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika 1; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 18; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh</i>, <i>MT Edut</i> 6:7; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed of Lublin OH</i> 15:4; <i>Teshuvot Brit Avraham EH</i> 71:2. Cf. others who claim that the presumption of being a single woman is applicable only if there is a factual doubt. See <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, MT Tumat Tzara’at</i> 2:1; <i>Teshuvot Masat Binyamin</i> 50; <i>Pri Megadim YD</i> 384:18; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 37. Cf. <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Kiddushin</i> 66a and <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 171 who argue that in a case of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt the original presumption of being a single woman is not reinstated.<br>In the wake of these various doubts regarding the efficacy of the <i>kiddushin</i>, we need to address the fact that they lived numerous years together as husband and wife and seemingly the act of <i>kiddushin</i> was established by intercourse (<i>bi’ah</i>). See infra chapter 4C, n. 63.</i> Following in the footsteps of some authorities who arrive at the conclusion that she is permitted to remarry rabbinically without a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 79a, s.v. <i>kidshei</i>; <i>Hadrei Lev, EH</i> 175.</i> we have decided to invalidate the <i>kiddushin</i> based upon the fact that the groom used a stolen ring, and given the various improprieties emerging from the wedding ceremony due to the <i>halakhic</i> incompetence of the officiating rabbi as per the view of Rabbi Ehrenberg.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying n. 36.</i>
Alternatively, we are dealing with a series of <i>doubts</i> concerning what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (<i>sefeika de’dina</i>, <i>halakhic</i> doubt). As we know, if there exists a double doubt in regard to the wedding ceremony (<i>seder kiddushin and nissuin</i>), there are numerous authorities that permit her to remarry in a case of a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak,</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 24 (48) (release from a levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22 (18), 62; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 1:68; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 2:51; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 130, <i>EH</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 21, 33, 48, 62, 64; <i>Teshuvot Ba’ei Hayyai, YD</i> 216; <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 5, 8, 35, 48; <i>Teshuvot Perah Shushan EH</i> 3:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:29, 31; <i>Get Pashut Gittin</i> 129:13; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Pesakim 37, Mahadura Tinyana 45; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo EH</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 111; <i>Kereti u-Peleti</i>, <i>YD</i> 110; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD</i> 110:5, 16; <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev</i> 12; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Minhat Moshe EH</i> 11; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 65, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 22–23 in the name of a dozen decisors; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Be’er Moshe</i>, Kuntres Binyan Yerushalyim 18; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev EH</i> 8; A. Makovsky, <i>Teshuvot Ohalei Aharon</i> 1:19; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19 (release from levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim EH</i> 3:11 (end); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 18, 6 <i>EH</i> 3 (8), 6, 8; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6; S. Messas, “ A doubt in coercion of a <i>Get</i>,” (Hebrew) 23 <i>Tehumin</i> 120, 123–124 (5763); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:73; <i>PDR</i> 21:10; File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, 11 Tishrei 5778; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rishon le-Tzion</i> 2:8. For additional authorities who endorse this posture, see numerous responsa, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike cited in <i>Sefer Rav Berakhot</i>, 126; <i>Mishpat ha-Get</i>, vol. 3, 495–499; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 3 (14), 6, <i>EH</i> 3 (9, 14–15), 6 (5) and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 159, n. 47.</i> <i>A fortiori,</i> when there are three doubts (or more) relating to doubts of what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be, it becomes effective to reinstate the presumption of being a single woman and thus nullify the presumption of being a married woman, in particular when dealing with <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110, 11–13; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher EH</i> 29; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon EH</i> 5; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6:5, 10, <i>OH</i> 52.</i>
Therefore, for both reasons, the plaintiff is permitted to remarry, without a <i>get,</i> any Jew except a <i>kohen</i>.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Final afterthoughts</b></span>
Even if the witnesses were eligible, as we have contended according to Rabbi Ehrenberg, the woman is presumed to be unmarried because the officiating rabbi was not competent in the <i>halakhot</i> of the process of marriage and divorce!
Should an arbiter reject his reasoning, seemingly we are left with validating the <i>kiddushin</i> based upon the presence of the Yemenite Jew under the canopy who may have been Torah observant or a rabbi who is known to be Torah observant, who “came and went” during the ceremony and either one may have observed the act of <i>kiddushin</i> . The question is can the act of <i>kiddushin</i> be established based upon the testimony of one witness? The Talmud teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 65a-b.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Rabbi Yehudah said: If a man betroths in the presence of one witness, we disregard (lit. have no fear of) his <i>kiddushin</i> . . . Rabbi Nahman stated in Shmuel’s name: If a man betroths in the presence of one witness, we disregard his <i>kiddushin</i> even if both (the couple) admit it . . . Rabbi Yitzhak ben Shmuel ben Marth said . . . If a man betroths in the presence of one witness, we disregard his <i>kiddushin</i> even if both admit it. Rabbah son of Rabbi Huna said: If a man betrothes in the presence of one witness, the Great Court rules, We disregard his <i>kiddushin</i>. Who is the Great Court – Rav. Others state, Rabbah ben Rabbi Huna said in Rav’s name: If a man betroths in the presence of one witness, the Great Court rules, we disregard his <i>kiddushin</i>. Who is the Great Court- Rabbi (Rabbi Yehudah ha-Nasi).</span>
In reliance upon these various Talmudic sages, the majority of post-­Talmudic legists will refrain from validating the <i>kiddushin</i> which was executed in the presence of one witness.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bahag, Kiddushin</i> 40; <i>Hagahot Maimoniyot Ishut</i> 4:4 in the name of Rabbeinu Tam and Rabbeinu Simha; <i>Ravan, Teshuvot</i> 64; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 6; <i>Rif, Kiddushin</i> 28a per Alfasi pagination; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Kiddushin</i> 3:13; <i>Semak</i>, Mitzvah 183; <i>MT, Ishut</i> 4:6; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:856; <i>Mordekhai, Kiddushin</i> 534 in the name of Maharam of Rothenberg; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 42:2; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 35; <i>Teshuvot Maharik,</i> shoresh 101; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 2:7; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i> EH 4. For additional decisors who align with the dominant view, see <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 12.</i> This position culminates in the Shulhan Arukh’s ruling, who states:<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 42:2.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If a man betroths without witnesses, even one witness, the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid even if they both admit to it.</span>
Elucidating upon Shulhan Arukh’s posture, one <i>beit din</i> teaches us: <sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR</i>) 7:175, 178.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i> are witnesses that establish (the <i>kiddushin</i>) rather than attest that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> transpired. In other words, the presence of the witnesses is part and parcel inorder to create the <i>kiddushin</i> and their validation. And if there are no witnesses who are eligible during the act of <i>kiddushin</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid even if it is crystal clear that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> happened.</span>
In the words of Rabbi Yehezkel Landau:<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 42:6 in the name of <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah, EH,</i> Mahadura Tinyana 75.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The qualitative majority (<i>rov binyan</i>) and the numerical majority (rov minyan) of the authorities argue that we disregard the <i>kiddushin</i> observed by one witness . . .</span>
Yet, there is a minority of authorities (<i>me’ut ha-poskim</i>) who endorse Semag’s view that one winess may validate a <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Semag</i>, Positive Commandments 48; <i>Sefer Yereim</i> 7; <i>Mordekhai, Kiddushin</i> 531, <i>Hagahot Maimoniyot Ishut 3:2; Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 212; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Padua</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Mintz</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 2:128.</i> As noted by others, for Semag we must regard the one witness’s presence to validate the <i>kiddushin</i> as a biblical stringency.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 7:4 in the name of Zikhron Yehudah, Re’em and others.</i> Given that this view has been labeled by decisors as a rejected opinion,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 42:9; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah EH</i> 42, <i>Hagahot Beit Yosef</i> 11.</i> we may conclude that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid and therefore the marriage may be voided. Furthermore, given that Rema argues that in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>), we do not endorse Semag’s posture, therefore in our case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance we would conclude that the marriage is invalid.
However, argues Rabbi Landau that in our case Semag would concur with the majority opinion that the marriage would be invalid.Given that he claims that Semag’s view is a rabbinic stringency, a position supported by numerous decisors,<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi Yevamot</i> 92a; <i>Tosafot Yevamot</i> 88a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 155; <i>Pnei Yehoshua Kiddushin</i> 65b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah,</i> Mahadura Kama <i>EH</i> 33; <i>Bah, EH</i> 42; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:260.</i> consequently should there be a basis for leniency, Semag would admit that we ought to be lenient and conclude that the marriage is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Noda be-Yehudah,</i> supra n. 80.</i> Clearly, we have demonstrated that due to the <i>halakhic</i> improprieties in the wedding ceremony there is a mandate for invoking leniency. As such, given the circumstances of our case, in the light of Rabbi Landau’s understanding, Semag would agree that the presence of one witness would not validate the <i>kiddushin</i>.
Seemingly, in accordance with the minority of arbiters who understand the Semag’s position to be one of <i>biblical</i> stringency,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yehudah Ya’aleh Assad</i> 17 in the name of Tosafot Rid; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 82.</i> the result would be that the <i>kiddushin</i> ought to be valid? Firstly, as we elucidated earlier in our decision, when dealing with a doubtful marriage (<i>kiddushei safek</i>), there are numerous early arbiters as well as other decisors who argue that we reinstate the original presumption of the wife, namely she has the presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) of being a single woman and she is <i>biblically</i> permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n.74.</i> Following in the footsteps of some authorities who arrive at this conclusion she is permitted to remarry rabbinically without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 72 and supra <i>Yabia Omer</i>, n. 77.</i> Moreover, there is the majority opinion, including: Bahag, Rif, Rambam, Rosh (possibly), Ra’vaad, Semag, Ramah, Meiri, Ritva, Rabbi Yosef Karo (possibly), Rabbi David ibn Zimra, Rabbi Mordekhai ha-Levi, Hida, Rabbi Ya’akov Emden and Rabbi Ovadiah Yosef, who rule that under certain conditions (e.g. a doubt what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be), a biblical doubt (a <i>sefeika d’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha’amek Sheilah</i> 68 <i>in the name of Bahag; MT, Tumat ha-Met</i> 9:12<i>, Avot ha-Tumah</i> 16:1<i>, Issurei Biah</i> 18:17<i>, Kilayim</i> 10:27<i>; Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> 310; <i>Alfasi, Pesahim</i> 40b, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 42 in the name of Rosh; <i>Teshuvot Mutzal me-Aish</i> 12 in the name of Rosh; <i>Sedei Hemed ha-Shalem</i>, 5, <i>Ma’arekhet Samakh</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 10 (Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Yoma</i> 8:7, <i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer OH</i> 30 and <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 2 in the name of Rosh); <i>Ra’vaad, Tumat ha-Met</i>, op. cit., <i>Kilayim</i>, op. cit.; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur</i> YD 228 in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Teshuvot Yad Eliyahu of Lublin</i> 49; <i>Semag</i>, Negative Commandments 121; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramah, Kiddushin</i> 39a; <i>Sefer Beit ha’Behirah, Shabbat</i> 23a, <i>Hagigah</i> 4a, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 39b; <i>SA OH</i> 616:2, <i>YD</i>, 110:9, 302:1, <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>OH</i> 3(1) in the name of Shulhan Arukh; (Cf. <i>Noam Siah,</i> vol. 1, 252–275 who argues that it is unclear what Rabbi Karo’s stance is concerning this issue)<i>; Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:93; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 8; <i>Birkei Yosef</i> 184:1; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 1, <i>OH</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Mutzal me-Aish</i> 1:12; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev YD</i> 1:87 (Cf. <i>YD</i> 2:4, 118); <i>Teshuvot Ta’alumot Lev</i> 2:15; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 3, 6, <i>EH</i> 3, 6 <i>YD</i> 24. See also, <i>Pri Megadim Petihah le-Birkot ha-Shahar</i>; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yosef YD</i> 19; <i>Mizgeret Shulhan</i> 110; <i>Mahazik Berakha</i> 589:6; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, 10, <i>YD</i> 6:9–10. Cf. <i>Pri Hadash</i> who claims that there is a consensus regarding <i>halakhic</i> doubts, even in pursuance to Rambam and Ra’avad that a doubt on the biblical level is to be treated stringently on a biblical level. See <i>Pri Hadash,</i> YD 110 in the name of the famous view of Ran on Rif, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b. Subsequently, <i>Pri Hadash</i> changes his mind.<br>There are over 25 additional “later authorities” (<i>Aharonim</i>) who either subscribe to this view and/or who state that this view is reflective of the majority of decisors. See <i>Noam Siah</i> 12 (2). In fact, some authorities note that the majority of arbiters subscribe to this view. See <i>Ridvaz</i>, op. cit.; <i>Pnei Yehoshua Pesahim</i> 9b, s.v. ha’nu; <i>Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> op. cit.; <i>Zikhron Yosef,</i> op. cit. Cf. other decisors who argue that the opposing approach is the dominant view. See <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen, Pesakim u-Ketavim</i> 129 (end); <i>Teshuvot Maharit YD</i> 1; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 1.</i>
Assuming that “a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level” applies to a doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (<i>sefeika de-dina</i> ),<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Gittin</i> 46a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur YD</i> 228 in the name of Ran; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 22:18; <i>Sheilat Ya’avetz</i>, supra n. 89; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed (</i>Lublin) <i>EH</i> 37 (end); <i>Pri Hadash, Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Nahalat Yehoshua</i> 6; <i>Yabia Omer</i>, supra n. 89. Cf. those who contend that in <i>sefeika de’dina</i> Rambam concurs that a <i>safek de’oraita</i> is resolved stringently on a biblical level. See <i>Kapot Temarim</i>, <i>Sefeika De’oraita</i>; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev, YD</i> 1:260; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim YD</i> 75, <i>EH</i> 1; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov EH</i> 43; <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama 1:256; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Tinyana 68; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 2:1; <i>Darkhei Teshuvah, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefek Sefeika</i>, 205; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, op. cit. In other words, according to these authorities, Rambam’s view, supra n.89 is only applicable to cases of factual doubt.</i> “if there exists a double doubt one must rule leniently” is to be understood as meaning that the first doubt is converted from a biblical prohibition to a rabbinic one and results in a stringent position (namely that the matter is prohibited rabbinically) which is compounded by the second doubt which creates a rabbinic doubt, and therefore, by dint of rabbinic <i>Halakhah</i> (“<i>din de’rabbanan</i>”) one may rule leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 34a; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Pesahim</i> 9b-10a, <i>Ketuvot</i> 9a; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh YD</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo EH</i> 39 (30); <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 20:3; <i>Hazon Ovadiah</i> 1:13; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Yavetz</i>, supra n. 6; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed of Lublin OH</i> 7:4; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Birkat Yosef Landa YD</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i> 8, s.v. <i>ha’ta’am ha’sheni</i>.<br>Whether one can rule leniently regarding a rabbinic doubt <i>a priori</i> (<i>le-khatehillah</i>) or <i>ex post facto</i> (<i>be’di’avad</i>) regarding a double <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty (<i>sefeik sefeika</i>) and/or a rabbinic doubt is subject to controversy. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>OH</i> 42 (2).</i> In other words, the ramifications of invoking a double doubt in a particular case is that the matter is permitted (“<i>heter gamur</i>”) without any taint of a prohibition (“<i>issur</i>”).<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Hayyim ben Attar, <i>Rishon le-Tzion</i>, <i>Sefek Sefeika</i>; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed</i>, <i>OH</i> 20:3.</i>
On the other hand, espousing the position that a biblical uncertainty ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level does not necessarily mean that the application of a double doubt will be ineffective. The consequent leniency associated with the implementation of a “double uncertainty” is due the rule of “follow the majority” (“<i>aharei rabbim le’hattot</i>”). The existence of one doubt creates a situation of a 50/50 uncertainty (“<i>ke-mehtza al mehtza dami</i>”), while the second uncertainty then creates a majority which results in treating the matter leniently on a biblical level.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Eruvin</i> 5b, s.v. ve-safek, <i>Kiddushin</i> 73a in the name of Rashi; <i>Shakh, SA YD 110</i>, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 27 in the name of Rashba; <i>Pri Hadash YD</i> 110:49; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed OH</i> 3:4; <i>Teshuvot Kol Eliyahu</i> 2, <i>YD</i> 3. For alternative explanations regarding the effectiveness of the double uncertainty even according to those authorities who contend that a biblical doubt is resolved stringently on a biblical level, see <i>Tal Hayyim</i>, 372, 379–383.</i> In accordance with many decisors, this approach is the dominant understanding as to why the employment of a double uncertainty will be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>YD</i> 14, 7 <i>EH</i> 6:5; <i>Taharat ha-Bayit</i> 1, 135–136.</i> That being said, it is unsurprising that some authorities who subscribe to the posture that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level will understand the dynamics of a double doubt based upon the rule of the majority principle (“<i>rov</i>”).<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumat ha-Deshen,</i> supra <i>n. 89; Maharit</i>, supra n. 89; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, ­supra n. 89.</i> In other words, the effectiveness of a double <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty (a <i>sefek sefeika</i>) may be based upon the majority and is unrelated to one’s position regarding the debate as to whether a biblical uncertainty (a ­<i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>biblical</i> level or by virtue of being a rabbinic innovation.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Yosef mi-Slutsk</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Shemen Rokeah, Tlitai YD</i> 3.</i>
Assuming that one endorses the position that a biblical doubt ought to be resolved stringently by dint of being a rabbinic decree, and therefore the deployment of a double doubt is operative, or that a double uncertainty is effective by virtue of its own inner <i>halakhic</i> logic unrelated to the ramifications of the existence a doubt regarding a biblical prohibition, are there grounds to void a marriage utilizing the mechanics of a double doubt in cases of a <i>halakhic</i> controversy?
Numerous decisors, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike, argue that in the wake of a “double doubt” or a series of doubts as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (known in <i>halakhic</i> terminology as “<i>sefeika de’dina</i>”) regarding biblical prohibitions including the <i>halakhic</i> impropriety of a ceremony of betrothal and marriage (<i>seder kiddushin ve’nissuin</i>), may, under certain conditions, nullify the presumption of a married woman and thus, in effect, will allow the woman to remarry without receiving a <i>get</i> from her <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 74.</i>
In short, even in light of the Semag’s view that we are concerned with the presence of only one witness at the <i>kiddushin</i>, given the circumstances of the case and the application of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt this marriage would be voided.

b) A marriage conducted by one who is not an expert in the nature of kiddushin

<b>B. A marriage conducted by One who is not an expert in the nature of <i>kiddushin</i></b>
In the summer of 1993, the wife (hereinafter: the plaintiff) and the husband (hereinafter: the defendant) were married in a ceremony conducted by a Conservative rabbi. In March 2015, the couple separated. The plaintiff asked the defendant for a <i>get</i>.
We summoned the defendant to a hearing in our <i>Beit Din</i>, but he refused to comply with the summons. We set a date for a hearing at which we heard the arguments of the plaintiff, and in light of what was said, we ordered the defendant to give his wife a <i>get</i>. We also watched a video of the wedding ceremony of the parties.
The parties obtained a civil divorce at the end of 2016, but the defendant persists until today in his refusal to give the plaintiff a <i>get</i>.
Rabbi Avraham Shapira, a former Chief Rabbi of Israel and presiding <i>dayan</i> of the Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, rules that in a case in which there is no cause for concern that the wife will become an <i>agunah</i>, “there is no need to search for all kinds of leniencies,” but “where an <i>agunah</i> is concerned, the <i>beit din</i> must search for ways to release her so that she not remain an <i>agunah</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Minhat Avraham</i> 4:6.</i>
In keeping with this ruling, we too have endeavored to bring to light the <i>halakhic</i> grounds for invalidating the <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated: the marriage) of the plaintiff and the defendant, in order to release the wife from the chains of her <i>aginut</i>, being unable to receive a <i>get</i> from the defendant.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>A. The Validity of the <i>Kiddushin</i> Conducted by a Conservative Rabbi</b>
The rabbi who conducted the wedding of the couple was a Conservative rabbi. This emerges from what he wrote in an article published in the <i>Sun Sentinel</i>: What are the rabbis supposed to do? They’re supposed to be like any ordinary human being: keep the commandments, observe the law, man to man.
In other words, observance of the Torah in his opinion is limited to the laws between man and man. Indeed, on the one hand, the officiating rabbi was ordained to the rabbinate in an Orthodox <i>yeshiva</i>, which at the time was known for producing graduates who were not conversant with <i>Halakhah</i>; on the other hand, however, the plaintiff told us that in a consultation with the Rabbi on the subject of marriage, he advised the parties that they must decide if they wish to observe <i>Shabbat</i> and the dietary laws. In this context, he told them that he himself travels by car on <i>Shabbat</i> because he has difficulty walking. Moreover, in a eulogy for him which appears on the web, his granddaughter said that her grandfather believed that all religions were valid.
We contacted a particular rabbi in Florida, and he informed us that the officiating rabbi was a Conservative rabbi, and this religious affiliation also appeared on Google. An additional important detail is that the officiating rabbi was a member of the Rabbinical Assembly, which is the official international organization of Conservative rabbis, founded in 1901 (see the website of the rabbinic organization: www.rabbinicalassembly.org).
Regarding the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> conducted by a Conservative rabbi, Rabbi Moshe Feinstein expounds, on the basis of Rambam’s rulings,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT</i>, <i>Teshuvah</i> 3:9.</i> as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 4:13.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even if he was among the best of them, one who did not eat forbidden foods and did not desecrate Shabbat in public by coming to his synagogue by car, nevertheless, apart from the fact that he certainly cannot benefit from the presumption of eligibility and he is in any case ineligible on account of a doubt, because he is a member of the Conservative Rabbinical Assembly, according to whom it is possible to discount even scriptural laws, and most of the rabbinical prohibitions, which amounts to heresy, they are ineligible as witnesses even if they themselves have said nothing . . . and there is no better evidence of the fact that he follows the Conservative approach which denies many of the scriptural precepts, and possibly even denies the very fundamental principle of the Revelation on Mount Sinai, when he is a rabbi in one of their synagogues. And even if he does this only to make a living, he is deemed to be a heretic who is ineligible as a witness.</span>
In another responsum dealing with a wife who was married by a Conservative rabbi, and was unfaithful to her husband, and she now wants to marry her lover, Rabbi Feinstein states:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 4:46. See also <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 2:26, 4:78, 5:7. Cf. <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:135 where Rabbi Feinstein contends that regarding a Conservative rabbi who dons phylacteries (<i>tefillin</i>) and observes <i>Shabbat</i> and is lenient regarding minor prohibitions, the <i>kiddushin</i> performed by him would be valid provided that the witnesses are Torah observant. However, in our present case, the rabbi was not Torah observant and aligned himself with an ideology which was in variance with traditional Torah theology.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Simply, in my view, he [the Conservative rabbi] is ineligible as a witness, even though he is reported to observe <i>Shabbat</i> and the dietary laws. Because the very fact of being a Conservative rabbi . . . is prohibited as a matter of heresy, and this disqualifies him as a witness.</span>
Rabbi Ahron Soloveichik espouses the same view that the marriage ceremony that was conducted by a Conservative rabbi, whose denial of some of the main principles of faith is known and who belongs to the Conservative Movement do not constitute a <i>halakhic</i> marriage, because the rabbi is ineligible as a witness to the validity of the <i>kiddushin.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">“<i>Kiddushin</i> Conducted by a Conservative Rabbi” (Hebrew) 20 <i>Tehumin</i> 297, 309–310. (5760). See also, Rabbi Chaim Jachter, “Conservative <i>Kiddushin</i>,” (Hebrew) 18 <i>Tehumin</i> 84 (5758). For Rabbi Feinstein’s invalidation of a wedding performed by a Conservative rabbi, see <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:46, 78.</i>
As such, according to the rulings of Rabbi Moshe Feinstein and Rabbi Ahron Soloveichik, due to the rabbi’s affiliation with the Conservative movement, the <i>kiddushin</i> he conducted was invalid.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>B. <i>Kiddushin</i> conducted by one who is not an expert in the nature of <i>kiddushin</i></b></span>
The <i>Talmud</i> states, “Rav Yehudah said in the name of Shmuel: Anyone who does not know the nature of divorce documents and <i>kiddushin</i> should have no dealings with them,”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 6a.</i> and the <i>Halakhah</i> was decided accordingly.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">SA <i>EH</i> 49:1 and the glosses <i>ibid</i>.; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 3:121; <i>Teshuvot Knesset Yehezkel</i> 72; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 119, 5:223; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak YD</i> 1:26(3). See addendum.</i> The reason is that <i>kiddushin</i> that is conducted by one who is not expert in their <i>halakhot</i> gives rise to doubts and defects in the act of <i>kiddushin.</i> Below we will discuss the doubts and the defects pertaining to the <i>kiddushin</i> of the plaintiff and the defendant.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>1. Failure to designate eligible witnesses for the <i>kiddushin</i></b></span>
In the present case, standing under the wedding canopy (<i>hupah</i>) were a quorum of relatives (men and women) together with the officiating rabbi and the cantor, who were not related to them.
At the time of the recitation of the words, “You are hereby betrothed . . .” by the groom and the handing over of the ring from the groom to the bride, the officiating rabbi and the cantor were both present; they both were members of the Conservative movement, and as noted, Rabbi Ahron Soloveichik’s view is that they are ineligible as witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soloveichik</i>, supra n. 5, 305.</i>
But even if we assume that the officiating rabbi and the cantor are eligible witnesses, the practice in many communities is to designate the witnesses who will attest that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> transpired, and they are deemed as the only witnesses who can confirm the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagahot ha-Semag</i>, mitzvah 183 (7); <i>Teshuvot Ridvaz</i> 2:707; <i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 36:8; <i>Beit Meir</i> 42:4.</i> One of the reasons for this practice is the concern that arises from Ritva’s view, namely that an ineligible witnesse disqualifies eligible witnesses,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 43a.</i> and some authorities have endorsed his position.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Yeshanim</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 113a in the name of <i>Rashbam</i>; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 81; <i>Rabbeinu Yeruham</i>, <i>Sefer Meisharim</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37:9, in the name of Ritva, who wrote: “And many held like him.”</i> However, other decisors factor Ritva’s posture into consideration only as a supporting argument (a <i>senif</i> ) for ruling leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Tinyana, 56:28; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:101; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 11.</i> In the present case, we factored Ritva’s opinion into consideration as an additional ground for invalidating the <i>kiddushin.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For extensive discussion of Ritva’s view in general and the basis for determining the <i>Halakhah</i> in accordance with a minority view in particular, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 245–252.</i> In the case at hand, the officiating rabbi did not designate witnesses.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. Lack of intention of the ineligible and the eligible witnesses to testify</b></span>
Another basis for leniency is the view of Shakh, namely that if there were relatives and ineligible witnesses standing under the wedding canopy and none of the witnesses (including the eligible ones) intended to testify, the entire testimony is nullified.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, <i>SA HM</i> 36:1. See <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 36:1 who is of the view that the opinion of <i>Shakh</i> must be taken into consideration.</i>
Amongst the guests in the hall at the time of the wedding ceremony, there were three men who were religiously observant, but even so, the opinion of Shakh must be taken into consideration;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, supra n, 14.</i> therefore, the situation here is one of a double doubt (<i>sefek sefeika</i>) that disqualifies these three people, as Rabbi Soloveichik contends:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soloveichik</i>, supra n. 5, 305.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even if it had emerged that amongst the guests in the hall, there were two men who refrained from desecrating the <i>Shabbat</i> in the presence of an ultra-Orthodox rabbi, even then there is a double doubt because we must adjoin the view of Shakh <i>. . .</i> whereby even though an eligible witness is fit to testify even if he did not intend to do so, in any case if amongst the witnesses there were relatives or ineligible persons then the eligible witness is fit to testify only if he had intent to testify. But if the eligible witness did not have intent to testify, then the presence of the relative and the ineligible witness disqualifies his testimony. And if so, even if we assume that amongst the guests there were two Jews in the category of <i>tinokot shenishbu</i> [this refers to those who sin inadvertently, but are not held accountable due to having been deprived of a Jewish education], who desecrated the <i>Shabbat</i> only in private, and they stood up in their places to watch the <i>kiddushin</i> – in any case, because the Conservative rabbi and the relatives who stood on the stage were certainly scripturally ineligible, therefore the fact that the Conservative rabbi and the relatives on the stage were watching, disqualifies the testimony of the two Jews who desecrated the <i>Shabbat</i> in private and were deemed to be <i>tinokot shenishbu.</i></span>
<b>3. Validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> by virtue of “We are witnesses”– <i>anan sahadi</i></b>
<i>Prima facie</i>, even in the absence of two eligible witnesses for establishing the <i>kiddushin</i> such as in our case, seemingly we should adopt the rulings of Havot Yair, Hatam Sofer <i>and</i> Iggerot Moshe whereby <i>halakhically</i> observant Jews who sat amongst the guests and watched the <i>kiddushin</i> ceremony, without hearing the husband’s statement, “You are hereby betrothed . . .” and without seeing the passing of the ring from the groom to the bride, may serve as witnesses by virtue of the concept of “we are witnesses” (<i>anan sahadi</i>).
Our response to this posture is that in the wake of the ineligibility of the witnesses, the <i>kiddushin</i> would have been voidable, and if our intention was to validate the marriage by means of other people amongst those present at the <i>kiddushin</i> ceremony, we would have considered invoking the views mentioned above.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:100, quoted in <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 42:11; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:76–77; 3:32; 4:13.</i> However, in the present case, we are seeking a way to invalidate the <i>kiddushin</i> due to the husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance! Therefore, notwithstanding the argument that there is a tradition in relation to rulings concerning forbidden relations, whereby the authorities must take into account all the strict views,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 17:15; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, <i>ibid.</i>, 31; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ibid., 61; <i>Mahatzit ha-Shekel</i>, ibid., 56; <i>Teshuvot Kedushat Yom Tov</i> 9, in the name of Rabbi Yom Tov Elgazi; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, <i>EH</i> 42:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Rahamim</i> (Rabbi Haim Rahamin Franco), <i>EH</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1:10, 13. <i>Cf. Taz</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 17:15.</i> in a situation involving <i>get</i> intransigence many legists argue that the ruling ought to be lenient and in certain situations may rely upon a minority view.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Rosh</i> 51:2; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 92; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 121; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:135; <i>Teshuvot Mas’at Binyamin</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i> (Krochmal) 103; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama, <i>EH</i> 29, 57; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:110; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 8:19.<br>For reliance upon a minority view, see <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 44, 105; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 36; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 17:47; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 188. Cf. <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 17:31. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 247–250, 252–256.</i>
As emphasized by Rabbi Sinai Sapir, a nineteenth century Lithuanian decisor:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Ani</i> 51.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In relation to the <i>halakhot</i> of being a chained woman (<i>igun</i>), it is not right to cobble together strictures on the basis of fine distinctions and subtle rejections of legal arguments; rather, we ought to prefer the superior path of lenient decision-making, for at the end of the day we are dealing with people’s lives. . . .</span>
Since we rule leniently, it is appropriate that we follow the majority of the authorities concerning invoking “we are witnesses” as a means to establish the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yehaveh Da’at</i> 6, <i>Kelalei ha-Hora’ah</i>, p. 32:9; <i>PDR</i> 4:164, 166 (Rabbi Hadaya, Rabbi Elyashiv and Rabbi Zolty).</i> Owing to the fact that the majority of decisors reject the view of Havot Yair, Hatam Sofer and Iggerot Moshe,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> 20; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh</i> 1:31; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 64; <i>Teshuvot Or Li</i> 73b.</i> we have refrained from adopting it in the circumstances of this case, particularly in view of the fact that in addition to the above decisors, their view is rejected by several leading contemporary legists such as Rabbi Eliezer Waldenberg, Rabbi Ovadia Yosef, Rabbi Osher Weiss and Rabbi Tzion Boaron.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 3, and 8:5; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 2:83; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 2:11, 3:22. Cf. R. Elyashiv, <i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 5:193 and R. Eliyahu, <i>Teshuvot Ma’amar Mordekhai</i> 1, <i>HM</i> 3 who endorse the view of Rabbi M. Sofer, Rabbi M. Feinstein and others. See supra n. 17.</i> Finally, in the wake of the designation of two witnesses to attest and establish the act of <i>kiddushin</i> which is a common practice today, there is no basis to endorse Havot Yair’s, Hatam Sofer’s and Iggerot Moshe’s posture.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahari Weil</i>, supra n. 22; <i>Teshuvot Perah Matteh Aharon</i> 2:117; <i>Teshuvot Ne’eman Shmuel</i> 79; <i>Teshuvot Yad ha-Levi EH</i> 23–24; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:157.</i>
Moreover, recourse to “we are witnesses” is conditional upon the fact that the officiating rabbi is <i>halakhically</i> observant in general, and is expert in the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>kiddushin</i> and marriage in particular, as stated above.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shevut Ya’akov</i>, supra n. 7; <i>Knesset Yehezkel</i>, supra n. 7; <i>Divrei Malkiel</i>, supra n. 7; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh</i>, supra n. 22; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 8:37:9.<br>According to <i>Taz</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 19:1, too, the officiating rabbi must have a basic knowledge of the <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to conducting the wedding ceremony.</i> The fact that the officiating rabbi did not designate witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i>, was not expert in additional <i>halakhot</i> relating to the <i>kiddushin</i> and marriage ceremony, and was unaware of the problem that was liable to arise as a result of adopting Ritva’s position, attests to a lack of <i>halakhic</i> expertise. As such, in the case before us one cannot invoke “we are the witnesses,” which is based on the implied assumption that it is possible to rely on the expertise of the officiating rabbi; hence, guests amongst the invitees who watched the <i>kiddushin</i> ceremony cannot serve as validating witnesses to the <i>kiddushin</i>. Finally, even Rabbi Feinstein, who aligns himself with the posture of Havot Yair and Hatam Sofer, agrees that in the wake of an officiating rabbi who is not versed in matters of <i>kiddushin,</i> the application of <i>“we are witnesses”</i> will be ineffective.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:77.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>4. Doubt as to whether the couple understood that the <i>kiddushin</i> was effectuated by the handing over of the ring</b></span>
The Torah teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 24:1.</i> “When a man taketh a wife . . .” and the Talmud explains exegetically, “When a man taketh a wife, and not when [a woman] taketh [a man].”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 4b.</i>
Moreover, at the beginning of Chapters 1 and 2 of Mishnah Kiddushin respectively, we read: “The man betroths” and “the woman is acquired.” In other words, as opposed to sale in which the money is handed over in consideration of the object that was acquired, here, in <i>kiddushin</i>, the money is given an act of undertaking an obligation(s) (a <i>kinyan</i>) of a prospective husband toward his prospective wife.
According to the literal interpretation of the Torah and the Mishnah, the man executes the act of undertaking a duty through <i>kiddushin</i> vis-à-vis the the woman, the prospective spouse is passive, and negates her mind and her will in the face of her husband and seemingly the wife is being acquired by the husband. This appears to be the case according to the well-known words of Ran who elucidates:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 30a, s.v. <i>ve-ishah</i>.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Since the Torah said, “When a man takes a woman,” and it did not say, “When a woman be taken to be with a man,” she has no legal capacity to transfer herself into his legal jurisdiction. Rather, in agreeing to be taken in marriage by the man, she negates her own will and mind and is then considered as ownerless property (<i>hefker</i> – AYW) vis-à-vis her husband. At that point, the husband transfers her into his domain, hence the act of “taking someone in <i>kiddushin</i>” can only be executed by a man and never by a woman.</span>
However, if one examines closely the words of Ran and other authorities, one sees that an act is required on the part of the woman, whereby she negates her will with respect to the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei R. Shimon Shkop</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 1; <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i> 7:12; <i>Kehillot Ya’akov</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 7 (Comments); <i>Mishnat Shlomo</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 2; File 870175/4, Haifa Regional Beit Din, 7 Tevet 5775; <i>Minhat Osher, Kiddushin</i> 1. In other words, whereas, the man establishes the <i>kiddushin</i> via the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>, the woman must accept (<i>kabalah</i>) the money in order to finalize the <i>kiddushin</i>. See <i>Minhat Osher, Kiddushin</i> 20.<br>As noted by some of the above-cited <i>legists</i>, <i>kiddushin</i> generates an acquisition of a prohibition (<i>kinyan issur</i>) that a married woman is prohibited to the world except to her husband rather than being a monetary acquisition (a <i>kinyan mamon</i>) like owning chattel. On the other hand, the <i>kiddushin</i> is created via the acquisition of money (<i>kinyan kesef</i> ). See <i>Teshuvot Avnei Millium</i> 17.<br>In other words, <i>kiddushin</i> is established by the transfer of money (e.g. a ring) from the man to the woman which symbolizes the undertaking by the man of his marital duties vis-à-vis his prospective wife based upon mutual consent rather than a medium of acquisition. Furthermore, whereas, a sales transaction executed via the transfer of money (a <i>kinyan kesef </i>) between the buyer and the seller is for the purpose of payment; in a matter of <i>kiddushin</i>, the transfer of an item of monetary value (<i>sha’vei kesef </i>) is a symbol for establishing intimacy (<i>kinyan ishut</i>). See <i>Seridei Esh, Kiddushin</i> 13.</i> Lest one construe the Ran’s words as suggesting that the consummation of <i>kiddushin</i> entails that the man is acquiring a monetary asset, namely his spouse, nothing could be further from the truth. As Ran notes elsewhere:<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ran on Rif, Gittin</i> 9a, s.v <i>ve’katvu</i>. See also, <i>Hiddushei ha-Ra’ah, Kiddushin</i> 2a; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Yoav</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 123:9.<br>Though in numerous instances Rashba argues that a husband possesses a <i>kinyan</i> vis-à-vis his wife (see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:40, 5:174; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Gittin</i> 75a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 59b) it should be understood as either a <i>kinyan issur</i> (see supra n. 30) or denoting that the husband has certain obligations and rights vis-à-vis his wife. Lest one argues that Rashba’s use of the term means that the husband owns his wife like chattel, Rashba explicitly states that the husband does not possess his wife. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Kiddushin</i> 6b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Bava Batra</i> 48b. The position of Netivot ha-Mishpat should be understood in the same fashion. See <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo EH</i> 18.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The woman is not the asset of the husband . . .</span>
The couple must be aware of the fact that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> is executed with intent for the purpose of <i>kiddushin.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 27:1–3; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, ibid. 1, 4; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ibid., 5, 7.</i> In other words, the man’s intent is that his engagement in conjugal relations will be exclusively with his spouse, namely personal exclusivity (<i>kinyan ishut</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Sameah, Ishut</i> 4:2; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 4:35; <i>Avnei Millium</i> 42:1; B. Lifshitz, “Topics regarding the nature of divorce, <i>kiddushin</i> and marriage,” (Hebrew) ed. A. Edrei et.al., <i>Studies in Law and Halakhah: Menachem Elon Memorial Volume</i>, Jerusalem, 2018, 532, n. 20.</i> Rema rules: “But a man is not believed when he says that his intention was not for <i>kiddushin</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 26:2.</i> Apparently, according to Rema, there is an assumption that a person executing the <i>kiddushin</i> intends for it to be for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i>. What does this refer to? It refers to the situation in which the person executing the <i>kiddushin</i> is <i>halakhically</i> observant in general, and knows that there is an obligation to have intention for the act of <i>kiddushin</i> in particular. However, in the circumstances of the present case, the person executing the <i>kiddushin</i> was brought up in a Conservative family, and attended Conservative Talmud torah studies classes (during extra-curricular time) and confirmation classes from the age of eight until the age of sixteen. It is therefore unreasonable to assume that the person was knowledgeable in <i>Halakhah</i> and intended that the act be executed for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i>. Moreover, in the absence of the witnesses hearing the <i>kiddushin</i> formula (“<i>harei at mekudeshet</i> . . .”), if it is crystal clear that the man and woman intended to consummate the <i>kiddushin</i>, then the <i>kiddushin</i> is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 27:3. In the absence of the man’s recitation of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula to the woman prior to the transfer of the ring, some contend that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid. See <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum, 12; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, ad locum 9; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ad locum, 19. For the source for these rulings see <i>Kiddushin</i> 6a; <i>SA EH</i> 27:1.</i>
In the present case, the couple exchanged rings. Although the plaintiff gave a ring to the defendant after the <i>ketubah</i> had been read, it is altogether extremely doubtful whether the plaintiff understood that the ring that had been given to her earlier at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> entailed a man’s undertaking to accept certain marital duties rather than a merely ceremonial act. Even if the officiating rabbi understood the <i>halakhic</i> difference between the respective rings exchanged, and therefore arranged it so that the plaintiff would give a ring to the defendant after the reading of the <i>ketubah</i>, he did not explain to the couple the differentiated significance of this ring exchange. Therefore, a doubt arises as to whether the officiating rabbi was able to explain the difference between the giving of the <i>kiddushin</i> ring by the groom to the bride, and the subsequent giving of the ring as a gift by the plaintiff to the defendant after the reading of the <i>ketubah</i>. In relation to both rings, the intention may have been only to exchange gifts.
As Rabbi Moshe Feinstein aptly notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 3:25.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even if he gives her a ring, the fact that she also gives him a ring proves that the ring that he gave was also a mere gift on account of them becoming man and wife, but it does not relate in any fashion to executing <i>kiddushin</i>.</span>
<b>5. Giving a ring before saying, “You are hereby betrothed”</b>
The groom must recite the statement of <i>kiddushin</i> – “You are hereby betrothed to me . . .” prior to giving the ring,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">MT, <i>Ishut</i> 3:1; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah</i>, <i>EH</i> 27, <i>Hagahot Tur</i> 8; <i>ha-Mikneh</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> – Kuntres Aharon 27:9.</i> and if the bride received the ring prior to the statement having been recited – the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Poskim</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 28:31:41–43.<br>Some contend that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid. See <i>Magid Mishneh, MT Ishut</i> 3:8 in the name of Rashba; <i>Bedek ha-Bayit, Tur EH</i> 36.</i>
In the present case, as appears in the video of the ceremony, the giving of the ring by the groom preceded his reciting of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula, “You are hereby betrothed.” In other words, the act of <i>kiddushin</i> was executed without the statement. Now, if the man would have spoken with the woman about matters of <i>kiddushin</i> and given her the ring in silence, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 6a; <i>SA</i>, <i>EH</i> 27:1.</i> Similarily, in the present case the <i>kiddushin</i> ought to have been equally recognized. However, as we noted, the parties were unaware of the fact that they were dealing with “matters of <i>kiddushin</i>,” (<i>iskei kiddushin</i>) for they viewed the ceremony as the exchange of presents, and had no awareness of the aspect of the prospective husband’s undertaking of his marital responsibilities at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>. As Rabbi Osher Weiss incisively observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, October 1, 2017. Rabbi Weiss’s utilization of the word of “acquisition” is to be used as denoting a man’s decision to undertake an obligation(s) upon marriage rather than viewing his prospective spouse as acquiring a piece of chattel. See further, <i>Minhat Osher, Kiddushin</i> 1, 2, 5; <i>Minhat Osher</i>, <i>Bereshit</i>, Hayei Sarah, 26. See further supra notes 30–31. Cf. others who contend that even if a Jewish couple live together and have an exclusive relationship of intimacy with each other (a husband’s <i>kinyan</i> of <i>ishut)</i> such as a civil marriage rather than intend to be intimate for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i>, should the ties dissolve; a <i>get</i> ought to be executed in order to nullify the husband’s undertaking of the duty of intimacy. See <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:25 in the name of Rabbi Henkin; Rabbi Sheinberg, <i>Ohr ha-Ner</i>, Tractate Gittin (end) in the name of Hazon Ish. See infra chapter 4c, n. 63.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A husband’s act of <i>kiddushin</i> is different according to our Torah . . . from what is customary in the modern world. According to the Torah, the husband acquires his wife by giving her a ring as <i>kiddushin</i> of money . . . The <i>kiddushin</i> is effectuated by him but one requires only her consent. . . . However, the modern understanding in the world is that we have a mutual obligation each for the other without any symbolic act of undertaking an obligation (<i>kinyan</i>) . . . it is a sign of endearment and love. Therefore, the practice is to exchange rings and the primary validity of the betrothal according to their outlook is in their declaration . . . rather than money.</span>
Therefore, it is very doubtful whether the couple understood that giving the ring is the actualization of this symbolic act or whether they regarded the ring simply as part of the marriage ceremony.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>6. Ownership of the ring by the person who executes the <i>kiddushin</i> (the <i>mekadesh</i>)</b></span>
The <i>kiddushin</i> ring must belong to the prospective husband who executes the <i>kiddushin</i>. The purchase of it by the groom,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <i>EH</i> 28.</i> excludes the use of a borrowed ring,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2.</i> a stolen ring<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 28:1.</i> or one belonging to his partner<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 28:18.</i> for <i>kiddushin</i>. It becomes the responsibility of the officiating rabbi to establish that the ring does indeed belong to the groom.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel SA</i> 28:49.</i>
In the present case, prior to the ceremony, the parents of the defendant told the plaintiff that they had purchased a ring as a gift, and it should be used in the ceremony. In the absence of an act of acquisition, ownership of the ring vested in the plaintiff, since the defendant’s parents announced that they bought the ring <i>for her</i> as a gift, indicating acquisition by way of a statement attesting to a <i>halakhic</i> transfer of ownership (<i>kinyan odita</i>). However, a preliminary condition for recognition of this type of acquisition is that the statement was said in the presence of two eligible witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 39:8; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 194:4; <i>Imre Binah</i>, <i>Halva’ah</i> 16, in the name of Maharsha and Maharam Lublin.</i>
Clearly, the defendant did not need to execute the symbolic act of acquiring the ring via lifting (<i>kinyan hagbahah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 86a.</i> Acquisition may be executed via possessing the ring in his hand (<i>kinyan yad</i>). However, such acquisition is valid provided that the person had intent to acquire the object.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Bava Batra</i> 54a, s.v. <i>adatah</i>; <i>Hiddushei Rabbi Shimon Shkop, Bava Metzia</i> 23. Cf. others who contend that the effectiveness of acquiring the ring via possession (<i>kinyan yad</i>) is contingent upon the execution of lifting the ring. See <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 268:2.</i> In our case, given the defendant’s lack of Jewish education in general and ignorance of the <i>halakhot</i> of acquisition in particular, the defendant lacked the intent to acquire the ring. Furthermore, seemingly since the defendant’s parents gave the ring to their son and we know that “when another mind conveys title” (<i>da’at aheret maknah</i>) the defendant may acquire title,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nimukei Yosef, Bava Batra</i> 41a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Gittin</i> 20b.</i> so the transfer ought to be effective. Nevertheless, given the parents lack of Jewish education in general and the <i>halakhot</i> of acquisition in particular, such a transfer never transpired.
To state it differently, before the <i>kiddushin</i> ring was given by the defendant to the plaintiff, ownership of it remained vested in his parents, and the <i>kiddushin</i> was in fact executed with a ring that did not belong to the defendant, hence the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid or minimally a doubtful marriage if construed as a borrowed ring or as a gift to be returned to the donor.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Concerning a borrowed ring, see <i>SA EH</i> 28:1; <i>Be’er Hetev SA EH</i> 28:4. Regarding a gifted ring from a third party, see <i>SA EH</i> 28:19, <i>Rema</i>, ad. locum; <i>SA EH</i> 28:20; <i>Otzar ha-Poskim SA EH</i> 28:20(105). Concerning a gifted ring from the defendant’s parents to the defendant, assuming that the parents had intentions to transfer ownership to him as a gift and the defendant had intention to acquire it, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be valid. See <i>Otzar ha-Poskim SA EH</i> 28, 1:5; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 28:84. Howver, as we mentioned neither the parents nor their son, the defendant were versed in the <i>halakhot</i> of acquisition. As such, the gift transfer of the ring never occurred. In short, we are dealing with a doubtful marriage.</i>
Finally, even if such a statement was declared in front of two witnesses and consequently the ring now belongs to the plaintiff, it is a matter of debate as to whether a second <i>kiddushin</i> has to be executed if the prospective wife gave her ring to her prospective husband to be utilized as the <i>kiddushin</i> ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Gittin</i> 20b; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:86; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger,</i> Mahadura Tinyana, 45; <i>Teshuvot Pri ha-Aretz</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 6:8; <i>Teshuvot ha-Sabba Kadisha</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Imrei David</i> 22.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>7. Absence of finality of intention in the <i>kiddushin</i> ring requiring appraisal</b></span>
A wife cannot be acquired by means of an object the value of which cannot be appraised by experts, since its value may be wrongly appraised; therefore, the bride cannot be assured of its appraisal.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 31:2, and the validity of such <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful (<i>safek kiddushin</i>). See <i>Rema</i>, ibid.; <i>Helkat Mehokeik</i>, ibid<i>.</i>, 4; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ibid., 3.</i>
In our case, the plaintiff was married by means of a ring containing several diamonds, and she had no idea of the value of the ring and its worth prior to the <i>kiddushin</i>. As we mentioned, she knew only that she would receive this ring in the wedding hall in the course of the wedding ceremony.
Moreover, when the face of the bride is covered at the moment of the giving of the ring, this indicates that she is not particular about the object with which she is being betrothed, and the value of the ring is not important to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 31:2, in the name of Rashba.</i> In the present case, however, the bride’s face was uncovered at the time that the ring was given.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Whether the witnesses are obligated to see the bride’s face is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:227–228; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 31:2; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:100; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1:62; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 31.</i> Furthermore, in order to dispel the concern about the absence of finality of intention on the part of the bride, the officiating rabbi ought to have asked her whether the ring was indeed worth one <i>perutah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 31:12. The value of a <i>perutah,</i> which is a type of coin, is 1/1244 of a troy ounce (1/40 gram) of pure silver. See <i>Hazon Ish HM</i> 16:30.</i> In the present case – he did not ask.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>8. Seclusion of the bride and groom (<i>yihud</i>)</b></span>
There are several interpretations of the definition of the marital canopy (“<i>hupah</i>”). According to some decisors, it is the bringing of the bride to the house of the groom for the purpose of marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 55:4; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ibid. 9.</i> Others are of the opinion that it is obligatory for the couple to seclude themselves in a special place for the purpose of marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 55:1; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, ibid. 9.</i> and there are other interpretations as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, <i>EH</i> 55:4–11.</i> According to the second view, which is the common practice in the Ashkenazic community,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">B. Adler, <i>Marriage in accordance to Halakhah</i> (Hebrew), vol. 1, 382.</i> the bride and groom seclude themselves in a “seclusion room” after the wedding ceremony, and two eligible witnesses must be present when the couple goes into the room.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Ridvaz</i> 1:121; <i>ha-Mikneh</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i>, <i>Kuntres Aharon</i> 55:1; <i>Teshuvot Imre David</i> 29.</i>
In the present case, the Ashkenazic couple was secluded without the presence of two witnesses. In other words, the officiating rabbi was evidently unaware that the <i>kiddushin</i> and marriage ritual require the presence of two witnesses at the time of the seclusion.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Decision</b></span>
In view of all the above, the <i>kiddushin</i> of the plaintiff and the defendant are voidable for the following reasons:
First, according to Rabbi Ehrenberg, who teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Devar Yehoshua</i> 3:20.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Since the witness was a transgressor of the Shabbat, there is a suspicion that the rabbi who performed the <i>kiddushin</i> for them also did not perform the <i>kiddushin</i> in a way that caused them to have effect . . . for the witnesses might not have watched the ring being given, or if the word <i>li</i> “to me,” as required by <i>Even ha-Ezer</i> 27:4, were missing, and as a result there is no valid <i>kiddushin</i>, and the officiating rabbi must check this; this is acceptable if the rabbi who is the officiating rabbi is an observant person who is expert in the nature of <i>kiddushin</i> – we can rely on the presumption that the <i>kiddushin</i> were in accordance with the law . . . If there are no witnesses to this, and in particular if the officiating rabbi is not an expert and cannot be presumed to have conducted [them] in accordance with the law . . . it is doubtful whether <i>kiddushin</i> took place, and the woman is presumed to be unmarried, even if we know that there were eligible witnesses at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> to attest to their validity.</span>
In other words, even if there were eligible witnesses, according to Rabbi Ehrenberg, the woman is deemed to be unmarried, because the officiating rabbi was not proficient in the <i>halakhot</i> of the ceremony of <i>kiddushin</i> and marriage!
Indeed, in our case, as we explained, the officiating rabbi did not attend to all that was required in order for the <i>kiddushin</i> to be valid and consequently “we are witnesses” (<i>anan sahadi</i>) was ineffective as a vehicle to establish the act of <i>kiddushin</i>.
To state it differently, when dealing with a doubtful marriage (<i>kiddushei safek</i>), there are numerous early authorities as well as other decisors who argue that we reinstate the original presumption of the wife, namely she has the presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) of being a single woman and she is <i>biblically</i> permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ran on Rif Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110, <i>Kelalei Sefeik Sefeika</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 18; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh</i>, <i>MT Edut</i> 6:7; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1<i>, YD</i> 111; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:57; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed of Lublin OH</i> 15:4; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 59. Cf. <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Kiddushin</i> 66a and <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 171 who argue that in a case of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt the original presumption of being a single woman is not reinstated.</i> Following in the footsteps of some authorities who arrive at the conclusion that she is permitted to remarry rabbinically without a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 79a, s.v. <i>kidshei</i>; <i>Hadrei Lev, EH</i> 175. See further supra Chapter 2.</i> we have decided to invalidate the <i>kiddushin</i> based upon the fact that the groom used a ring which did not belong to him, and given the various improprieties emerging from the wedding ceremony due to the <i>halakhic</i> incompetence of the officiating rabbi, as per the view of Rabbi Ehrenberg.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying n. 61.</i>
Secondly, notwithstanding some who contend that a wedding officiated by a Conservative rabbi is a case of a doubtful marriage (<i>safek kiddushin</i>) where one can either obligate or coerce the giving of a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 3360-21-2, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 10, 2010. Though the <i>Beit Din</i> argues that based upon <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, supra n. 3 we are dealing with a doubtful <i>kiddushin</i>, however a review of <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, supra n. 5 will show that Rabbi Feinstein invalidates the marriage.</i> in accordance with the rulings of Rabbi Moshe Feinstein and Rabbi Ahron Soloveichik, due to the fact that the officiating rabbi was affiliated with the Conservative Movement in general, and subscribed to its views in particular as well as Rabbi Ehrenberg’s position of the <i>halakhic</i> incompetence of the officiating rabbi the <i>kiddushin</i> that he conducted are not to be recognized.
Accordingly, the plaintiff is permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i> any Jew, including a <i>Kohen</i>.
This decision was approved by a renowned <i>halakhic</i> authority.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Whether there is a <i>halakhic</i> requirement devolving upon a <i>beit din</i> to enlist the support of “a second opinion” or this rabbinic approval is reflective of a practice (“<i>nohag</i>”) is subject to controversy. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 256–262.</i>

c) The wedding ring; A borrowed ring or a gift on the condition to return it?

<b>C. A <i>kiddushin</i> ring: A borrowed ring or a gift on the condition to return it?</b>
During January 1993, the couple in question married. In September 2010, the couple separated and the civil divorce was finalized in September 2016. We summoned the husband (hereinafter: the defendant) to appear in <i>beit din</i> and he refused to respond to the summons. We scheduled a <i>beit din</i> hearing, we heard the arguments of the wife (hereinafter: the plaintiff), and during January 2018 we obligated the defendant to give a <i>get</i> to the plaintiff. To date, he has been recalcitrant in regard to giving the <i>get</i>.
Prior to the defendant’s giving a <i>kiddushin</i> ring (loosely translated: the wedding ring) to the plaintiff, the rabbi who officiated at the wedding ceremony (the <i>mesadeir kiddushin</i>) asked the defendant – “Is the ring yours and have you paid for it?” His reply was that the ring belongs to him and he purchased it. However, the plaintiff, as well as other relatives that stood under the wedding canopy (<i>hupah</i>) during the time of the <i>kiddushin,</i> said that the defendant lied regarding the ownership of the ring.
As described, “a crisis” occurred when the individuals standing under the wedding canopy realized that there was no wedding ring. Suddenly, one of the people standing there, a friend of the defendant, removed his ring and he gave it to the defendant, and with this ring the act of <i>kiddushin</i> was consummated. After the ceremony, the defendant’s friend approached the plaintiff and requested that his ring be returned to him. The plaintiff agreed, she removed the ring and returned it to the defendant’s friend.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Discussion</b></span>
Even though many authorities claim that traditionally, all strict views must be considered in matters concerning forbidden sexual relations,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 17:15; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 17:31; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 17:61; <i>Mahatzit ha-Shekel, SA EH</i> 17:56; <i>Teshuvot Kedushat Yom Tov</i> 9 in the name of Rabbi Yom Tov Algazi; <i>Erekh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 42:2; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan, EH</i> 42:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Rahamim Franco, EH</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher EH</i> 29; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1:10, 13. Cf. <i>Taz, SA EH</i> 17:15.</i> nevertheless, in a matter of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>) many contend that the lenient view ought to be adopted.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 51:2, <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 92; <i>Teshuvot Maharik,</i> shoresh 121; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:135; <i>Teshuvot Mas’at Binyamin</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 103; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah EH</i> Mahadura Kama 29, 57; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:110; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 8 (19).<br>For reliance upon a minority view, see <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 44, 105; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 36; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 17:47; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 188. Cf. <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 17:31. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 247–250, 252–256.</i> As Rabbi Yitzhak Bacher teaches us, if every Torah scholar abstains from addressing these issues and declares that he will not enter “a place where there is a mighty flame” due to the stringency of prohibited sexual relationships, this is not the proper approach to adopt. Every Torah scholar, may he be a minor or major scholar, is obligated “to search with candles in the holes and cracks,” and maybe he will discover a medicine for the daughters of Yisrael in order to save them from <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Emet</i> 9. See also, <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Nidah</i> 10:3; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 44, 109; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 57.</i>
As Rabbi Avraham Shapiro, former Chief Rabbi of the State of Israel, observes in a case which did not entail <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>):<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Minhat Avraham</i> 4:6.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">We do not work excessively hard to consider carefully all potential leniencies, but in the case of an <i>agunah</i>, the <i>beit din</i> must work hard to identify a leniency in order that she not remains an <i>agunah.</i></span>
As Rosh states:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 51:2.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Every teacher is to review all the sides of the issue in order to permit (her to remarry).</span>
Accordingly, we have canvassed the <i>halakhot</i> to void the <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated: the marriage) of the above couple and to release her from the chains of <i>igun</i>.
The emerging question from the facts of the case is: at the time the defendant’s friend transferred his ring to the defendant, what was his intention? Did he intend to lend his ring for the act of <i>kiddushin</i> or did he intend to gift it on condition that it would be returned after the ceremony?
Let us address both possibilities.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>1. The validity of a borrowed wedding ring</b></span>
The threshold question is whether he asked for the ring in order to marry his prospective wife or requested to borrow it without specifying the reason for borrowing the ring. Clearly, given the circumstances, the reason for borrowing the ring was for the purposes of marrying his prospective wife.
Let’s begin with the words of Rabbi Shmuel Kol, a contemporary commentator on Rambam’s Mishneh Torah, regarding a borrowed <i>kiddushin</i> ring, who expounds:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Har ha-Melekh</i>, 7, <i>Ishut</i>, pp. 424–428.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Many problems emerge regarding <i>kiddushin</i> with a borrowed ring by men who are not Torah observant. They live many years together (as spouses – AYW) and exchange rings between themselves as a sign of trust and mutual agreement to maintain joint lives as a husband and wife. . . . Frequently, after having sired children, they turn to the rabbinate (Israeli rabbinate of the Chief Rabbi – AYW) in order to have a marriage (a <i>halakhic</i> union – AYW) executed. The rabbi who officiates at the marriage asks and clarifies in the following manner: “Is the ring yours? Did you purchase it with your money?” The secular groom, who does not understand the import of these strange questions, responds frequently: “Yes, absolutely.” And he thinks, what do they want for me, did I steal the gold ring? . . . He fails to raise in his mind the significance of how the marriage ring reached his possession for a second time via the bride that lent it to him for the purpose of the marriage ceremony. She does not raise in her mind that she has to gift it to him. Rather, she gave him the ring for the ceremony and the rest is in the hands of the rabbinate.</span>
As we will see, this fear of dealing with individuals who are ignorant of <i>Halakhah</i> is factored into consideration by the decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">However, if the husband requested of his wife to gift him the ring, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be valid. See <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:86; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger,</i> Mahadurah Tinyana 45.</i> Can we speak of a groom <i>halakhically intending</i> to give a borrowed ring to his bride?
Rosh states:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Kiddushin</i> 1:20.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If he lent the ring for a specified time and he gave permission to the borrower to lend it to a third party in order to marry her, notified him that the ring is lent for a specified time and it was given to her, she is deemed married due to deriving benefit from its use as well as the adornment until a prescribed time. One must appraise that the benefit from the adornment is worth the value of a <i>perutah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The value of a <i>perutah,</i> which is a type of coin, is 1/1244 of a troy ounce (1/40 gram) of pure silver. See <i>Hazon Ish HM</i> 16:30.</i> She is married even though the actual ring does not belong to her.<br>If he says let me borrow the ring in order to marry her with it, she is married due to the fact that he borrowed it on the assumption that he wanted to marry a woman. . . . We are witnesses that he resolved in his heart to give it to him in language which would be effective regarding the act of <i>kiddushin</i> that will enable her to be married. . . .</span>
According to Rosh’s presentation, does the borrower have a right of possession (“a <i>zekhut kinyanit</i>”) in the borrowed ring?
