As we know “a lease (<i>sekhiru</i>t) for a day is a sale.”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 15a.</i> In other words, though a leased item is not considered to be owned by the lessee, nevertheless the lessee possesses the asset and the lease is for a set time.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Sekhirut</i> 7:1<i>, She’elah ve-Pikadon</i> 1:5.</i> The right of possession of the lessee manifests itself in the fact that the lessor transferred to him the benefit from the asset rather than the actual ownership of the asset.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Mekhirah</i> 23:1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1028; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 1:6; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Bava Metzia</i> 103a in the name of Ra’ah<i>; Yam shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i> 5:33; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra HM</i> 315:3; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 311:1.</i> Similarly, Rosh ascribes to this approach that the nature of the lessee’s right vis-à-vis the asset is a possessive right.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 1:6.</i> As Rosh writes elsewhere:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Bava Kama</i> 5:10.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A lease for a day is a sale and the sale is for his benefit.</span>
If we turn to the nature of borrowing (<i>she’elah</i>) in accordance with Rosh’s words, the borrower acquires a possessive right in the ring for a prescribed time for a specific use, i.e. the act of <i>kiddushin</i>. Similar to a lease where the lessee does not acquire an ownership right in the asset, analogously the husband does not receive such a right in the ring. However, as noted by Rosh, similar to a leased item, the prospective wife (<i>mekudeshet</i>) must derive a benefit from utilizing the ring during the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>.
His ruling is equally clarified in another responsum,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 32:2</i> and cited by Tur and Shulhan Arukh, with the commentaries on the Shulhan Arukh not citing a dissenting view.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 29; <i>SA EH</i> 29:19.</i> Other decisors concur that the borrower has a possessive right for use just as the lessee.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 210; <i>SA HM</i> 341:3; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 28:48; <i>Levush EH</i> 28:19; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 106. For other authorities, see <i>Otzar ha-Poskim EH</i> 28:19, pp. 12–15.</i>
Adopting the posture of Rosh raises a few difficulties.
First, in our present case, regarding the friend of the defendant who is interested in aiding the <i>kiddushin</i> to transpire, we may assume that he intends to transfer (<i>hakna’ah</i>) the ring in a <i>halakhically</i> effective fashion. However, in our situation, we have reason to suspect that the lender is not versed with the <i>halakhot</i> of executing a transfer! While he studied in a Yeshiva day school for his elementary school years, afterwards he enrolled and studied in public school and did not gain an advanced Jewish education.
Secondly, as we noted, Rosh teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh,</i> supra n. 8. However, <i>ex post facto</i>, the transfer to a sub-borrower is valid and consequently the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid. See <i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i> supra n. 17.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If he lent him the ring for a specific time and gave him permission to lend it to a third party to marry a woman, she is deemed married.</span>
Generally, the borrower is proscribed from lending the borrowed item to a third party. In our case, the defendant would be unable to lend his friend’s ring to his prospective spouse for the consummation of <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i>, supra n. 17 in the name of Ma’harikh.</i> However, Rosh argues that if the lender permits the borrower to lend it to a third party, the loan is valid. And in our case, the circumstances demonstrate that the <i>lender</i> (the defendant’s friend) authorized the defendant to lend the ring to his prospective wife. As such, the <i>kiddushin</i> ought to be valid. However, Rabbi Ephraim Navon demurs and understands that Rosh contends that the lender can expropriate the lent item from the sub-borrower, and therefore she would not be married.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahaneh Ephraim, She’elah u-Pikadon</i> 6.</i> In fact, elsewhere, Rabbi Navon’s interpretation is found supported in Rosh’s ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2.</i> Given that expropriation is valid, therefore the benefit derived from the use of the ring cannot serve as a medium to establish the <i>kiddushin</i> (<i>kesef kiddushin</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For additional problems with adopting Rosh’s posture, see <i>Otzar ha-Poskim, EH</i> 28:19, pp. 13–14.</i>
Furthermore, in our circumstances, the borrower and the lender did not make the plaintiff aware that the ring was borrowed. The reason is that one has to inform her that the borrowing was for a specified time, lest she think that the ring was hers forever, and therefore she agreed to adorn herself with the ring. For otherwise, when she would realize that it wasn’t given to her permanently, there would be no <i>kiddushin</i> even though she had derived benefit from the adornment which was valued at least a <i>perutah,</i> as she had no intent for this benefit to serve as the <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Beit David EH</i> 21.</i>
The following question is posed to Rashba:
<span class="poetry indentAll">Some say that a borrowed ring for the purpose of marrying a woman deems her married . . . And here, where it was borrowed in order to marry a woman, and it was lent for that purpose, she is deemed married, and this is how the Geonim ruled . . .</span>
Rashba replies:<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:273.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Those Geonim who ruled in this fashion – I never heard about them, and I do not agree with their posture . . . We are dealing with a loan, not a gift or a sale. For neither a sale nor a gift is ever [formulated] in the language of a loan. . . . A person knows that one does not marry with a borrowed utensil, and resolves to give it as a gift . . . Firstly, most people are not proficient in these <i>halakhot</i>. Moreover, most people err in this matter because they do not know the <i>halakhah</i> of <i>kiddushin</i>.</span>
Rashba claims that consummating a <i>kiddushin</i> with a borrowed ring is invalid due to the fact that lender does not intend to transfer the ring to the groom (the <i>mekadesh</i>), because he is unaware that for the <i>kiddushin</i> to be effective one requires that the ring be transferred. In rabbinic parlance, one requires the knowledge of the lender to transfer it in accordance with the rules of undertaking duties (<i>da’at makneh</i>) accompanied by the execution of a symbolic act to undertake a duty (a <i>kinyan</i>), and the lender is bereft of such knowledge.
One might argue that it suffices if the lender had a general intent to transfer the item to the groom at an unspecified time and without designation of a particular symbolic act.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 275:4; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 200:14.</i> However, in our situation, the lender was bereft of such intention. Nonetheless, in pursuance to Shakh and Netivot ha-Mishpat,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh, SA HM</i> 358:1; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 197:4.</i> if it is clear that he desires that his friend benefit from the monetary item, even if he is unaware of the <i>halakhot</i> regarding a <i>halakhic</i> transfer, then the transfer is valid. As such, in our present case, it is clear that the groom’s friend (i.e. the lender) wants his ring to be used in the ceremony and consequently his transfer ought to be effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 2:80.</i> As such, Rashba’s stance is problematic.
Rashba’s line of reasoning stands in variance with Rosh’s rationale that the lender and borrower want the <i>halakhic</i> status change from being a single woman to being a married woman (known as the <i>halot</i> condition), and consequently he intends to transfer the item in a <i>halakhically</i> effective manner.
Yet, numerous other decisors subscribe to Rashba’s opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 170; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2 in the name of Rabbi Barukh; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 98:4 in the name of Rash mi-Shantz; <i>Teshuvot ha-Saba Kadisha</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6.</i> In fact, Rabbi Avraham Borenstein observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 136.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even though in a case of a borrowed ring by the prospective husband, Shulhan Arukh rules that she is married, in pursuance to Rosh, Rashba in 4:273 disagrees, and the Beit Yosef surely did not see this responsum, because if he did, he would have cited it. Also, Knesset ha-Gedolah . . . who is uncertain whether Rashba agrees with Rosh, and also Maharhash did not see. And if Beit Yosef would have seen it, he would not have ruled like Rosh, and marrying a woman with a borrowed ring creates a doubtful marriage.</span>
In another responsum, Rashba adds:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 6:2.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">She becomes married via the benefit derived from her adornment which is worth the value of a <i>perutah</i>. . . . However, if he lent it to her for an extended period of time and stated that the benefit derived from the span of time is worth the value of a <i>perutah,</i> and he said to her explicitly, “be married to me by the benefit derived of the adornment of this ring for an allotted time,” she is married, because he did not marry her with the actual ring but with the benefit she derived.</span>
In other words, according to Rashba, the <i>halakhic</i> status change from being a single woman to being a married woman is grounded in the benefit derived from wearing the ring of <i>kiddushin</i> rather than from possessing the ring.
As we pointed out, while there are differences between the views of Rosh and Rashba concerning the validity of utilizing a borrowed ring for the act of <i>kiddushin</i>, nevertheless the common denominator of their postures is that the woman is married due to the use or adornment which is worth the value of a <i>perutah</i>.
The question which emerges from our presentation is whether deriving the benefit of adornment which is valued at a <i>perutah</i> is considered monetary substance equivalent to legal tender (“<i>mamon</i>”) and is therefore a means to execute or undertake an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>), or is it something lacking a physical property (<i>a “davar shein bo mamash”</i>)?
We read in the Talmud that there is a dispute between Ulla and Rabba as to whether a betrothal performed with a priestly tithe on produce (<i>terumot</i>) and other tithes (and gifts) which are a benefit is valid. Rabba is of the opinion that the benefit of these rights (<i>tovat hana’ah</i>) is not considered to have monetary value and thus it is invalid; whereas Ulla is of the opinion that this benefit is considered to have a monetary value and thus it is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 58a.</i>
Decisors were also in disagreement about this matter. Some authorities agreed with Ulla that this benefit is considered to have monetary value<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahaneh Ephraim, Tovat Hana’ah</i>, s.v. <i>venimzah</i>; <i>Teshuvot Na’ot Ya’akov</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2, <i>HM</i> 93.</i> and the woman is considered married. On the other hand, there were numerous authorities who decided in accordance with Rabba that this benefit has no monetary value and consequently is not a means of acquisition and therefore she is not married.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Ishut</i> 5:6 and <i>Magid Mishneh</i>, ad. loc. <i>Mordekhai, Shevuot</i> 766 in the name of Maharam; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 203:1; <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:46; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 276:4.</i>
Even if we would ascribe to the view that an accrued benefit has monetary value, in the present case, at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> the groom (the defendant), refrained from stating to the bride (the plaintiff) that she is being married via the vehicle of derivate benefit of being adorned by the wearing of his friend’s ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbeinu Yeruham, Sefer Meisharim, Netiv</i> 22, 1; <i>ha-Sabba Kadisha</i>, supra n. 28.<br>The utilization of the word of “acquisition” is to be used as denoting a man’s decision to undertake an obligation(s) upon marriage rather than viewing his prospective spouse as being acquired as chattel. See further, <i>Minhat Osher, Kiddushin</i> 1, 2, 5; <i>Minhat Osher</i>, <i>Bereshit,</i> Hayei Sarah, 26. See supra chapter 4a, n. 21.</i>
Accordingly, in pursuance to the view that the benefit of adornment does not constitute a means to acquire a woman via the act of <i>kiddushin</i> where the defendant gave his prospective wife his friend’s ring, the marriage is invalid.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. A <i>kiddushin</i> ring- a conditional gift</b></span>
After proposing the utilization of a borrowed ring for <i>kiddushin</i>, Rosh continues:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, supra n. 8.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And if using terminology of a loan will not be effective then it should be in the language of a gift, minimally conditioned upon the groom returning the gift, she will be deemed married and he will acquire it from the woman and return it to him.</span>
In accordance with the authorities who reject the validity of the act of <i>kiddushin</i> via the use of a borrowed ring, Rosh contends that there is an assumption that the lender intended to give the ring as a conditional gift. In other words, it is gifted for the <i>kiddushin</i> and afterwards the donor requests its return. Other decisors endorse Rosh’s view.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Padua 77</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1000; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:240; <i>SA OH</i> 658:5, <i>EH</i> 28:19–20; <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv,</i> Mahdura Tinyana 3:147; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:106; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed</i> 2:24.</i>
In a case where a donor gifts an item on the condition that it is returned, the item, i.e. the ring, belongs to the recipient, i.e. the groom, during the time it is in his possession, provided that he will subsequently return the gift to the donor, i.e. the groom’s friend. And the donor allows the recipient to give the gift to a third party, contingent upon the third party returning the gift to the recipient.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Nedarim</i> 7:16; <i>Bah, Tur HM</i> 207:8.</i>
In our case, it clear that the defendant’s intent was to give the ring to the plaintiff for the purpose of the <i>kiddushin</i> and that subsequently the plaintiff will return the ring to the donor, namely the defendant’s friend, or that she would return it to the recipient and he would then give it back to the donor.
As we know, due to the severity of incest (<i>arayot</i>), <i>Halakhah</i> establishes that the validity of giving a conditional gift is contingent upon the execution transpiring in accordance to the <i>halakhot</i> of conditions (<i>mishpetei ha’te’naim</i>), such as the principle that a condition, when stipulated, must be formulated as “a double condition” (a <i>tenai kaful</i>). In other words, the condition must be stated in the affirmative (if he complies with the condition, he is entitled to the arrangement) and in the negative (if he does not comply with the condition, he is not entitled to the arrangement). Moreover, the affirmative must precede the negative (<i>hen kodem le-lav)</i>. Furthermore, it is required that the condition must be stated prior to the actual transaction (<i>tenai kodem le’ma’aseh)</i>. Finally, the condition must be possible to fulfill (<i>davar she-afshar le’kayyemo)</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Ishut</i> 6:1–13; <i>Tur HM</i> 241:16 in the name of Rosh; <i>SA EH</i> 38:1–4, <i>HM</i> 241:12.</i>
In our case, it is clear that the donor and the recipient neither couched the gift in terms of the aforesaid four requirements nor did the donor stipulate that he was giving the gift for an expressed period of time or that it be returned after a prescribed time.
In the absence of implementing the aforementioned rules of conditions, there are legists who will impart validity to the arrangement of the gift in light of the circumstances which would indicate a clear expectation (<i>umdana</i>) that in fact the gift was given for a specific time and would be returned after the time had lapsed.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 93a, s.v. <i>zavin</i>; <i>Tosafot Kiddushin</i> 6b; s.v. <i>lo</i>; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 267.</i> However, there are others who mandate the application of the rules of conditions to validate the transfer of a conditional gift rather than invoking an assessed expectation.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Gittin</i> 75b; <i>Magid Mishneh, MT Ishut</i> 6:14.</i> In our case, as mentioned, the rules of conditions were not implemented. As such, the gift may not serve as a vehicle to establish <i>kiddushin.</i> Consequently, she is not married.
Another difficulty arises from another <i>halakhah</i> dealing with gifting. The recipient of the gift that executed a symbolic act of undertaking vis-à-vis an asset was unaware that the asset received was a gift; he acquires it because there is no duty that the recipient of a gift must intend to acquire it. And the reason for this <i>halakhah</i> is that the donor has the intent to transfer it to him (<i>da’at aheret makneh</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 197:4; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer OH</i> 342:2, <i>HM</i> 134; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:107–108; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:150.</i> Lest one argue that in our case, given that the defendant’s friend was ignorant concerning the <i>halakhot</i> of undertaking obligations, therefore one cannot assert <i>da’at aheret makneh.</i> There are decisors who contend that intent to transfer is determination, and a symbolic act of undertaking a duty need not be executed.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Bava Kama</i> 102b, s.v. <i>who de’amar</i>; <i>Taz SA OH</i> 434:6.</i> However, others claim that there is a requirement of executing a symbolic act of undertaking an obligation, though the defendant’s friend is incapable of fulfilling this duty.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hevel Ya’akov</i> 1:8.</i>
Moreover, in contradistinction to other authorities, upon return of the gift from the sub-recipient (the plaintiff), the recipient has to <i>halakhically</i> transfer the gift back to the donor, the defendant’s friend.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot ha-Rid, Sukkah</i> 41b; <i>Teshuvot Ri Megas</i> 42; <i>Avnei Mi’lluim</i> 28:53.</i> In the wake of the absence of any formal <i>halakhic</i> transfer, the gift, the ring, remains in the possession of the groom, the defendant.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva Kiddushin</i> 6b; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Sukkah</i> 3:30; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick OH</i> 330 in the name of Hatam Sofer.</i> On the other hand, there are decisors who do not mandate the execution of a <i>kinyan</i> upon returning a gift, due to the fact that the gifting was for a limited time or because the donor retained a residual right (known as a “<i>shiyur”</i>) in the gift, that at the end of the agreed time the gift would be returned to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 241:4; <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv,</i> Mahadura 3, 2:5. For examples of residual rights in a field and in a house respectively, see <i>SA HM</i> 209:7, 212:3.</i> In our present case, the donor, the defendant’s friend, was neither well versed in the <i>halakhot</i> of undertaking obligations in general nor in the entitlement to retain a residual right in particular, and thus there was no meeting of the minds regarding the length of time that the ring would be kept by the groom and the bride.
One might challenge our conclusion based upon the <i>halakhah</i> that a person’s hand (<i>kinyan yad</i>) can acquire an object even without his awareness.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 200:15; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 268 in the name of Tosafot; <i>Teshuvot Havatzelet ha-Sharon</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 61.</i> Hence, if the recipient took the ring into his hand, his hand would acquire it without his awareness. However, in our case the ring was returned directly from the sub-recipient, the plaintiff, to the donor, the defendant’s friend.
In short, the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> with either a borrowed ring or a conditional gift is subject to a debate among the authorities, and this <i>beit din</i> panel arrived at the conclusion that construing the <i>kiddushin</i> ring as either a conditional gift or a borrowed one is <i>halakhically</i> ineffective, and therefore the <i>kiddushin</i> of the couple is void.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>3. <i>Halakhic</i> matrimony established by an exclusive conjugal relationship</b></span>
In the wake of the <i>halakhic</i> doubts regarding the transfer of the ring (<i>kiddushei kesef</i>) as a vehicle to establish the act of <i>kiddushin</i>, should we fear that marriage has been created via intercourse by living together (<i>kiddushei bi’ah</i>), based upon the Talmudic presumption that a man does not engage in sexual relations promiscuously (the <i>hazakah</i> of “<i>ein adam oseh be’ilato be’ilat zenut</i>”)?<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 81b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 72b, 74a; <i>Yevamot</i> 110a.</i>
First, there is the view represented by Rabbi Yosef Rozanne and Rabbi Yosef Henkin that even if the parties did not intend to marry according to the prescribed form of <i>kiddushin</i>, nonetheless the above presumption applies equally to non-observant couples who engage in an exclusive conjugal relationship.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tzofnat Paneah</i>, (Dvinsk) 1–4; <i>Perushei Ivra</i> 1:4.</i> Though numerous decisors reject this posture, authorities such as Rabbi Moshe Feinstein factor this minority view into consideration and mandate a Jewish writ of divorce as a stringency (a <i>get le’humra</i>) due to the presumption of the woman being a married woman (<i>eishet ish</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 2:19. Clearly, in a case of <i>igun</i>, we should not invoke a writ of Jewish divorce for stringency if there is basis to free a woman without a <i>get.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev EH</i> 25; <i>Teshuvot Moshe ha-Ish EH</i> 6 in the name of Ma’sat Binyamin.</i> Clearly, in our case, we are dealing with a couple who wanted to, and did, live together for years in an exclusive conjugal relationship and the above-named two rabbis would have viewed them as <i>halakhically</i> married based upon this presumption. As such, a <i>get</i> would have been mandated.
An additional reason for mandating a <i>get</i> is that if a wife is released from matrimonial bonds without a <i>get,</i> we are concerned that the community will say she is leaving a marriage without a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24.</i>
Finally, even though at Mount Sinai, Jews were obligated to perform certain <i>halakhot</i> dealing with <i>kiddushin</i>, nonetheless, they still remained dutybound by the <i>halakhot</i> of marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In other words, the marital relationship between non-Jews is identified as a marriage (the undertaking of mutual duties and trust) rather than <i>kiddushin</i> with all its attendant <i>halakhot</i>. See <i>Talmud Yerushalmi, Kiddushin</i> 1:1; <i>Rashi, Sanhedrin</i> 52b, s.v. <i>le’eshet aherim</i>.</i> and divorce (albeit different <i>halakhot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ridvaz</i>, infra n. 54; <i>Lev Shomeia le-Shlomo</i> 1:11.</i>) mandated to the non-Jewish community.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Melakhim</i> 9:8; <i>Ridvaz</i>, ad locum; <i>Yad Ramah</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 52b. See further <i>Teshuvot Tzofnat Paneach,</i> Warsaw collection, 26–27; <i>Teshuvot Tzofnat Paneah</i>, Dvinsk collection, 1–4; <i>Perushei Ivrei</i> 1:4; <i>Lev Ibra</i> 3; File no. 4276/03, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, November 11, 2003 (Rabbi Daichovsky’s opinion).</i>
Without addressing the <i>halakhic</i> history of this marriage in general and the improper <i>kiddushin</i> in particular, the common denominator of these three reasons for mandating a <i>get</i> is, in effect, that living together within the context of an exclusive conjugal relationship is to be construed either as a recognition of the establishment of <i>kiddushei</i> via intercourse or a marriage as mandated by <i>Halakhah</i> to the non-Jewish community (<i>bnei Noah</i>).
As we will show, this posture reflects the opinions of a minority of decisors (<i>me’ut ha-Poskim</i>), and protects the institution of the Jewish family. Consequently, it is unsurprising to encounter the following posture of Rabbi Aharon Walkin, an early twentieth century arbiter. A <i>sine qua non</i> to create the act of <i>kiddushin</i> (<i>ma’aseh kiddushin</i>) is the presence of two adult Jewish males who are Torah observant, not blood relatives to either the groom or the bride, who witness the man’s recitation of the <i>kiddushin</i> formula, “<i>harei at mekudeshet</i> . . .,” observe the man transferring the ring to the woman and observe her acceptance of the ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA, EH</i> 42:2, 5.</i> Addressing the case of an <i>agunah</i> who requests that her marriage be voided due to the fact that both witnesses during the <i>kiddushin</i> were disqualified since they violated the <i>Shabbat</i> and committed other sins, Rabbi Walkin, who resided in Pinsk-Karlin, Ukraine, responds to such a petition in the most trenchant terms:<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 1:81.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is improper in my eyes to adopt this path, to criticize the act of <i>kiddushin</i> eight years after the wedding . . . you must understand that such advice can destroy the purity of Israel . . . to multiply <i>halakhic</i> bastards in the midst of a holy nation, as the marital bond from now will not be sustained . . . And the stringency of being married will be nullified. . . . Therefore, eight years after the wedding . . . we should not inquire for guidance to void the marriage.</span>
Both Rabbi Walkin’s opposition to voiding a marriage as well as the above legists who would obligate a <i>get</i> in circumstances that there is exclusive conjugal relationship in lieu of a proper <i>kiddushin</i> under the <i>hupah</i> are concerned about the stringency of a married woman and “the purity of Israel.”
Even though many decisors claim that traditionally, all strict views must be considered in matters concerning forbidden sexual relations,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 17:31; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 17:61; <i>Mahatzit ha-Shekel, SA EH</i> 17:56; <i>Teshuvot Kedushat Yom Tov</i> 9 in the name of Rabbi Yom Tov Algazi; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan, EH</i> 42:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Rahamim Franco, EH</i> 19; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yitzhak</i> 1:10, 13. Cf. <i>Taz, SA EH</i> 17:15.</i> nevertheless, in a matter of a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance, many hold that the lenient view ought to be adopted.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 51:2, <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehudah</i> 92; <i>Teshuvot Maharik,</i> shoresh 121; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:135; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 103; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah EH</i> Mahadura Kama 29, 57; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:110; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 8 (19).</i> Given that in our circumstances we are dealing with a woman who has been an <i>agunah</i> for over seven years, we have adopted the lenient approach, and will address the reasons for rejecting the validation of the couple’s marriage based upon consummation of <i>kiddushin</i> via intercourse and the marital obligations of the non-Jewish community.
Regarding applying the laws of marriage of non-Jews to Jews, most authorities reject this posture.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Dvar Avraham</i> 3:29; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov EH</i> 74; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 10.</i>
Concerning the argument that <i>kiddushin</i> via intercourse should be effective, as Rabbi Uzziel aptly notes, if a groom assumes that the <i>kiddushin</i> was executed properly and he was unaware that there was an impropriety (such as ineligible witnesses or the use of a stolen <i>kiddushin</i> ring), why would he have intercourse for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i> when his intercourse is reliant upon the fact that the first <i>kiddushin</i> were valid!<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel EH</i> 57. See <i>Ketuvot</i> 73a (a man knows that <i>kiddushin</i> is ineffective with an item valued less than a <i>perutah</i> and had intercourse for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i>). In other words, he was betrothed to her on the understanding that the first <i>kiddushin</i> under the marital canopy was effective rather than consummating the betrothal via intercourse. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 5–6; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 26:1; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 26:9; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 64; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:76, 5:11; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>EH</i> 6, 10 <i>EH</i> 15.</i>
A similar line of reasoning is advanced by Rabbi Shalom Schwadron:<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:111.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If we would be concerned that they lived together like husband and wife publicly, known by the world, it is similar to Rivash’s dealing with marriage effected in civil court that fail to follow the religion of Moshe and Israel, that one is unconcerned that they lived together for a few years, as this was done with the intent of relying upon the first marriage (i.e. civil court – AYW) rather than for the sake of additional <i>kiddushin</i>.</span>
Finally, the application of this principle based on the presumption that a man does not engage in sexual relations promiscuously is inapplicable in recent generations, since we do not establish a <i>halakhic</i> marriage via intercourse. As Sha’agat Aryeh observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 42 in the name of Sha’agat Aryeh. See also, <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 209; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:58; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak EH</i> 29 in the name of Rabbi Kutnah; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:29; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i> 3:3; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6<i>, EH</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 4, <i>EH</i> 2 (2).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It seems to me that the logic is that the presumption that a man does not engage in sexual relations promiscuously, and for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i> he engaged in intercourse, applies only to the early generations, that they were accustomed sometimes to marry via intercourse, which was impossible without witnesses who attested to the seclusion of the couple, and this <i>halakhah</i> was clear and known by all. However, in our times and in these countries, there is no practice to marry through intercourse and this <i>halakhah</i> is only known by Torah scholars who are proficient in the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>kiddushin</i>. In contrast, this particular man, one can expect to find that he does not know and is not versed in <i>hilkhot</i> (Jewish laws – AYW) <i>kiddushin</i> that we would say that he had intercourse for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i> and witnesses to the seclusion are not proficient at all. Would that, I wish that the majority of the arbiters in our times knew <i>hilkhot kiddushin</i>. . . . Therefore, in our case we do not fear that <i>kiddushin</i> transpired.</span>
We will conclude with the words of Rabbi Yitzhak Taib:<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Erekh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 34:10.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">There is nobody who believes that the <i>kiddushin</i> are proper when a man engages in intercourse for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i> except for the Rashbatz (3:47), and he stands in opposition to the Talmud and the authorities.</span>
<i>A fortiori</i> in our present case, where the couple clearly did not know of the concept of establishing <i>kiddushin</i> via intercourse, that we cannot say that their intent in living together was for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i>.
As we mentioned, the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i> with either a borrowed ring or a conditional gift is a subject of debate among the authorities, and this <i>beit din</i> panel arrived at the conclusion that construing the <i>kiddushin</i> ring as a conditional gift or as borrowed is <i>halakhically</i> ineffective, and therefore the <i>kiddushin</i> of the couple is void.
Alternatively, there are grounds to void the marriage based upon invoking a double doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be (<i>sefek sefeika de’dina</i>) in a biblical prohibition including a matter of personal status (<i>ishut</i>). Based upon the foregoing, various doubts have emerged regarding the act of <i>kiddushin</i>. The first uncertainty is if a borrowed ring may be used to establish the <i>kiddushin,</i> and as we have demonstrated, this is subject to <i>halakhic</i> debate. The second doubt is whether one can use a ring for <i>kiddushin</i> which is a conditional gift, and as we have shown, this is equally a matter of <i>halakhic</i> controversy. When the creation of the <i>kiddushin</i> is viewed as a doubtful marriage, there are numerous early authorities as well as other decisors who argue that we reinstate the original presumption of the wife, namely that she has the presumption (<i>hazakah</i>) of being a single woman, and she is permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i>.
As such, we are dealing with a series of <i>halakhic</i> doubts (<i>sefeik sefeika de’dina</i>). As we know, if there exists a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt in regard to an act of <i>kiddushin</i>, we may permit her to remarry without a <i>get</i> in a case of a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak,</i> 1 <i>EH</i> 24 (48) (release from a levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 22 (18), 62; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 1:68; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 2:51; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 130, <i>EH</i> 59; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 21, 33, 48, 62, 64; <i>Teshuvot Ba’ei Hayyai, YD</i> 216; <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 5, 8, 35, 48; <i>Teshuvot Perah Shushan EH</i> 3:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:29, 31; <i>Get Pashut Gittin</i> 129:13; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Pesakim 37, Mahadura Tinyana 45; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo EH</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 111; <i>Kereti u-Peleti</i>, <i>YD</i> 110; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>YD</i> 110:5, 16; <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev</i> 12; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Minhat Moshe EH</i> 11; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 65, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 22–23 in the name of a dozen decisors; M. Yerushalamski, <i>Teshuvot Be’er Moshe</i>, Kuntres Binyan Yerushalyim 18; <i>Teshuvot Nediv Lev EH</i> 8; A. Makovsky, <i>Teshuvot Ohalei Aharon</i> 1:19; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19 (release from levirate marriage-<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim EH</i> 3:11 (end); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 18, 6 <i>EH</i> 3 (8), 6, 8; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 6; S. Messas, “A doubt in coercion of a <i>Get</i>,” (Hebrew) 23 <i>Tehumin</i> 120, 123–124 (5763); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:73; <i>PDR</i> 21:10; File no. 1126792/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, 11 Tishrei 5778; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rishon le-Tzion</i> 2:8 For additional authorities who endorse this posture, see numerous responsa, Ashkenazic and Sephardic alike cited in <i>Sefer Rav Berakhot</i>, 126; <i>Mishpat ha-Get</i>, vol. 3, 495–499; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 3 (14), 6, <i>EH</i> 3 (9, 14–15), 6 (5) and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 159, n. 47. See the addendum.<br>For the <i>halakhic</i> underpinnings of invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt to void a marriage, see supra chapter 2.<br>In the wake of these various doubts regarding the efficacy of the <i>kiddushin</i>, lest one contend the fact that they lived numerous years together as husband and wife should be sufficient justification that the act of <i>kiddushin</i> was established by intercourse (<i>bi’ah</i>); concerned with the promotion of licentiousness, most arbiters disagree. See <i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur EH</i> 31:2–3 in the name of Mordekhai; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 49; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 31:9; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:29. For another rationale for invalidating intercourse as a vehicle to establish <i>kiddushin,</i> see also supra text accompanying n. 62. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Rashi,</i> Elfenbein ed., 198; File no. 94289/8, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, February 3, 2011.<br>Cf. the position of those few authorities (<i>me’ut haposkim</i>) such as Rabbi S. Steinberg, <i>Ohr ha-Ner</i>, end of Tractate Gittin in the name of Hazon Ish, Rabbi Y. Rozanne, <i>Teshuvot Tzofnat Paneach</i> 1:1–4 (Dvinsk ed.) and <i>Tzofnat Paneach,</i> 26–27 (Warsaw ed.), Rabbi Y. Henkin, <i>Perushei Ibra</i> 1:4, Rabbi M. Ratta, <i>Teshuvot Kol Mevaser</i> 1:7, 2:18 and Rabbi A. Walkin, <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 2:10 who mandate the execution of a <i>get</i> due to stringent considerations (a <i>get le-humra</i>) in a situation of a civil marriage where the parties are establishing ties of conjugal exclusivity with each other for the purpose of establishing a family (rather than a relationship of philandering – <i>zenut</i>), albeit in variance with <i>halakhic</i> requirements would agree in our case dealing with a <i>halakhically</i> improper <i>kiddushin</i> that a <i>get</i> ought to be given.<br>In fact, addressing the case of a common law marriage where the parties agree between themselves to be married, present themselves to the community as husband and wife and live together for a significant period of time, concludes Rabbi A. Sherman should the relationship be terminated, a <i>get</i> as a stringency ought to be executed. The rationale for his judgment is based upon the need to execute such a <i>get</i> should a civil marriage be dissolved. See File no. 448866/3, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Rabbinical Court, <i>ha-Din ve-ha-Dayan</i> 35, February 2014; <i>Teshuvot Kol Mevasair</i> 2:18 and many other Israeli Rabbinical Court rulings. (A special <i>get</i> ought to be required in such a situation. See <i>Tzofnat Paneach</i> 1:1–4) <br>In other words, in his mind there should be no difference between a common law marriage and a civil marriage in so far as the seriousness of the couple’s engagement in conjugal ties exclusively with each other. As such, in the wake of dissolution of the common law marriage, a <i>get</i> for stringent reasons ought to be executed. See File no. 7247/67, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, 16 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 89 (Rabbi A. Sherman’s opinion). As such, the woman has the <i>halakhic</i> status of a divorcee (a <i>gerusha</i>) and therefore she is prohibited to marry a <i>kohen</i>. See <i>Rema SA EH</i> 6:1; <i>Taz, SA EH</i> 6:2. (Adopting such a posture of a <i>get</i> being given for stringent reasons regarding a common law marriage and civil marriage is problematic in light of the numerous decisors who argue that dissolution of a common law marriage does not require of the man to give a <i>get</i> to his partner. See <i>MT</i>, <i>Gerushin</i> 10:19; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 32:1; <i>SA EH</i> 26:1, 149:5; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 209; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 13:6; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 47; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 121–122; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 2:33; Rabbi G. Felder, <i>Nahalat Tzvi</i> 2:293–294.)<br>Consequently, such a posture ought to be equally adopted in our case where we are dealing with a couple who chose to engage in conjugal ties exclusively with each other and were interested in establishing a Jewish family rather than unintentionally opting out of matrimonial ties grounded in executing an act of <i>kiddushin</i>. See <i>MT</i>, Ishut 1:1–2.<br>In the wake of the absence of eligible witnesses under the wedding canopy, argues Rabbi Osher Weiss that the <i>kiddushin</i> may be validated based upon the notion that “the admission of the litigant of his own liability (<i>ho’do’at ba’al din</i>) has the power of one hundred witnesses”, a concept applicable to a matter of personal status. See <i>Kiddushin</i> 65b. Namely, the acknowledgment by the husband that in fact the <i>kiddushin</i> transpired. See <i>Minhat Osher, Bereshit</i> 101, Yerushalayim, Sivan 5779. Rabbi Weiss’s approach requires further deliberation.<br>In short, based upon the foregoing view, despite the fact that in our case the <i>kiddushin</i> was invalid due to the absence of eligible witnesses, nevertheless, they will be considered married in the eyes of some decisors<i>.</i> <br>Said conclusion would be equally applicable in a case where a couple married in accordance to <i>Halakhah</i>, divorced and subsequently decided to rent an apartment and lived together. Should such a couple ever separate, a <i>get</i> for stringency would be mandated. See File no. 1042070/1, Tel Aviv- Yaffo Regional Beit Din, May 8, 2017 in the name of Rashba and Ohr Sameah.</i>
Therefore, the plaintiff is permitted to remarry any Jew, including a <i>Kohen</i>. In short, in the wake of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, we deem the matter as “an hour of emergency” (<i>sh’at ha-dehak</i>) and a <i>priori</i> (<i>le-hatehillah</i>) we can void the marriage based upon a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Ma’arkekhet Get 30(6) and ha-Sameah 30(3) in the name of Rashba and Ridvaz.</i>

d) A husband who does not engage in conjugal relations ("onah")

<b>D. A husband who does not engage in conjugal relations (“<i>onah</i>”)</b>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Facts</b></span>
In April 2010, Sara Cohen (the plaintiff) and Avraham Cohen (hereinafter: the defendant) were married in accordance with <i>Halakhah</i>. During the course of their marriage, a son was born. The couple separated in the summer of 2014. We summoned the defendant to appear at the <i>Beit Din</i> in order to address the matter of the <i>get</i>. However, he refused to appear for a hearing. In July 2017, we ordered the defendant to give the plaintiff a <i>get</i>. To this day, he refuses to do so. The issues of maintenance and custody of the child have not yet been resolved, nor has there been a civil divorce.
Let us discuss whether there are grounds for releasing the plaintiff from the chains of a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>) by invalidating the <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated: the marriage).
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Discussion</b></span>
One of the reasons that the plaintiff left her husband was the absence of “intimacy” between the couple during the act of intercourse. The plaintiff claims that when the defendant had intercourse with her, he refrained from talking to her or from demonstrating any affection such as embracing or kissing (in <i>halakhic</i> parlance such conduct is known as “<i>kiruv basar</i>”). In the eyes of the husband, the duty (the <i>mitzvah</i>) of marital relations (“<i>onah</i>”) was fulfilled by the physical act alone. The defendant had intercourse with the plaintiff once or twice a month. Outside of the bedroom, the defendant almost never embraced or kissed his spouse.
One of the <i>halakhic</i> obligations of a husband towards his wife is the wife’s right to conjugal relations.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shemot</i> 21:10.</i> Although the source of the husband’s obligation is in the duty of intimate relations, Rashba opines:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Nedarim</i> 15b, s.v. <i>ve-amar rav kahana</i>. See also, <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak, EH</i> 1:32 in the name of Rosh, File no. 113995/3, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, January 4, 2018.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It would appear that it is not merely a matter of performing a religious duty (<i>mitzvah</i> -AYW); there is also an element of mutual commitment to sexual gratification, which is implicit in their agreement to marry.</span>
In other words, in addition to this obligation, the husband has a commitment vis-à-vis his wife, as pointed out: “The sexual aspect of marriage goes to its essence and therefore the obligation is a very strict one.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shitah Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 63a, s.v. <i>beshlamah</i>.</i>
The scope of the obligation to engage in marital relations varies in accordance with the husband’s physical ability and the requirements of his daily work.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 76:1–2; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum 5.</i> In view of this defendant’s occupation, his obligation is to have intercourse with his wife twice a week, provided his wife is not a menstruant.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 76:2.</i>
As Talmud states:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 48a.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A husband who said: I will not (perform conjugal relations) unless she wears her clothes and I mine, must divorce her and also give her her <i>ketubah</i>.</span>
Subsequently, this ruling is crystallized in Shulhan Arukh,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 76:13.</i> accompanied by the identical Talmudic mandate by Rema for the wife, that she cannot say I am unable to have intercourse unless I wear clothes and he wears clothes.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 76:13. For the wife’s duty to engage in conjugal relations accompanied by caressing and kissing, see <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 4:76.</i> Such behavior will equally result in her being divorced without receiving the value of her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid.</i>
As Ritva teaches us, such conduct demonstrates the absence of love, and as other decisors note, such behavior engenders pain for either spouse and/or is deemed to be rebellious (a <i>moreid</i>/<i>moreidet</i>) due to such inappropriate conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i> 48a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Hadashot</i> 176; <i>Bah, Tur EH</i> 76; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 76:20; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum, 19; <i>Hazon Ish Ketuvot</i> 69:20; <i>Piskei Halakhot Yad Dovid, Ishut</i> 14:26.</i>
In the present case, the defendant would have intercourse with his wife twice a month, and even then, it was unsatisfactory. In other words, plaintiff’s description of the defendant’s behavior during intercourse was that even when she was aroused and there was penetration, it would end extremely quickly. To state it differently, on the one hand, there was an actual act of intercourse and not only first stage coitus (insertion of the corona of the male organ into the vagina). It is well-known that this duty cannot be fulfilled by first-stage coitus alone<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sha’ar ha-Melekh</i>, <i>Issurei Bi’ah</i> chap. 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:48.</i> and in the present case, the defendant completed the act of intercourse with the first stage of coitus, but on the other hand, after penetration, the act of intercourse was over very abruptly.
Rabbi David ibn Zimra (known by the acronym: Ridvaz) provides an apt description of a situation, similar to ours:<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:118.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It makes sense to argue that since the husband is not fulfilling the duty, he is obliged to divorce her and pay her (the value of – AYW) her <i>ketubah</i>. This applies even when the wife’s claim is that there is erection and penetration, but immediately upon penetration the erection fails and the organ becomes flaccid: she is believed, according to this opinion. The reason is that if she is lying, then her husband will immediately refute her, and the rule is that in relation to an immediately-refutable claim, a person is not suspected of lying. Now, it is arguable that she should not be believed because she is not under any obligation to procreate. This argument, however, is incorrect, since a woman is entitled to request “a staff [a child] to lean upon in her old age and someone to use a spade to dig a grave.” In any case, even if there is no such duty, she would still be believed because the husband is in breach of this obligation, and therefore her pain is especially great. Even though, in <i>halakhic</i> terms, penetration is treated as intercourse in many areas of <i>Halakhah</i>, it is not sufficient for the purpose of fulfilling the duty . . . because it is entirely lacking in affection. Therefore, even if penetration is considered to be intercourse for the purpose of fulfilling the duty, in this case, the element of affection in performing conjugal relations is absent. . . . The woman in this case is not claiming that her husband is impotent, but merely that she is unable to enjoy intercourse as it is normally thought of with her husband, and this is the gravity of her claim.</span>
As ruled by an Israeli rabbinical court, such conduct exhibited on the part of a husband towards his wife constitutes grounds for divorce. The <i>Beit Din</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 12:96, 100–101.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">When he was aroused there was penetration, but it would be over abruptly, and clearly this cannot be called “impotence.” (It is indeed possible that in cases in which “it is over abruptly” and she has no sexual gratification, there is a ground to obligate him to give a <i>get</i>, as appears in Tosafot Yevamot 65a, s.v. she’beino: “Because she derives little pleasure from intercourse, similar to the case of a man who does not ejaculate with sufficient force,” and further investigation is required.</span>
In short, fulfillment of the duty of engaging in intimate relations is not limited to the act of intercourse, but it includes the sufficient duration of the act in order that a wife derives sexual gratification.
The Talmud and the early authorities (<i>Rishonim</i>) note the importance of sexual gratification. For example, Rashi writes as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 61b.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Mishnah: One who takes a vow prohibiting his wife to have marital relations – for example, he said that he would be forbidden to enjoy the marital relations with her [he is within his rights], but she was not forbidden to enjoy the marital relations with him [as this is not within his rights], for he is committed to her as is written, “He shall not withhold her sexual intercourse” . . .</span>
The early legists by and large agree with Rashi, and in our context, Rashba’s words are instructive:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Nedarim</i> 15b. Cf. <i>MT, Nedarim</i> 12:9 who argues that a husband’s commitment (<i>shi’abud</i>) is derived from the Biblical commandment to engage in conjugal relations and the wife’s commitment to engage in intimacy is based upon the essence of marriage.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And the same applies to one who says to his wife: I take an oath that you will derive no sexual pleasure from me, namely, we force him to engage in full sexual intercourse with her, since he is duty-bound to provide her with sexual gratification, and this oath is in effect depriving her of that which is legally hers. The point is not only that the <i>mitzvah</i> is not being fulfilled, but that he is in breach of a mutually-undertaken obligation at the time of marriage. In this light it is clear that the objection raised by the Talmud is not framed in terms of the duty of intimacy, in the same fashion that if a man takes an oath not to have any benefit from a tabernacle (a <i>sukkah</i>), the oath is critiqued on the grounds that residing in a tabernacle entails the performance of a duty, and the rule is that commandments are not defined according to one’s enjoyment of them. In this case, however, the commandment itself is not as relevant as the mutual commitment to sexual gratification.</span>
In other words, a woman gets married with the intention of finding sexual gratification. The commandment and mutual commitment are not limited to the technical aspect of marital relations, but include the duty to induce the wife’s pleasure from their relationship of intimacy.
Implicitly relying upon earlier authorities, Rabbi Moshe Feinstein states:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 4:66. For earlier decisors, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 154:9; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113.</i> “Expressions of affection such as embracing and kissing are also part of the obligation of sexual intercourse.” In the wake of the absence of proper conjugal relations, in the mind of Rabbi Feinstein, the Talmudic presumption,“it is better off to be married to anyone rather than be single” (<i>tav le-metav tan du me-le-meitav armelu</i>), which sometimes appears in decisors’ writings as “she is satisfied with anyone” (<i>niha lah be-khol de-hu</i>) is inapplicable.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:79, 3:48, 4:52, 113. For the interchangeability of these two formulations, see <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:3; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 15:34.<br>For other arbiters who concur that the presumption is applicable only when intimacy is possible or where there exists kissing and embracing between the couple, see <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 1:24 (31, 34); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:101.<br>Whether the presumption is applicable in a situation where it is prohibited to live intimately with a married man (e.g. living with an apostate Jew) is subject to controversy. See <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague edition, 1022, Bloch edition, 4:564; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 1:179; <i>Teshuvot Be’air Yitzhak EH</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot ha-Re’em</i> 68; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 2:20, <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27; <i>Iggerot Moshe, EH</i> 1:79, 3:48, and <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, Bayit 9, Heder 6, 11.<br>However, should the wife be lax in Torah observance, the presumption may be applicable to a husband who became an apostate. See <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov EH</i> 85; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 7:122; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:83</i>
Rabbi Ya’akov Yisrael Kanievsky (known as “the Steipler”) elucidates:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggeret ha-Kodesh</i> 3:2, Bnei Brak, 5746.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If the husband performs the duty [of <i>onah</i>] abruptly without intimate contact with his wife, if he withdraws just as soon as the act is over and keeps his distance from her, he may think that he has thereby ascended to a high [spiritual] level. . . . His wife has experienced no pleasure at all. On the contrary, she is distressed and humiliated. . . .</span>
Rabbi Yitzhak Shor, head of the Slobodka Yeshiva in Lithuania, teaches us in a similar vein:<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kedushat Yisrael</i>, 23.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is forbidden for the husband to withdraw from his wife in the middle of sexual intercourse, since this causes her great distress and constitutes a great torment. The talmudic rule in relation to a woman who is subject to coercive sexual relations is that initially the intercourse is unwanted, however, by the end, it is desirable. Nevertheless, the entire act is considered coercive, since it was forced upon her, and it is her nature that makes it ultimately desirable. How much more does this apply where the intercourse begins voluntarily, but her husband ceases abruptly and causes her distress after having brought her to a state of arousal? Clearly, had she known at the outset that this would be the result; she would never have consented to the intercourse in the first place.</span>
In a somewhat different context, Rabbi Kolitz notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll">“Even if he is erect, but he finishes abruptly, this does not constitute the fulfillment of the duty of conjugal relations; on the contrary, she is more distressed.”<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 12:103, 115–116.</i></span>
As opposed to those who think that such a husband is acting with piety, Ra’avad writes that ascetic conduct by the husband requires the consent of the wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ba’alei Nefesh, Sha’ar Kedushah.</i></i> In the present case, it is clear that the defendant did not receive the plaintiff’s consent to withhold his affection from her.
Rabbi Yehezkel Abramsky observes that “the whole essence of marriage is for the purpose of marital relations,” and “all are aware why a bride is getting married:”<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Yehezkel</i> on <i>Tractate Zevahim</i> and <i>Likkutim</i> on the Talmud <i>EH</i> 8, 278. Similarly, others argues that the performance of conjugal relations is the primary purpose of marriage. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Bava Batra</i> 126b, s.v. <i>harei zu mekudeshet</i>; <i>Shitah Mekubetzet, Ketuvot</i> 70a in the name of Ra’ah; <i>Bnei Ahuvah, Ishut</i> 17:2. As noted by various arbiters, the performance of the duty of intimacy is grounded in <i>halakhic</i> logic (<i>sevara</i>). See <i>Birkat ha-Netziv, Mekhilta de R. Yishmael</i> 3 ed. Horowitz- Rabin, 258–259; <i>Teshuvot Shevet ha-Levi</i> 5: Kuntres ha-Mitzvot, 23.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">“All are aware” and nobody needs to explain (Rashi, Shabbat 33a), and a marriage that is devoid of marital relations is undoubtedly a mistaken marriage, since she did give herself over to marriage with this in mind.</span>
In the present case, fulfillment of this duty was not realized and she had no sexual gratification, but he also caused his wife distress due to his limited involvement both in terms of frequency and in terms of the scope of his performance in the duty of intimate relations.
The significance of this determination is that suppression of sexual desire after it is aroused entails suffering for the person whose desire is suppressed. It is true that the modes of sexual arousal and their expression differ between men and women and differ between one woman and another one, but it is clear that when either a man or a woman is at the stage following physical contact and desire has already been aroused, termination of the act and suppression of the desire by the one partner constitute suffering for the other partner.
The question arises as to whether engaging in the act of intercourse in the absence of affection in the bedroom, as in the present case, is a consideration in determining the <i>Halakhah</i>.
We will cite two responsa in order to demonstrate the <i>halakhic</i> importance of demonstrating affection in performing this obligation. One example is found in a ruling of Rabbi Ya’akov Reischer, a renowned 18th century legist<i>,</i> whereby it was known that the husband was impotent prior to the <i>kiddushin</i> but the wife had no knowledge of this fact, and she claimed that her <i>kiddushin</i> were invalid due to being a mistaken <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For an analysis of this technique to void a marriage, see further this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 134–157, 294–327, vol. 4, 176–298.</i> According to Rabbi Reischer, she is not to be released without a <i>get</i>. The reason for this conclusion, according to the respondent, is that “although he is not capable of having intercourse, nevertheless she is satisfied with physical contact and embracing and kissing, and ‘a woman prefers one <i>kav</i> of food and physical intimacy”.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:101.</i>
Rabbi Reischer cites the Talmudic maxim that “a woman is satisfied with a small measure,” which is an expression equivalent to the maxim that “it is better to live as two together than to be alone.”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 118b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 75a; <i>Kiddushin</i> 7a, 41a; <i>Bava Kama</i> 110b–111a. See supra n. 17.</i> In other words, although there is no possibility of bringing children into the world due to the husband’s impotence, the wife wishes to stay with her husband on condition that the normal trappings of intimacy are maintained, i.e. “physical contact and embracing and kissing.” Therefore, in his opinion, there are no grounds for accepting the wife’s claim and for voiding the <i>kiddushin</i>.
The following incident occurred in the twentieth century. These are the facts, as described by Rabbi Yitzhak Weiss:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 7:122.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A man married a woman in accordance with the <i>Halakhah.</i> Several months later, the woman turned to the <i>beit din</i> and claimed that her husband was impotent and that she could not and did not wish to live with him any longer. After she was examined by a doctor, and it was discovered that she was still a virgin and had never had sexual intercourse, she received a civil divorce . . . the judgment stated that the marriage was voided . . . She subsequently asked her husband for a <i>get</i>. The son’s parents convinced him not to give her a <i>get</i> unless she gave him three hundred pounds sterling. The woman was poor and could not agree to this. They separated and the woman married another man in a civil ceremony . . . she had two boys and a girl with him. The woman has now come to the <i>beit din</i> . . . and begs us to remove the taint of <i>mamzerut</i> (<i>halakhic</i> bastardy- AYW) from her children . . .</span>
The woman’s argument is that the <i>kiddushin</i> ought to be voided on grounds of mistaken transaction, since she did not know prior to the marriage that her husband was impotent. Rabbi Weiss’s response was that a claim of mistaken transaction can be raised on condition that when the true situation was discovered (i.e., awareness of the husband’s impotence), she was ready to leave him. However, if she complains “about the situation” but does not leave her husband after a certain time, the <i>kiddushin</i> are unassailable. As Rabbi Weiss states:
<span class="poetry indentAll">In the present case, when she was in intimate contact with him for approximately four years, in the knowledge always that he was incapable of coitus, one must say that she accepted the situation, due to [the maxim] “it is better to live together . . .”</span>
In other words, due to the fact that there was intimacy (i.e. caressing and kissing) albeit only through “physical contact,” in addition to other reasons, the respondent refused to remove the taint of <i>halakhic</i> bastardy (<i>mamzerut</i>) from the children.
From the above it emerges that according to both Rabbi Reischer and Rabbi Weiss, intimacy through “physical contact” constitutes a consideration for refraining from invalidating <i>kiddushin</i> and removing the taint of <i>halakhic</i> bastardy.
Above we have seen two examples in which intimacy (i.e., physical contact) leads to the conclusion according to two authorities that the marriage is <i>valid</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For a third example, see <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24.</i>
As opposed to the above, there are several decisors who contend that if there is no sexual intercourse, the presumption that “it is better to live together . . .” is overruled, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are invalidated.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ein Yitzhak</i>, supra n. 27 (<i>halitzah</i> case); <i>Teshuvot Beit Av</i>, 7, 28:3; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 7; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, <i>EH</i> 1:79. See supra n. 17.</i>
The common denominator of all the above <i>halakhic</i> sources is the significant weight and importance attributed towards the promotion of intimacy in marital relations. In the present case, there was virtually no intimacy.
Rabbi Kanievsky teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggeret ha-Kodesh</i> 3:2. See also <i>Iggeret Hatanim</i>.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">With regard to the element of mating in the commandment of marital intercourse, we find in the Siddur Ya’avetz in the section dealing with the <i>halakhot</i> of Friday night – and the relevant passage ought to be read with great care – that close union between a man and wife is a <i>halakhically</i> binding obligation. Indeed, according to Torah law, it is forbidden to engage in sexual intercourse in such a way that the wife has no emotional gratification and therefore the husband is required to please her by the intimacy of embracing and kissing so that she is brought to a state of desire. . . .</span>
As Rabbi Shor expounds:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kedushat Yisrael</i>, 6.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">One who performs intercourse without a significant measure of desire transgresses the prohibition against depriving one’s wife of sexual gratification. Note the following in Ramban’s Commentary on the Torah: “Food, raiment and sexual gratification – this [last] means physical intimacy and the raiment refers to the bed covering, and food to the arousal of passion. According to the Torah, none of these are to be withheld, etc.” In other words, the food is in fact the husband’s flesh: see there. Here it is made quite clear that just as it is forbidden to abstain from the sex act, it is forbidden to abstain from loving physical intimacy, for the wife desires the closeness of her husband’s body, and this is above and beyond the actual act of intercourse. The husband is therefore commanded to satisfy his wife’s physical yearning: see Talmud Yevamot 62 and Pesahim 72, where it explains that the term sexual intercourse (<i>onah</i>) refers to physical desire only, and this may occur even if she is not in the fertile phase of her cycle.</span>
Hence, we see that there is a prohibition against withholding desire in general and the absence of physical contact in particular, in the intimate relations between a husband and his wife.
Moreover, Rabbi Hayyim Sofer furnishes an incisive example in our context – an example that has significant <i>halakhic</i> consequences. In clear and trenchant terms, Rabbi Sofer elucidates:<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahaneh Hayyim</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 41. See also the words of <i>Bah, Tur YD</i> 184, <i>SA YD</i> 184:2; <i>Rema SA YD</i> 184:10, <i>Shakh</i>, ad. locum. 27; <i>Be’air Hetev</i>, ad. locum. 14; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 76:18; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 76:20; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, Ketuvot 69:20; <i>Hilkhot Yad Dovid</i>, Ishut 14:26 and File no. 1004627/1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, December 7, 2016 where one may infer that various types of embracing and acts of love are components of the <i>mitzvah</i> of intimacy (<i>onah</i>).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In my humble opinion, in understanding the words of the Talmud Yerushalmi (Ketuvot, Perek af al pi - A.Y.W.), that the mutual commitment of a husband and wife in the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>onah</i> lies in two matters: (a) the Torah understood human nature, that marital relations are made pleasurable by lying in close physical contact, by embracing and kissing . . . such pleasures are part of intimacy and . . . (b) the essence of intercourse . . . that each husband has an obligation to have sexual intercourse with his wife which involves actual coitus, even if she is pregnant and even if he has already fulfilled the duty of procreation, since this entails physical pleasure, and there is a duty to complete the act of intercourse in a loving fashion, in accordance with the husband’s individual obligation . . .<br>And if a person does not fulfill either of these two obligations, he is considered to be rebellious, both if he desires to have intercourse but does not wish to have intimate contact or if he wishes to be intimate through embracing and stroking but does not desire actual intercourse.</span>
In other words, a husband who refrains from having intercourse with his wife according to the timetable set by the Sages is labeled a rebellious husband (a <i>moreid</i>). A husband who refrains from providing his wife with sexual gratification, namely he neither prolongs the act of intercourse nor engages in “acts of physical contact,” is labeled a rebellious spouse.
Rabbi Ovadia Hadaya, twentieth century authority elucidates:<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, 6, <i>EH</i> 106 (1).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In this context, there is another argument concerning tormenting the person in relation to <i>onah</i> itself: that he lives with her but he does not live with her in the normal fashion, then . . . this is similar to the case of a husband who demands that his wife turn over immediately after intercourse so that she does not become pregnant, in relation to which the Shulhan Arukh Even ha-Ezer 76:12 that he is compelled to divorce her and to pay out her <i>ketubah</i>. <i>A fortiori</i> this ruling is applicable where she derives no satisfaction whatsoever from the intercourse . . . and she is greatly distressed by the fact that her husband does not remain lying on her stomach as he ought to and has not satisfied her thirst for intimacy. This is a clear-cut case of a sexual rebel who fails to perform the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>onah</i> in the required manner.</span>
As we know, a husband who is rebellious vis-à-vis his wife in matters of sexual intercourse is compelled to give her a <i>get</i>. Contrary to what is advanced amongst some decisors,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 70a and <i>Tur EH</i> 154 in the name of Rabbeinu Hananel; <i>Teshuvot mi-Ba’alei ha-Tosafot</i> 75 in the name of Maharam; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 154:7 citing <i>Gevurat Anashim.</i></i> many authorities decided in accordance with Shulhan Arukh that a <i>get</i> ought to be compelled.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT Ishut</i> 14:15; <i>Tur EH</i> 77, 154; <i>Tosafot Rid, Ketuvot</i> 63a; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i> 5:32; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 693; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i> 63a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 46; <i>Teshuvot Maharah Ohr Zarua</i> 126; <i>SA EH</i> 76:1; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad locum, 5; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 72 (63).<br>Refusing to engage in conjugal relations due to taking a vow (a <i>neder</i>) is another issue which is subject to dispute as to whether a <i>get</i> ought to be compelled due to his rebelliousness, and that issue is beyond the scope of this decision.</i> Such conduct means that the defendant has transgressed a negative commandment, and is rebellious in matters of cohabitation.
As we know, when there is no possibility of compelling the <i>get</i> in the Diaspora, there are grounds for invoking the mechanism of “She did not give herself over to marriage with this in mind” (a clear expectation - <i>umdana de’mukha</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tzal’ot ha-Bayit</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehonatan</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 5; <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama, 198; <i>Teshuvot Ramatz OH</i> 15; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 27(4) in the name of Maharshal and Noda be-Yehudah.</i> In the present context, the couple lives in the United States, and therefore it is legally impossible and consequently <i>halakhically</i> inappropriate to compel the <i>get</i>. Therefore, invoking a clear expectation is justified. The nature of the clear expectation, “She did not give herself over to marriage with this in mind” is noted by Rabbi Abramsky – “The main reason for marriage is for the purpose of cohabitation, and we know for what purpose the bride entered the wedding canopy.”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Yehezkel</i>, supra n. 22. For further elaboration of this point, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 228–236.</i>
In the present case, the defendant did not fulfill his duty of intimate relations properly, “he penetrated and withdrew,” i.e., he withdrew himself from her abruptly, and also had intercourse only once or twice a month. Furthermore, he refrained from fulfilling the second aspect of the obligation, namely, the intimacy of “embracing and kissing until she desires to be joined”.
In other words, the failure of the defendant to fulfill the duty appropriately is equivalent to a husband who stipulates that the <i>kiddushin</i> is conditional upon being exempt from the duty of conjugal relations, which constitutes making a condition, and such a condition which undermines <i>Halakhah</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Hiddushei Ramban, Bava Batra</i> 126b; <i>Shitah Mekubetzet, Ketuvot</i> 56a in the name of Rashba; <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 56a-b in the name of Rabbi Elhanan; <i>MT</i>, <i>Ishut</i> 6:10, 12:7; <i>SA EH</i> 38, 5, 69:6; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, <i>SA EH</i> 93:6.<br>Numerous decisors mandate that a condition must comply with the <i>halakhoth</i> of conditions (<i>mishpetei ha-tena’im</i>) in matters of marriage and divorce due to the fact that we are dealing with a matter of prohibition (i.e. the prohibition of being a married woman). See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rif</i> 31; <i>Rashbam, Bava Batra</i> 137b, s.v. ve’im lav; <i>Tosafot Kiddushin</i> 49b, s.v. <i>devarim she’belev</i>; <i>Mordekhai, Succah</i> 758; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 37(4). Nevertheless, in certain circumstances <i>Halakhah</i> will validate an implied condition. See <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 97a, s.v. zavin; <i>Tosafot Gittin</i> 75a, s.v. le’afukei; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:121(end); B. Lifshitz, <i>Promise: Obligation and Acquisition in Jewish Law</i> (Hebrew), Jerusalem: 1988, 138, n. 106. In other words, despite the fact that we are dealing with the prohibition of “a married woman”, nonetheless, in accordance to Rabbi Rozin we may invoke here, the mechanism of the clear expectation and void the marriage based upon an implied condition (rather than an explicit condition formulated in pursuance with the <i>halakho</i>t of conditions) of marriage. <br>On the other hand, voiding a marriage based upon “an error in the marriage”(<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) is grounded upon the notion that <i>Halakhah</i> mandates a meeting of the minds(<i>gemirat da’at</i>) which is consummated with the act of <i>kiddushin</i> which is executed by an act of undertaking an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>). See <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:3; <i>Hazon Ish EH</i> 56 (9); <i>Teshuvot Helkat Yoav EH</i> 25; Rabbi Shimon Shkop, <i>Shiurei Roshei Yeshivot Lita</i>, page 90; Rabbi Y. Abramsky, <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, Bnei Brak, 5729.The emergence of an error in the <i>kiddushin</i> due to the husband’s failure to disclose a major flaw to his prospective wife prior to the marriage effectively prevents “a meeting of the minds”. See <i>Hazon Ish, EH</i> 56:9, 77:6. For a contemporary adoption of this approach and the application of the concept of a sale in error to “an error in marriage” see <i>Moreshet Moshe</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 60:4 and <i>Teshuvot Beriti Shalom</i> 5:15. <br>Cf. Rabbi Akiva Eiger who contends that a sale in error (<i>mekah ta’ut</i>) is grounded in the violation of a condition (<i>tenai</i>) rather than intrinsically linked to the performance of undertaking a duty. See <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 2:51, 106. To state it differently, despite the fact that we are dealing with the prohibition of “a married woman”, nonetheless, in accordance to Rabbi Eiger we may invoke here, the mechanism of “an error in marriage” and void the marriage based upon an implied condition (rather than an explicit condition formulated in pursuance with the <i>halakho</i>t of conditions) of marriage. For other decisors who view “a sale in error” through the prism of a violation of a condition, see <i>Rashi, Ketuvot</i> 73b, s.v. ve’omar and <i>Yevamot</i> 89a, s.v. seifa; <i>Ohr Sameach, Ishut</i> 10:2, 11:11; <i>Hafla’ah, Ketuvot</i> 73a.</i> Due to the fundamental breach of this condition, as twentieth century legist Rabbi Rozin argues, the clear expectation that “She did not give herself over to marriage with this in mind” should be implemented, and therefore the <i>kiddushin</i> may be invalidated.
