See <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama, <i>EH</i> 88, Tinyana, <i>EH</i> 80.</i> we have adopted the approach of many authorities who used the mechanism of a clear expectation (major <i>umdana</i>) in order to invalidate <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 43.</i>
Therefore, in the present case, there is a major <i>umdana</i> that a woman does not want to live with a philanderer. In any case, the <i>kiddushin</i> are invalidated for the reason that she did not give herself over to marriage with this in mind. This, in addition to what we said about the voiding of the <i>kiddushin</i> due to the husband deceiving his wife by hiding the fact that he was a pedophile – these are cases of mistaken <i>kiddushin</i> due to pedophilia and due to deceit and misrepresentation.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>7. Implementation of a Double Doubt as to the <i>Halakhah</i> in Matters of Biblical Law</b></span>
It has been established that it is possible to invalidate <i>kiddushin</i> by means of the technique of “mistaken <i>kiddushin</i>” due to two matters that had not been disclosed to the plaintiff before the <i>kiddushin</i>, i.e., that the husband was a pedophile and acted deceitfully and misrepresentation, and also due to a proven assessment that a wife is not prepared to marry a man who is having intimate relations with her sisters.
Alternatively, in light of the fact that there are many authorities who object on principle to implementation of “mistaken <i>kiddushin</i>” and “<i>umdana</i>” as techniques for invalidating the <i>kiddushin</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> can be invalidated through use of “double doubt as to the <i>halakhah</i> in matters of biblical law.”
One of the rules of decision-making in relation to the <i>halakhot</i> of doubts is that we rule strictly in relation to doubts in biblical law, and leniently in relation to doubts in rabbinic law.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beitzah</i> 3b; <i>Yevamot</i> 31a; <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a.</i> Rambam argues as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Rambam <i>MT, Tum’at Met</i> 9:12.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is well known that all these and other similar instances which are ruled impure, although there is a doubt involved, are rabbinic safeguards. According to biblical law, only one who has definitely contracted impurity is deemed impure. All stringencies stemming from doubt, whether with regard to ritual impurity, forbidden foods, forbidden intimate relations or the observance of the Sabbath, are only rabbinic in origin . . .</span>
The principle that under biblical law, doubtful cases are to be resolved leniently has already been stated in other rulings in Rambam’s <i>Mishneh Torah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">MT, <i>Issurei Bi’ah</i> 15:29; 16:17, <i>Kil’ayim</i> 10:27, <i>Avot Ha-tum’ah</i> 16:1.</i> In his Teshuvot ha-Rambam, Rambam teaches us: <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i>, 310. See also <i>Iggerot ha-Rambam,</i> Shilat edition, 382 ff, and quoted in <i>Maggid Mishneh, MT Shabbat</i> 27:3. <i>Teshuvot Beit Yosef, EH</i> 2, citing Rif; <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 42 in the name of Rosh; Rabbi Dovid Bonfid, <i>Hiddushei R. David Bonfid, Pesahim</i> 9b; Meiri, <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Kiddushin</i> 5b; <i>Semag</i>, lavin 121; <i>Mordekhai, Yevamot</i> 21; <i>Teshuvot She’elat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Pri Hadash YD</i> 110; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 9a<i>, s.v.</i> ve-omer Ri<i>; Pesahim</i> 10a, s.v. <i>haynu shnei kupot</i>; <i>Kereti u-Peleti, Beit Hasafek</i> 110:1; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed, OH</i> 7:4; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110:66, <i>Sefek Sefeka</i> 34, citing Mordekhai; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:93, citing most of the authorities.</i>
“That we rule strictly in relation to a doubt of biblical law is a rabbinical dictate and not by biblical law.”
On the assumption that we accept the position that we rule strictly on a biblical doubt by virtue of a rabbinic ruling, as opposed to most of the authorities,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishneh le-Melekh, MT Tum’at Tzara’at 2:1; Taz, SA YD</i> 110:15; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, EH</i> 68; <i>Hagahot Tur</i> 23<i>; Teshuvot Tumim Yesharim</i> 149; <i>Teshuvot Ra’anah</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Maharhash</i> 30,</i> in the said case, we have relied on a string of later authorities in general, and legists of Ashkenaz and Sepharad alike, that a double doubt can be invoked in order to restore the woman to her original presumed unmarried status.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">See supra Chapter 2, n. 23.</i>
According to Rambam and those who agree with him, the double doubt is effective because the first doubt reduces the biblical prohibition to a rabbinic prohibition, leaving the second doubt, which is a rabbinic doubt, with regard to which we practice leniency.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 9a, sv. v<i>e-omer Ri; Teshuvot Shem Aryeh, YD</i> 2.</i>
As opposed to this posture, according to Rashba, Ran and those who hold a similar opinion that doubtful cases on a biblical level are to be resolved strictly, the double doubt is effective because the single doubt is “like a fifty percent chance”, whereas the two doubts constitute an absolute majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:401; <i>Pri Hadash, SA YD</i> 110:49; <i>Teshuvot Maharimat, YD</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed, OH</i> 3:4. According to most authorities, the doubt is effective due to the principle of majority. See <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 36:5).</i>And indeed, Rabbi Hizkiya Medini notes: <sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sdei Hemed Hashalem, Get</i> 4, s.v. <i>veharav</i></i>
“From what we have written, it emerges that when the need arises due to a situation of <i>igun</i>, if there is a doubt regarding disqualification, and it is accompanied by another doubt, she may be released by virtue of a double doubt.”<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashba, supra n. 54; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Kiddushin</i> 73a; <i>Torat ha-Bayit, Bayit Revi’i</i> 1) and Ran (on Rif, <i>Kiddushin</i> 15b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 51), ruling strictly in the case of a doubt of biblical law is mandated by biblical law. The later authorities subscribed to this view in the name of the majority of authorities. See <i>Teshuvot Bnei Shmuel</i> 41; <i>Teshuvot Hasaba Kadisha</i> 1:23; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, MT Gerushin</i> 8:11; <i>Kehunat Olam</i> 266; <i>Penei Yehoshua, Hullin</i> 10b, s.v. <i>ela</i>; <i>Teshuvot Shev Ya’akov, YD</i> 48 citing <i>Penei Yehoshua; Beit Meir, YD</i> 228; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer, YD</i> 286; <i>Teshuvot Mishkenot Ya’akov, OH</i> 136; <i>Yeshu’ot Ya’akov OH</i> 17:3; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev, YD</i> 1:118; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 1, <i>OH</i> 8. The view of Rashba and like-minded authorities, whereby strictness is mandated by biblical law in the case of a doubt of biblical law, the reason for leniency in a case of a double doubt is that one doubt involves a fifty-fifty chance, whereas two doubts constitute a majority. See <i>Teshuvot Rashba, ibid.; Pri Hadash, YD</i> 110:49; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed me-Lublin, OH</i> 3:4. See <i>supra</i> chapter 2.<br>Grounds for invalidating <i>kiddushin</i> through use of a double doubt are also found in Rambam’s approach (“That we rule strictly in relation to a doubt of biblical law is a rabbinic dictate”) which is the view of the majority of authorities. See: <i>Teshuvot Ridbaz</i> 4:93; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Pesahim</i> 10, s.v. <i>hinei</i>; <i>Teshuvot She’ilat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yosef, YD</i> 19; <i>Noam Si’ah</i> 12 (b).</i>
In the present case, the first doubt regarding invalidation of the <i>kiddushin</i> due to the fact that the husband is a pedophile is the dispute amongst the decisors as to whether the mechanism of mistaken <i>kiddushin</i> can be applied in these circumstances. The second doubt is that the arbiters disagree as to whether the technique of mistaken <i>kiddushin</i> can be applied regarding a husband who acted deceitfully in order to invalidate the <i>kiddushin</i>. The third doubt is as to whether the clear expectation that “she did not give herself in marriage with this in mind” can be invoked in a case in which the husband is a philanderer.
In applying the double doubt to the present <i>agunah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 18; 6 <i>EH</i> 3 (9 14–15), 6 (5).</i> we presume:
(1) Since all the rivers run into the sea and in effect, all the various doubts distill into one large doubt as to whether or not the marriage is a valid one, Tosafot maintain that we are confronted with a <i>halakhic</i> situation in which “all the factual doubts fall into one conceptual category” and, according to Tosafot, the principle of double doubt – <i>sefek sefeika</i> – is inapplicable in such a situation.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 9a s.v. <i>ve’iba’iteima</i>. See also <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 110, <i>Sefek Sefeika</i> 11–13.</i> Nevertheless, it has already been proven at length by the Kereti u-Peleti (Beit Safek, ad. loc.) that the principle of double doubt is applied ubiquitously both in the Talmud and the Shulhan Arukh even in this type of situation, in order to reach lenient decisions.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kereti u-Peleti Beit Safek,</i> supra n. 50<i>.</i> See also <i>Teshuvot Mahari b. Lev</i> 3:41; <i>PDR</i> 21:10, 23.</i>
However, according to Shakh and Rabbi Reicher,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 58, at 12; <i>Minhat Ya’akov, Kuntres ha-Sefeikot</i> 20.</i> the double doubt can be implemented in the same rubric (“<i>shem ahad”</i>) only if the second doubt is more lenient.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4<i>, OH</i> 43:6.</i> In the present case, the second doubt is more stringent, but several authorities are of the opinion that it is only at the biblical level that a double doubt cannot be applied in the same rubric. Under rabbinic law, however, as is the case before us, a double doubt is applicable even if they both belong to the same rubric.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">see <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama, 2:82; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>OH</i> 19; 16:5; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 1:385, end; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Olam, YD</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel,</i> 2:39; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:33; <i>Darkhei Teshuvah, YD</i> 110:11:280.</i>
(2) An opinion that was not cited in Shulhan Arukh may not be a component of a double doubt (a <i>sefek sefeika).</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am ve-Da’at,</i> 2nd ed., 219; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo, YD</i> 101<i>; Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:7.</i>
In the present case, the three doubts that are found in the disagreements of the decisors who are not mentioned in the Shulhan Arukh, and apparently a double doubt is not effective in such circumstances. However, according to several authorities, the above rule does not pose an obstacle, and it is possible to attach to a double doubt militating for leniency another view that was either not mentioned in the Shulhan Arukh or that contradicts it.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sdei Hemed Hashalem</i> 9, <i>Kelalei ha-Poskim</i> 13:5; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>YD</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 8 (18), 8, <i>YD</i> 6 (4); Rabbi Yitzhak Yosef, <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 3:118–119. For understanding the divergence of opinion regarding whether one may advance an argument for a double doubt when it contradicts the ruling of the Shulhan Arukh, see supra page 50, n. 47.</i>
(3) Regarding invalidation of <i>kiddushin</i> on the grounds that the husband is a pedophile: there is a dispute amongst the legists as to whether the mechanism of mistaken <i>kiddushin</i> can be applied in such circumstances. Regarding a husband who deceitfully hid his defect, i.e. that he is a pedophile, prior to the marriage; there is a dispute amongst the decisors as to whether this would allow for recourse to the mechanism of mistaken <i>kiddushin</i> in order to invalidate the marriage. Furthermore, can the clear expectation that “she did not give herself in marriage with this in mind” be applied? The case in which the husband is a philanderer is a subject of dispute amongst the decisors. The common denominator of all these disputes is that the number of arbiters permitting and the number of authorities prohibiting in each dispute are never even (“<i>shakul</i>”). And therefore, the doubts are not evenly balanced.
As opposed to some decisors who require a balanced double doubt,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 58, at 33; <i>Erekh ha-Shulhan HM</i> 3, <i>Kuntres Sefer ha-Zikaron,</i> 1:52.</i> in accordance with their approach that a Torah doubt entails Torah-mandated strictness, according to other authorities who support Rambam’s approach, ruling strictly in the case of a Torah doubt is only rabbinically mandated, it is not necessary to have a balanced double doubt (<i>sefek sefeika</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed, EH</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Shemen Rokeah Tlitai, YD</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4, <i>YD</i> 12 (14), 6, <i>YD</i> 2. <br>To understand a doubt that is balanced between those who permit and those who prohibit see: <i>Get Pashut</i> 129:26; Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, File no. 917387/1, 14 Kislev 5774.</i>
We accepted this approach.
(4) We apply double doubt in relation to <i>kiddushin</i> and permit a woman to remarry in order to avoid being chained to her husband (<i>igun</i>); <i>a fortiori</i> when there are three doubts (as in the present case), even when there is disagreement amongst the authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh,</i> supra n. 58, at 29<i>; Teshuvot Ra’anah</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hayyim</i> of <i>Maharhash</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>OH</i> 28(17), 4, <i>EH</i> 5(11), 6, <i>EH</i> 6(5).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>8. Conclusion</b></span>
On the basis of the above, Mindy is permitted to marry any Jewish man, even a <i>Kohen</i>. In short, in the wake of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, we deem the matter as “an hour of emergency” (<i>sh’at ha-dehak</i>) and a <i>priori</i> (<i>le-hatehillah</i>) we can void the marriage based upon a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Ma’arekhet Get 30(6) and ha-Samekh 30(3) in the name of Rashba and Ridvaz.<br>The implicit premise of invoking a double doubt in order to void a marriage in this particular case is that the <i>beit din</i> must initially identify at least two grounds for divorce which in effect give credence to a wife declaring “a marriage in error” or “on this understanding she did not give herself in marriage” and therefore, the execution of a <i>get</i> is only required on rabbinic grounds lest the public infer that a married woman is exiting her marriage without a <i>get.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Devar Eliyahu</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27. Given that the giving of the <i>get</i> is only mandated rabbinically, consequently, we may deploy a double doubt and void the marriage based upon “a marriage in error” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) and the clear expectation standard (<i>umdana de-mukhah</i>).</i>

i) A childless widow whose lot fell to an apostate brother in law

<b>I. A childless widow whose lot fell to an apostate brother-in-law:</b>
Sarah Stein v. Andrew Stein
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>The facts of the case</b></span>
Sarah Stein (hereinafter: the plaintiff) and the brother of Andrew Stein (hereinafter: the defendant), Isaac Stein, married according to Orthodox Torah law on January 1, 1988. They did not have children and they adopted two boys.
Approximately ten years ago, the defendant informed his brother, the husband of the plaintiff, that he converted to the religion of The Jehovah’s Witnesses. We spoke to a rabbi who is a family relative and he conducted a few conversations with the defendant regarding his undergoing baptism and becoming a minister of this religion.
On June 2, 2015, the brother of the defendant passed away and the family notified the defendant of his brother’s demise. However, due to the dictates of his religion, he refused to participate in his brother’s burial.
After his brother’s death, the family notified him that he is obligated to participate in the ceremony of <i>halitzah</i> in order that the plaintiff would be permitted to marry another Jewish person. However, due to his religious beliefs, he refused to participate in this Jewish ceremony of <i>halitzah</i>.
After the third summons of the defendant requesting him to appear for a hearing at our <i>beit din</i>, we received an e-mail dated February 6, 2019, which states the following:
<span class="poetry indentAll">I, Andrew Stein, became an ordained minister of Jehovah God and underwent baptism to show my loyalty and allegiance to the true God Jehovah. I took an oath disavowing my previous beliefs and promised not to follow any practices of any other religion and thusly I am not under Mosaic Law. Please respect my feelings and do not contact in this matter (<i>halitzah</i> – AYW) again.</span>
It is our understanding that any person who affiliates with this religion becomes a member and a priest simultaneously via baptism. In his role as a minister, he communicates to the wider community the beliefs of his religion.
We summoned the defendant three times for a hearing, but he refused to respond to the summons and therefore the deliberation regarding this matter was conducted with the plaintiff in the absence of the defendant.
The issue that we will address is the <i>halakhah</i> regarding the tie (<i>zikah</i>) between the plaintiff who is a <i>yevamah</i> and a <i>yavam</i> who is a Jew who converted to another religion (hereinafter: an apostate-<i>mumar</i>).
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. The <i>halakhic</i> duty of <i>halitzah</i></b>
It is prohibited for a Jewish man to marry his brother’s wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra</i> 18:16, 20:21.</i> and the execution of <i>kiddushin</i> (loosely translated – a marriage) under such circumstances is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 15:22.</i> The notable exception to this proscription is the case of the <i>kiddushin</i> of a <i>yevamah,</i> where the brother of the husband who died without having children (a <i>yavam</i> – levir) is obligated to marry his widow, known as a <i>yevamah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 25:5, <i>Tur</i> and <i>SA EH</i> 156:4.</i> If the <i>yavam</i> does not desire to marry the <i>yevamah,</i> she is prohibited from marrying another man unless the <i>yavam</i> fulfills the duty of <i>halitzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 25:6–10. <i>Halitzah</i> entails a ceremony severing the bond between the levir and the <i>yevamah.</i></i> There is a debate among the sages of the Talmud and post-talmudic decisors whether the duty of <i>yibum</i> has priority over the duty of <i>halitzah</i>. Normative <i>halakhah</i> is that <i>halitzah</i> assumes priority over a levirate marriage primarily because the levir did not intend to marry the <i>yevamah</i> for the sake of performing a religious duty.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Bekhorot</i> 1:7; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema EH</i> 165:1.</i> Upon completing the <i>halitzah</i>, the widow (the <i>yevamah</i>) is permitted to marry a Jewish man.
The prohibition of the <i>yevamah</i> to marry any other man prior to the performance of the <i>halitzah</i> is contingent upon the notion that the duty of <i>yibum</i> is upon them. In other words, if there is a tie between the widow and her brother-in-law (a <i>zikat yibum</i>), there is an obligation of <i>halitzah</i>. If not – there is no <i>halitzah</i>.
We must address the question of whether the <i>yavam’s</i> conversion to another religion (Jehovah’s Witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The religion has been described in the following fashion: It is a millenarian restorationist Christian denomination with non-trinitarian beliefs from mainstream Christianity. (Wikipedia). For the principles and the theology of this religion whose adherents believe in Jesus Christ and the new testament, see Robert Bowman, Jehovah’s Witnesses, Grand Rapids, Michigan, 1995; Walter Martin and Norman Klann, Jehovah of the Watchtower: Minn.: MN, 1974; Ron Rhodes, Reasoning from the Scriptures with the Jehovah’s Witnesses, Eugene, OR; 1993.</i>) via baptism which establishes him as an apostate Jew (<i>mumar meshumad</i>) severs the tie between the <i>yavam</i> and <i>yevamah</i>.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. A <i>yavam</i> that converted to his faith after his brother’s marriage – Views of the Authorities</b></span>
Upon the completion of the Talmud, the majority of the Geonim who lived in the subsequent time period, including those who issued classical restatements of <i>Halakhah</i> such as Rabbis Hilai, Amram, Yosef b. Avitur, Natronai, Paltoi, Sherira, Hai and Shmuel b. Hofni argue that a widow who is childless and has a brother-in-law who is an apostate Jew remains an <i>agunah</i> for her lifetime until such time that he performs <i>halitzah</i> (release from levirate marriage). There is a tie between the <i>yevamah</i> and the <i>yavam,</i> she is not permitted to marry until <i>halitzah</i> is performed.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Geonei Mizrah u-Ma’arav</i> 182; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzedek</i> 3, Sha’ar 1, Simanim 28–29; <i>Teshuvot Geonim</i>, Coronel ed., 96; <i>Otzar ha-Geonim, Yevamot</i> 1, 34–37.</i> Subsequently, other decisors endorsed this position.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbeinu Gershom Me’or ha-Golah</i>, Edelberg ed., 48; <i>Teshuvot Rashi,</i> Elfenbein ed., 173; <i>MT</i>, <i>Yibum va-Halitzah</i> 1:6; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 22a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 24a; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Yevamot</i> 16b; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:31; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rid</i> 64; <i>Teshuvot Maharil ha-Hadashot</i> 207; <i>Tur EH</i> 157; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 85; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 223; <i>Orhot Hayyim</i>, 2, <i>Yibum</i> 5; <i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 157; <i>SA EH</i> 157:4; <i>Levush Malkhut EH</i> 157:4; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah,</i> Mahadura Tinyana <i>EH</i> 150; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 157:7.</i> In accordance with this approach, there was no difference with regard to when the conversion transpired. As Rashi notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll">And there is no difference if the husband married her prior to his conversion to the other religion or if he converted subsequently after the marriage.</span>
The emerging question is: what are the reasons for this view? Among the reasons that are mentioned in the responsa that the tie of <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i> remains intact when dealing with a Jewish brother-in-law who is an apostate are the following:
1. The well-known Talmudic dictum is “a Jew, even though he sins, remains a Jew.”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 44a; <i>Rashi,</i> ad. locum.; <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed., 864 in the name of Geonim; <i>Ritva</i>, supra n. 8.; <i>Rid</i>, supra n. 8; <i>Yakhin u-Boaz</i>, supra n. 8.</i>
2. If an apostate Jew executes <i>kiddushin</i> – his <i>kiddushin</i> are valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rabbeinu Gershom</i>, supra n. 8; <i>Rid</i>, supra n. 8; <i>Rashi</i>, supra n. 8.; <i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i>, supra n. 8; <i>Yakhin u-Boaz</i>, supra n. 8.</i>
While in pursuance to the above position, an apostate Jew is obligated to perform <i>halitzah</i>, the bond is of a rabbinic nature. The most compelling proof to this conclusion is found in responsa of Mahari Weill and Mahari Mintz which serve as a precedent for Rema’s ruling that if she remarried without the performance of <i>halitzah</i> by the apostate Jew, she may remain with her new husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharil</i> 54; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Mintz</i> 12; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 157:4.</i> It is just a stringency, since <i>ex post facto</i> if she marries without <i>halitzah,</i> the marriage is valid. In other words, fundamentally (<i>ikar hadin</i>), the marriage is permissible and consequently, <i>ex post facto</i> we follow the fundamental stance. Similarly, Ridvaz concurs that the <i>kiddushin</i> of an apostate Jew is invalid on a Torah level since he is viewed as a non-Jew. Consequently, if he extended a <i>kiddushin</i> (marriage) offer to a woman, the marriage is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 1:351. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 1:93.</i> Other authorities agree that the <i>yibum</i> bond is grounded in rabbinic law.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 159 in the name of Mahari Weill; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 157:8; <i>Teshuvot Sheilat Ya’avetz</i> 1:29; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 4:75.</i>
A contrasting opinion was adopted by Rabbi Yehudai Gaon and Rabbi Hanoch Gaon. In accordance with their position, apostasy which was executed before the brother’s marriage to the <i>yevamah</i> severs the <i>yavam</i> bond. However, if the conversion transpired after his brother’s marriage, as in our case, and at the time of the marriage he was presumptively a bona fide Jew (<i>hezkat kashrut</i>), in pursuance to the above Geonim, the apostate Jew has a bond for <i>yibum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai, Yevamot</i> 28; <i>Magid Mishneh</i>, <i>MT, Yibum va-Halitzah</i> 1:6 in the name of R. Yehudai Gaon; <i>Otzar ha-Geonim. Yevamot, Teshuvot</i> 88, p. 37.</i> Some later authorities espoused this view.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv EH</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 90; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick EH</i> 144; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 2:223.</i>
Both views agree that in the situation of apostasy of the <i>yavam</i> after his brother’s marriage, the apostate retains a bond for <i>yibum</i>. Based upon the foregoing, in accordance with both opinions, we cannot permit the plaintiff to marry without receiving <i>halitzah</i> from the defendant since his apostasy occurred approximately ten years after the plaintiff’s marriage to his brother.
A third view which disagrees with the other two opinions and contends that a <i>yavam</i> who became an apostate after the plaintiff’s marriage to his brother severs the bond of <i>yibum</i> is memorialized in the words of Mordekhai, who writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagahot Mordekhai</i>, supra n. 14 and a portion of this responsum can be found in <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed., 1022 (<i>halitzah</i> case – in theory rather than in practice).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It says in Ohr Zarua Katan . . . Rabbi Nahshon Gaon . . . and Gaonic responsa . . . that a <i>yavam</i> that was an apostate to idol worship during the marriage of his brother, and still when his brother dies remains an apostate, he is not bound to the wife of his brother and she is exempt from <i>halitzah</i> . . . And in the commentary of Rabbeinu Hananeil, from the moment of death she becomes subject to the levirate marriage . . . and Maharam advances a proof since on such an understanding (that she would marry an apostate Jew – AYW) she never would have married him . . .</span>
In order to prove the absence of a <i>yibum</i> bond in the case of a <i>yavam</i> who converted to another religion after his brother’s marriage, Mordekhai directs us to the view of Rabbeinu Hananeil, who argues that “death creates the moment of subjection of the <i>yevamah</i> to the marriage.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See also <i>Ohr Zarua</i> 1:605.</i> In the Talmud there is a controversy whether the “first marriage subjects her to levirate marriage” or “death subjects her to levirate marriage.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 13b, 30b, 109a.</i> If we adopt the former position, then if the <i>yavam</i> was already an apostate Jew during his brother’s marriage, the <i>yibum</i> tie has been established. On the other hand, if we endorse the view that “death creates the <i>yevamah’s</i> subjection to the marriage,” then if the <i>yavam</i> converted after his brother’s marriage, as in our case, the <i>yibum</i> bond has been severed and consequently she is permitted to remarry without receiving <i>halitzah</i>.
According to Rava,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 13b.</i> one must subscribe to the position that “death subjects the <i>yevamah</i> to marriage” and we follow his view due to the fact that “the <i>halakhah</i> is like the latter authorities” (<i>hilkheta ke’batrai</i>) and it is known that the application of this principle of adjudication begins with Talmudic controversies from the time of Abaye and Rava and onwards.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Seder Tannaim and Amoraim</i>, 10; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Ketuvot</i> 7:13; <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i> 2:15; <i>Yavin She’muah</i>, Kelalim 273–274 in the name of Ra’ah, Rosh, Rif, Ba’al ha-Maor, Rav’yah, and Rashba. Cf. <i>Yad Malakhi</i>, Kelalim 167–168; <i>Pahad Yitzhak</i>, entry, “<i>ein halakhah ke’talmid.</i>”</i>
Mordekhai relies upon Rabbeinu Hananeil and Rabbeinu Barukh b. Shmuel who claim that “death subjects the <i>yevamah</i> to marriage.”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See also, <i>Ohr Zarua</i>, supra n. 17; <i>Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, supra n. 16; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 105; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:1143 (94); <i>Ba’air Hetev, SA EH</i> 157:3; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Enzel</i> 47; <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Lublin</i> 36, 56; <i>Teshuvot ha-Re’eim</i> 68; <i>Teshuvot Be’air Oshek</i> 76; <i>Teshuvot Nivhar mi-Kesef</i> 61; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 6:191; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:152, 3:48; <i>Teshuvot Dibrot Eliyahu</i> 7:83.</i> Consequently, even if the <i>yavam</i> is a Jew and a member of the covenant Jewish faith community during the time of his brother’s marriage and he became an apostate after the marriage, a bond has not been established for <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i>.
In his presentation, Mordekhai offers three reasons why, in our circumstances, a <i>yavam</i> who is an apostate does not have ties to <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i>.
Firstly, <i>yibum</i> is dependent upon brotherhood, and an apostate is not construed as a brother. Secondly, zealots punish a woman who has sexual intercourse with an apostate. Therefore, consorting with an apostate is considered “a matter of licentiousness” (<i>davar she’be’ervah</i>), and consequently there is no bond of <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i> in such a scenario. Finally, if she had known that she would have to marry an apostate Jew, she never would have consented to the original marriage. As Rabbi Moshe Feinstein notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:48.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">According to the rationale of Maharam [from Rothenberg] . . . even if he becomes an apostate after the <i>kiddushin,</i> if she had known that she would have to marry an apostate Jew she never would have consented to the original marriage.</span>
We may now formulate our conclusion that an apostate has no ties to <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i> based upon the teaching of Rabbi Aharon Lichtenstein who astutely states:<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">“Brother Daniel and the Jewish Fraternity” in 12 <i>Judaism</i>, Summer 1963, 260, 267, 276.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If we ask, in purely descriptive terms, whether anyone born of Jewish parents is a Jew, the answer must be yes. As an epithet, the term ‘Jew’ remains applicable to any individual who was ever endowed with Jewish status – even to a <i>meshumad</i> (apostate). . . . However, if we ask whether a <i>meshumad</i> has anything of a Jewish personality and character, and whether, therefore, he continues to be endowed with the personal status of a Jew, the answer is a ringing no. He remains a Jew without Jewishness. . . . His personal status as a Jew – be it for marriage or any other purpose is lost. The hallmark of Jewry, what being a Jew essentially means, is effectively erased.</span>
<b>3. <i>Umdana</i> – “with this understanding she would not have married him”</b>
In contrast to <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> (loosely translated: a marriage in error), where the focus is upon a major defect (<i>mum gadol</i> ) that preexisted the marriage and a husband intentionally or unintentionally failed to disclose the flaw, the clear expectation (the <i>umdana de’mukha –</i> hereinafter: <i>umdana</i>) “with this understanding she would not have married him” deals with a major defect that transpired during the marriage. For example, “if I had known that my husband would become an apostate, a criminal, or psychologically dysfunctional during the marriage, I never would have married him.” These are examples of a clear expectation which under certain conditions can lead to voiding the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, supra n. 16.; <i>Teshuvot Beit ha-Levi</i> 3:3; <i>Teshuvot Hesed le-Avraham</i>, Mahadura Tinyana, EH 55; <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, Bayit 9; <i>Teshuvot She’eilot Moshe EH</i> 2 (<i>halitzah</i>); <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehonatan</i> 1, <i>YD</i> 5; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Hefetz</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot Radad</i> (Meisels), <i>EH</i> 40; <i>Teshuvot Meshivat Nefesh, EH</i> 73, 76–77; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim EH</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 7:65 (a wife who is psychologically dysfunctional); <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:121 (a <i>halitzah</i> case accompanied with the argument of <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>); <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 1:79, 2:133.<br>Whether one can utilize a release from a levirate marriage (<i>halitzah</i>) judgment to conclude that one can equally void the marriage is open to debate. Eventhough a widow waiting for her deceased husband’s brother to perform a release is biblically prohibited to anyone else and her status is not as stringent as the status of a married woman who is biblically prohibited to anyone else, one may nonetheless apply a <i>halitzah</i> ruling to a matter of marriage which entails voiding marriage. See <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 250; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Tinyana <i>EH</i> 66 (Cf. Mahadura Kama <i>OH</i> 21); <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 1:95, 99. See further this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 154, n. 45.<br>Based upon the foregoing, therefore we have cited the rulings of Maharam and She’eilot Moshe which deal with levirate marriage and advance the argument that a clear expectation (<i>umdana de’mukha</i>) may serve as a ground to permit remarriage for the wife without the brother-in-law performing a release and these judgments may be equally applied to any marriage as a means to void the marriage. See the ruling of Rabbi Y.S. Nathanson argues that if in a case of levirate marriage one can free without <i>halitzah</i>, <i>a fortfiori</i> one may in a case of voiding a marriage due to an error in the marriage (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>). See <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Tlita’a 61.<br>For our conceptual distinction between <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> and <i>umdana</i>, see <i>Teshuvot Ohr Sameah</i> 2:29; <i>She’eilot Moshe</i>, op. cit.; <i>Zikhron Yehonatan</i>, op. cit. . It is important to stress that this <i>umdana</i> has been employed in the responsa both regarding <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> as well as emerging <i>after</i> the onset of the <i>kiddushin</i>. In other words, concerning <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>, once the major latent defect has been identified, some legists will employ the <i>umdana</i> by stating, “had she known prior to the marriage about this major defect she never would have married him.” On the other hand, if the inappropriate behavior or <i>mum</i>, flaw only began after the onset of the marriage and did not preexist prior to the marriage, there may be grounds to employ an <i>umdana</i> where she would (for example) exclaim, “had I known that he would become mentally dysfunctional during the marriage I never would have married him.” For a discussion of these two types of <i>umdana</i>, see <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yehonatan</i>, vol. 1, <i>YD</i> 5 (17).</i> An inquiry was submitted to Maharam of Rothenberg regarding an apostate <i>yavam</i> who refuses to perform <i>halitzah</i> and he teaches us the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, supra n. 16 which was subsequently cited by <i>Maharam Mintz</i>, supra n. 21, <i>Teshuvot Maharil</i> 205 and <i>Mordekhai</i>, supra n. 14.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Concerning an apostate we can say this, since we are witnesses that a woman is not ready to undergo levirate marriage with him . . . with such an understanding she didn’t marry him (the first brother) originally being that he was an apostate, and it is to be construed as if she conditioned (the marriage – AYW) that she would not undergo a levirate marriage with him.</span>
Maharam establishes that there exists a clear expectation that the <i>yevamah</i> does not want to marry an apostate Jew.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See also <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 43; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:73–74; <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 1:79.<br>Compare Rabbi Dovid Korfo (<i>Teshuvot ha-Radakh</i>, Bayit 9, Heder 11) who writes: “However, regarding a woman whose husband has become an apostate, since he engages in conjugal relations and he satisfied her desire and personal need, perhaps she wants him, even though he is proscribed from having intimate relations with her due to the fact that her paramount concern is satiating her body and isn’t afraid of the prohibition . . .”. In other words, according to Radakh, she enjoys living with an apostate because he satisfies her desire through the engagement in conjugal relations.</i> Furthermore, Maharam contends that there is an implied condition at the time of the establishment of the marriage that she would not undergo such a levirate marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Numerous decisors mandate that a condition must comply with the <i>halakhot</i> of conditions (<i>mishpetei ha-tena’im</i>) in matters of marriage and divorce due to the fact that we are dealing with a matter of prohibition (i.e. the prohibition of being a married woman). See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rif</i> 31; <i>Rashbam, Bava Batra</i> 137b, s.v. ve’im lav; <i>Mordekhai, Succah</i> 758. Nevertheless, in certain circumstances <i>Halakhah</i> will validate an implied condition. See <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 97a, s.v. zavin; <i>Tosafot Gittin</i> 75a, s.v. le’afukei; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:121 (end); B. Lifshitz, <i>Promise: Obligation and Acquisition in Jewish Law</i> (Hebrew), Jerusalem: 1988, 138, n. 106. In other words, despite the fact that we are dealing with the prohibition of “a married woman”, nonetheless, we may invoke here in accordance to Rabbi Rozin, the mechanism of the clear expectation and void the marriage based upon an implied condition of marriage. <br>On the other hand, voiding a marriage based upon “an error in the marriage” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) is grounded upon the notion that <i>Halakhah</i> mandates a meeting of the minds (<i>gemirat da’at</i>) which is consummated with the act of <i>kiddushin</i> which is executed by an act of undertaking an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>). See <i>Hazon Ish HM</i> 22; Rabbi Y. Abramsky, <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, Bnei Brak, 5729. The emergence of an error in the <i>kiddushin</i> due to the husband’s failure to disclose a major flaw to his prospective wife prior to the marriage effectively prevents “a meeting of the minds”. See <i>Hazon Ish, EH</i> 56:9, 77:6. For a contemporary adoption of this approach and the application of the concept of a sale in error to “an error in marriage” see <i>Moreshet Moshe</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 60:4 and <i>Teshuvot Beriti Shalom</i> 5:15. <br>Cf. Rabbi Akiva Eiger who contends that a sale in error (<i>mekah ta’ut</i>) is grounded in the violation of a condition (<i>tenai</i>) rather than intrinsically linked to the performance of undertaking a duty. See <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 2:51, 106. To state it differently, despite the fact that we are dealing with the prohibition of “a married woman”, nonetheless, in accordance to Rabbi Eiger we may invoke here, the mechanism of “an error in marriage” and void the marriage based upon an implied condition (rather than an explicit condition formulated in pursuance with the <i>halakhot</i> of conditions) of marriage. For other arbiters who subscribe to Rabbi Akiva Eiger’s view, see supra p. 142, n. 37.</i>
One must conclude that this clear expectation can only be applied in a case where a <i>yevamah</i> befell a <i>yavam</i> who converted to another religion prior to his brother’s wedding. However, if the apostasy transpired after the brother’s wedding, it is illogical to assume that the wife had this clear expectation at the time of the wedding. Clearly, the prospects that the <i>yavam</i> would become an apostate and that her husband would die without siring offspring are much too remote to imagine. Similarly, Rabbi Feinstein argues:<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:44. Cf. <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, supra n. 22.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">We are not concerned at all that every man who is presumptively proper (<i>hezkat kashrut</i>) would possibly convert to another religion.</span>
On the other hand, if the <i>yavam</i> was an apostate at the time of the wedding, there are clear prospects that the woman was conscious of this possibility that she may fall to <i>yibum</i>. Therefore, one can contend that there was an implied condition of the woman at the time of the marriage and consequently, for the apostate <i>yavam</i> there was no bond for <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i>.
Nonetheless, though Maharam’s responsum focuses upon a case where the apostasy happened prior to the apostate’s brother’s wedding, one should not conclude that his ruling is limited to this situation. As Maharam Mintz duly observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharam Mintz</i>, supra n. 21.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">We have not found that Maharam distinguished explicitly between when he was an apostate during the marriage or afterwards; rather, he permitted invoking the expectation under every circumstance even <i>a priori</i>.</span>
Similarly, Rabbi Eliyahu Mizrahi insightfully teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Re’eim</i> 68.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is not logical to claim that only if the apostasy transpired prior to her marriage to his brother that there was an understanding as if it was an explicit condition that she would undertake a levirate marriage without <i>halitzah,</i> and that if the apostasy happened after the marriage to his brother that at the time of the marriage she didn’t assume that apostasy would occur without <i>halitzah</i>. If this would be correct, then the Talmud should have asked: that a childless widow whose lot fell to a levir afflicted with skin boils during the time that she was married to his brother ought to be released without <i>halitzah</i>, one should have said on this understanding she never consented to marry him.</span>
As such, in a situation of an apostate <i>yavam</i>, one should not differentiate between whether his conversion occurred before or after the marriage. Even though Maharam refrained from disagreeing with the words of Rashi who opines that a <i>yevamah</i> who fell to an apostate has to undergo <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i>, in theory rather than practice, he sanctioned the y<i>avam</i> of an apostate to remarry without <i>halitzah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For reliance upon rulings which are in theory rather than in practice, see <i>Sdei Hemed</i>, Kelalei ha-Poskim 16 (47); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 8.</i>
In short, even a woman who claims, “had I known that my brother-in-law would become an apostate I wouldn’t have married my husband,” may serve as grounds to void a marriage.
A precondition for implementing this <i>umdana</i> is that the wife is Torah observant.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot be-Tzeil ha-Hokhmah</i> 4:91; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Moshe</i> 123; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 2:81; <i>Nahalat Tzvi</i> 2, 229; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov</i> 85; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:83.</i> Upon verification, we determined that the plaintiff observed Shabbat, dietary laws (<i>kashrut</i>) and <i>halakhot</i> dealing with family purity.
Though there are authorities who reject the deployment of the <i>umdana</i> as a means to void a marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avodat Gershuni</i> 235; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i> 1:106; <i>Teshuvot Nishmat Hayyim</i> 129; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama, <i>EH</i> 85, Mahadura Tinyana, <i>EH</i> 80; <i>Teshuvot Oholei Aharon</i> 2:44; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:19; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 2:25.</i> as we noted earlier in our decision, we adopted the approach of decisors who utilize <i>umdana</i> as a vehicle to void a marriage.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>4. Double doubt with a <i>yevamah</i> who was befallen to an apostate <i>yavam</i></b></span>
One of the rules of decision-making in relation to the <i>halakhot</i> of doubts is that we rule strictly in relation to biblical doubts, and leniently in relation to rabbinic doubts.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beitzah</i> 3b; <i>Yevamot</i> 31a; <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a.</i> Rambam rules as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT, Tum’at Met</i> 9:12.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is well known that all these and other similar instances which are ruled impure, although there is a doubt involved, are rabbinic safeguards. According to biblical law, only one who has definitely contracted impurity is deemed impure. All stringencies stemming from doubt, whether with regard to ritual impurity, forbidden foods, forbidden intimate relations or the observance of the Sabbath, are only rabbinic in origin . . .</span>
The principle that under biblical law, doubtful cases are to be resolved leniently has already been stated in other rulings in the <i>Mishneh Torah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Issurei Bi’ah</i> 15:29; 16:17; <i>Kilayim</i> 10:27; <i>Avot Ha-tum’ah</i> 16:1.</i> In his <i>responsa</i>, Rambam writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> 310. This responsa is also cited in <i>Iggerot ha-Rambam,</i> Shilat ed., 382 ff, and quoted in <i>Magid Mishneh, MT Shabbat</i> 27:3.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">That we rule strictly in relation to a doubt by biblical law is a rabbinical dictate and not by biblical law.</span>
There are many authorities which cite Rambam’s opinion and/or subscribe to his position.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Rif and Rosh, <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b. See <i>Teshuvot Beit Yosef, EH</i> 2, citing Rif and <i>Bnei Shmuel</i> in the name of Rosh; R. David Bonfid, Rambam’s pupil, <i>Hiddushei R. David Bonfid, Pesahim</i> 9b; <i>Meiri, Beit ha-Behirah, Kiddushin</i> 5b; Semag, Negative Commandments 121; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 21; <i>Teshuvot She’elat Ya’avetz</i> 2:143; <i>Pri Hadash</i> 110; <i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 9a<i>, s.v.</i> ve-omer Ri<i>; Pesahim</i> 10a, s.v. <i>haynu shnei kupot</i>; <i>Kereti u-Peleti, Beit ha-Safek</i> 110:1; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed, OH</i> 7:4; <i>Shakh</i>, <i>SA YD</i> 110:66, <i>Halakhot</i> of <i>Sefeik Sefeika</i> 34 in the name of Mordekhai; <i>Teshuvot Ridbaz</i> 4:93, in the name of most of the authorities.</i>
On the assumption that we accept Rambam’s position that we rule strictly on a doubt of biblical law by virtue of a rabbinic ruling, the double doubt is effective because the one doubt transforms the biblical prohibition to a rabbinical prohibition, and the second doubt is a rabbinic doubt and therefore entails leniency.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pnei Yehoshua, Ketuvot</i> 9a, sv. v<i>e-omer Ri; Teshuvot Shem Aryeh, YD</i> 2.</i>
On the other hand, espousing the view that a biblical uncertainty ought to be resolved stringently on a biblical level, a double doubt is effective due to the rule of “following the majority.” The existence of one doubt creates a situation of a 50/50 uncertainty while the second doubt then creates a majority which results in treating the matter leniently on a biblical level.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashba, Torat ha-Bayit</i>, 4:1; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Kiddushin</i> 73a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:401; <i>Mishneh le-Melekh, MT Tumat Tzara’at</i> 2:1; <i>Taz, SA YD</i> 110:15; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah EH</i> 68, <i>ha-Gahot Tur</i> 23; <i>Teshuvot Tumim Yesharim</i> 149; <i>Teshuvot Ranakh</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Maharhash</i> 30.</i>
Moreover, we have relied on a string of later authorities <i>(Aharonim</i>) in general, and Sages of Ashkenaz and Spain in particular, that a double doubt can be invoked in order to restore the woman to her original, presumed unmarried status.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Niddah</i> 45a, s.v. <i>hi</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:49; <i>Teshuvot Ra’anah</i> 1:68; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:138; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak, EH</i> 1:92, end; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak, EH</i> 1:35; 2:16 (3–4); <i>Teshuvot Minhat Moshe, EH</i> 11<i>; Teshuvot Be’er Moshe, Kuntres Binyan Yerushalayim</i> 18. For additional responsa, see supra chapter 2, n. 23.</i>
In pursuance to the ruling of Rabbi Avraham Yudlovitz,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Beit Av</i> 7, <i>EH</i> 25.</i> Rabbi Ovadia Yosef formulates the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>EH</i> 36:10.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In the <i>halakhah</i> of apostate’s bond (for <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i> – AYW) – we have a double doubt which has ramifications towards leniency. Possibly the <i>Halakhah</i> is like the Geonim who permit it, and if you argue that the <i>halakhah</i> is according to ones who prohibited, possibly it is only according to rabbinic law, in pursuance to the words of Mahari Mintz and Knesset Yehezkel and their followers. Consequently, a double doubt grounded in rabbinic law is resolved in pursuance to its lenient implications since it entails a double doubt on a Torah level and one doubt grounded in rabbinic law.</span>
Since we are dealing with a rabbinic doubt, thus there was no rabbinic mandate in this situation of a bond to <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Beit Av</i> 3, 106. For an identical conclusion, see <i>Dibrot Eliyahu</i>, supra n. 21.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>5. Conclusion</b></span>
Based upon the foregoing, there are three reasons which sanction the <i>yevamah</i> who befell an apostate <i>yavam</i> who converted after his brother’s marriage to remarry without performing the <i>halitzah</i>. Firstly, the apostate <i>yavam</i> does not have a tie of <i>yibum</i> and <i>halitzah</i> (<i>zikkat yibum</i>). Secondly, we invoke the <i>umdana</i> that her understanding is that she would not have consented to marry her first husband had she known she would have to subsequently marry an apostate. Finally, there is double doubt which is to be resolved in accordance to its lenient implications when dealing with a <i>yevamah</i> who was befallen to an apostate <i>yavam</i>.
As such, the plaintiff is permitted to marry any Jewish man without the performance of <i>halitzah</i>.

j) The battered woman syndrome

<b>J. The battered woman syndrome: Response to a Question</b>
<b>Question</b>
Sarah approached us and asked us as qualified expositors of <i>Halakhah</i> (<i>mo’reih hora’ah</i> rather than <i>dayanim</i> sitting in judgment) to void her <i>kiddushin</i> and to permit her to marry any other Jew without receiving a <i>get</i> from her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In other words, this is a question of <i>halakhic</i> laws of prohibitions and permissibility (<i>dinei issur ve-heter</i>) whether a husband is obligated to give a <i>get</i> to his wife. It is a matter of dispute whether one requires a <i>beit din</i> of three, a single rabbi or an individual Jew to address matters of divorce such as <i>get</i> coercion, obligating a <i>get</i> and voiding a marriage. See <i>Yam shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i> 3:9; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 3:1–2; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, <i>HM</i> 3:1; <i>Teshuvot Yehudah</i> (Gordin), <i>EH</i> 51:2; <i>Teshuvot Ma’aseh Hiyah</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer, EH</i> 2:64–65, <i>HM</i> 177; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer EH</i> 167:1<i>; PDR</i> 6:265, 269; File 957-61, Beit Din Yerushalayim for Monetary Matters and Yuhasin, vol. 7, 515; File no. 448866/3, Tel-Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, July 11, 2013; File no. 1086123/1, Be’er Sheva Regional Beit Din, December 20, 2018; Rabbi Z.N. Goldberg, <i>Lev Mishpat</i> 1, 149–150.<br>Whether the individual Jew must be knowledgeable in <i>Halakhah</i> is subject to controversy. See <i>Yam shel Shlomo</i>, op. cit; <i>Ma’aseh Hiyah</i>, op. cit.; File no. 448866/3, op. cit.<br>In the present matter, three rabbis convened to decide on a matter of ritual <i>Halakhah</i>.<br>In the absence of a party attending a divorce proceeding regarding the matter of a <i>get</i> rather than a divorce-related monetary matter, the <i>beit din</i> or rabbinic authority(ies) may render a decision provided that the participating party is known to possess integrity and would not lie. See <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah</i>, <i>Tur HM</i> 17:19. See further, this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 216, n. 2.</i>
<b>Answer</b>
<b>1. <i>The facts</i></b>
Sarah and Michael were married in January 2011. The marriage was the third marriage for Michael and the second one for Sarah. They sired two boys from this marriage.
In the wake of being physically abused during her pregnancy of their first child during May 2012, Sarah left the marital home and decided not to return to her home. A copy of a letter from her doctor who observed the wounds caused by her battery was submitted to us, stating that she was a victim of Michael’s battery. Sarah remained with her mother for approximately a month, but subsequently she returned to Michael due to the acts of violence perpetrated by her mother, which were more severe than the acts of abuse that she had suffered from her mother’s abuse during her childhood. Michael promised to register for a course in anger management and that he would collaborate with his rabbi in order to improve his conduct. In light of his acts of spousal assault during June 2013, Sarah separated from Michael for the second time and she went to live with her mother. After eight months she decided to leave her mother due to the latter’s abuse.
In May 2015, Michael left her and filed for divorce in New Jersey. In July 2015, Sarah filed for divorce in Massachusetts. A civil divorce was executed in May 2017. Due to outstanding obligations for spousal and child support totaling $48,302.60, Michael fled the United States and presently lives in Moscow. Michael claims that he is prepared to give a <i>get</i> to Sarah on the condition that she waives her right to the aforesaid debts. However, in these types of cases, after the husband’s demands are successfully met by the wife, there is a slim chance that he will comply with his promise to give the <i>get</i>.
Sarah claims that during the time that the couple was living together, Michael assaulted her ten times in a cruel fashion, three times breaking the doors of their home. In addition to the physical abuse, Sarah argues that Michael degraded her and verbally abused her, including cursing her. She complained to the police regarding his reckless behavior. Additionally, there were four of five times that Sarah wanted to contact the police but she restrained herself because she feared that the acts of violence would escalate. In fact, she hid a cell phone under her bed should she need to call the police in an emergency, or to enable her to contact others who could assist in her time of need.
Three or four months after her wedding, Sarah discovered amongst Michael’s papers copies of a police report and restraining order dating back to his first marriage, as well as a letter authored by his <i>first</i> wife which provides a detailed report of the violence that he committed against her. Michael persuaded Sarah that everything written in his former’s wife’s letter was replete with lies. At the time, Sarah expressed confidence that Michael was telling the truth. Yet, a year and half later, after their marriage, a rabbi confided in her that Michael had battered his <i>second</i> wife.
As we noted earlier, in order to corroborate her claim that Michael engaged in domestic violence, she submitted a copy of the aforementioned doctor’s note to us. Additionally, Sarah provided us with copies of two police reports dealing with incidents of intimate violence during her marriage, a copy of a New Jersey domestic violence civil complaint and temporary restraining order, as well as a copy of a police report of domestic violence committed by Michael vis-à-vis his first wife, along with a copy of the aforesaid letter written by his first wife.
As we noted, during May 2012, approximately a year and half after the wedding, Michael assaulted her while she was pregnant for the first time and it was only then that she realized that his first wife was writing the truth and she then regretted that she had married Michael. Despite the fact that a series of domestic violence incidents followed her first pregnancy, nevertheless, notwithstanding two periods of marital separation, Sarah remained with Michael until May 2015 when Michael left her and filed for divorce.
Given that the marriage was marked by both physical and emotional abuse, we asked Sarah why she remained in the marriage. Sarah explained that she remained in the marriage for various reasons. Firstly, she needed to have a place to live with her two children. Her parents were divorced and her mother abused her during her childhood and continued this behavior while she resided with her during the time she had separated from Michael. Moreover, even though she was prepared to divorce him after the first three months of the marriage due to Michael’s intimate ties with his second wife after they were divorced, she remained with him because she desired to have children, to establish a family and didn’t want to live alone and she thought that the situation would improve. Furthermore, given that this was her second marriage, she was embarrassed as to what the community would say if she got divorced again. Finally, should she divorce him, she was scared that he would kill her. And despite living in an abusive environment with the fear of death lest she leave him, Sarah continued to express her love for him (as well as her love for her abusive mother). In other words, Sarah had emotional ties to Michael even though he battered her. A clinical expert in social worker who serves as a therapist for Sarah communicated to us the same reasons that Sarah remained in the marriage.
Though the couple went to marriage counseling and Michael took courses to deal with spousal abuse, nothing helped and the abuse intensified over time.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. A cognitive and emotional understanding of intimate partner violence</b>
We are dealing here with a violent man who utilizes physical and emotional violence in order to physically and emotionally cause pain to his spouse. As a result, his spouse becomes “the beaten one” and the man is labeled “a batterer.”
Women who become victims of domestic violence are fearful that they do not have the capacity to deal with this violence. Emotionally, the beaten woman fears that she is responsible for the transpiring of the battery and therefore her self-confidence is undermined. The husband’s actions, the violence, the breaking of objects and even the husband’s facial expressions engender in the woman feelings of fear, the inability to confront the batterer, and the loss of hope. In the context of the domestic violence, the batterer has strategies such as attempts at loving contrition of his victim, abstention from clashes and surrendering to the batterer’s desires. The victim’s inability to confront the batterer explains her remaining in this environment of violence. Her inability to control her life transforms her into a being who lives in accordance with the directives of an external cause, namely the batterer. Nonetheless, the violence occurs intermittently. In other words, on one hand her share and responsibility in the initiating of the conflict via inappropriate conduct creates the provocation of the batterer to use physical and emotional violence. On the other hand, there are incidents of positive behavior from the batterer that are expressed as self-improvement, promises that he will refrain from touching her in the future, declarations of love, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For professional literature which is descriptive of the battered woman’s syndrome, see E. Pence and M. Paymar, Power and Control, Tactics of Men who Batter, Duluth, Mn., 1986; M.A. Dutton, Empowering and Healing the Battered Woman, N.Y.: Springer, 1992; M. Dutton, “Understanding Woman’s Responses to Domestic Violence: A Redefinition of Battered Woman Syndrome,” 21 <i>Hofstra Law Review</i> 1190 (1993); L. Brash, “Wife Battery in the Eyes of the Beholders: A Functional Cognitive Perspective, (Hebrew),” Doctoral Thesis, Bar Ilan University, 2010; A. Michal, The Associations between Violent Characteristics, Sense of Control and Parenthood among Battered Women, (Hebrew)” Master’s Thesis, Bar Ilan University, 2012.</i>
The batterer attempts to be a lover, and in particular, expresses pain regarding his conduct. And these feelings are significant because of the fears that the victim may leave him and for the purpose of being able to be in control in the future. Love and appeasement are the trap and snare of the woman that in the final analysis turn her into a victim. The batterer’s apology is a means to diminish the tensions and fights between the couple. The apology fulfills two purposes. Firstly, from the batterer’s vantage point, the apology expresses his rehabilitation. Secondly, the apology stems from the batterer’s feelings of guilt. To state it differently, the apology serves a significant and decisive factor that allows the batterer to remain with his spouse and thus allows him to continue to assault her!
The power of control leaves the woman bereft of the ability to confront the batterer and creates a relationship of traumatic bonding. As Dutton and Painter observe in their oft-cited essay:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">D.G. Dutton &amp; S. Painter, “Emotional Attachments in Abusive Relationships: A Test of Traumatic Bonding Theory,” 8 <i>Violence and Victims</i>, 105, 109 (1993).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Dutton and Painter (1981) have elaborated a theory of “traumatic bonding,” whereby powerful emotional attachments are seen to develop from two specific features of abusive relationships: power imbalances and intermittent good-bad treatment . . .<br>The process of detaching from, or emotionally leaving, an abusive relationship should be more difficult, since traumatic bonding is theoretically increased by relationship dynamics. Hence, intermittent abuse develops an emotional bond which interferes with leaving and remaining out of an abusive relationship. Dutton and Painter (1981) liken this attachment process to an elastic band which stretches away from the abuser with time and subsequently “snaps” the woman back. As the immediate trauma subsides, the strength of the traumatically-formed bond reveals itself through an incremental focus on the desirous aspects of the relationship, and a subsequent sudden and dramatic shift in the woman’s “belief gestalt” about the relationship. This shift in phenomenology alters her memory for past abuse, and her perceived likelihood of future abuse. The point of this original formulation by Dutton and Painter (1981) is that this belief shift is attachment-derived. That is, it follows from a shift from avoidance to return at an affective-attachment level.</span>
In other words, traumatic bonding is established by power imbalance and intermittent abuse.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See also, J. A. Christman, “Expanding the Theory of Traumatic Bonding as it Relates to Forgiveness, Romantic Attachment and the Intention to Retain,” Master’s Thesis, University of Tennessee, 2009, 4–5.</i>
Another type of traumatic bonding is called Stockholm syndrome. As one researcher observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">V. George, “Traumatic Bonding and Intimate Partner Violence,” Master’s Thesis, Victoria University, 2015, 11–12. See further, D.L.R. Graham, et al, “<i>A Scale for Identifying ‘Stockholm Syndrome’ Reactions in Young Dating Women: Factor Structure, Reliability and Validity</i>,” 10 <i>Violence and Victims</i>, 1995, 3–22.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The term Stockholm syndrome was coined in 1973 by Bejerot, who used the term to describe the enigmatic behavior of three women and one man who were held hostage for six days in the vault of the bank in Stockholm, Sweden (Bejerot, 1974). These hostages developed an emotional bond with their captor that was so strong that they threatened the officials who were trying to help them. The captives refused to leave the vault before the captors, fearful that the police would harm the captors. The bond (also known as capture, traumatic of terror bonding) continued long after the release of the hostages, including scenes of the captives kissing and hugging their captors once freed from the vault. Reports that two of the female hostages were romantically involved with two of the captors surfaced some months later (Graham, 1994). This seemingly paradoxical response has also been seen in other contexts and populations, specifically concentration camp prisoners, prisoners of war, civilians held in Chinese Communist prisons, cult members, abused children, incest victims . . .<br>Graham (1995) developed the Stockholm syndrome theory, specifically linked to intimate partner violence, based on the psychology and behavior of these groups. She argued that four precursors were necessary for the development of Stockholm syndrome: perceived threat to survival, perceived kindness, isolation, and the perceived inability to escape.</span>
In our case, Sarah told us that one of the reasons she did not leave Michael was due to feelings of love toward him. In light of what we have just explained, one can understand her feelings as reflecting her traumatic bonding rather than authentic love of a wife for her husband.
However, the emerging issue is how does one understand her claim that her feelings of love persisted for many years after their separation and the civil divorce? To understand the import of her claim, we sought out the advice of a practicing clinician in domestic violence with twenty years of experience, and this was her reply:
<span class="poetry indentAll">This is probably the most common question I’m asked in my work. It’s an incredibly important question, but I believe the answer to be incredibly complex and again, completely individual, based on the person who’s in the situation themselves.<br>As you and I have conversed about, no abusive relationship I’ve ever worked with is completely bad. Not one. There are always elements of positive in some way. These things, combined with sharing a life together, are the building blocks of connection and love for any one of us. Someone could be horrifically hurt by someone in a host of ways, and human nature dictates that this abuse often doesn’t erase the love and connection. So, what I have seen over and over again in my work – and is wholly supported by the literature – is that survivors love their abusive partners because it is a deeply normative human response to feel that way – and may well feel that love and connection in some form for the rest of their lives. Someone who shares a life together with their partner – even if so much of it was abusive – it does not necessarily erase the love that was built. The love likely takes many different evolving forms, but someone still stating they love their abusive ex-partner, in my view, is just being authentic. This emotion is both understandable and does not in any way contradict the abuse and pain that is also present in these relationships.</span>
In other words, the authentic love that was developed prior to the acts of abuse continues to exist even after the divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Alternatively, we are dealing with traumatic bonding rather than authentic love which serves as the reason that there exists emotional attachment even after the divorce. See supra text accompanying n. 5.</i> These feelings do not in any fashion contradict the abuse perpetrated by a husband vis-à-vis his spouse. In short, in addition to traumatic bonding there is authentic love which continues to persist after the divorce. As such, victims of domestic violence continue to remain with their batterers in a coercive environment, akin to prisoners of war.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">D. L. R. Graham, <i>Loving to Survive: Sexual Terror, Men’s Violence and Women’s Lives</i>, N.Y., NYU Press, 1994.</i>
Clearly, the psychological realities of Sarah do not fit a singular profile. In fact her case, similar to other situations of battered women, vary considerably from each other. Yet, in pursuance to Sarah’s arguments and submitted evidence, the presentations of her therapist who specializes in social work, the expert advisory opinion of a practicing clinician in domestic violence and our research of the professional literature, we arrived at the conclusion that the intimate partner violence between this couple reflects the phenomenon known as Stockholm syndrome.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>2. <i>Kiddushei Ta’ut</i> due to Physical Abuse</b></span>
Prior to implementing the mechanism known as <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> (loosely translated as an erroneous marriage) in order to void the marriage and advance the argument that an error transpired in the creation of a particular marriage due to the fact that the prospective husband failed to disclose the defect to his prospective wife prior to the marriage, three preconditions must obtain.
1. The flaw must be severe (a <i>mum gadol</i>) such as a husband’s sexual impotence, mental dysfunction, homosexuality, or if a wife was exposed to a contagious and dangerous disease such as syphilis or A.I.D.S. All these examples are crystalized in the responsa as major defects.
One of the yardsticks to determine whether a particular behavior is an example of a major defect is by ascertaining whether the behavior such as physical abuse serves as a ground for divorce (<i>ilat gerushin</i>) that a <i>beit din</i> may coerce the husband to give a <i>get</i> to his wife.
Let’s elucidate the various authorities who addressed the <i>halakhah</i> of a husband who is a batterer and discern whether there are grounds to coerce a <i>get</i>.
Ohr Zarua teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ohr Zarua</i> 2:161.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If he customarily assaulted and publicly shamed her, we coerce him to divorce her. Already there was an incident with one who frequently hit his wife, and Rabbeinu Simhah was asked, and he replied that we coerce him to divorce her . . . a person does not live with a snake in the same basket.</span>
However, Rabbi Meir of Rothenberg argues:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed. 81.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Regarding a husband who assaults his wife, one must be more stringent than when dealing with somebody who hits his friend . . . And if she wants to leave him, he ought to divorce her and give her the <i>ketubah</i> (the value of <i>ketubah</i> – AYW).</span>
Similarly, Ramban writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 102.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A husband ought to refrain from hitting and torturing his wife . . . and we inform him that if he hits in violation of <i>Halakhah</i>, that he will be obligated to divorce her and give the <i>ketubah</i>.</span>
In other words, in opposition to Rabbeinu Simhah and Ohr Zarua who obligate <i>get</i> coercion, Maharam and Ramban only obligate the giving of a <i>get</i>.
And Beit Yosef opines:<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 154, end.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It seems to me that one cannot rely upon the Sefer Agudah, Rabbeinu Simhah and Ohr Zarua to coerce since it is not found amongst the renowned authorities.</span>
Yet, Rema in his Darkhei Moshe demurs:<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur EH</i> 154:17.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">I don’t see this matter accordingly, as we can rely upon Geonim. <i>A fortiori</i>, Ramban and Maharam agreed in their responsa in the matter of battering a wife and brought clear proofs to their words. And <i>halakhic</i> logic concurs with them . . . however not to coerce a <i>get</i> . . . rather to comply with what one is dutybound to do. In [Teshuvot] Binyamin Ze’ev (no. 68), he cites many responsa regarding this issue and concludes that if he is the cause, he is obligated to divorce her.</span>
It seems from the language of Ramban and also from the language from Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev that one does not coerce, rather one is obligated to divorce, and the flow of the language from the passage of Darkhei Moshe seems to indicate the same conclusion. Similarly, other decisors align themselves with the position that <i>get</i> coercion is inappropriate.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hut ha-Meshullash, Tur</i> 3:35 in the name of Rosh, Rashba, Tur and Rivash; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 1:156, 4:157; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 1:60; <i>Teshuvot Perah Matteh Aharon</i> 1:60; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 107.</i>
Nonetheless, there are authorities who espouse <i>get</i> coercion even if the assaults are infrequent and do not endanger the wife’s life.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:2 in the name of “some say”; <i>Hut ha-Meshullash,</i> supra n. 13. <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Moshe</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:60; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 154:15.</i>
Based upon the foregoing and the circumstances of the abuse in our case, according to this latter position there is a basis to coerce a husband to give a <i>get</i> to his wife.
Given that in the Diaspora, generally speaking countries do not enforce a rabbinical court decision to coerce a <i>get</i>, Rabbis Spektor, Feinstein, Frank, O. Yosef and others argue that, under certain conditions, one can invoke the mechanism of <i>kiddushei ta’ut</i> in order to void the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24 (38); <i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 2:159; <i>Piskei Halakhot</i>, <i>Ishut</i> 1, <i>Yad David</i> 372 (in theory not in practice); <i>Teshuvot Dvar Eliyahu</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:79; <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 2:81; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>EH</i> 38; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Avraham</i> 2:10.</i>
In light of the fact that one cannot legally (and therefore <i>halakhically</i>) coerce a <i>get</i> in the United States, under certain conditions there are grounds to void this marriage.
Alternatively, even if we would have subscribed to the view that there is no basis of get coercion in the case of spousal physical abuse, there exists another approach that arrives at the conclusion that we are dealing with a major defect and consequently we can void the marriage.
In a judgment of Rabbi Shlomo Daichovsky, a former presiding Dayan of the Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, the highest rabbinical court under Israel’s Chief Rabbinate, he states as follows regarding the definition of defects on the part of the husband:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">File no 1-22-1510, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, September 7, 2004.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The matter of defects for which a woman may sue for her get is not Torah-ordained law, but reason and logic, as Rambam writes (Hil. Ishut 25:2): “These matters are concepts that reason dictates; they are not decrees of the Torah.” The whole subject of defects is a matter of human reason, and the emotional unwillingness of one spouse to endure an intolerable situation on the part of the other spouse. For this reason, we have the <i>halakhah</i> of “he assessed and accepted” or “she assessed and accepted.” It is possible to say that if the general opinion concerning a particular defect changes, then it may not be said that “she assessed and accepted” on the basis of her past acquiescence. A defect may not necessarily be a physical one: defective behavior, such as consorting with prostitutes, may entail an order to give a <i>get</i>. Indeed, this <i>halakhah</i> concerning consorting with prostitutes appears in Shulhan Arukh 154, which is the section dealing with defects.
