In all civilized countries, it is very humiliating for a woman to share her bed with another woman, and a husband who takes another wife will be ordered to give a <i>get</i>, not only due to the ban of Rabbenu Gershom, but also due to the humiliation and the flaw in this. The parties came from Yemen, where it was acceptable to marry two wives. Here, this is considered a great disgrace, and must be regarded as a major defect. The wife’s past acquiescence does not obligate her at present, and therefore this is not only a matter of “he is repulsive to me,” but also a matter of a defect in the husband, due to which the wife cannot live with him. In the case of divorce due to a defect, the wife does not lose her <i>ketubah</i>. It is plausible to say that even if the wife married a philandering husband, and later repented of her decision and can no longer tolerate this situation, the husband will be ordered to give her a <i>get</i>, and will not be able to claim that she “assessed and accepted.” Equally, in my opinion, the claim “She assessed and accepted” cannot be made in a situation in which a woman married a man who was an avowed homosexual, even though she was perfectly aware of his proclivity, and later learnt about the severity of the prohibition, and wishes to divorce him due to the husband being repulsive to her. Here, too, there is no room to argue that “She assessed and accepted.”</span>
Implicit in Rabbi Daichovsky’s understanding of the definition of what constitutes a major defect is that a husband’s defects (as well as a wife’s defects) are grounded under certain conditions upon defects found in real estate or personal property (see further in our decision), namely the universe of <i>halakhic</i> civil law (Hoshen Mishpat). In short, under certain conditions the buyer is entitled to void the sale if the community considers it a defect in the item. However, if the community does not deem a particular flaw to be a defect in the item, then the buyer cannot rescind the sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rif</i> 153; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 72b; <i>MT, Mekhirah</i> 15:5; <i>Semag</i>, Negative Commandments 170; <i>SA HM</i> 232:6; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Ribot</i> 300; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 1:19; <i>Teshuvot Tzedakah u-Mishpat HM</i> 36; <i>Malbushei Yom Tov</i> 4.</i>
Following in the footsteps of Rabbi Daichovsky who argues that a major defect is defined by communal standards, Rabbi Shlomo Amar teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 1:15.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">In contemporary times, we must act stringently in regard to battered wives, since every man who is respectable and intelligent is embarrassed by this behavior, a woman cannot tolerate this at all and it is a major shame today in comparison to earlier times . . . battery of a wife is repulsive and disgusting in the eyes of a woman . . . that she can no longer live with him.</span>
In accordance with Rabbis Daichovsky and Amar, a woman undoubtedly has a valid claim that her husband’s conduct is disgusting. As such, it is unsurprising that numerous contemporary Israeli rabbinical courts construe such behavior as an example of a woman who is pleading “he is repulsive to me - <i>ma’is alai.”</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR</i>) 7:201; 12:3, 84, 324, 16:145; File no. 284462/9, Netanya Regional Beit Din, April 9, 2014; File no. 980712/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, October 27, 2014; File no. 269629/9, Netanya Regional Beit Din, June 19, 2016; File no. 1078402/6, Haifa Regional Beit Din, June 5, 2017.</i>
Consequently, it is unsurprising that some legists argue that the Talmudic presumption “it is better to live as two than to remain in widowhood” (<i>tav le-meitav tan do mi-le-meitav armalu</i>) has been understood as inapplicable to a husband who engages in domestic violence.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i> 4, <i>EH</i> 2:60; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak EH</i> 1:43.</i>
Moreover, Michael’s behavior is not only “repulsive and disgusting” but is an outward expression of a psychological flaw in his persona.
In the absence of a psychological diagnosis of Michael, scholars have noted the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">P.A. Ali and P.B. Taylor, “Intimate Partner Violence: A Narrative Review of the Biological and Psychological Explanations for its Causation,” 10 <i>Aggression &amp; Violent Behavior</i>, (2013), 373–382. For the cited professional literature, see Ali &amp; Naylor, ibid. 379–382. See also, D. George et al., <i>A Model Linking Biology, Behavior, and Psychiatric Diagnoses in Perpetrators of Domestic Violence</i>,” 67 <i>Medical Hypotheses</i> (2006), 345.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The initial theories related to IPV (intimate partner violence – Flynn) were based on the psychopathological orientation of violence. Based on studies conducted on known violent men in prisons, community based settings or victim women in shelters, some theorists believed that men who perpetuate violence and women who experience violence suffer from mental health problems such as depression (Hastings &amp; Hamberger, 1994; Julian &amp; McKenry, 1993; Murphy, Meyer &amp; O’Leary, 1993; Pan, Neldig &amp; O’Leary, 1994), personality disorders including antisocial personality, borderline personality disorder. . . . (Edwards, Scott, Yarvis, Paizis &amp; Panizzon, 2003; Ehrensaf, Cohen &amp; Johnson, 2006; Holtzworth-Munroe et al., 1997; Levy, Meehan Weber, Reynoso &amp; Clarkin, 2005; Mauricio, Tein &amp; Lopez, 2007; Porcerelli, Cogan &amp; Hibbard, 2004)</span>
During our hearing with Sarah, she submitted to us pictures of drugs with a conventional prescription on each bottle which states the name of Michael, the name of the medicine, the prescribed dosage and the date of the purchase of the medicine. In January 2014, he took venlafaxine for depression and in May 2014, he took divalproex for a bi-polar disorder. Michael admitted to Sarah that he suffered from depression and Michael’s second wife thought he had a bi-polar disorder.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Additionally, he took gabapentin, lamotrigine and lorazepam to deal with other psychological disorders.</i> All the taking of medications did not assist Michael in addressing his problems. Since Michael never received a professional diagnosis, it is unsurprising that the various drugs did not help him. In the couple’s estimation, Michael was suffering from a borderline personality disorder. As noted above, studies show that a person suffering from this illness may become physically abusive.
In accordance with the above-cited studies, it stands to reason that Michael’s violent behavior was a by-product of his psychological dis­order.
Dealing with an epileptic husband, contends Rabbi Yosef ben Moshe Trani of seventeenth-century Constantinople who writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Reuven married a woman and after the marriage she became aware that he had epilepsy . . . subsequently the illness became severe until he lost his mind and then Reuven, with his mental dysfunction (<i>shetut</i>), wanted to choke his wife in the presence of two witnesses. . . .</span>
Similarly, centuries later, addressing a case of a man who assaulted people by throwing stones at them and hitting his wife, Rabbi Moshe Feinstein states:<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:46.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">From his conduct it is clear that he is mentally dysfunctional (“<i>shoteh</i>”) since in regard to his interpersonal relations he immediately (after the marriage ceremony - AYW) hit her as he does with other people, and from this we may conclude that he does not comprehend what it means to have a wife.</span>
In the absence of a professional psychological diagnosis, Rabbis Trani and Feinstein argue that engaging in domestic violence <i>ipso facto</i> identifies a husband as mentally dysfunctional (a <i>shoteh</i>). In other words, his improper conduct is a by-product of the psychological flaws in his persona.
Consequently, in accordance with the views of Rabbis Trani, Daichovsky, Amar, and Feinstein, spousal battery is a major defect.
2. In order to void the marriage, it must be clear that the major defect existed prior to the marriage and the prospective wife was unaware of the flaw prior to their marriage.
Pursuant to one witness’s testimony, prior to the marriage he informed Sarah that Michael was suffering from depression and abused his second wife. Sarah admitted that she was told about his psychological condition prior to her marriage. However, concerning his past conduct of spousal abuse, Sarah claims that she was told about his behavior a few months after the onset of the marriage.
Firstly, when the witness submitted testimony in the presence of the rabbis, he testified with certainty that he disclosed to Sarah prior to the marriage her prospective husband’s psychological condition. On the other hand, when he testified regarding his past spousal abuse, he formulated his words utilizing the language – “I believe”.
We are of the opinion that it is illogical to assume that the witness told Sarah about Michael’s inappropriate behavior prior to the wedding. A woman is not prepared to marry a man who is known to have been violent to a previous wife. Should she have been aware of his conduct, she would have refused to marry him. Moreover, since she suffered many years living with her abusive mother, she would not have been ready to marry a man who exhibited violent behavior towards his previous wife.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>A. The credibility of a wife to claim that that her marriage was “a marriage in error” (</b><i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>).</span>
Rabbi Osher Weiss rules as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Osher</i> 1:73(3). Given that Rabbi Weiss’s ruling is contingent upon dealing with “an hour of emergency”, we are able to apply it to our situation of <i>get</i> recalcitrance which is viewed <i>halakhically</i> as an hour of emergency. See <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov EH</i> 110; <i>Teshuvot Agudat Ezori Midbari EH</i> 9:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 14 (21–22). Though the cited responsa address the case of the classic <i>agunah</i> when the whereabouts of the husband are unknown, nonetheless, arbiters employ identical terms such as “an hour of emergency” regarding the modern day <i>agunah</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6(end).</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Since there is a doubt concerning the essence of the <i>kiddushin,</i> it is to be construed as if she isn’t a married woman and therefore “one witness is trustworthy regarding prohibitions” in “a matter of nakedness” (<i>davar she’bervah</i>), when it is bereft of the status of being prohibited, in accordance to the view of Maharik, shoresh 72. Similarly, such as the opinion of Tosafot, Gittin 2b . . . as it is written in Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger, section 107. . . .<br>Though seemingly the majority of authorities disagree with this position, and in accordance with their opinion a single witness cannot be trusted in a matter of nakedness even if it is devoid of the status of being prohibited, as it is explained in the words of Ramban. . . . in the words of Rashi . . . and as in Rashba . . . Seemingly, one must stringent in this matter.<br>However, renowned later legists tended to be lenient, and that is what was written in the Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah in numerous places. See Mahadura Kama, 54 . . . 59 . . . 38 and that is what he wrote in 43 . . . 61 . . . and 85. . . .<br>And study also Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger . . . 93 who cited the words of Noda be-Yehudah, who took a lenient stand, even though he had doubts due to the opinions of the early decisors. Nevertheless, he attached his position as an appendage (<i>senif</i>) to being permissive.<br>And they are worthy these pillars of the world, Noda be-Yehudah, Hayyei Adam and the Gaon Rabbi Akiva Eiger to rely upon them in an hour of emergency.</span>
In other words, relying upon respected authorities, argues Rabbi Weiss that one can trust her as one witness in “a matter of nakedness”.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The identification of “a matter of nakedness” in our case is due to the fact that we are dealing with voiding a marriage. See <i>Iggerot Moshe YD</i> 227:5. For other definitions of “a matter of nakedness” such as testimony regarding the <i>halakhot</i> of prohibited sexual relations (<i>arayot</i>), testimony which is linked to an act of illicit intercourse (<i>bi’at zenut),</i> testimony if the <i>kiddushin</i> is valid, testimony which changes the status of a man due to ritual <i>Halakhah</i> or any testimony regarding a person, see <i>Tosafot Gittin</i> 2b, s.v. eid ehad ne’eman be’issurim; <i>Mordekhai, Yevamot</i> 12:58; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 125 in the name of Netivot; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 124–125; <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i>, Sha’ar 6, Perek 10; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer HM</i> 20.</i> Consequently, in our case we may impart credibility to Sarah’s representation that at the time of their marriage that she was unaware that Michael had been physically abusive to both his previous wives.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>3. The <i>halakhic</i> propriety of a battered wife staying with the marriage after the realization that the marriage was “in error”</b></span>
The emerging question is whether Sarah was obligated to leave the marriage immediately upon becoming aware that he was a batterer or not is a dispute amongst rabbinic authorities. Although among many of the scholars she was obligated to bolt the marriage immediately after discovery of this major defect,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur &amp; Beit Yosef EH</i> 154; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 39:9; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, ad. loc. 16; <i>Teshuvot Maharik,</i> shoresh 24; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 57.</i> Rabbi Moshe Feinstein and <i>Dayanim</i> Goldschmidt and Bavliki contend that she may continue to remain in the marriage on the condition that there were reasonable explanations for this decision.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:45, 48, 4:113; <i>PDR</i> 1:5, 11–12.</i> However, if in the final analysis, the problem(s) could not be resolved, such as if the husband was mentally impaired and psychological counseling and/or medication did not improve the situation, then the wife must leave the marriage.
In order to analyze this matter, we must study the <i>halakhot</i> of a mistaken transaction (<i>mekah ta’ut</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, one can quantify the model of mistaken transaction, whereas when dealing with a marital life we are dealing with marital relations. Despite the differences between these two topics, Torah scholars utilized principles of a mistaken transaction in order to solve problems dealing with marital matters. See <i>Mishnah Ketuvot</i> 1:6, 7:8; <i>Tosefta Ketuvot</i> 7:10; <i>Sefer ha-Yashar of Rabbeinu Tam</i>, Nidah 18.</i> The emerging question is whether an erroneous marriage may serve as a model in order to establish the principles of a mistaken transaction of Jewish civil law or the opposite.
Notwithstanding Ramah’s position,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nimukei Yosef, Bava Metzia</i> 49b, Rif pagination.</i> in pursuance to some decisors, under certain circumstances, the <i>halakhot</i> of an erroneous transaction are equivalent to a wife who discovers a husband’s defects after the onset of her marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 72b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ri Megash</i> 129; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 33:1; <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, <i>Teshuvot Ketzarot</i> 77; <i>Magid Mishneh, MT</i>, <i>Mekhirah</i> 15:3; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA HM</i> 232:9.</i>
In opposition to the above, in pursuance to Magid Mishneh,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Magid Mishneh</i>, supra n. 31.</i> the <i>halakhah</i> of waiving a defect when he used the item is derived from the <i>halakhot</i> of Jewish family law.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In pursuance to <i>Magid Mishneh</i>, ibid., <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 117:9 and <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA HM</i> 232:11 this <i>halakhah</i> is learnt from <i>Ketuvot</i> 75a-b.</i>
On the other hand, numerous legists derive many <i>halakhot</i> of dealing with an erroneous marriage from the <i>halakhot</i> concerning the discovery of a defect in a sales transaction.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 24:7; <i>Hazon Yehezkel, Zevahim EH</i> 8; Z.N. Goldberg, “An exemption from support due to a psychological disorder,” (Hebrew), 4 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 54 (5758); <i>Teshuvot Bikkurei Osher</i> 1:21; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> 12; File no. 141993/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 5, 2003; File no. 1129170/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 18, 2018.</i>
Adopting this latter approach, let us shed light upon the principles of an erroneous transaction in order to apply them to the situation of a marriage in error.
Relying upon Rambam’s ruling, Shulhan Arukh states:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA HM</i> 232:3.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If one sells to his friend land, . . . or other moveable property, and a defect (appears), which the buyer was unaware of, the buyer may return it (to the seller and receive his money back) even if a number of years have lapsed (since the sale), given that the sale was based upon a basic error, provided that the buyer did not continue to use the object after he became aware of the defect, However, if the buyer continued to use the object after he saw (or became aware of) the defect, he has waived his right to rescission and cannot return (the defective object to the seller and receive his money back).</span>
One may derive the above principle of an erroneous sales transaction from the <i>halakhot</i> dealing with price fraud (<i>ona’ah</i>). As we know, if the discrepancy in price exceeds one-sixth of the market price, the sale is voidable.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA HM</i> 227:2.</i> However, if the buyer used the item after discovering the price fraud and he failed to protest the discrepancy in price, he waived his right to void the transaction.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen, Ona’ah</i> 11:19 in the name of later decisors (<i>Aharonim</i>).</i>
In the event that the buyer informed the seller concerning the price discrepancy, even if he used the item after his notification, use of the item does not constitute a waiver of the discrepancy in price. As Ritva writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva Bava Metzia</i> 50b, s.v. ye’tar al she’tut.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If the use was prior to becoming aware of the price discrepancy, he returns it “as is” . . . and if he used it after realizing the price discrepancy, he waived the discrepancy . . . if he became aware of the price discrepancy and he informed him and subsequently used it, he did not waive the price discrepancy. However, he is obligated to pay the seller for the use of the item or what he lost by using the item.</span>
Notwithstanding some decisors,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT Mekhirah</i> 15:3, <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Bava Metzia</i> 4:15; <i>Teshuvot Galyah Massekhet</i> 10; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 232:1.</i> other authorities argue that we may equate the <i>halakhot</i> of erroneous sales with the <i>halakhot</i> of price fraud.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 3:38; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA HM</i> 232:1 in the name of Rabbeinu Ephraim.</i> In other words, just as in a situation of price discrepancy the buyer may use the item after notifying the seller and we do not say he waived his right of restitution, analogously we do not say he waived his right to voiding the sale marked by a defect and may use the item.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishpat Shalom</i> 232:3; <i>Divrei Mishpat</i> 232:3; <i>Mahaneh Ephraim, Ona’ah</i> 5; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah, HM</i> 232, ha-Gahat Beit Yosef 37.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, in accordance with <i>halakhot</i> of price fraud and a mistaken transaction, there is a duty to inform the seller that the sale is voidable due to the awareness of a defect. Similarly, in the context of “a marriage in error” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>), there is a duty that the wife notifies her husband regarding his failure to disclose his major defect prior to the marriage. Yet, in our case, Sarah failed to inform Michael that she was divorcing him due to his failure to disclose prior to the marriage that he was abusive to his wives during his earlier marriages.
Nonetheless, the two marital separations may be understood not only as a protest regarding his behavior, a desire to distance herself from him, but even as an indication that she wanted to divorce him. One may say that these separations indicate the lack of a prospect for marital peace and therefore a desire to be divorced. As we know, some authorities posit that a separation for twelve or eighteen months with no prospects for marital reconciliation serves as a reason for a <i>beit din</i> to obligate a husband to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbeinu Yeruham, Sefer Meisharim</i>, Helek 8, Netiv 23; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:112, <i>Iggerot Moshe YD</i> 4:15 (2).</i> Despite the absence of an explicit notification that she wants to be divorced (read: an erroneous sale – <i>mekah ta’ut</i>), nonetheless her two marital separations serve as an implied public statement (<i>gi’lui da’at</i>) that Sarah wants to be divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">We have refrained from determining whether Michael is obligated to give a <i>get</i> due to these separations. We cited the views of Rabbeinu Yeruham and others only in order to demonstrate that the lapse of time of living separately from her husband and no prospects for marital reconciliation indicate that she has intentions to be divorced from him.<br>Cf. others who would contend that the separation was to seek help from family rather than wanting to be divorced from him. However, given that the “help source” was inadequate, she had little alternative but to return to the batterer. See E. Gondolf and E. Fisher, <i>Battered Women as Survivors: An Alternative to Treating Learned Helplessness</i> (1988), excerpted in <i>Domestic Violence Law: A Comprehensive Overview of Cases and Sources</i> 79 (Nancy K. D. Lemon ed., 1996).<br>As we noted earlier, the psychological realities of Sarah do not fit a singular profile. In fact her case, similar to other situations of battered women, vary considerably from each other. As such, the purpose of the two separations was to exit the marriage rather than to address “mending the marriage”.</i>
However, as we observed, Sarah was embarrassed to dissolve the marriage since she had left her first marriage and therefore feared the reactions in her community upon finding out that she now decided to leave her second husband. Additionally, she needed a place to live, she was scared that he would kill her if she left him, and finally she was emotionally tied to Michael and therefore did not leave him. Consequently, after separating from him due to his behavior (read: using the defective item) and living with her abusive mother, she returned to him. One should not extrapolate from her decision to return to Michael that she accepted to endure the continuing violence, but due to the circumstances mentioned above and Michael’s promises that he would change his way, she was motivated to return to the marital home. In the final analysis, the presence of traumatic bonding propelled her to return to an environment marked by violence.
The power of control leaves a woman bereft of the capacity to confront her batterer, and as we explained, a traumatic bond is established between the batterer and his victim. The power of this emotional bond played itself out in an event that transpired after their marital separation in May 2015. At that time, Michael started to look for women on the internet and he interacted with a German woman. During the summer of 2018, one rabbi from another <i>beit din</i> asked her – “If you had known about his interaction before the separation, would you have left him?” Her reply was – “I don’t know”. The social worker explained to us that her reply was due to the emotional ties that Sarah developed with Michael due to the Stockholm syndrome.
Even if Michael had developed intimate relations with this German woman, the expert for domestic violence explained to us:
<span class="poetry indentAll">Certainly much of the answer centers on many reasons, leaving an abusive situation is so complex, layered and nuanced. It is assuredly something that has been shown to escalate the danger in the situation, even when there has been no prior violence, and simply speaking, so many of my clients have sincere hope that things can improve. So, although an extramarital affair is quite obviously a painful occurrence, I suspect the honest response of “I don’t know” was one that had to do with a likely fluid equation so many of my clients have expressed to me over the years – what are the trade-offs to leaving versus staying?<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See further, K. Thomas et al., “I have lost everything – Trade-offs of seeking safety,” 85 <i>American Journal of Orthopsychiatry</i>, March 2015, 170–180.</i></span>
If we apply the aforementioned principle of an erroneous transaction which is grounded in the <i>halakhot</i> of price discrepancy to the matter of an erroneous marriage, we would arrive at the conclusion that if the wife separates from her husband due to inappropriate behavior (read: a public statement that reflects the desire to void a marriage), we would conclude the following in our scenario. In our case, though Sarah returned to her husband twice after leaving due to his abuse (read: using the item), one should not assume that she had accepted the abusive relationship. On the contrary, each time Sarah was promised by Michael that he would “change his ways.”
Despite his empty promises, the acts of violence continued and escalated (known in the scientific literature as “the cycle of violence”<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">L. Walker, <i>The Battered Woman</i>, 55, at n. 29 (1979).</i>), and she remained with him due to her distress regarding housing accommodation, fear for her life and she loved him. Finally, <i>he</i> left her!
In the wake of the fact that he left the marriage, is there a basis for voiding the marriage due to Michael’s failure to disclose to Sarah prior to the marriage that he had abused both of his previous wives?
Our reply to this question is predicated upon the second principle of a mistaken transaction. Assuming a man purchases a horse and discovers a defect while traveling with it, continuing to travel with the horse does not imply that he waived his right to void the sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, supra n. 40; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan HM</i> 232:4.</i> Pithei Teshuvah advances the following reason for this position. Even though the purchaser is riding the horse, one may void the sale because he is under duress. Though Pithei Teshuvah concludes that this view requires further deliberation, others contend that he is riding the horse due to the necessity of the circumstances and therefore the sale may still be voided.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot ha-Mishpat HM</i> 232:2, <i>Orhot ha-Mishpat</i> 32:17, <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, supra n. 46; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat Shlomo</i> 4:27.</i>
To state it differently, the premise here is that we are dealing with a case of voiding a marriage and upon her return to the marital home (read: using the item) she was under duress and therefore she never waived her willingness to void the marriage. Seemingly, the series of events demonstrates otherwise! We are dealing with a separation that occurred in May 2012, and a return to the marital home one or two months later. Subsequently, she left him for eight months and returned for a year and quarter and then Michael left her! Given the times of separation vs. the period of living together, it is somewhat difficult to believe that Sarah was under duress for two and quarter years and never wanted to sustain the marriage.
If we translate the above circumstances into concepts of a mistaken transaction, it would seem that the circumstances of the case are similar to a purchaser who placed the item in the domain of the seller, took it back before the seller took it, then returned it to the seller’s domain where it was again taken by the purchaser.
Due to the problematic nature of our position that she was under duress for a long time, we asked the expert on domestic violence to address our issue, and here is her reply:
<span class="poetry indentAll">What is also in countless pieces of literature, and I will say has absolutely been corroborated in my twenty years of practice in the domestic violence field over and over again – that it is simply not one or the other. I imagine this is quite true for this client – that she was not either under duress or accepting of the marriage and its myriad of abusive elements. Rather, she was likely under incredible amounts of duress (at various levels of escalation) and also aimed to find moments, pockets, etc. of acceptance in an effort to survive the abuse and perhaps be able to maintain her and her children’s lives with as little upheaval as possible. The “both/and” concept might serve as some part of the explanation why she, and countless others – return to and then leave abusive relationships many times (incidentally, the evidence-based average number of attempts to leave an abusive relationship is seven). As we’ve conversed about previously, no relationship I have ever worked with someone about is entirely bad – and so the confounding factor of someone having moments of peace, happiness, etc. in relationships that are also incredibly traumatic is a factor to note in response to your inquiry as well.</span>
In other words, as an outsider casting one’s eyes upon the scene, it would seem that Sarah’s conduct is strange. Does her behavior reflect a wife who is under duress and therefore reluctantly submissive to Michael or is she a woman who wants to be married to him? The expert is informing us that we are dealing with the conduct of a battered woman who is traumatically bonded to a batterer who at times is living in captivity and at other times living outside of captivity. In short, we are focusing upon a woman who lives in captivity as well outside of captivity and under both sets of circumstances she is under duress.
As we noted, our conclusion is based upon the advisory opinions of experts in the field of domestic violence as well as the findings of professional literature.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>A. There is no waiver of bodily pain.</b></span>
Moreover, our position is rooted in the principles of the <i>halakhic</i> system. The Talmud and Shulhan Arukh have already established:<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 93a; <i>SA HM</i> 421:12. See further <i>Nishmat Avraham EH</i> 3, 80, 4:193.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">If a person says to his friend – “hit and wound me on the condition that you will be exempt (from responsibility)’’ and his friend maimed him, he is exempt.</span>
Despite the fact that agreeing to being assaulted entails a violation of the prohibition of maiming (<i>ha’valah</i>), nonetheless, <i>ex post facto</i>, if the friend complied with the condition, the condition is valid and he is exempt from paying for the injury. To state it differently, there is an intrinsic distinction between the prohibitive result and the <i>halakhically</i> illegal obligation.
The issue is whether Sarah “accepted” (“<i>savrah ve-kiblah”</i>) the abuse. Based upon the foregoing, one cannot waive bodily pain. As Rabbi Asher b. Yehiel observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 68:10.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A condition is invalid if it sanctions a friend’s bodily harm which engenders pain.</span>
Rivash concurs with this conclusion.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 484.</i> In opposition to Rabbi Shaul Nathanson who validates a one-time waiver of bodily harm,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama, 1:197.</i> others oppose any sanction of bodily harm in a continuous fashion.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishneh le-Melekh Ishut</i> 6:10, 15:1.</i>
Yet, when addressing spousal relations, the picture changes. In contradistinction to some decisors,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 56a, s.v. <i>harei zo</i> in the name of Rabbeinu Elhanan; <i>Mordekhai Bava Metzia</i> 83a, 369 in the name of Ritva; <i>Mordekhai Ketuvot</i> 83a, 213; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Bava Batra</i> 126b s.v. <i>verav Yehudah</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b, s.v. <i>divrei Rav Yehudah</i>; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 10 in the name of Rabbeinu Tam; <i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur EH</i> 38:8; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 69:5.</i> the majority of authorities opine that one is proscribed from stipulating a condition regarding conjugal relations, since abstention from conjugal relations engenders pain for the wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Kiddushin</i> 19b, s.v. <i>bedevar shel mamon</i>; <i>Rivash</i>, supra n. 49; <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 26b; <i>Ran, Kiddushin</i> 25b as per Rif pagination; <i>MT Ishut</i> 6:10 (Cf. <i>MT, Ishut</i> 14:7); <i>Tur EH</i> 38, 69; <i>SA EH</i> 38:5, 69:6. See supra Chapter 4D, Final Afterthoughts.</i>
In pursuance to the dominant view, though the wife waives her right to having intimate relations, we assess her expectations that she is not truly waiving it wholeheartedly.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havalim ba-Nei’imim</i> 1:33.</i> In other words, the waiver is effective assuming one can prove that it is being executed wholeheartedly.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Darkhei Noam HM</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim HM</i> 3:46; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:31.</i> In the absence of executing an act to undertake an obligation (a <i>kinyan</i>) to waive or an explicit statement of waiving, if there is a fear that she did not waive wholeheartedly, the waiver is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2, <i>HM</i> 118; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon HM</i> 12.</i> Furthermore, given that the duty of conjugal relations is incumbent upon the husband, the wife cannot waive his duty.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT Ishut</i> 6:6, 12:7; <i>Rashbam Bava Batra</i> 126b, s.v. <i>be ‘davar</i>; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick YD</i> 218.</i>
But as we mentioned in pursuance to the opposing view, the stipulation of a condition is effective in regard to intimate relations provided that an explicit condition was made. In the absence of an explicit condition, the duty of conjugal relations remains operative. Given that in our case, Sarah failed to execute a condition, there is a <i>halakhic</i> consensus that Michael was proscribed from assaulting Sarah.
Based upon the foregoing, accompanied by additional reasons, we do not conclude that Sarah acquiesced (“<i>savrah ve-kiblah”</i>) to the acts of battery.
Firstly, Sarah as a Torah observant Jewess did not waive bodily pain. The <i>halakhic</i> norm directed the appropriateness of her behavior. Secondly, as we noted earlier, the cultural norm prevalent in Western society is that spousal battery is generally abhorrent in the eyes of people, and of Sarah in particular as a victim of childhood abuse. In accordance with both the <i>halakhic</i> and cultural norms, Sarah understood that there is a need to bolt a marriage that is marked by domestic violence.
Moreover, as an Israeli <i>beit din</i> rules:<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 1393-14-1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din [unpublished], March 5, 2003.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Even if she continues to live with her husband in a loving and affectionate manner, this is no proof that she waived, since she is in a situation where she has no choice.</span>
Similarly, in our case, we described Sarah’s situation as one “where she has no choice.” Consequently, even though she engaged with Michael in conjugal relations, one cannot say that the notion that “it is better for a woman to be married” (<i>tav le-metav tan du</i>) applies to her, or that “a woman is satisfied with anything” (<i>niha lah be-khol de-hu</i>) in an environment of domestic violence.
Initially, Sarah thought she could tolerate and deal with the abusive behavior. However, with the passage of time, the abuse escalated. In other words, abusive behavior is ever-changing, and as such one cannot say that Sarah acquiesced to the situation. Said conclusion may be distilled from one responsum which focuses upon a man who married a mentally dysfunctional woman. It teaches us:<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Harei Besamim</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 72.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It was known when she was a virgin, that the majority of her life she was mentally dysfunctional and only infrequently was she mentally stable. And it seems that the husband knew before the marriage that her mind was feeble but not an idiot. And after the marriage, when he observed her moments of insanity he was comforted by his father-in-law and family members when they said the doctors said that after her pregnancy she will be cured from her lunacy. He trusted their words and continued to live with her . . . However, it did not last long and the sickness of lunacy overwhelmed her . . . and now she was placed in a hospital for lunatics. . . .</span>
In his ruling, the authority provided various reasons to lift the excommunication of Rabbeinu Gershom and permit him to divorce her against her will, and among the various reasons, he states the following:
<span class="poetry indentAll">This logical argument that he acquiesced (“<i>savur ve-kibel”</i>) does not only apply to a defect that is constant and does not change unlike idiocy which changes with the passage of time . . . and one cannot say he acquiesced.</span>
Similarly, the Sanzer Rov writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 1:51.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A man was engaged to a virgin when they both were minors, and the father of the bride told the groom’s father that sometimes the girl becomes weak. But there is no need to worry because she is weary and that is due to being physically weak and after she grew . . . and the groom’s family was scared that possibly she suffers from the illness of epileptics. . . . Another time, the bride’s father said there is no need to worry, it is only weakness, and the groom and his father believed him . . . and the groom married the young woman. And after the marriage, with the passage of time, the sickness overcame her to the point that it became clear to the husband that she had epilepsy and he left his wife. . . . Until now (before the marriage – AYW) before it became clear they said she did not have epilepsy . . . but seeing now that she is epileptic, he separated from her and there was no waiver.</span>
Analogously, relying upon Divrei Hayyim, Rabbi Meir Arik contends that regarding an illness that changes with the passage of time, a man who suffers from such a sickness is not obligated to continue to remain in the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 2:119.</i> To state it differently, his agreement was contingent upon the fact that the illness was less severe.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">See also <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i> 77a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 77a.</i>
Though the above responsa deal with a wife’s defects, one can extrapolate (employing a <i>hekesh</i>) from the <i>halakhot</i> of a wife’s defects to the <i>halakhot</i> of a husband’s defects.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Rid Ketuvot</i> 74a; <i>Shitah Mekubezet, Ketuvot</i> 72a in the name of Rivash and Maharit; <i>Imrei Yosher</i>, supra n. 62; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer,</i> 8 <i>EH</i> 3 (16); <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 2:180–181.<br>For the dynamics of analogical reasoning, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 53–57.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, notwithstanding Shulhan Arukh and Rema,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>EH</i> 154:1.</i> we can argue in our case, which deals with the husband’s defect, that one cannot say that she acquiesced after conditions deteriorated. As Rabbi Tzvi Hirsch Orenstein notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Birkat Retzeh</i> 109.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">When there is a change, one can say, “it is unacceptable to me.”</span>
Moreover, addressing the illness of epilepsy, Rabbi Arik contends:<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Imrei Yosher</i>, supra n. 62.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And concerning these major defects, which, in accordance with the doctors, deals with the disease of the nerves and the brain, and it is known that this illness intensifies each time . . . Consequently, the rule of “she is acquiescing” is inapplicable.</span>
And the identical conclusion is applicable to our case. The act of physical assault by its very nature becomes more severe. Therefore, the rule of acquiescence is inapplicable under our circumstances.
Her real will was to leave him. However, her will was impaired by a coercive environment which was marked by a traumatic attachment to her batterer, loneliness, embarrassment and fear which motivated her to stay with her abuser and therefore there is a basis to void this marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">For the prevalence of these factors in staying in an abusive relationship, see K. Arenella, “<i>Perceptions of Domestic Violence: Leaving vs. Staying in Abusive Relationships</i>,” 2014 Scripps Senior Theses, Paper 408, 6, 8, 19.</i>
In light of the above, Sarah is permitted to remarry without a <i>get</i> any man except a Kohen.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><b>Final afterthoughts</b></span>
Based upon the cumulative evidence submitted to this panel and in light of the absence of a professional psychological evaluation of Michael, it was the couple’s estimation that Michael was suffering from a borderline personality disorder, while his second wife’s impression was that he was suffering from a bipolar disorder. Despite the absence of a professional identification of Michael’s disorder, nevertheless, according to Rabbis Yosef ben Moshe Trani, Yosef Steinhardt and Moshe Feinstein, a husband who is a batterer is <i>ipso facto</i> deemed by <i>Halakhah</i> as a mentally dysfunctional individual.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharit</i>, supra n. 23; <i>T</i>e<i>shuvot Zikhron Yosef,</i> Likkutim 11; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, supra n. 24. See infra n. 70.</i>
Upon accepting their view that a husband who engages in battery is deemed mentally dysfunctional, we can examine this behavior in terms of two grounds for divorce, namely the inappropriateness of the conduct as well as the psychological underpinnings of the behavior.
Let’s begin by examining whether there ought to be grounds to coerce a mentally dysfunctional husband to give a <i>get</i> to his wife. The criteria for being classified mentally dysfunctional are set down in Tosefta and subsequently cited in Talmud:<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefta Terumot</i> 1:3; <i>Hagigah</i> 3b. A fourth behavior which is deemed <i>shoteh</i>-like conduct is mentioned in <i>Hagigah</i>, op. cit.<br>Alternatively, we can posit without attempting to identify whether the husband exhibits certain psychological behavior that in fact a batterer is to be construed as mentally dysfunctional. Dealing with an epileptic husband, contends Rabbi Yosef ben Moshe Trani writes:<br>Reuven married a woman and after the marriage she became aware that he had epilepsy . . . subsequently the illness became severe until he lost his mind and then Reuven, with his mental dysfunction (<i>shetut</i>), wanted to choke his wife in the presence of two witnesses . . . (<i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113).<br>Similarly, centuries later, addressing a case of a man who assaulted people by throwing stones at them and hitting his wife, Rabbi Moshe Feinstein states:<br>From his conduct it is clear that he is mentally deranged (<i>shoteh</i>) since in regard to his interpersonal relations he immediately (afer the marriage ceremony- AYW) hit her as he does with other people, and from this we may conclude that he does not comprehend what it means to have a wife. (<i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 3:46)<br>In the absence of a professional psychological diagnosis, Rabbis Trani and Feinstein argue that engaging in domestic violence <i>ipso facto</i> identifies the husband as mentally dysfunctional. Consequently, Michael’s conduct as a batterer of his wife places him in the category of being mentally deranged (a <i>shoteh</i>) without the need of a professional diagnosis and/or a <i>halakhic</i> examination of the criteria required to label him as mentally dysfunctional.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Our Rabbis taught: Who is a <i>shoteh</i>? One who goes out alone at night, one who spends his night in a cemetery and one who tears his clothing.</span>
Given that Michael did go out alone during the night and appeared naked on a highway, there is a relevant Talmudic controversy as to whether one requires one type of erratic behavior to be deemed mentally dysfunctional or one requires all three types of abnormal conduct to establish mental incompetence. Related to this, a subsequent passage in the Talmud adds a fourth form of aberrant behavior, namely one who destroys that which is given to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hagigah</i> 4a.</i>
Notwithstanding some authorities,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, <i>Tur EH</i> 119 and 121 in the name of Rabbeinu Simhah of Speyers; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 19 in the name of Rabbi Avigdor; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 153; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:4 in the name of Rashba.</i> as we learn from Rabbis Yosef Karo’s and Moshe Issereles’s rulings regarding the disqualification of witnesses, the list is not a closed list but encompasses any form of irrational conduct which may establish him as mentally incompetent.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 121; <i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur, EH</i> 119:5; <i>SA HM</i> 35:8.</i> In fact, numerous decisors endorse their rulings and contend that the list is not exhaustive.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 52; <i>Teshuvot Ateret Hakhamim EH</i> 18; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim EH</i> 53 (compare nos. 74–75); <i>Teshuvot Nefesh Hayah EH</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Ohr Sameah</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 153; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer, EH</i> 2:2; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim EH</i> 89; <i>Teshuvot Sefer Yehoshua</i> 71; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:137; <i>Teshuvot Tzofnat Pa’neah</i> 103–107; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 6:159; <i>Teshuvot Mishnat Aharon</i> 56.</i> In our case, implicitly endorsing the latter approach, both Rabbis Trani and Feinstein view the batterer as a mentally dysfunctional personality. Moreover, numerous authorities argue that the psychotic behavior must have occurred at least three times in order to label him mentally dysfunctional, a <i>shoteh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam ben Barukh</i> 455; <i>Pri Megadim YD</i> 1:23; <i>Sha’agat Aryeh, Ohr ha-Yashar</i> 28–31; <i>Beit Ephraim</i>, supra n. 74; <i>Teshuvot Zikhron Yosef</i> 10.</i> In our situation, as we mentioned earlier, it happened ten times.
Given that Michael refuses to give a <i>get</i> to his spouse, are there grounds to coerce a <i>get</i>? Mishnah Ketuvot states:<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 7:9–10.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">A man in whom defects arose; we do not compel him to divorce (his wife – AYW). Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said: When is this case – when the blemishes are minor. But in the case of major flaws, we compel him to divorce (her – AYW). And these are (the flaws) for which we compel (a man – AYW) to divorce (his wife): one who is afflicted with skin boils, one who has a polyp, and one who collects excrement, the copper smelter, the tanner, etc.</span>
Accordingly, there is a list of major defects concretized in the Mishnah (as well as in the Talmud) which serve as the grounds to compel a husband to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Additionally, certain instances of a husband’s emotional abuse may justify <i>get</i> coercion. See <i>Mishnah Ketuvot</i> 7:1; <i>Kiddushin</i> 2:5; <i>Ketuvot</i> 48a, 70a, 71a-b; <i>Yevamot</i> 64a, 65a.</i> Consequently, it is unsurprising that numerous authorities will invoke only these specific flaws as grounds for issuing a <i>get</i> compulsion judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yevamot</i> 65b, s.v. <i>hu amar</i>; <i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 70a, s.v. <i>yotzi</i>; <i>Tosafot, Yevamot</i> 64a, s.v. <i>yotzi</i>; <i>MT, Ishut</i> 15:7; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Yevamot</i> 6:11; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 17:6, 42:1 in the name of Ra’avyah, 43:3 (Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 4:3, 5:34; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 43:6); <i>Mordekhai Ketuvot</i> 194; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban Ketuvot</i> 63b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba Ketuvot</i> 64a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Me’yuhasot le-Ramban</i> 138; <i>Sefer Meisharim, Netiv</i> 23, <i>Helek</i> 8 in the name of Rashba; <i>Tur EH</i> 154 in the name of Ramah; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Ketuvot</i> 63a; <i>Teshuvot of Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed., 946; <i>Hagahot Maimoniyot, Ishut</i> 25:4; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 93; Tur SA 154 in the name of Rosh; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 211; <i>SA EH</i> 154:5, 21; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 1:88; Rabbi A. ha-Levi, <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:108 (1180), 1331 (260); <i>Teshuvot Be’air Sheva</i> 61; <i>Teshuvot Mekor Barukh</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Emunat Shmuel</i> 8; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 2:14; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 3:42; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra, SA EH</i> 154:50, 65; <i>Tosafot Yom Tov, Tur EH</i> 154; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 154:7, 29; <i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i> 1; Rabbi Eliyahu ha-Levi, <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 149; <i>Hazon Ish, Ketuvot</i> 69:23.</i> The implications of this approach, namely that the scope of <i>get</i> compulsion is defined by a list of specific grounds enumerated in the Mishnah and the Talmud, means that certain conduct and types of illness such as insanity, homosexuality, child abuse or a contagious disease such as AIDS or syphilis will not be a ground for compelling a <i>get</i>. As the famed ruling of Rabbi Asher b. Yehiel (known by the acronym: Rosh) notes:<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 43:3.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">One cannot add to what the Scholars enumerated.</span>
The identical conclusion ought to apply to a case of a husband’s mental dysfunctionality.
Moreover, Rabbi Yosef S. Elyashiv argues that given that there is a <i>halakhic</i> controversy as to whether one can coerce a mentally dysfunctional husband to give a <i>get</i>, constructive consent of the husband to give the <i>get</i> is nonexistent. Relying upon Rabbi Moshe Sofer’s classic position,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 1:116.</i> Rabbi Elyashiv contends that a husband’s constructive consent to a give a <i>get</i> is predicated upon the fact that all legists agree that in the given circumstance, a <i>get</i> ought to be coerced. Should one opinion disagree, one cannot speak of “a husband’s real will” to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:178. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 34–43.</i> Despite the view of Rabbis Sofer and Elyashiv as well as others,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Our interpretation of Rabbi Moshe Sofer’s posture resonates amongst many decisors. See <i>Sdei Hemed</i>, vol. 7, <i>Ma’arekhet Gerushin</i> 1:15; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei De’ah</i> (Litwin) 1:119; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak, EH</i> 1:2; <i>Teshuvot Dvar Yehoshua EH</i> 3:30; <i>Teshuvo</i>t <i>Seder Eliyahu</i> 13. Though R. Yitzhak Elhanan Spektor of Kovno does not explicitly endorse this position, nonetheless his argumentation in resolving two cases belies such an approach. See <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2:35, <i>anaf</i> 2 (9), <i>anaf</i> 5 (34). Cf. other decisors who contend that we do not employ <i>get</i> coercion where there is a <i>halakhic</i> doubt what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be. See <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema EH</i> 154:21.</i> there are authorities who will mandate coercion regarding a <i>get</i> recalcitrant mentally dysfunctional husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Yitzhak ben Meir, <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1; <i>Teshuvot Maharhash EH</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Ne’eman Shmuel</i> 66; <i>Teshuvot Mohr ve-Oholot EH</i> 10; <i>Dvar Yehoshua</i>, supra n. 82; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:80; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Yosef</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 8.</i> In sum, there is a debate as to whether a mentally dysfunctional husband may be coerced to give a <i>get</i> to his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For definitional guidance for determining the scope of mental dysfunction which is required in order to mete out <i>get</i> coercion, see <i>Divrei Yosef</i>, ibid.; Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu she-Kofin Le-hotzi</i>, Yerushalayim, 5773, 116–117.</i>
The second ground for divorce is the question of whether the acts of domestic violence perpetrated by Michael serve as justification for <i>get</i> coercion. One approach, which first appears in the writings of Ohr Zarua, Rabbeinu Simhah and is later espoused by others, is to issue a compulsion order in the wake of a husband who assaults and insults his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ohr Zarua</i> 3, <i>Bava Kama</i> 161; <i>Teshuvot Maharah Ohr Zarua</i> 127 in the name of Rabbeinu Simhah and Rabbeinu Menahem; <i>Beit Yosef Tur EH</i> 154 in the name of Rabbeinu Simhah; <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Rothenberg</i>, Prague ed., 927; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:693 (Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 7:477); <i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur EH</i> 154:16 in the name of Rabbi Shemaryah; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 88 in the name of Ri and Rabbeinu Tam; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 2:130; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer EH</i> 2:60; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 154:15; <i>Teshuvot u-Mitzur Devash EH</i> 10; <i>Teshuvot Hina ve-Hisda</i> 3, <i>Ketuvot</i> 77a. See also the opinion of a contemporary of Rabbi Karo, <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 154:24 and <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 154:18 in the name of Maharshal and <i>Teshuvot Noseh Ephod</i> 32.<br>Whether <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8 aligns himself with this view is subject to debate. See <i>Yad Aharon EH</i> 154; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:38; <i>Teshuvot va-Yomeir Yitzhak EH</i> 135.</i> A contemporary rationale for <i>get</i> coercion is articulated by an Israeli <i>beit din,</i> which teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:112–133.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The <i>halakhah</i> of <i>get</i> coercion is not due to the sins committed by the individual, but rather for the havoc wreaked in family life which is a result of the husband’s behavior and therefore the wife is entitled to a <i>get</i>.</span>
Responding to this posture of Ohr Zarua and Rabbeinu Simhah, Rabbi Yosef Karo in Beit Yosef demurs, stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 154:3.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">We cannot rely on their words . . . to coerce . . . since it is not mentioned by any one of the renowned authorities.</span>
In light of the view of the Beit Yosef’s predecessors who argue that since the Mishnah and/or Talmud failed to explicitly mention that spousal battery is a ground for divorce (<i>ilat gerushin</i>) which mandates <i>get</i> coercion, a <i>beit din</i> may not issue a compulsion order.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yevamot</i> 65b, s.v. <i>hu amar</i>; <i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 70a, s.v. <i>yotzi</i>; <i>Tosafot Yevamot</i> 64a, s.v. <i>yotzi</i>; <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 70a, s.v. <i>yotzi</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Yevamot</i> 6:11; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1 in the name of Ravyah, 43:3; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 138; <i>Sefer Meisharim</i>, <i>Netiv</i> 23, <i>Helek</i> 8 in the name of Rashba; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban, Ketuvot</i> 77a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 77a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva,</i> ad locum; <i>Ran on Alfasi, Ketuvot</i> 36a<i>; Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127; <i>Semag</i>, Positive Commandment 48; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 211; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:1331 (260); <i>SA EH</i> 154:5, 21; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA EH</i> 154:50, 65; <i>Teshuvot Be’er Sheva</i> 61; <i>Be’air ha-Golah, SA EH</i> 77:6; <i>Hazon Ish, Ketuvot</i> 69:23; R. Eliyahu ha-Levi, <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 10, 149.<br>In the wake of a debate whether <i>get</i> coercion can be mandated, one is prohibited from issuing a <i>get</i> compulsion order lest it be deemed a coerced <i>get</i> which is null and void. See <i>Tosafot Ketuvot</i> 70a; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra,</i> op. cit.; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 154:6.</i> And this view was subsequently established in Rabbi Karo’s Shulhan Arukh.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 154:21 in the name of <i>yesh omrim</i>.</i> Yet, Rabbi Karo concurs that a husband may be obligated to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 74 (end), citing Ramban’s responsum.</i>
Therefore, the rationale for the opposition to coercing a husband who is a batterer to give a <i>get</i> is based upon their understanding that the resultant <i>get</i> is deemed “a coerced <i>get</i>” (“a <i>get me’useh</i>”) and consequently it is invalid. Consequently, should the wife remarry relying upon this <i>get</i> and have children, the offspring would be labeled as <i>halakhic</i> bastards (<i>mamzrerim</i>). Since a <i>halakhic</i> bastard is the product of an incestuous relationship, the fact that the <i>get</i> was invalid means that in effect she was still married to her first husband when she had children with her “second marriage.”
In the wake of Beit Yosef’s posture, Darkhei Moshe rules:<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Moshe, Tur, EH</i> 154:17.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">I don’t see his words at all because it is worthwhile relying upon the <i>Geonim</i>, <i>a fortiori</i> given that Ramban and Maharam [also] agree in their responsa concerning assaulting a wife (that these are grounds to obligate a <i>get</i> – AYW) and they brought clear proofs to their words and logic agrees with them.<br>And the fact that it isn’t mentioned (the reason that spousal battery is not mentioned in the Talmud as a ground for a <i>get</i> – AYW), one possibly could say that it was obvious in their eyes . . . and it did not happen in their days (that there was a phenomenon of spousal battery – AYW). . . .</span>
Numerous decisors subscribe to Beit Yosef’s and Darkhei Moshe’s posture that one cannot coerce a husband who assaults his wife to give a <i>get</i> when he is mentally dysfunctional and physically abusive is a matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 3:888 (447), 4:157 (1228); <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 1:88; <i>Teshuvot Lehem Rav</i> 31; <i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 2:130; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Tzedek</i> 1:59; <i>Teshuvot Perah Matteh Aharon</i> 1:60; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Moshe</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Mo’hari ha-Levi</i> 9; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> (in manuscript) <i>EH</i> 55; <i>Teshuvot Mishneh Halakhot</i> 14:146; <i>Teshuvot Noseh ha-Ephod</i> 32:15.</i>
In sum, there are two grounds for divorce (<i>ilot gerushin</i>), namely that Michael was mentally dysfunctional and engaged in spousal battery. Whether there are grounds to coerce him to give a <i>get</i> is a matter of debate.
In terms of translating these two disputes in terms of doubts, we may state that there exist two <i>halakhic</i> doubts (<i>sefeikot de’dina</i>): one doubt is whether one can coerce a <i>get</i> when dealing with a mentally dysfunctional husband, and a second <i>halakhic</i> uncertainty is whether one may coerce a husband who assaults his wife to give her a <i>get</i>. The doubt regarding domestic violence emerged later than the doubt dealing with his psychological state. In other words, initially Michael suffered from a psychological disorder and subsequently he engaged in battery.
Lest one challenge the position that we are dealing with a double doubt due to the fact that both doubts did not emerge at the same time, clearly there is a debate as to whether two <i>factual</i> doubts must emerge simultaneously. However, in our case when dealing with a <i>halakhic</i> doubt, the two doubts may emerge one after the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei Rabbi Akiva Eiger, Berakhot</i> 2a, <i>Yevamot</i> 113b; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hesed EH</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4, <i>OH</i> 43 (4), 5 <i>OH</i> 6 (5), 6 <i>YD</i> 23 (6), 6 <i>EH</i> 4 (5), 7 <i>OH</i> 28 (6).</i>
As we examined in the earlier portion of this monograph,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">See supra chapter 3.</i> there is a Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din ruling which addresses our issue.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 917387/1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, November 17, 2013.</i> As we stated earlier:
<span class="poetry indentAll">The aforementioned decision of the Yerushalayim Regional <i>Beit Din</i> focuses upon a suit for divorce filed by a woman on grounds of repulsion of her husband following a verdict in the civil court that convicted the husband of obscene acts carried out on minors. In its argumentation, the panel addresses whether there is a basis for coercing a <i>get</i> when a wife advances a plea of “he is repulsive to me” accompanied by a clear pretext. Though there were a few decisors who subscribe to <i>get</i> coercion under these circumstances, the majority reject this view. Moreover, some authorities would not even obligate a <i>get</i> in these circumstances. Secondly, the <i>beit din</i> explores whether there would be grounds to coerce a <i>get</i> due to the fact that the husband acted deceitfully when he refrained from disclosing to his wife prior to their marriage that he was a convicted pedophile. Here again, upon <i>beit din</i> review; it was found that the propriety of a <i>get</i> compulsion order was equally a matter of <i>halakhic</i> debate.</span>
Without elucidation, the panel posits that since a doubt regarding the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion entails a biblical doubt, therefore we are dealing with a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt on a biblical level, where a <i>get</i> may be coerced.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A doubt involving the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion under particular circumstances is to be construed as a biblical doubt. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban Yevamot</i> 46a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, ad locum; <i>Pithei Teshuvah SA EH</i> 70:3 in the name of Hatam Sofer and Beit Meir. In other words, if there is a <i>halakhic</i> controversy whether to coerce a <i>get</i>, due to the existence of a <i>halakhic</i> doubt we refrain from coercing a <i>get</i>. See <i>Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 70a; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 154:21. Should a <i>get</i> be coerced under such conditions, the <i>get</i> is null and void.<br>The assumption is that if <i>Halakhah</i> mandates the <i>get</i> ought to be coerced we are dealing with a situation that the wife can no longer live with her spouse and consequently, she is not bound (<i>shi’bud</i>) to him. As Dayan Izirer notes,<br>“The nullification of the servitude empowers her with the right to be liberated from all of the husband’s servitudes, including the primary servitude that she is prohibited due to him to others and she is preempted to receive all her marital rights because she was married to him.” See H. Izirer, “The Duty of the <i>Get</i> and Maintenance to a Rebellious Wife who is Repulsed by Him,” (Hebrew) 2 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> 64, 99 (5754). See supra, p. 65, n. 45.<br>In short, the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion entails a biblical doubt regarding the presumption that she is a married woman. Whether there are grounds to coerce a <i>get</i> is a biblical issue, but the actual implementation of the mechanism of <i>get</i> coercion is a rabbinic enactment, see Izirer, op. cit., 102–103. Therefore, a doubt regarding whether to apply coercion entails a biblical doubt whether there is a basis to coerce a <i>get</i>. Two contemporary decisors mandate <i>get</i> coercion due to the invoking of a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt (<i>sefek sefeika</i>) comprised of either three or four <i>halakhic</i> doubts. See <i>Teshuvot Osher Hanan</i> 4, <i>EH</i> 77 (10); <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18 (4).<br>Whether one can void the status of “a married woman” in case of a compounded biblical doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>), see the debate in <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 167; Y. Goldberg, <i>Elu She-kofin Le-hotzi</i>, 51, n. 46, 131, n. 18.</i>
As we mentioned, it is clear from reading the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din’s decision that the fact that most authorities rule against the propriety of <i>get</i> coercion prevents the invoking of a double doubt, and said conclusion is also supported by other authorities. However, it is our understanding that Bahag, Rif, Rambam, Rosh (possibly), Ra’avad, Semag, Ramah, Meiri, Ritva, Rabbi Yosef Karo (possibly), Rabbi David ibn Zimra, Rabbi Mordekhai ha-Levi, Hida, Rabbi Ya’akov Emden and Rabbi Ovadiah Yosef all rule that under certain conditions (e.g. a doubt as to what the <i>Halakhah</i> ought to be), a Torah doubt (a <i>sefeika de’oraita</i>) ought to be resolved stringently on a <i>rabbinic</i> level. In pursuance to this tradition that we are dealing with a rabbinic matter, there is no requirement that the double doubt represent that the authorities are equally divided (i.e. <i>shakul</i>) to determine whether one may coerce a <i>get</i> in order to free the wife without a <i>get</i>. Even if one side of the doubt reflects a minority opinion, the double <i>halakhic</i> doubt will be effective. Finally, given that the rule of “following the majority” is applicable only to resolving issues within the confines of a <i>beit din</i> proceeding, one cannot speak of a majority opinion and minority opinion concerning intergenerational disputes. As such, the requirement that the decisors be equally divided regarding a pending issue is not mandated regarding a double doubt.
Moreover, even according to Rashba and others that a doubt by biblical law ought to be resolved stringently on a biblical level, that does not necessarily mean that the application of a double uncertainty will be ineffective. The consequent leniency associated with the implementation of a double uncertainty is due to the rule of following the majority (“<i>aharei rabbim le’hattot</i>”). The existence of one doubt creates a situation of 50/50 uncertainty (“<i>ke-mehtza al mehtza dami</i>”), and then the second doubt creates a majority which results in treating the matter leniently on a Torah level. In accordance with many legists, this approach is the dominant understanding as to why the employment of a double doubt will be effective. Given the above lines of reasoning, there is no prerequisite that the doubt must be even, namely that the arbiters would be equally divided concerning the propriety of the issuance of a <i>get</i> compulsion judgment prior to employing the rule of the double doubt. As such, relying upon a well-trodden tradition (<i>mesorah)</i> of Ashkenazic as well as Sephardic legists that sanctions the employment of a double uncertainty concerning a matter of personal status (<i>ishut</i>), the invoking of the double doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion under certain conditions will trump the presumption that she is a married woman and will reinstate the original presumption of being a single woman (a <i>penuyah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>halakhic</i> underpinnings of invoking a double doubt, see supra chapter 2 and for the foundations of invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion, see supra chapter 3.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, in our case, we have a double doubt which is grounded in the two <i>halakhic</i> debates whether there are grounds for <i>get</i> coercion when dealing with a husband who is mentally dysfunctional and engaging in domestic violence. Relying upon the precedential opinion of the Yerushalayim Beit Din,<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 94 and supra n. 95 (end).</i> we have grounds to void the marriage based upon invoking a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding <i>get</i> coercion. In short, in the wake of a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, we deem the matter as “an hour of emergency” (<i>sh’at ha-dehak</i>) and a <i>priori</i> (<i>le-hatehillah</i>) we can void the marriage based upon a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sedei Hemed</i>, Ma’arekhet Get 30(6) and ha-Samekh 30(3) in the name of Rashba and Radvaz.</i>

Epilogue

In the wake of rabbi or a <i>beit din’s</i> inability to persuade a husband to give a <i>get</i> to his wife, there are various techniques, one of them dating back to early thirteenth century Ashkenaz, which empower <i>halakhic</i> authorities under certain conditions to free a wife to remarry without the issuance of a <i>get</i> by her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See this monograph and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality</i>, vol. 3, 134–176, 231–269, 294–333; vol. 4, 143–297.</i> Yet as we have noted elsewhere,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3 supra n. 1, 14–16, 134, 140, n. 8.</i> there are decisors who are reluctant to void a marriage.
Addressing a situation of an <i>agunah</i> who requests that her marriage be voided due to the fact that both witnesses during the <i>kiddushin</i> were invalid, as they violated the Shabbat and committed other sins, following implicitly earlier rulings, Rabbi Aharon Walkin, who resided in Pinsk-Karlin, Ukraine, responds to such a petition in the most trenchant terms:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 1:81.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">It is improper in my eyes to adopt this path, to criticize the act of <i>kiddushin</i> eight years after the wedding. . . . You must understand that such advice can destroy the purity of Israel . . . to multiply <i>mamzerim</i> in the midst of a holy nation; the marital bond from now will not be sustained. . . . And the stringency of being married will be nullified. . . . Therefore, eight years after the wedding . . . we should not inquire for guidance to void the marriage. . . .</span>
His view is by far not one of “a lone ranger”. Concerned about “the slippery slope,” Rabbi Hayyim Berlin, the son of the renowned Rabbi Naftali Tzvi Berlin, lambasts those arbiters who engage in voiding a marriage (<i>bittul kiddushin</i>) via invoking a clear expectation (<i>umdana de’mukha</i>), by stating the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Nishmat Hayyim</i> 87. See also <i>Teshuvot Nishmat Hayyim</i> 128–129. For other arbiters who advances the same argument for opposing the implementation of a clear expectation as a means to void a marriage, see <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov EH</i> 85; <i>Teshuvot Meil Tzadakah</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Matteh Aharon</i> 1:41.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">One cannot imagine the damage and the breaches that can emerge from this in our dissolute generation . . . they will begin comparing one matter to another one – in the beginning, they will permit based upon <i>umdanot</i> (assessed expectations) . . . they will permit (to remarry) every wife whose husband who has traveled overseas to America or Africa. . . . And afterwards they will permit . . . if a person will promise a certain amount of <i>nedunya</i> (dowry) and subsequently it will be discovered . . . that he cannot keep his promise, they will say, “in her mind she would not have submitted to this marriage” . . . and <i>mamzerim</i> will multiply in Israel.</span>
Finally, in situations where a husband fails to disclose to his prospective spouse prior to the marriage that he possesses a major defect, such as being impotent or being gay, these authorities will direct the husband to give a <i>get</i> on rabbinic grounds lest people would err, thinking that a married woman leaves her husband without the execution of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 73b; <i>Lehem Mishneh, MT</i>, <i>Ishut</i> 4:10; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 27; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 1, <i>EH</i> 24.</i> In other words, they will not know that the voiding of the marriage in the particular case was due to an error and therefore that execution of a <i>get</i> is otherwise required. Clearly, based upon this line of reasoning, some decisors may equally oppose utilizing the techniques of the double doubt as well as “a clear expectation” (<i>umdana de’mukha</i>) of the wife to void a marriage involving a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband.
In the wake of this strident opposition to voiding marriages, it is unsurprising to encounter today numerous rabbis and rabbinical courts throughout the world that endorse this <i>halakhic</i> tradition, and staunchly refuse to void marriages. The net result is that there are many women, whether they are Orthodox, Conservative, Reform or unaffiliated Jews, who remain victims of <i>get</i> recalcitrance. Though many of these women remain committed to their values and continue to live for years exhibiting fidelity to their estranged spouses, regretfully others become irreligious and engage in promiscuity.
In light of the Orthodox rabbis and rabbinic courts who adopt the posture that voiding a marriage is not an option for addressing the plight of the <i>agunah</i>, what can be done to deal with the plight of the <i>agunah</i>? As we will see, both the rabbis as well as the rabbinical courts may be capable of improving the state of affairs, namely, the husband’s willingness to give the <i>get or</i> the wife’s readiness to receive a <i>get</i> without deliberating as to whether there is a possibility to void a marriage.
For many years, we have observed situations where a recalcitrant spouse chooses to condition the giving or the acceptance of a <i>get</i> upon a prior resolution of all end of marriage matters such as dividing marital assets, parenting and custody arrangements, child support and the execution of a civil divorce. Regretfully, on at least two occasions, there is one <i>beit din</i> located in Monsey, New York which has allegedly given the dispensation of 100 rabbis (<i>heter me’ah rabbanim</i>) enabling a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband to marry a second wife. In both situations, a <i>get</i> has been deposited at the <i>beit din</i> and would be given to the wife on the condition that the husband’s demands regarding custody and renumeration of hundreds of thousands dollars would be met. Given that in both cases, the wife wants to receive her <i>get</i>, in accordance with the views of Rabbis Elyashiv and Feinstein one cannot place any obstacles in her receiving her sought after <i>get</i> and consequently there is no basis for such a dispensation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yi’sa Yosef, EH</i> 8; <i>Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 4:3.</i> Such conduct raises a variety of <i>halakhic</i> issues which we will address here in the context of <i>get</i> recalcitrance on the part of the husband.
To understand the ramifications of conditioning the giving of a <i>get</i>, let me share a few cases that I have encountered in recent years in my work as a <i>dayan</i> (a rabbinic arbitrator).
A couple has been separated for over eighteen years. Approximately a dozen years ago, a civil divorce was executed. To this day, the defendant has made the giving of a <i>get</i> conditional upon the withdrawal of the plaintiff’s threat to file a claim in civil court for the defendant’s failure to pay alimony by executing a post-divorce agreement that states that the parties mutually agreed that all of their end of marriage matters have been resolved. Given that the plaintiff refuses to accede to his request, she remained an <i>agunah</i> until we recently freed her. Such egregious conduct is not an isolated incident. In another case, after ten years of litigation in civil court followed by the subsequent issuance of a civil divorce, the wife then received her <i>get</i>. The delay in resolving the end of marriage matters such as the division of marital assets and alimony was due to the fact that on numerous occasions, the husband changed his attorney. In other scenarios, albeit very common ones, from the time of the onset of litigation in a civil court, the process generally takes one to two years to be completed before a civil divorce has been executed, and only then does the wife receive her <i>get</i>.
