During this period of litigation, many American rabbinical courts do not address whether there is a duty of the husband to give a g<i>et</i> to his wife. <i>Halakhah</i> recognizes two distinct grounds for obligating a <i>get</i>. Firstly, as we know, whether a husband is <i>obligated</i> to give a <i>get,</i> generally speaking, hinges upon whether there exists an <i>ilat gerushin,</i> a ground for divorce which would require a man to give a <i>get</i>. These grounds for divorce may be subdivided into two categories. One category is a wife’s physical defect such as the inability to conduct conjugal relations with her husband, such as being afflicted by a contagious and/or dangerous disease or by dint of her revulsion of his body odor which is linked to his occupation.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 77a; <i>Yevamot</i> 65b.</i> On the other hand, a husband’s inappropriate behavior may serve as a justification for divorce. For example, spousal rape, refusal to cohabitate with his wife, physical and/or emotional divorce of his wife, or refusal to financially support her may serve under certain conditions as a claim for coercing or obligating a husband to give a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 76:1, 154:1, 6; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:3.</i> Secondly, according to various decisors, a <i>get</i> ought to be given in the wake of a couple being separated for over a year or eighteen months where there are no prospects for marital reconciliation (<i>shalom bayit</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbeinu Yeruham, <i>Sefer Meisharim</i> Netiv 23, Helek 8; <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, Bayit 3, s.v. u’le’ravha; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:112; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh EH</i> 8; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe YD</i> 4:15 (2). Should a <i>beit din</i> obligate a <i>get</i> based upon a marital separation of one year or eighteen months and should a husband fail to comply with the ruling or a divorce judgment based upon one of the grounds for divorce, the wife is to be identified as a <i>mesurevet get</i> and therefore a chained woman (an <i>agunah</i>). In such a situation, the identification of the woman as an <i>agunah</i> may serve as grounds to compel or obligate a husband to give a <i>get</i>. See this writer’s, <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 328–333.<br>Some contend that even if a particular <i>get</i> that was received by the wife poses certain <i>halakhic</i> issues and the husband demands money from his wife in order to execute a second <i>get,</i> since there is a fear that she will be without a <i>get</i> “many days,” she is to be labeled an <i>agunah</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua EH</i> 80; <i>Teshuvot Simhat Yom Tov</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:251 (1). In other words, the absence of having a <i>get</i> for a short period of time may label the woman as an <i>agunah</i>. In other words, though the absence of having a <i>get</i> for a short period of time may label the woman as an <i>agunah</i>, nevertheless it is a label bereft of any <i>halakhic</i> consequences regarding leniency.</i> Numerous contemporary Israeli rabbinical courts adopt this position.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Rabbanayim</i> (hereinafter: <i>PDR</i>) 7:112–113, 11:364, 12:193–203, 13:267, 14:183, 194; 19:52; File no. 4276-63, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, November 11, 2003; File no. 3599-22-1, Tiberias Regional Beit Din, <i>Plonit v. Ploni</i>, November 24, 2004 (R. Yoezer Ariel’s opinion); File No. 7479-21-1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, November 18, 2007; File no. 8801-21-1, Tel Aviv Regional Beit Din, June 24, 2009; File no. 289477/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, December 28, 2010; File no. 842462/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, January 16, 2012; File no. 248769/3, Netanya Regional Beit Din, <i>Ploni v. Plonit</i>, May 16, 2012; File no. 587739-6, Haifa Regional Beit Din, July 17, 2012; File no. 289799-1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, <i>Ploni v. Plonit</i>, January 2, 2013; File no. 862233-1, Tiberias Regional Beit Din, <i>Plonit v. Ploni</i>, January 8, 2013; File no. 901912/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, May 7, 2013; File no. 8426111, Ashdod Regional Beit Din, <i>Plonit v. Ploni</i>, June 10, 2013 (R. Avraham Atiyah’s opinion); File no. 284462-9, Netanya Regional Beit Din, May 14, 2014; File no. 764231-6, Haifa Regional Beit Din, May 25, 2014; File no. 869531/2, Netanya Regional Beit Din, July 31, 2014; File no. 849440/19, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, July 14, 2015; File no. 847350/3, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, July 27, 2015; File no. 146033/11, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, June 21, 2016; File no. 1066559/1, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, October 30, 2016; File no. 1043346/1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, May 8, 2017; File no. 865704/1, Tzfat Regional Beit Din, May 8, 2017; File no. 1011050/3, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, October 24, 2017; File no. 1083672/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, January 25, 2018; File no. 1063300/10, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, April 13, 2018; File no. 905329/1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, March 31, 2019; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18 (13); <i>Teshuvot Ateret Devorah</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 89.</i> Clearly, these American rabbinical courts may be espousing the opinion of others who argue that “a dead marriage” <i>per se</i> will not serve as grounds for obligating the husband to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:144–145; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Shmuel</i> 3:145; <i>Teshuvot ha-Gaon Avraham Herzog EH</i> 154; A. Herzog, <i>Pesakim u-Ketavim</i> 7:133–134; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 6:42, 17:52; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 19; <i>PDR</i> 1:162, 4:112, 7:108–109, 112–113, 9:200, 211–212, 10:173, 11:362, 364; 12:206, 13:360, 14:183, 193; File no. 4827-21-2, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, (R. Izirer’s opinion), July 3, 2005; File no. 172-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, February 18, 2009; File no. 1750-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, (R. Izirer’s opinion), May 5, 2009; File no. 290506/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, November 21, 2010; File no. 77890/5, Be’air Sheva Regional Beit Din, May 29, 2014; File no. 698719/15, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, July 26, 2015; File no. 1083672/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, January 25, 2018 (a supporting argument); File no. 1147208/2, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, July 2, 2018.</i> As such, they generally refrain from issuing a divorce judgment based upon irretrievable marital breakdown. Yet, generally they equally refrain from rendering a divorce decision stemming from a ground for divorce.
Even assuming a <i>beit din</i> would hand down a divorce decision that obligates a husband to give a <i>get,</i> and, assuming the husband readily agrees, can a <i>get</i> be properly executed while resolution of other end of marriage matters are still pending? In other words, the emerging issue is whether all the end of marriage issues, such as awarding the value of the <i>ketubah</i>, parenting arrangements, child support and the division of marital assets must be resolved prior to the execution of the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For a lively exchange regarding this matter, see R. Menashe Klein and R. Shimon Ya’acobi, “The giving of a <i>get</i> and financial arrangements: which precedes the other?” (Hebrew), 22 <i>Tehumin</i> (5762), 157–183.</i> Many American rabbinical courts (<i>battei din</i>), as well as American Orthodox rabbis, will counsel their clientele and constituency respectively that a <i>get</i> must be given only after all end of marriage issues have been resolved and/or a civil divorce has been executed. Consequently, it is unsurprising to encounter situations such as the ones described above in which a wife remains <i>halakhically</i> married to her spouse despite the fact that there has been longstanding marital breakdown and separation, a period marked by the absence of conjugal relations, no spousal support, and no prospects for marital reconciliation accompanied by years of divorce litigation. Yet, relying upon the aforesaid rabbinic counsel under such circumstances, the woman has no realistic expectation that the <i>get</i> will be forthcoming in an expeditious fashion.
The question is whether there is a basis for such a <i>halakhic</i> posture. Relying upon Mahari Mintz’s guidelines for executing a <i>get</i>, Rema states:<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema SA EH</i> 154, <i>Seder ha-Get</i> 81.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And the scholar who is preparing the execution of the <i>get</i> says to her: “Please know that you will be divorced with this <i>get</i> from your husband.” And the rabbi will inquire after the <i>ketubah</i> (the husband paying the value of the <i>ketubah</i> – AYW) that the husband will return the (value of the – AYW) <i>ketubah</i> or she will waive her right to it lest they start quarrelling due to the (value of the – AYW) <i>ketubah</i> with the result that the husband will say, “on this condition I didn’t divorce her.”</span>
As such, given that that the <i>get</i> was given in error (a <i>get mut’eh</i> – AYW), the consequence will be a retroactive annulment of the <i>get</i>. Other authorities, albeit only a few, agree with this position.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam of Lublin</i> 122; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov EH</i> 34; <i>Levush EH</i> 123–125; <i>Teshuvot Amek Sheilah EH</i> 116; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Yoav EH</i> 25.<br>Other decisors adopt this approach on the condition that the husband was misled prior to the giving the <i>get</i> and he was under the impression at that time that everything was to materialize as mutually agreed upon. See <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama, <i>EH</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Yo’av EH</i> 25; <i>Erekh Shai EH</i> 134; <i>Teshuvot Malbushei Yom Tov</i> 2 <i>EH</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Hesed le-Avraham</i>, Mahadura Kama <i>EH</i> 42.</i>
Addressing this minority view, Taz notes that it was rejected:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taz, SA EH</i> 145:6.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">And it is not on the side of truth and that nearly all rabbis have already disagreed with it, and forcefully rejected it.</span>
Explaining this view, Rabbi Ya’akov Ettlinger writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 144.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">Since not everyone is versed in <i>Halakhah</i>, Rabbi Mintz argues that there will always be slander if the husband shouts that he divorced her in error and therefore the <i>get</i> is null and her children will be <i>halakhic</i> bastards (<i>mamzerim</i>), even though the truth is otherwise.</span>
Based upon the fear of a wrongful <i>get</i>, we can understand the position that all end of marriage issues ought to be resolved prior to executing a <i>get</i>.
However, the majority of authorities argue, explicitly or implicitly, that the <i>get</i> procedure (the <i>seder ha-get</i>) entails a husband’s nullification of all prior conditions (<i>bittul moda’ot</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 145:16; <i>Teshuvot Ma’sat Binyamin</i> 76; <i>Teshuvot Bah ha-Hadashot</i> 90–91; Sema and Levush, <i>Bah ha-Hadashot, ibid. Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek EH</i> 290:1; <i>Noda be-Yehudah</i>, supra n. 14; <i>Beit Meir EH</i> 145:9; <i>Avnei Mi’luim</i> 10:2; <i>Teshuvot Mahariz Enzel</i> 81; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 1:84; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 145:30; <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 154. For additional decisors who ascribe to this position, see <i>Teshuvot Ateret Devorah</i> 2:86.<br>For the requirement of nullifying all pre-existing conditions prior to a husband’s giving of the <i>get,</i> see <i>SA EH</i> 134:1–3; <i>Rema</i> ad locum.<br>Absent a clear stipulation <i>during this procedure</i> that the <i>get</i> is conditional upon a wife’s compliance of a particular condition(s), a husband is preempted from contending that it was an erroneous divorce due to the fact that his wife reneged on an earlier commitment concretized in a divorce agreement, or that had he known that a particular matter which was resolved after the execution of a <i>get</i> was to his detriment, he never would have divorced her. See <i>Tosafot Gittin</i> 46a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash ha-Hadashot</i> 10; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheikh</i> 78; <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 25; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak EH</i> 2:37 (4); <i>Teshuvot Ridvaz</i> 1:83; <i>Teshuvot Maharit EH</i> 13. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharam of Lublin</i> 122; <i>Teshuvot Mishkenot Ya’akov EH</i> 34.</i> Consequently, there is no basis for a husband claiming that it was an erroneous divorce due to the fact that his wife reneged on an earlier commitment concretized in a divorce agreement, or that had he known that a particular matter which was resolved after the execution of a <i>get</i> was to his detriment, he never would have divorced her.
Even if one adopts the majority opinion that opposes the retroactive annulment of a <i>get</i> due to a breach of the divorce agreement or the resolution of a matter to the husband’s detriment after the execution of the <i>get</i>, there is an additional reason that all matters must be resolved before the giving of a <i>get</i>. One arbiter states that with the advent of the execution of a Jewish divorce, “the husband and wife should not be bound by any connection or condition in the world,”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Mintz</i> 123.</i> which has been understood to mean that neither spouse should file any claim after the execution of the <i>get,</i> lest the couple remain susceptible to committing a sexual prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 27b; <i>MT, Issurei Bi’ah</i> 21:2, 27, Rabbi Menashe Klein, supra n. 12, 171; <i>Teshuvot Mishneh Halakhot</i>, Mahadura Tinyana 357.</i>
Upon a closer scrutiny of the <i>Halakhah</i>, we encounter a more nuanced approach to how a divorced couple ought to conduct themselves. On one hand, to avoid the engagement in intimate relations or even a suspicion thereof, an ex-husband shall refrain from living with her in the same courtyard, and to avoid social interaction, the couple ought not to proceed to a <i>beit din</i> proceeding together.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 119:7, 9; <i>Beit Shmuel,</i> ad locum 17<i>.</i> Nevertheless, if the husband appoints an agent to pursue a particular claim, no prohibition has been violated. See <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 119:8.<br>For the prohibition of a divorced couple to reside in the same apartment or home, see <i>Tur EH</i> 119 and <i>SA EH</i> 119:7–11.</i> However, according to certain opinions, should he enter a home or her house by chance there is no prohibition, since he is not living there or interacting with her. And some adopt a stricter opinion, lest such meetings lead to the engagement in prohibitions.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 119, <i>Taz, SA EH</i> 119:19. For decisors who sanction a divorcee’s entry into his ex-wife’s home for two or three hours for the purpose of discussing business matters, see <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 243; <i>Rema</i>, supra n. 20; <i>Helkat Mehokeik, SA EH</i> 119:24; <i>Pri Hadesh EH</i> 119:22. Some interpret Rema’s position that such entry is contingent upon the presence of the wife’s new husband or other people. See <i>Mahazit ha-Shekel, SA EH</i> 119, <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 119:31, and <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 91.</i>
On the other hand, to minimize interaction with one’s ex-spouse, should a wife have lent money to her ex-husband, she should appoint an agent to demand its return.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">SA and Rema, <i>SA EH</i> 119:8.</i> Similarly, an ex-husband may support his ex-wife on the condition that he refrains from interaction with her and that he appoints an agent to implement support measures.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema, SA EH</i> 119:8.</i>
Consequently, it is unsurprising that there will be instances when the value of the <i>ketubah</i> will be paid to the wife <i>after</i> the <i>get</i> has been executed. As we know, accompanying a decision to obligate a <i>get</i> there is a judgment to obligate the husband to pay the value of the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1192; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva, Ketuvot</i> 76a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Teshuvot Maharalbah</i> 33; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 3:102; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra SA EH</i> 154:69; <i>Teshuvot Beit Meir</i> 39.</i> Though numerous legists argue that the value of the <i>ketubah</i> ought to be paid prior to executing the <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1192, 1254; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1, 3:227; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 100:24, 119:6; <i>Helkat Mehokeik SA EH</i> 119:5; <i>Pri Hadash SA EH</i> 119:6; <i>Teshuvot Yismah Lev EH</i> 25 in the name of 26 authorities; <i>Hazon Ish EH</i> 69:13.</i> there are decisors who allow the <i>ketubah</i> to remain a debt which can be paid by the husband after the <i>get</i> is executed.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1; <i>Beit Yosef, Bedek ha-Bayit, Tur EH</i> 119; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 119:6.</i> Others argue that if the husband is giving the <i>get</i> voluntarily then the value of the <i>ketubah</i> must be paid prior to the divorce. However, if the <i>beit din</i> is obligating him to give a <i>get</i> then the value of the <i>ketubah</i> may be paid after the execution of the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Tashbetz</i> 4, <i>Hut ha-Meshullash</i> 1:4; <i>Yad Aharon</i>, ha-Gahot Beit Yosef 4; <i>Helkat Mehokeik SA EH</i> 119:5; <i>Get Pashut</i> 119:18; <i>Pri Hadash, EH</i> 119:6(8); <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 119:11–13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 5, <i>EH</i> 1. Cf<i>. Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 119:6.</i> Finally, in a situation of a second marriage of spouses who despise each other, when there are no prospects for marital reconciliation and each one wants to be divorced, one may rely upon those authorities who argue that divorce ought to occur immediately and the value of the <i>ketubah</i> may be paid after the couple is <i>halakhically</i> divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 119:6; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 3:566; <i>Torot Emet</i> 119:6; <i>Teshuvot Lev Meivin EH</i> 116; <i>Teshuvot va-Yomeir Yitzhak EH</i> 179.</i> In sum, under certain circumstances a husband may pay the value of the <i>ketubah</i> after the execution of the <i>get</i>.
In contemporary times, a cursory review of some of the rabbinical court judgments handed down by the courts under the Israeli Chief Rabbinate will show that in fact divorce judgments are rendered without being contingent upon a prior resolution of the outstanding financial issues and parenting arrangements of the divorcing couples. Regardless of whether the claims are being dealt with in <i>beit din</i> or in civil court, the <i>beit din</i> issued decisions which recommend, obligate or coerce the giving of the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Collection of the Rabbinical Court Decisions of the Chief Rabbinate in Israel, ed. Z. Warhaftig, 97; <i>PDR</i> 1:129, 4:68, 9:94; File no. 61/82, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, 18 Sivan 5762 (unpublished decision); File no. 1-21-5035, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, January 21, 2005; File no. 1-21043387083, Tiberias Regional Beit Din, 28 Iyar 5764 (unpublished decision); File no. 1-21-022290027, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, 12 Ellul 5764 (unpublished decision); File no. 47126/9, Ashkelon Regional Beit Din, June 18, 2012; File no. 289160/5, Netanya Regional Beit Din, September 19, 2012; File no. 901912/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, May 7, 2013; File no. 927170/1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, July 11, 2014; File no. 965579/2, Netanya Regional Beit Din, July 23, 2015; File no. 514847/9, Haifa Regional Beit Din, December 28, 2015; File no. 1097040/10, Haifa Regional Beit Din, November 6, 2017; File no. 8293/5, Ashdod Regional Beit Din, February 18, 2018; File no. 1103694/2, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, January 6, 2019; File no. 905329/1, supra n. 10; File no. 220814/10, Petah Tikva Regional Beit Din, March 27, 2019; File no. 1201519/5, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, April 7, 2019; File no. 1150841/6. Haifa Regional Beit Din, July 3, 2019. For additional Israeli rabbinic court rulings, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 61, n. 19.<br>For understanding these different types of divorce judgments, see infra n. 36.</i> In fact, on January 26, 2020, I was invited to listen to five divorce cases at the Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din. In the midst of one of the cases where the parties were exchanging arguments regarding an end of a marriage issue, the <i>beit din</i> allotted time to execute the <i>get</i> procedure (<i>seder haget</i>) for this couple. By the end of the session, the discussion of the end of marriage matter by the parties was finished and the <i>get</i> had been given to the wife.
Rather than advising divorcing couples that the arrangement of the <i>get</i> may await the resolution of all their monetary issues either in a <i>beit din</i> or in civil court (<i>halakhically</i> sanctioned – <i>heter ar’kaot</i>) and the issuance of a civil divorce, American rabbinical courts and rabbis ought to follow the approach that once it is clear that there is a <i>halakhic</i> basis to give a <i>get</i>, its execution ought to transpire, and that any financial matters and parenting arrangements will be addressed afterwards.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See the addendum. Various contemporary <i>dayanim</i> have aptly noted that parenting arrangements that entail a third party’s interest, namely those of a child, and consequently may not serve as a reason to delay the execution of a <i>get</i> which focuses upon claims which directly relate to a divorcing spouse, such as the value of the <i>ketubah</i> and the division of marital assets. See S. Landesman, “Can a husband who is obligated to grant a divorce impose conditions?” (Hebrew), 2 <i>Divrei Mishpat</i> 145<i>,</i> 151–152; S. Daichovsky, “A husband who makes the granting of a divorce contingent on cancellation of his previous obligations,” (Hebrew), 26 <i>Tehumin</i> 149, 157 (2005); File no. 029612306-68-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, July 17, 2007, <i>ha-Din veha-Dayan</i>, gilyon 19, 4–5; File no. 863382/4, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, unpublished decision, November 9, 2013.<br>For an extensive analysis of the propriety of a conditional divorce in the wake of a <i>beit din</i> obligating a <i>get</i>, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 55–81. See also <i>Iggerot Moshe YD</i> 4:15. In recent years, Rabbi Feinstein’s posture has been subscribed to by numerous Israeli rabbinical court decisions. For one of the most recent endorsements, see File no. 975408/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, April 28, 2019.</i>
In effect, the American Orthodox rabbinic network ought to adopt the protocols employed by Israel’s Chief Rabbinate rabbinical court which has been described in the following fashion:<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbi Shimon Ya’acobi</i>, supra n. 12, 160.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">The common practice in the rabbinical courts in Israel is that before the giving of a <i>get,</i> the <i>beit din</i> which executes the <i>get</i> (the <i>mesadeir ha-get</i> – AYW) informs the husband that he should know that there is no connection between the financial matters which were resolved and concretized in an agreement which was signed and reviewed by the <i>beit din</i> . . . and the <i>get</i>. And the husband should be aware that if the wife breaches the entire agreement or portions of it, he still is giving the <i>get</i> voluntarily . . . unconditionally and he cannot say (due to the breach – AYW) I have not divorced her . . . Only after he understands and affirms his agreement, the <i>beit din</i> executes the <i>get</i>.<br>The rabbinical courts make every effort to persuade the parties to resolve all the monetary issues and children (parenting arrangements – AYW) prior to the <i>get</i>. However, there are instances where it is impossible (to finalize these matters – AYW). . . . In such cases, the <i>beit din</i> agrees that each party shall retain its right to file a claim, and they warn the husband that he is giving the <i>get</i> unconditionally even if it emerges that he erred. Namely, a claim that he intended to submit against his wife was ultimately rejected in a proceeding which took place after the <i>get,</i> or a claim was advanced by the wife which he thought she would never succeed according to <i>Halakhah</i> (or secular law if the claim is occurring in a civil court – AYW), yet she won the suit. . . .<br>This “etched in stone” (“<i>nehe’retzet</i>” – AYW) determination that all financial matters are to be completed prior to the <i>get</i> is a stringency that may potentially lead to a leniency. . . . Delaying the arrangement of the <i>get</i> by a <i>beit din</i> when the parties are agreeable in order to wait until the monetary claims and children are completed . . . will cause many stumbling-blocks of remaining a married woman (potential of incestuous relationships – AYW) and God forbid the proliferation of bastards. And this is when the <i>beit din</i> is the one that delays the <i>get,</i> while the husband stands and screams that he is willing to give a <i>get</i> unconditionally.</span>
In the aforementioned case of the couple who was separated for eighteen years,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See supra chapter 4E.</i> the husband was a secular Jew, who was only willing to give a <i>get</i> conditional upon the withdrawal of the plaintiff’s financial claims in the civil court. The fact that he was separated from his wife since 1998 and a civil divorce was executed in 2006 did not propel him to date to give a <i>get</i> to his wife. Since he is irreligious, he had no interest in having the matter of the <i>get</i> adjudicated in a <i>beit din</i> setting. Regretfully, even some divorcing husbands who identify themselves as being members of the Torah-observant Jewish community have equally refused to accede to their wives’ requests to address the matter of the <i>get</i> even after a civil divorce has been executed. As such, we need to execute the <i>get</i> prior to resolving the end of marriage issues lest we continue to experience <i>get</i> recalcitrance even after the execution of a civil divorce!
Invoking a Mishnah and a Talmud passage found in Tractate Ketuvot,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 108b-109a.</i> post-Talmudic arbiters have endorsed this position, namely that end of marriage issues may be resolved after the giving of a <i>get.</i> As we know, <i>halakhic</i> engagement (<i>kiddushin</i>) entails a promise between a Jewish man and a Jewess to contract a marriage. One of the consequences of this engagement is the establishment of a personal status which remains until the death of either spouse or their divorce. As such, should the marriage fail to materialize, a divorce is mandated.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA EH</i> 26:3.</i>
That being said, if one promises money to his son-in-law as a dowry and then he defaulted, the Rabbis argue that he may leave her in the state of engagement until the father-in-law redeems his pledge. However, Admon contends that the groom must marry her without the dowry or divorce her. In other words, according to Admon, the issue of the nonpayment of the dowry has no impact upon the giving of the <i>get</i>. And many authorities endorse Admon’s view.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MT Ishut</i> 23:16; <i>Tur EH</i> 52; <i>SA EH</i> 52:1; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Ribot</i> 191; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam EH</i> 64. Cf. <i>Bah, Tur EH</i> 52; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 52:1.<br>In a different factual context, some arbiters reached the same conclusion. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 317; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 72.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, we have shown that there is a persuasive and strident <i>halakhic</i> tradition of resolving financial issues and parenting arrangements after a <i>get</i> has been given. As such, both the American Orthodox rabbinate as well as the American <i>battei din</i> ought to insist that a husband address the matter of the <i>get</i> even prior to the resolution of end of marriage issues. Following their Israeli counterpart, American rabbinical courts ought to be willing and ready to recommend the giving of a <i>get,</i> issue a judgment of “<i>mitzvah</i> to divorce,” or obligate a <i>get unconditionally</i> to a divorcing couple who consent to their jurisdiction and will hopefully heed their rulings.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas coercing a <i>get</i> (<i>kofin le-garesh</i>) by a <i>beit din</i> may entail imprisonment or flogging, rendering a decision of obligating a <i>get</i> (<i>hiyuv le-garesh</i>) involves verbal persuasion such as labeling the <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband as a sinner. See <i>Sefer ha-Yashar, Teshuvot</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21. Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh Yevamot</i> 6:11 who contends that the consequence of a failure to adhere to a ruling of obligating a <i>get</i> may result in a social ban (<i>niddui</i>). Notwithstanding the Rosh’s posture, the level of sanctions differs when a <i>beit din</i> obligates a <i>get</i> rather than compels a <i>get</i>. Yet, there is a minority of authorities who argue that rendering a judgment to obligate the giving of a <i>get,</i> similar to coercing a <i>get,</i> runs afoul of the strictures of a coerced <i>get</i> (a <i>get me’useh</i>). See <i>Hazon Ish EH</i> 99:2; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 2 <i>EH</i> 10; Shlomo Karelitz, <i>Teshuvot Ateret Shlomo</i> 1:32 (6) in the name of Rashba and Rivash; <i>PDR</i> 7:201, 204 (Rabbi Elyashiv in the name of Rosh); File no. 1083672/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, January 25, 2018. See the addendum. Therefore, in certain instances a <i>beit din</i> may choose to advise the husband to fulfill the divine imperative to give a <i>get</i> or recommend the giving of it rather than obligate a <i>get.</i><br>In pursuance to the majority of decisors, the consequence of a coerced <i>get</i> is that on a Torah level, the <i>get</i> is null and void. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 30, n. 11. Whereas in Israel, the <i>battei din</i> are empowered to coerce a <i>get</i>, in the United States the rabbinical courts are legally authorized to obligate/recommend a <i>get</i> or to counsel that there is a divine commandment to give a <i>get</i>.<br>In contradistinction to the <i>get</i> compulsion and obligating orders, and in the wake of the concern for avoiding the specter of a coerced <i>get</i>, some American rabbinical courts may choose either to recommend a <i>get</i> or direct the husband that there is a divine commandment to give a <i>get</i> rather than obligate a <i>get</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 10; File no. 1083672/1, Haifa Regional Beit Din, January 25, 2018. The divine commandment to give a <i>get</i> has been understood as a directive of rabbinic advice for the husband to give the <i>get</i>. See <i>Rabbeinu Yonah, Shittah Mekubetzet, Ketuvot</i> 64a; <i>Tashbetz,</i> op. cit. To state it differently, whereas, the consequence of failing to comply with the divorce decree of “a religious duty” to give a <i>get</i> labels a husband a sinner with certain ramifications such as his ineligibility to serve as a witness in a <i>beit din</i>, should a husband fail to heed a <i>beit din</i> decree of recommending a divorce he is not following the mandate of Torah scholars rather being classified as a sinner.<br>In other words, whereas the issuance of a <i>get</i> compulsion order and the rendering of a judgment of obligating a <i>get</i> are predicated upon a husband’s physical defects such as sterility or behavior that potentially may destroy the family unit such as engaging in illicit affairs, the handing down of a decision to be duty bound to give a get or recommend a divorce is related respectively, to the husband being a sinner or a spouse receiving counsel to heed the words the instruction of a Torah scholar. See <i>Teshuvot Mishpatekha le-Ya’akov</i> 6:4 (12).<br>Alternatively, in a case of an <i>agunah</i>, some rabbinic courts may decide to hand down a judgment directing the husband that there is a divine commandment to be divorced (<i>mitzvah le-garesh</i>). See <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen, Pesakim u-Ketavim</i> 58; <i>Beit Yosef Tur EH</i> 134 in the name of Tashbetz; <i>Teshuvot Ma’amar Mordekhai</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 11.<br>Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 411 who contends that the issuance of a <i>beit din</i> directive that under certain conditions there is a commandment to be divorced is employed regarding a wife who is a sinner.<br>Once a <i>beit din</i> obligates a <i>get</i>, the giving of the <i>get</i> must be performed <i>unconditionally</i>. In other words, a husband cannot argue that the giving of a <i>get</i> is contingent upon the resolution of certain end of marriage issues such as dividing marital assets and/or parenting arrangements. The execution of the <i>get</i> must be done immediately. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 55–81.<br>On one hand, a divorce judgment of obligating a <i>get</i> precludes a husband from advancing a condition(s) prior to giving a <i>get</i> since such an order is grounded in rabbinic legislation. On the other hand, should a <i>beit din</i> either render a decision that there is a divine commandment to be divorced (<i>mitzvah le-garesh –</i> see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 208, 383; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz, Hut ha-Meshullash</i>, Tur 1, 6; <i>Teshuvot Nofet Tzufim EH</i> 129) or to recommend the giving of a <i>get,</i> such a ruling is not based upon rabbinic legislation. Consequently, a husband may advance a condition(s) prior to giving a <i>get</i> provided that the wife may easily fulfill the condition(s). See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:256; <i>Bedek ha-Bayit</i> on <i>Beit Yosef, Tur EH</i> 143 in the name of Rashba; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 208; <i>Tashbetz, Teshuvot Hut ha-Meshullah</i> 1:6; <i>Teshuvot Nofet Tzufim EH</i> 129; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:60.<br>Nonetheless, upon a <i>beit din</i> issuing a decision that there is a divine imperative to give a <i>get</i> or to recommend a <i>get</i> in order to pre-empt the possibility of the wife becoming an <i>agunah</i>, should the husband continue to refuse to give a <i>get</i> and/or advance preconditions (prior to giving the <i>get</i>) which are irrational and impossible to fulfill, then the community can be directed to implement isolating measures (known as <i>Harhakot de-Rabbeinu Tam)</i> vis-à-vis the <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband<i>.</i> See written communications dated February 10, 2019 and December 21, 2019 (on file with author) from Rabbi Uriel Lavi, Presiding Dayan of Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din; Rabbi U. Lavi, “The Obligation of divorce in a plea of repulsion or only a duty to divorce,” (Hebrew), <i>Kenes ha-Dayanim,</i> 5776, 311; <i>Teshuvot Ateret Devorah</i> 2:80. The foregoing is based upon the application of <i>halakhic</i> logic (<i>sevara</i>). As we know, there are lacunae (matters which are left unaddressed) in the <i>Halakhic</i> legal system. Consequently, the system mandates that <i>under certain conditions</i> we may employ <i>halakhic</i> logic in order to resolve these issues. See <i>Bava Batra</i> 130b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ri Megas</i> 114; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> 66; <i>Sefer ha-Yashar, Teshuvot</i> 8; <i>Ramban, Commentary on the Torah, Vayikra</i> 19:2, <i>Devarim</i> 6:18; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 20:27, 55:9, 78:3; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:9; <i>Teshuvot Maharil</i> 109; <i>Sedei Hemed ha-Shalem</i>, Kelalim, Ma’arekhet sameakh, 63. For additional decisors who have addressed this matter, see Rabbi E. Shochetman, “On analogy in decision making in Jewish Law and the Foundations of Law Act,” (Hebrew) 13 <i>Shenaton Ha-Mishpat Ha-Ivri</i> 307, 315–350 (1988).<br>For the authoritativeness of invoking <i>halakhic</i> logic in decision making in twentieth-century writings, see Rabbi Menahem Kasher, <i>Mefaneah Tzefunot</i>, Yerushalayim 5736, Rabbi E. Berkovits, <i>Not in Heaven: The Nature and Function of Halakha</i>, NY: 1983, chapter 1, and this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 53–57. <br>For a brief discussion of these isolating measures, see this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 291–293.<br>Based upon the foregoing, there are four different levels of divorce enforcement: recommending a <i>get</i>, being duty bound to give a <i>get</i>, obligating a <i>get</i> and coercing a <i>get</i>. On one hand, issuing a divorce judgment without identifying the ground(s) for the divorce (<i>ilat gerushin</i>), the divorce is valid. See <i>Gittin</i> 90a; <i>Hiddushei Hatam Sofer, Gittin</i> 90b. On the other hand, pursuant to numerous decisors, a prohibition has been transgressed. See <i>Rashi Gittin</i> 90b, s.v. <i>im she’nuah</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:18; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 411; <i>Bah, Tur EH</i> 119; <i>Beit Shmuel SA EH</i> 119:2. Compare others who contend that invoking one of the above mentioned grounds entails a recommendation rather than an infraction of a prohibition. See <i>Bahag, Gittin, Beit ha-Behirah, Gittin</i> 90a; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan EH</i> 119:5; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Lublin</i> 123. See further, Y. Sharabi, “Marital reconciliation: Intervention in the resolution of marital crises and their avoidance in Halakhah and Law,” (Hebrew), Doctoral thesis submitted to Bar Ilan University, Kislev 5773, 207–209.</i>
Should a <i>beit din</i> fail to summon the husband to a hearing regarding the matter of the <i>get</i>, American orthodox rabbis ought to function as arbiters of prohibitions and permissibility (“<i>mo’reih hora’ah</i>”), and rabbinic courts ought to function as arbiters of prohibitions and permissibility, addressing the wife’s inquiry as to whether there are grounds to give a <i>get.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In other words, one may resolve this question of whether a husband is obligated to give a <i>get</i> to his wife or for that matter voiding a marriage, as these are “<i>halakhot</i> of prohibitions and permissibility” (“<i>issur ve-heter</i>”), in the absence of the husband (i.e. either a husband who was summoned to a hearing but refuses to appear, or one who was not summoned to the hearing) while being in the presence of one individual Jew, one rabbi or three rabbis functioning as arbiters of these <i>halakhot.</i> See <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen, HM</i> 3:1–2; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, <i>HM</i> 3:1; <i>Teshuvot Yehuda</i> (Gordin), <i>EH</i> 51:2; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer, OH</i> 51, <i>EH</i> 2:64; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, <i>SA EH</i> Seder ha-Get 6, 8; <i>PDR</i> 6:265, 269; File 957-61, Beit Din Yerushalayim for Monetary Matters and Yuhasin, vol. 7, 515; File no. 448866/3, Tel-Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, July 11, 2013; File no. 1086123/1, Be’er Sheva Regional Beit Din, December 20. 2018.<br>Whether the individual Jew must be knowledgeable in <i>Halakhah</i> is subject to controversy. See <i>Yam shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 3:9; <i>Teshuvot Ma’aseh Hiyah</i>, 24; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen HM</i> 3:1; File no. 448866/3, op.cit.</i> Should the arbiter determine that there is a ground(s) for the husband to give a <i>get,</i> and if upon notification of that determination to the husband he refuses to give one, the <i>beit din</i> ought to direct the community to religiously, socially and economically isolate him, measures known in rabbinic parlance as “<i>harhakot of Rabbeinu Tam.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Yashar</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 133, 166; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21.<br>Whether there is a precondition for a <i>beit din</i> to issue a <i>get</i> compulsion order or a judgment of obligating a <i>get</i> rather than a ruling that there is a commandment to give a <i>get</i> prior to directing the community to isolate a <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband is subject to debate. See File no. 8455-64-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, September 17, 2008; File no. 862233/1, Tiberias Regional Beit Din, January 8, 2013.<br>For the authority of a <i>beit din</i> to direct the community to isolate the <i>get</i> recalcitrant husband, see <i>Rema SA EH</i> 154:21; <i>Levush</i> 77:3, 134:4; <i>Seder Eliyahu</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer EH</i> 7:23.<br>Though this power does not entail excommunication (<i>niddui),</i> the same empowerment is delegated to a <i>beit din</i> concerning excommunication. See <i>Rema SA YD</i> 334:6. For <i>beit din</i> authorization in other communal matters, see <i>Rema SA HM</i> 15:5, 358:5.</i>
Furthermore, in the event that the <i>beit din</i> renders a judgment that he is obligated to give his wife a <i>get</i> and he refuses to give one, the husband remains obligated to support his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 12b; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 30; <i>SA EH</i> 160:4<i>; PDR</i> 7:290, 294, 10:94. On the other hand, some argue that the wife’s entitlement to support is contingent upon the fact that a <i>beit din</i> issues a divorce judgment. See <i>PDR</i> 12:257.<br>Even if the wife is earning a salary under these circumstances that a <i>get</i> has not been forthcoming from the husband, according to most authorities, the husband must continue to support her as a penalty for refusing to give her a <i>get</i>. See File no. 833000/12, Netanya Regional Beit Din, July 28, 2014. Cf. <i>Ha’sagot ha-Ra’vad, Ketuvot</i> 63a.</i> As such, a wife may advance a claim for this maintenance in the <i>beit din</i> should her spouse fail to pay her.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 819158/3, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, October 5, 2011; File no. 965171/1, Netanya Regional Beit Din, October 28, 2014.</i> In effect, the imposition of this support duty is a means to pressure the husband to give a <i>get</i>, a claim which does not run afoul of the strictures of “a coerced <i>get</i>” (a <i>get me’useh</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">We are dealing with “coercion by way of choice” (<i>kefiyah be-derekh bereirah).</i> In other words, the husband must choose whether to give the <i>get</i> or to pay spousal support. Such a decision is an example of indirect coercion and therefore <i>halakhically</i> does not constitute coercion and consequently under such circumstances the subsequent execution of the <i>get</i> is valid. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 1227; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1:63; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 154:21. See further, this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 142–156.</i>
<i>This “etched in stone” (“nehe’retzet” – AYW) determination that all financial matters are to be completed prior to the get is a stringency that may potentially lead to a leniency</i>. . . . <i>Failure to follow such a procedure to address the enforcement of the get in beit din accompanied by mandating support due to get recalcitrance has and only will continue to exacerbate the suffering of women whose delivery of their divorces (gittin) are delayed, to pose “many stumbling-blocks of remaining a married woman (potential of incestuous relationships – AYW) and God forbid the proliferation of bastards” as well as potentially promote a husband’s engagement in illicit affairs.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 12 text accompanying n. 33.</i>
On the other hand, those who invoke all or some of the techniques to void a marriage are well aware that “the conditions of the time” mandate such action. Distinguishing between the period of the Talmud and contemporary times, Rabbi Tzvi Pesach Frank observes:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 2:181. See also <i>Teshuvot Dvar Eliyahu</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:79.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll">During the time of the Talmud there were Jewish arbiters who were empowered to coerce him (a husband to give a <i>get</i>- AYW) and today where we are not authorized to coerce him, we may say that it is a transaction in error.</span>
Moreover, in the wake of the absence to mete out <i>get</i> coercion, the empowerment to void a marriage under certain conditions is not based only upon “a marriage in error” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) but as Rabbi Frank argues is equally upon utilizing “the wife’s clear expectation of the marriage” (<i>umdana de’mukha</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi EH</i> 1:133. See also, <i>Teshuvot Shoeil u-Meishiv</i>, Mahadura Kama, 198.</i>
Finally, the fear “lest that the daughters of Yisrael may succumb to licentiousness” (<i>shema tetzena benot Yisrael le’tarbut ra’ah</i>) due to their husband’s recalcitrance in giving a <i>get</i> has propelled authorities throughout the ages to find solutions for both the classical <i>agunah</i> whose husband has disappeared as well as the modern day <i>agunah</i> whose husband refuses to give a <i>get.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:149, 3:54; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridvaz</i> 4:1331; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 3:110; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:1; Rabbi Sternfeld, <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzion</i> 3:14; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 9, <i>EH</i> 16(8).</i> As recently noted,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For a woman’s promiscuity, see <i>Yevamot</i> 25a, 65a; <i>Ketuvot</i> 63b; <i>Nedarim</i> 90b–91a; <i>Tosafot, Nedarim</i> 90b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Nedarim</i> 90b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Gittin</i> 32a; <i>Mordekhai, Kiddushin</i> 542 in the name of Maharam; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 1:179; <i>Rema SA EH</i> 17:2; <i>Teshuvot Maharbil</i> 3:102; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:101; <i>Teshuvot Ezrat Kohen EH</i> 44; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe EH</i> 1:139, 4:83. Whether later generations are promiscuous compared to earlier generations dating back to the time of the Talmud is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 4, <i>EH</i> 11.<br>For contemporary studies noting the existence of promiscuity in the American orthodox Jewish community, see S. Grossman, “Resolving the debate over human pappiloma virus vaccination for cancer prevention in the religious world,” 51 <i>Tradition</i> 50, 67–68 (2019).<br>Clearly, there are <i>get</i> recalcitrant husbands who identify with the American Orthodox Jewish community who engage in illicit affairs and/or sire a child (children) from the relationship. Whereas, offspring sired by a wife from an illicit affair results in <i>halakhic</i> bastardy, a child sired from a husband from an act of licentiousness does not create a <i>halakhic</i> bastard. As such, <i>halakhic</i> opposition regarding voiding marriages may promote promiscuity among husbands and <i>mamzerut</i> as well as licentiousness amongst wives.</i> a husband or wife engaging in illicit affair(s) is a prevalent phenomenon today in the world of orthodox Jewry. As such, the fear of promiscuity may emerge in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (<i>igun</i>). And in the over 155 cases that I have dealt with during the span of over six years, unfortunately licentiousness does exist in all segments of the orthodox Jewish community and has transpired either when the couple is living together and/or when a couple is separated and no <i>get</i> has been forthcoming. Though in the past, this fear has driven arbiters in Israel to coerce a <i>get</i> under such conditions,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 18:13, 20:34; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 4:21, 5:26; File no. 464539/2, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Beit Din, February 18, 2017; File no. 846913/2, Haifa Regional Beit Din, June 12, 2017; File no. 1157365/1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, July 30, 2019.</i> today in the Diaspora such an option fails to exist and as we mentioned earlier we are instructed by our decisors that under such conditions we may void marriages.
Whereas others who refuse to void a marriage are being lenient regarding the prohibition of <i>halakhic</i> bastardy (<i>mamzerut</i>) since a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance may and has resulted in the wife engaging in an illicit affair(s) and/or the siring of offspring from this relationship, there are those rabbinic arbitrators (<i>dayanim</i>) or arbiters of prohibitions and permissibility (“<i>morei hora’ah</i>”) who are being stringent regarding this prohibition by invoking under certain conditions various techniques to free the wife without a <i>get</i> lest promiscuity and/or <i>halakhic</i> bastardy transpire.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For the pitfalls of staking out a position of unnecessary stringent rulings which may result in “the breach of the wall of religious observance”, see <i>Taz, SA YD</i> 293:4; Rabbi A. Kook, <i>Teshuvot Orah Mishpat, OH</i> 112. See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 16–17.<br>The common denominator between those who are endorsing under certain conditions to void a particular marriage and those who reject such an avenue to address the plight of the <i>agunah</i> is the concern to avoid bastardy in order to prevent a future marriage between bastards and individuals of legitimate birth<i>.</i> See <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov,</i> 2:16; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe</i> 3 <i>EH</i> 5.<br>Addressing the debate regarding the scope of the prohibition to bake bread with milk, lest the dairy bread might be eaten together with meat, Rabbi Yosef Karo citing Sha’arei Durah states: “Both interpretations are <i>halakhically</i> valid, since both are logically sound” (<i>Beit Yosef, Tur YD</i> 97).<br>Analogously, despite the fact that the <i>halakhic</i> propriety of voiding a marriage is open to controversy; nevertheless both positions are logically persuasive, namely the concern for avoiding <i>mamzerut</i>.</i>

Addendum

<b>Page 12, n. 4.</b> What is the difference between voiding a <i>kiddushin</i> (<i>halakhic</i> betrothal-loosely translated as marriage) and annulling a <i>kiddushin</i>?
The four scenarios in six discussions in the Talmud Bavli serve as the Talmudic precedents for empowering our rabbis (“<i>kol ha’me’kaadesh adata de’rabbanan me’kadesh</i>”) to annul a <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 3a, <i>Yevamot</i> 90b and 110a, <i>Gittin</i> 33a and 73a, <i>Bava Batra</i> 48b.</i> One type of annulment addresses a husband who instructed a <i>get</i> ought to be delivered to his wife and either granted it to her contingent upon the fulfillment of a certain condition or forwarded it to her via an agent and subsequently changed his mind and attempted to annul the <i>get</i>. Under such circumstances, <i>Halakhah</i> allows for the retroactive annulment of the <i>kiddushin</i> (“<i>afk’inhu rabbanan le-kiddushin minei</i>”).A second type of annulment transpires after the <i>kiddushin</i> and/or the marriage (<i>nissuin</i>)where a <i>get</i> was drafted and delivered either to an agent of the wife or to the wife herself but was subsequently invalidated. In both instances, the annulment is contingent upon the wife receiving a <i>get</i> (a <i>get kol de’hu</i>) even if the giving of it is against the husband’s will.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Ketuvot</i> 3a, s.v. <i>kol hamekadesh</i>; <i>Rashi, Gittin</i> 33a, s.v. <i>adatei de’rabbanan mekadesh</i>; <i>Tosafot Ri ha-Lavan, Ketuvot</i> 3a, s.v. <i>kol ha-mekadesh</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1162; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Shitah Mekubetzet, Ketuvot</i> 3a in the name of Ritva; <i>Beit ha-Behirah, Ketuvot</i> 3a;<i>Ri Megas, Beit ha-Behirah</i>, op.cit.; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ra’ah, Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Pnei Yehoshua</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 3a; <i>Yam shel Shlomo Ketuvot</i> 3:5.</i>
However, the two Talmudic cases recorded in <i>Yevamot</i> 110a and <i>Bava Batra</i> 48b dealing with annulling a <i>kiddushin</i> due to a prospective husband acting inappropriately during the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> does not require the giving of a <i>get</i>. Lest one argue that we are empowered to annul a marriage without a <i>get</i> based upon the above Talmudic precedent in other situations such as the modern-day <i>agunah</i> where a husband refuses to give a <i>get</i>, our authorities rule that annulment is limited to the cases memorialized in the Talmud.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba, Ketuvot</i> 3a, s.v. <i>kol de’mekadesh;Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1185;<i>Teshuvot ha-Radakh,</i> page 19,s.v. <i>ve’ain lomar</i>; <i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i> 124; <i>Teshuvot Zekhor Simha</i> 177; <i>Teshuvot Melamed le-Hoeil</i> 3:22.</i>
On the other hand, there are various techniques recognized under certain conditions by our authorities for voiding a <i>kiddushin</i>. For example, if a husband intentionally or unintentionally fails to disclose to his prospective wife before the marriage a major defect(<i>mum gadol</i>) such as being mentally dysfunctional, gay, a criminal, impotent, or sterile. In other words, it is “a marriage in error” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) and if the marriage is voided the wife is free to remarry without a <i>get</i>. In sum, whereas invoking marital annulment mandates the giving of a <i>get</i>, voiding a marriage has no such requirement. Whereas, marital annulment is premised upon a bona fide <i>halakhic kiddushin</i>, voiding a <i>kiddushin</i> is predicated upon the conclusion that there never was a <i>halakhic kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As we have noted elsewhere, there are authorities who utilize the term of annulling a marriage when in actuality the marriage is being voided. As such, in their minds there is one type of annulment which nullifies <i>a priori</i> the act of <i>kiddushin</i> and there is a second type of annulment which is predicated upon the notion that the <i>kiddushin</i> actually was established and <i>ex post facto</i> arbiters are empowered to annul it. See Rabbi S. Cohen, “<i>Get</i> Coercion in Contemporary Times,” (Hebrew) 11 <i>Tehumin</i> 195, 199 (5750).</i>
<b>Page 13, n. 4.</b> On the one hand argues Rabbi Avraham Sherman that given that the Torah scholars of the Jewish community are capable of identifying who are “the Torah giants of their generation” therefore we are dutibound to comply with their rulings in particular in the realm of marriage, divorce and conversion.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Rabbi A. Sherman, “The authority of the scholars of the generation in matters of personal status and conversions,” (Hebrew) 30 <i>Tehumin</i> 163 (2070). See also Rabbi A. Sherman, “The authority of the scholars of the generation in matters of personal status and conversions,” (Hebrew) 30 <i>Tehumin</i> 163 (2070); <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i>, Introduction by Rabbi Y. Abramski, Machon Yerushalayim ed.</i>
Consequently, as we noted some contemporary authorities rely upon this position to invoke the argument that voiding a marriage is within the jurisdiction of Torah giants of the generation only.
However, as we have observed, there is well-grounded tradition (<i>mesorah</i>) that opine that voiding a marriage resides in the hands of credentialed <i>halakhic</i> decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See also, Rambam, <i>Introduction to Mishneh Torah</i>, <i>Mishneh Torah, Mamrim</i> 2:1; <i>Tur HM</i> 25; Rabbi Refael Shlomo Daichovsky, <i>Lev Shomeia le-Shlomo</i> 2:37</i>
Similarly, Rabbi Dovid Babad, a renowned 19th century authority communicates to us the following tradition that he received:
<span class="poetry indentAll">“I heard from <i>ha-Gaon</i> Rav Barish Rappaport . . . that he had a <i>tradition</i> from his Rav, <i>ha-Gaon</i> Noda Bashearim, ha-Av Beit Din of Lublin, that upon receiving a question to address, he would first weigh in his mind the truthfulness of the matter according to what human reason dictates and if in his estimation human reason the matter is true, then he will delve into <i>Halakhah</i> to arrive at a decision.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havatzelet Hasharon</i> 2:28</i></span>
By invoking this tradition which addresses the mission of the credentialed arbiter in rendering a ruling in every realm of <i>Halakhah</i>, twentieth century Torah giants, Rabbis Ya’akov Breisch and Yitzhak Weiss convey to us that resolution of all cases including classical <i>agunah</i> questions where the husband is missing is within the purview of a rabbinic authority rather than only to be addressed by a Torah giant.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov, EH</i> 56 and <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 9:130.</i> Moreover, a credentialed decisor may overrule a Torah giant provided that he submitted proofs to support his position.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh, Sanhedrin</i> 4:6; <i>Teshuvot Rid</i> 72; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i>, Pesakim u-Ketavim 238; <i>Rema, SA YD</i> 242:3; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 116<i>; Pri Hadash OH</i> 496:11; <i>Noda be-Yehudah</i>, Mahadura Kama YD 55;<i>Teshuvot Ya’avetz</i> 1:5; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan YD</i> 242:23,36; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, Kil’ayim 1, letter; <i>Teshuvot Iggerot Moshe OH</i> 109,<i>YD</i> 3:88; <i>Teshuvot Mishneh Halakhot</i> 8:137; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 1, Introduction, section 12. <br>In short, with the absence of a Sanhedrin, there is no central authority which obligates the entire Jewish community. A Torah giant’s ruling is obligatory only upon those who have accepted his authority. See Rabbi S. Daichovsky, “<i>Da’at Torah</i> in <i>Halakhah</i>,” (Hebrew), 30 <i>Tehumin</i> 174,182(5770). In the absence of accepting his authority, decisions are rendered by credentialed arbiters who are accepted by members of the community. See further this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 1, 15–64. Cf. others who construe a Torah giant as a mentor for the community. See <i>Tosafot Berakhot</i> 31b, s.v. <i>moreh</i>; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 1:138.<br>Lest one claim that the system is reduced to the arbiter’s whims, there are guidelines for overruling a contemporary decisor or a predecessor which guarantee the integrity of the decision making process. See <i>Rashbam, Bava Batra</i> 131a, s.v. <i>ve’al tigmeru</i>; <i>Teshuvot of Rabbi</i> Avraham, son of Rambam 97; <i>Beit ha-Behira</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 130b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322 and the abovementioned sources. For example, one of the guidelines is to follow under certain conditions the view of the wiser decisor. See <i>Avodah  Zarah</i> 7a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 94:5; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba </i> 1:253; <i>Rema, SA HM</i> 25:2; <i>Shakh, SA YD</i> 242:8; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev, OH</i> 496:96. (Cf. <i>Rid</i>, op. cit.; <i>Kesef Mishneh, MT , Mamrim</i> 1:5 in the name of Rambam.) Consequently, some authorities contend that in light of the opinions of Torah giants of earlier generations, an arbiter must refrain from rendering an autonomous judgment. See <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, shoresh 159; Rabbi Y. Karo, <i>Introduction to Beit Yosef</i>; <i>Get Pashut</i>, Kelal 5; <i>Urim ve-Tumim HM</i> 25.</i>
Clearly, when dealing with an issue such as the propriety of introducing the solution of a conditional marriage (<i>tenai be-nissu’in</i>) as one of the avenues of establishing marital procedures for the Jewish community,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 4, 21-48.</i> there exists a practice (a <i>nohag</i>) to enlist the consensus of Torah giants of the generation in order to validate these procedures.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Shalom Schwadron and Rabbi Hayyim Ozer Grodzinsky, <i>Ein Tenai be-Nissu’in</i>, Vilna, 1930, 22, 37, 56.</i>
However, when dealing with private matters of conversion and marriage and divorce, every <i>beit din</i> and/or rabbinic authorities are empowered to resolve these issues.
<b>Page 17, n. 17</b>. Voiding a marriage may under certain conditions be executed in a situation of a dire need upon the reliance of a singular view entailing the Biblical matter of the prohibition of a married woman (an <i>eishit ish</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8, <i>OH</i> 34(6), 10 <i>YD</i> 43(3-4).  See further this writer’s <i>Rabbinic Authority</i>, vol. 3, 245–256.</i>
Given that the application of following the majority rule is limited to resolving issues within the confines of a <i>halakhic</i>-judicial proceeding (a <i>moshav beit din</i>) where give and take transpires face to face between the <i>dayan</i> and the minority may express his view, throughout the ages we usually are dealing with an intergenerational and/or international dispute where we do not have the ability to decide which opinion carries greater or lesser weight.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Get Pashut, Kelalim , Kelal</i> 1,5; <i>Teshuvot Maharlbah</i> 147; <i>Beit Yosef HM</i> 13(end) in the name of Rashba,<i>Teshuvot She’elat Ya’avetz</i> 1:157; <i>Hazon Ish, Kil’ayim</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Havatzelet ha-Sharon OH</i> 2.</i>
That being said some arbiters contend that even if normative <i>Halakhah</i> is reflective of the majority issue regarding a particular issue, nevertheless one may invoke a double <i>halakhic</i> doubt even with the minority view in variance with the majority opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Zivhei Tzedek YD</i> 110:155; <i>Teshuvot Beit Dovid YD</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Devar Moshe</i> 3, <i>YD</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Shemesh u- Tzedakah YD</i> 50; <i>Teshuvot Hesed le-Avraham, OH</i> 13.</i>
<b>Page 55, n. 12.</b> Upon a pending divorce where a <i>beit din</i> obligates a <i>get</i> and a wife refuses to accede to her husband’s demands, it may be viewed by the Israeli rabbinical courts as “chaining herself” (<i>me’agenet et atzma</i>) to the marriage . Said conclusion is contingent upon the <i>beit din</i> understanding that these demands are rational such as a husband requesting custody be given to him rather than to his parentally unfit wife or recovering monies stolen from him by his spouse. In other words, the <i>beit din</i> panel may attempt to persuade the wife to remit stolen assets or other benefits which are reasonable demands in order that he may be willing to give a <i>get</i>. Under such conditions, should a wife refuse to comply with such demands, a husband’s <i>get</i> recalcitrance is <i>halakhically</i> legitimate.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 21:176,181; File no. 1-64-5082, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, May 29, 2002; File no. 7479-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, November 18, 2007; File no. 7041-21-1, Beit Din ha-Rabbani ha-Gadol, March 11, 2008; File no. 272088/6, Netanya Regional Beit Din, January 1, 2011.</i>
<b>Page 61, n. 31.</b> On the other hand, we should note that there are many authorities who will invoke as a supporting argument for leniency (<i>senif le’hakeil</i>) Rambam’s controversial posture memorialized in Mishneh Torah, Ishut 14:8 that upon a wife advancing a plea of repulsion rather than attempting to torment him, a <i>get</i> may be compelled in a situation of “an hour of emergency” and <i>get</i> recalcitrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8, 4:35(end); <i>PDR</i> 1:18, 3:3, 8:126, 9:171; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3, <i>EH</i> 20(34).</i>
<b>Page 80,n. 23.</b> Lest one contend that one should validate the witnesses and thus the act of <i>kiddushin</i> based upon the adoption of the stringent view, Rabbi Yitzhak Elhanan Spektor opines otherwise. In Rabbi Spektor’s mind, in a case of invalid witnesses one ought to follow the lenient opinion which he characterizes as “the letter of <i>Halakhah</i>”(<i>ikar hadin</i>) and concludes that the <i>kiddushin</i> be voided rather than act strictly based upon a stringency.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 64(2).</i>
<b>Page 83, n. 37.</b> For precedent for those decisors who argue that “we are witnesses” may be employed as a means to validate testimony for the establishing the act of <i>kiddushin</i>, see <i>Mordekhai, Kiddushin</i> 531; <i>Beit Shmuel, SA EH</i> 42:12.Cf. additional antecedents for those arbiters who reject the mechanism of “we are witnesses” as an avenue to validate testimony, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1193; <i>Rema, SA EH</i> 42:4; <i>Tumim, HM</i> 90:14.

<b>Page 128, line 21.</b> As we have demonstrated in the wake of the controversy between Rosh and Rashba, the propriety of utilizing a borrowed ring generates a <i>halakhic</i> doubt regarding the validity of the act of <i>kiddushin</i>. As such one may combine this doubt with the other doubt(s) which we presented concerning the act of <i>kiddushin</i> and rule leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6, <i>EH</i> 6(7) in the name of various decisors.</i>  Cf. other authorities who mandate the existence of at least three doubts prior to ruling leniently.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avnei  Nezer EH</i> 119(64); <i>Seder Eliyahu Rabbah</i> ,Sha’ar 1 (end); <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon EH</i> 5; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 1:22 (in a case of <i>get</i> recalcitrance).</i>
<b>Page 181, n. 74.</b> Construing the voiding of a marriage based upon an “error in the marriage” (<i>kiddushei ta’ut</i>) to be grounded in “the meeting of the minds” (<i>gemirat da’at</i>) which is consummated by the act of <i>kiddushin</i> or based upon the violation of a condition (<i>tenai</i>) is subject to debate amongst other decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekor Hayyim</i>, Biurim 448:9.Cf.<i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i> 255; <i>Teshuvot Birkat Retzeh</i> 14; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak HM</i> 64.</i>

<b>Page 230, n. 74.</b> Cf. other arbiters who argue that if epilepsy is viewed as a major defect (a <i>mum gadol</i>), then the wife never accepted her husband’s epilepsy.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Mikneh</i>, Kuntres Aharon 39:5; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 2, <i>EH</i> 9.</i>
<b>Page 318, n. 36.</b> . . . January 25, 2018. There are some rabbinical courts who refrain from obligating a <i>get</i> due to the decisors who contend that should there emerge a <i>halakhic</i> error in obligating a husband to give a <i>get</i> in the particular circumstances, a coerced <i>get</i> (a <i>get me’useh</i>) is generated. In other words, his willingness to give a <i>get</i> was predicated upon his perception that <i>Halakhah</i> mandated that a <i>get</i> ought to be given. As such, he willingly gave a <i>get</i> to his wife. In the wake of the emergence of the error, it became clear that <i>Halakhah</i> did not mandate a <i>get</i> ought to be given. As such the giving of it is <i>halakhically</i> construed as being coerced. And there are others who argue “a <i>get</i> in error” (<i>a get mut’eh</i>) has been produced. In other words, had the husband known that there was no basis to execute a <i>get</i>; he never would have given it. Consequently, the divorce via the <i>get</i> is null and void.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, op. cit.; <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov EH</i> 168(244b-245a).</i>
For one of the most systematic and comprehensive treatments of the <i>halakhic</i> concept of “a <i>get</i> in error”, see <i>Ateret Devorah</i> 1:86. For a recent practical application of this concept, see File no. 1066559/12, Yerushalayim Regional Beit Din, March 27, 2020.
